Chapter 1: The Tribunal Opens in Ash

Sable Array had been built to hear enemies before they arrived.

Now it listened to the dead.

Ira Senn saw the station through the shuttle's scarred forward glass as a broken ring of black spars and amber windows turning above the Ash Belt. Half its old military dishes still faced outward, fixed on routes no fleet used anymore. The other half had been folded inward, repurposed as court receivers, press relays, witness locks, crowd-control sensors, and archive dishes. From a distance the conversion looked graceful. Up close it looked like someone had taught a weapon to sit politely at a table.

"First time?" the pilot asked.

Ira kept her hands folded over the hard case in her lap. "To Sable, yes."

"To a tribunal?"

"No."

The pilot snorted, not unkindly. "Then first time."

He was old enough to have been alive during the last years of the Fracture Wars. Most people who made that fact visible did it by accident: a hesitation before old gate names, a reflexive glance toward pressure doors, a dislike of countdown sounds. The pilot wore his war in the way he left every channel open at low volume, as if silence were more dangerous than noise.

Sable Array rotated, and the sun caught the rebuilt tribunal hall along its inner curve. The windows there were new, too large and too clean, a ribbon of legal confidence welded onto military bone. Beyond the station, the Ash Belt spread in dim fragments: mining shells, station graves, salvage yards, convoy shelters, half-melted mirrors, and the blue-white pricks of habitats that had survived by becoming small enough to be missed.

Ira had grown up under those lights. From her childhood bunk in Cinderwake Minor, the Belt had not looked ruined. It had looked busy. Adults called wreckage inventory, hunger ration discipline, grief continuity, delay process. Children learned the true names later, usually from someone too tired to lie.

The shuttle's docking alarm chimed.

Every passenger in the cabin flinched except the court clerks.

The clerks had trained themselves out of it. Ira distrusted them immediately.

Her case warmed against her knees as the tribunal credential inside answered Sable's lock. The display embedded in the handle flashed green:

IRA SENN

ASSOCIATE ADVOCATE, EVIDENCE REVIEW

MERIDIAN REMNANT TRIBUNAL

TEMPORARY AUTHORITY: CINDER GATE DOCKET

Temporary authority. The phrase had seemed grand when the appointment arrived. On Sable's approach, it looked like a narrow pressure patch over a hull breach.

"You carrying anything alive?" the pilot asked.

"No."

"Anything that claims it is alive?"

Ira glanced at him.

He shrugged. "Glassward traffic today. Court wants all the careful questions."

"Only archive material."

"Documents can be worse."

The docking collar took them with a metal sigh. The cabin pressure equalized in three stages, each one a little too slow. Around Ira, passengers unlatched restraints: survivor delegates in dark ash cloth, two routeholder observers with immaculate cuffs, a press woman already recording, a Compact liaison pretending not to watch anyone, three station militia officers, and a child with a memorial tag pinned to his coat. The tag bore a name too old to be his.

Ira waited until the aisle cleared. Her mother had taught her that: never hurry toward a court that wanted you small.

The corridor beyond the lock smelled of sealant, hot metal, and too many bodies breathing recycled air. Sable Array had doubled its population for the opening session, and the station was handling the insult with visible resentment. Temporary guide lines glowed along the deck. White for tribunal staff. Blue for witnesses. Green for medical. Gray for press. Red for restricted evidence. Black for survivors who had refused color assignment and won.

Ira followed white.

At the first checkpoint, a security woman scanned her credentials, her case, her palms, and the inside of her mouth. The woman's uniform bore no faction patch, only the tribunal's temporary mark: a circle broken in four places so no one could mistake it for unity.

"Advocate Senn," the woman said. "You are assigned to Evidence Review Subroom Three until called."

"I was told Magistrate Om wanted junior counsel present before opening."

"Magistrate Om wants many things before opening."

Ira held the woman's gaze.

The security woman looked down at the case. "You have a private instruction packet."

"Yes."

"From whom?"

"The appointment seal says tribunal internal."

"That was not my question."

Ira felt the first small test open under her feet. Sable Array had been a listening station; it still believed every arrival should reveal what it carried if pressed from the correct angle.

"Then ask the tribunal internal office," Ira said. "I am not authorized to improve their labeling."

The security woman studied her for another breath. Then she returned the credential.

"White line. Do not cross black unless invited. Do not answer press questions in motion. Do not say gate failure in public corridors unless you enjoy being shouted at."

"What should I say?"

"Nothing works."

Ira took the corridor.

The station opened around her in layers. To her left, a viewing well showed the public concourse below, where survivors had already filled the permitted assembly zones. They stood in family clusters, station blocs, union rows, militia knots, and solitary islands of people who had outlived everyone authorized to stand with them. Their signs were not uniform. That comforted Ira more than it should have.

At the edge of the concourse, a tribunal clerk knelt beside a portable access terminal and tried to explain capacity law to a family of five.

Only two of them had blue witness bands.

The oldest man kept offering the clerk a folded death certificate, as if paper could turn into oxygen allowance if pressed into the right hand. Beside him, two women held the elbows of a third, younger woman whose face had the bright stillness of someone refusing to collapse in public. A toddler slept against her shoulder, one fist caught in the fabric of her collar.

The clerk's voice reached Ira through a maintenance pickup, thin and embarrassed.

"The hearing hall cannot seat all related claimants during opening. Additional family members may enter under observer status after primary authentication."

"She is the mother," one of the women said.

"The system lists her as associated claimant."

"The child was born after. That is why."

The clerk looked at the toddler and then at the terminal, where the terminal was winning.

Ira felt the first anger of the day arrive with legal precision. Not the hot anger that made people foolish. The colder kind, the kind that noticed which rule had harmed whom and began arranging words around it.

Above the concourse, the white guide line waited for her.

Below it, the clerk changed nothing.

CINDER WAS SELECTION.

NO VERDICT WITHOUT NAMES.

WE WERE NOT CARGO.

GATE LOGS OR LIES.

WHO BOARDED FIRST?

One sign carried only a number:

11,402

The child from the shuttle stood beneath that sign.

Ira recognized the memorial tag on his coat before she recognized him. The tag's printed name was older than his face by at least three generations, the letters laminated in the style used before the Compact standardized grief documents. Someone had punched a second hole through the plastic and threaded it with black wire, not ribbon. Ribbon meant ceremony. Wire meant the family had stopped trusting things that could be cut politely.

The child was perhaps nine. Old enough to understand that adults lower their voices around useful lies, young enough to keep looking up at every uniform as if authority might accidentally become honest. Beside him stood a woman with one sleeve pinned empty at the elbow. She held a stack of paper copies against her chest. Not petitions. Petitions had margins, seals, submission codes. These were personal lists, names written at different times by different hands and then recopied until ink became inheritance.

A press lens drifted toward them.

The woman turned the child behind her without looking.

The lens tried to adjust around her shoulder.

Black-line stewards moved first.

They were not tribunal security. That mattered. Security protected procedure. Black-line stewards protected the people who had refused the tribunal's colors. Three of them stepped between the lens and the child with the practiced coordination of people who had spent years shielding grief from documentation. No one touched the press device. Touching it would have made the device a victim. They simply stood so close that every angle became cloth, scar, and silence.

The press woman below raised both hands in theatrical innocence.

One steward pointed to the child's tag, then to the sign, then to the floor.

Ira could not hear the words through the viewing glass, but she understood the grammar.

You can film the number. You cannot harvest the boy.

The press lens withdrew.

Not far. Far enough to pretend restraint.

Ira's white guide line pulsed at her feet, urging motion. She stayed one second longer than necessary.

A routeholder observer in the upper corridor stopped beside her. He wore immaculate cuffs and a mourning pin shaped like a broken gate. The pin was expensive enough to insult the dead. He looked down at the concourse with the weary pity of a man rehearsing moderation.

"They will make this impossible," he said.

Ira did not know him. That made the sentence worse; strangers offered generalizations when they wanted accomplices.

"Who?" she asked.

He tilted his head toward the black-line stewards, the signs, the woman with the papers, the child hidden behind her coat. "People who need the tribunal to be a memorial service instead of a court."

Ira looked at his mourning pin. "Courts also hear the living."

"Courts hear evidence."

"Evidence is usually brought by the living."

His polite expression thinned. "Associate Advocate Senn, is it? I hope Magistrate Om teaches you the difference between grief and proof before the opening record begins."

He knew her name. Of course he did. Sable had listened to everyone before they arrived.

Ira thought of the hard case warming against her palm, the temporary authority in its handle, the private instruction packet she had not yet opened. Then she looked down again at the woman with the lists. The woman had bent to the child now and was adjusting the old tag so it lay flat. The boy stared past her toward the white line corridor where Ira stood.

Not accusation.

Worse.

Expectation.

"I hope so too," Ira said.

The routeholder heard agreement because agreement was what he had paid his life to expect. He gave a small approving nod and continued down the corridor.

Ira waited until his steps faded before she moved.

The white line brightened under her shoes, impatient as a clerk.

She followed it and carried the boy's tag with her in the part of the mind where numbers stopped being abstract and began asking who had been allowed to count.

The official casualty range for the Cinder Gate Abandonment was 8,900 to 12,700, depending on which authority counted legal residents, temporary labor, unregistered children, military dependents, station-copied minds, or people who had entered evacuation queues under another person's ration name. Ira had read the numbers until they stopped looking like numbers. Then she had read witness statements until they became numbers again.

Below, a survivor saw the white line above the concourse and raised two fingers to her own eyes, then pointed at Ira.

I am watching you.

Ira did not nod. A nod could become promise. A promise could become evidence. She kept walking and felt cowardly for being correct.

Subroom Three was smaller than she expected. A half-moon table faced a wall of dark displays. Evidence clerks had stacked sealed packets in physical trays despite the station's digital capacity, a concession to Ash Belt distrust. If something could be held, it could at least be stolen honestly.

Magistrate Om stood alone at the center display.

He was taller than Ira had imagined, thin in the way old Belt people became when they had survived ration discipline and never fully stopped obeying it. His robe was plain charcoal. No gold, no Compact blue, no routeholder braid. His head was shaved. His left hand was artificial from the wrist down, an older model with visible service screws. He was reading a list of names when Ira entered.

He did not turn. "Associate Advocate Senn."

"Magistrate."

"You are late."

Ira checked the room clock. "The docking collar delayed equalization."

"That is why you are late. It does not make you less late."

"No, Magistrate."

He touched the display. The names vanished. "Good. People who argue with clocks should not work Cinder."

Ira set her case on the table only after he gestured permission. "My appointment packet mentioned evidence review, not advocacy."

"It mentioned both."

"It obscured both."

Om looked at her then. His eyes were very dark and very tired. "Yes."

That answer did more to unsettle Ira than a rebuke would have.

He tapped the case. "Open it."

The lock accepted her palm. Inside lay a thin evidence wafer, a printed docket authority, and a strip of black ash cloth folded around a metal tag. Ira did not touch the cloth.

Om watched her notice it.

"The tag is not evidence," he said.

"Then why is it in an evidence packet?"

"Because the person who sent it does not trust categories."

"Who sent it?"

Before Om could answer, the wall display lit.

A woman appeared in relay grain, seated somewhere with poor light and excellent encryption. Her hair was cut close to the scalp. Archive fatigue showed in the set of her mouth. Ira recognized her from Shoals Compact coverage: Vela Cho, the archive specialist who had helped file the agreement no one in the route offices could summarize without causing an incident.

"Magistrate Om," Vela said. "Associate Senn. I apologize for the timing."

Om's expression did not change. "No, you don't."

"No," Vela admitted. "But the words remain customary."

Ira kept silent.

Vela's gaze shifted toward her through the delay. "You are younger than I expected."

"I can become older during the hearing if it helps."

Om made a small sound that might have been disapproval.

Vela almost smiled. "Keep that until it becomes dangerous, then stop."

The evidence wafer in Ira's case woke and projected a narrow line of text:

PROTECTED RECORD TRANSFER REQUEST

SOURCE: SHOALS COMPACT CARRIED WARNING CHAIN

ATTACHED MATTER: CINDER GATE TIMING DISCREPANCY

HANDLING LIMIT: DO NOT SUMMARIZE SILENCE

The room seemed to lose a degree of warmth.

Om read the line twice.

"This docket opens in nineteen minutes," he said.

"Then you have eighteen minutes to decide whether the tribunal begins with an incomplete lie," Vela answered.

Ira looked at the phrase again: Cinder Gate timing discrepancy.

Every public brief, every school module, every memorial line she had ever read began the same way. During the seventeenth year of the Fracture Wars, hostile action destabilized the Cinder Meridian gate during emergency evacuation. Military command narrowed the transit window. Civilian losses were catastrophic. Responsibility remained disputed because primary telemetry was corrupted.

Gate failure.

The phrase she had been warned not to say in corridors.

Vela's packet said timing discrepancy instead.

Om folded his artificial hand over his living one. "What kind of discrepancy?"

Vela did not soften her voice.

"The gate narrowed before the attack alarm."

For a moment, Sable Array's old listening systems seemed to wake inside the walls.

Ira heard the crowd below through three decks of metal.

Or imagined she did.

Om closed his eyes.

When he opened them, he looked older, but not surprised enough.

That was the first thing Ira would remember later: not the packet, not the ash cloth, not Vela's face in relay grain, but the exact insufficiency of Magistrate Om's surprise.

"Associate Senn," he said, "seal this room."

Ira reached for the control.

Outside, the tribunal bells began to sound.

The dead had arrived early.

Chapter 2: A Gate Failure With Too Many Clocks

Magistrate Om sealed Subroom Three with three authorities and one old habit.

The authorities were visible: his tribunal key, Ira's evidence review credential, and Vela Cho's protected transfer seal burning on the display in white. The old habit was quieter. Om stood between the door and the evidence table as if pressure, violence, and history all entered rooms through the same opening.

Ira noticed. She wished she had not.

"Associate Senn," he said, "read the packet header aloud."

The tribunal bells continued outside, low and formal. Each tone marked a procedural stage: assembly, witness protection activation, public recording, judicial entry. In nineteen minutes, now seventeen, the main hall would expect Magistrate Om to walk through the inner door and declare that the Meridian Remnant Tribunal recognized the Cinder Gate docket. Survivors below would listen for the shape of the first lie.

Ira lifted the wafer. "Protected record transfer request. Source: Shoals Compact carried warning chain. Attached matter: Cinder Gate timing discrepancy. Handling limit: do not summarize silence."

"Again," Om said.

She read it again.

"What does it not say?"

Ira looked at the line until the absence sharpened. "It does not say gate failure."

"Good."

Vela's image flickered as the relay corrected for route delay. "The phrase gate failure is a conclusion disguised as a description."

"It is also the tribunal's docket title," Om said.

"Then the tribunal begins contaminated."

Om accepted the blow without flinching. "Most courts do."

Ira set the wafer into the reader. "May I open the attached matter?"

"That is why you are here."

"I thought I was here because my appointment packet obscured both evidence review and advocacy."

"Also that."

The reader unfolded the record into four vertical streams. Ira expected one timeline. She got four clocks.

CLOCK A: SABLE ARRAY MILITARY ARCHIVE

CLOCK B: ROUTEHOLDER CONVOY INSURANCE LOG

CLOCK C: CINDER NINE MAINTENANCE RELAY

CLOCK D: SHOALS CARRIED WARNING ALIGNMENT

Clock D did not display numbers. It showed pressure intervals translated into human time approximations and surrounded by warning marks Ira had seen only in the public reporting around the Shoals Compact. The marks made the neat columns beside them look rude.

"Why is Orison data in an Ash Belt war-crime docket?" she asked.

Vela answered, "Because Book Seven happened."

Om's eyes moved toward her.

Vela sighed. "Because the Shoals carried warning chain routed a Pale Arc rhythm through several human hazard offices. One office matched its boundary-pulse structure against old meridian disturbance archives. Cinder Gate produced an anomalous correction burst with a related shape."

"Related how?" Ira asked.

"Enough to open a question. Not enough to answer it."

That was an honest archivist's answer. Ira respected it and hated it.

Om touched Clock A. "Official sequence."

The first stream expanded.

17:42:08 - evacuation queue stable.

17:43:31 - hostile alarm received from outer picket.

17:44:02 - gate corridor turbulence detected.

17:44:19 - military command narrows transit window.

17:45:56 - Cinder Nine civilian queue loses lock.

17:46:10 - enemy fire confirmed.

17:48:44 - gate collapse begins.

17:51:03 - surviving convoy clears.

Ira had seen those numbers in every training brief. They were as familiar as prayer and just as edited.

Om touched Clock B.

The routeholder insurance log opened with more precision and less shame.

17:41:57 - priority convoy mass revised downward.

17:42:04 - civilian attachment risk marked excessive.

17:42:11 - unbonded dependent category removed from protected transfer estimate.

17:42:38 - transit premium adjusted.

17:42:55 - gate corridor narrowing recommended.

17:43:00 - military liaison acknowledgement pending.

17:43:29 - hostile alarm received.

Ira felt the room tilt.

"Before the alarm," she said.

Om did not answer.

Vela did. "Yes."

The log had another field folded under the timestamp.

Ira touched it before asking. The reader hesitated, then opened a narrow sidecar labeled LOCAL DISPLAY TRANSLATION. It should have been useless, the kind of auxiliary table clerks stripped away when evidence became respectable.

Instead it showed how the insurance categories had been rendered for the people standing in Cinder Gate's bay.

GREEN - BOARDING PRIORITY MAINTAINED.

AMBER - HOLD PENDING LOAD BALANCE.

GRAY - REASSIGNMENT SUBJECT TO COMMAND CONFIRMATION.

BLACK - NO CURRENT TRANSIT EXPECTANCY.

At the bottom sat a checksum from the bay lamp controller.

17:42:20 - display translation accepted.

17:42:21 - public routing lamps updated.

"That is not just modeling," Ira said.

Om's jaw moved once.

Vela leaned closer to her relay pickup. "No. It is model language leaving the room where everyone could later pretend it was harmless."

Ira imagined the lamps changing above the queue while nobody had yet announced an attack. Green doors brightening. Amber lines freezing. Gray families hearing reassignment as a bureaucratic delay instead of a verdict. Black stations becoming future arguments over whether expectation had ever existed.

She wrote one more phrase on her pad.

Classification can move faster than orders.

Ira forced herself to keep looking. The insurance log was not an order. That would be the defense. Recommendation was not command. Risk modeling was not selection. Premium adjustment was not murder. Every line had been written by people who understood exactly how to stand one step away from culpability.

"What is unbonded dependent?" she asked.

Om's living hand closed.

Vela's face hardened. "Children without a registered guardian in the evacuation queue. Wartime labor minors. Informal dependents. Some elderly. Anyone attached socially but not cleanly attached administratively."

"Cargo?"

"Not even cargo," Om said. "Cargo had manifests."

Ira wrote the phrase because it sounded like a verdict pretending to be logistics.

Cargo had manifests.

Children had attachment risk.

The distinction looked absurd on paper. It would look less absurd in argument, she knew, because advocates were paid to make cruelty sound like classification. A child without a registered guardian became an administrative uncertainty. An administrative uncertainty became a risk variable. A risk variable became excess mass. Excess mass became the thing nobody had ordered removed, only failed to preserve.

"Show me the category table," Ira said.

Om's eyes narrowed. "Associate."

"Clock B references the category. If the table traveled with the log, it is part of the record."

Vela's image held still for half a second, the relay delay making her silence seem deliberate. "It traveled."

Om opened the appendix.

The category table appeared without drama, which made it worse. Rows of abbreviations, thresholds, priority colors, and transfer assumptions arranged in the clean geometry of people who expected never to meet the bodies their work sorted.

BONDED CREW FAMILY - GREEN

CONTRACTED TECHNICAL DEPENDENT - GREEN

LICENSED MEDICAL LOAD - GREEN

ROUTEHOLDER PROPERTY RECOVERY - AMBER

REGISTERED CIVILIAN KIN - AMBER

UNBONDED DEPENDENT - GRAY

UNDECLARED SOCIAL ATTACHMENT - GRAY

STATION-LOCAL NONCONTRACTED - BLACK

Below the table, a caution note blinked in old insurance language:

GRAY AND BLACK CATEGORIES DO NOT CONSTITUTE REJECTED PERSONS WITHOUT COMMAND CONFIRMATION.

Ira almost laughed. The sound stayed trapped under her ribs.

"Rejected persons," she said.

"Without command confirmation," Om replied.

"A mercy added by counsel."

"A shield added by counsel."

"Those are sometimes the same object viewed from different sides."

Om looked at her then, not as if she had spoken well, but as if she had stepped near a hole in the floor.

Vela said, "Look at the color names."

Ira looked again.

Green.

Amber.

Gray.

Black.

The child's voice had said they moved the green ships first.

Not official ships. Not medical ships. Not rescue ships.

Green ships.

"They were using the table in the queue," Ira said.

Om did not deny it.

"Maybe not verbally," Vela said. "Maybe with light bands. Gate bays used colored routing lamps during overload. Children notice colors before categories."

Ira imagined a station concourse under evacuation light: families pressed against railings, routeholder craft at the forward locks, militia trying to shout order over alarms, and somewhere above or beneath it a child hidden in duct M-14 learning the law by color.

"Can we identify Mara?" she asked.

The question changed the room more sharply than she expected. Om's face closed. Vela looked down, perhaps at notes Ira could not see.

"There are seventeen possible Maras in the Cinder Nine unconfirmed lists," Vela said. "Three were children. Six were registered adults. One was a maintenance apprentice. Seven appear only as kin-name fragments in survivor testimony."

"And the speaker?"

"Unknown."

"Child-size mass detected."

"That proves scale, not identity."

"It proves someone was small enough for the system to notice as an exception."

"Yes."

Ira's pen had pressed so hard into the paper that the word exception tore at the edge.

Om said, "If you take this into opening as a named child without authentication, Kett will turn the hearing into a lesson on evidentiary hygiene before survivor counsel speaks."

"I am not proposing that."

"You are thinking it."

"I am thinking that the law recognizes a child only when it can spell the child's name correctly."

"The law recognizes what it can test."

"And when the thing worth testing was designed to vanish?"

Vela answered before Om could. "Then you preserve the shape of the absence. You do not fill it with your own need."

That was the handling limit again. Do not summarize silence.

Ira looked at the table until the colors lost their bureaucratic neatness and became positions in a line. Green moved. Amber waited. Gray could be removed from the estimate without anyone signing a death order. Black had already been written outside the room.

"What happens if we ask for the lamp logs?" she said.

Om gave her a look that contained irritation, calculation, and something almost like approval. "The defense says routing lamps are mechanical indicators, not boarding authority. Routeholders say insurance categories do not control military action. Military counsel says category advisories did not compel command. Everyone asks why Evidence Review is chasing lights instead of orders."

"And if the lamp colors match the table before the alarm?"

No one answered quickly.

Vela said at last, "Then the child in the duct becomes corroboration, not spectacle."

Ira wrote that too.

Corroboration, not spectacle.

For the first time since the wafer had opened, the packet felt less like a wound and more like a set of handles. Not enough to lift the dead. Nothing was enough for that. But perhaps enough to lift the lie without breaking it into pieces too small for court.

The third bell sounded outside.

Public recording had begun.

Ira imagined the main hall filling with the sound of people trying not to breathe too loudly.

"Open Clock C," she said.

Om looked at her.

"Please," she added, because anger was not authority even when it was correct.

He opened it.

Cinder Nine Maintenance Relay was corrupted. Half the stream displayed as gray loss. The surviving lines came from environmental systems, pressure locks, duct thermometers, emergency lamps, and hand terminals too cheap to receive official erasure protocols.

17:41:52 - Duct M-14 manual panel opened.

17:41:59 - unauthorized child-size mass detected.

17:42:03 - local audio begins.

The reader offered playback.

No one moved.

Ira did not ask permission this time. She started it.

The sound was terrible because it was ordinary. Metal vibration. A child's breath too close to the pickup. Someone far away shouting about line numbers. A gate tone pulsing through station structure like a giant heart trying to remember its work.

Then a voice, young and thin:

"Mara, don't go back. It changed. It changed before they said."

A second voice, farther away: "Stay in the duct."

"They moved the green ships first."

"Stay in the duct."

"The family line is still outside."

Static swallowed the rest.

Ira stopped the playback.

The room had become very small.

Om's artificial hand clicked once at the thumb joint. "That audio was ruled inadmissible in the first inquiry."

"On what basis?"

"Chain of custody."

"Who challenged it?"

"Military logistics counsel."

"Who ruled?"

He looked at the blank wall behind her. "A wartime inquiry panel."

Ira understood before he said it.

"You were on it."

"No. I was clerk to it."

"That is not no."

Om returned his gaze to her. "Correct."

The fourth bell sounded. Judicial entry in ten minutes.

Vela spoke before Ira could decide whether she had just become insubordinate. "Clock D is why I sent this now."

The Orison-marked stream expanded. It did not give Ira the comfort of sequence. It showed a relation between pulses: Cinder Gate correction burst, Pale Arc boundary rhythm, Lumen missing-neutrino interval, Shoals carried warning, Moonwake receipt delay. The human time approximations crawled beside them like embarrassed translations.

"This is not saying Orison knew about Cinder," Vela said. "Do not let anyone say that."

"Then what is it saying?" Ira asked.

Oru-Kel's name appeared in the annotation field:

WARNING PATTERNS DO NOT BELONG TO ONE PLACE BECAUSE HUMANS NAME PLACES SEPARATELY.

Ira read the line twice.

Om said, "That will not survive opening argument."

"It is not opening argument," Vela said. "It is handling instruction."

Ira leaned closer. "Clock D says the correction burst has the same shape as a later warning."

"A related shape," Vela corrected.

"Related enough that if the tribunal says gate failure, it may erase the wrong thing."

"Yes."

The fifth bell sounded.

Om closed all four streams except the headers. "We cannot introduce this entire packet in opening session."

Ira stared at him. "Why not?"

"Because the tribunal will collapse into admissibility argument before it establishes jurisdiction."

"If jurisdiction is established over a false premise, what exactly are we preserving?"

"The only room in which the false premise can be attacked."

Ira had no immediate answer. She disliked that because it meant he might be right in the narrow way that trapped people.

Vela looked between them through relay grain. "The packet's handling limit is not a suggestion. If you summarize the silence, you damage the evidence."

"Define silence," Om said.

"Missing telemetry. Removed dependent categories. The child's unfinished audio. The gap between recommendation and alarm. Orison warning relation that cannot be translated into causation yet. The parts every institution will try to replace with smoother words."

Ira wrote the list by hand on the notepad she carried for moments when screens felt complicit.

Missing telemetry.

Removed dependent categories.

Unfinished child audio.

Gap before alarm.

Warning without ownership.

"What do you need from me?" she asked.

Om answered before Vela could. "You will sit behind Evidence Review and say nothing unless called."

"That is not what I asked."

"It is what I am ordering."

Vela said, "I need someone young enough that the old settlements have not trained every useful outrage out of them, and disciplined enough not to spend it in the first hour."

Ira's pen stopped.

Vela continued, "I need the packet preserved. I need the docket language challenged at the first procedural opening. I need the child audio not played for spectacle. I need Sable Array's internal clocks copied before someone discovers they matter. And I need you to watch Magistrate Om."

The magistrate did not object.

That was worse.

The sixth bell sounded.

Five minutes.

Ira closed the case and sealed it under her credential. The wafer disappeared into evidence lock, but the four clocks stayed in her mind with a persistence that felt physical.

"Magistrate," she said, "if the gate narrowed before the alarm, then the abandonment was not a response."

Om moved toward the door. "Not necessarily."

"What else could it be?"

He paused with his artificial hand over the release.

"A plan that found its excuse."

Then he opened the room.

The noise from the public hall rose through the corridor: anger, grief, machinery, expectation. Ira took her place two steps behind him, carrying the case that was now much heavier than its mass.

As they walked toward the tribunal chamber, she passed a window looking down into the survivor concourse.

The person with the two-finger gesture was still watching.

This time Ira did not nod.

She lifted the case by one centimeter.

It was not a promise.

It was weight.

Chapter 3: Magistrate Om Names Procedure

The main tribunal chamber had been designed by people who feared crowds and needed to pretend otherwise.

Its public gallery rose in steep tiers behind pressure glass, close enough for survivors to see the faces of counsel and far enough that a thrown object would become a symbolic failure instead of a physical one. The press deck floated above the west rail in a cage of sensor arms. Witness entries opened from three directions so no one could accuse the tribunal of making victims and defendants share the same corridor. At the center, beneath Sable Array's old listening spine, stood the judicial dais: a half circle of dark material salvaged from decommissioned warship armor.

Law sitting on armor.

Ira wondered if the designers had noticed.

Magistrate Om entered through the inner door without announcement. The chamber did not quiet all at once. The Ash Belt had never trusted synchronized respect. Voices fell in pockets: clerks first, counsel next, press reluctantly, survivors last. The final sound to fade was not speech but the tap of someone in the gallery striking a memorial tag against the glass.

One tap.

Then another.

Then a third.

Om waited until the tapping stopped by choice.

Then he sat.

"This tribunal recognizes the opening of the Cinder Gate docket under the Meridian Remnant Authority, Ash Belt Convention, and post-Fracture continuity amendments."

His voice was softer than Ira expected. The chamber made room for it.

"This tribunal is not a memorial service. It is not a reconciliation ceremony. It is not a routeholder audit, a military honor court, a salvage arbitration, a Compact legitimacy review, or a public theater for grief already proven. It is a legal proceeding with limited authority over defined questions."

The gallery reacted exactly as the sentence deserved. Anger moved behind the glass like weather.

Om did not raise his voice.

"Defined questions matter because undefined outrage is easy to praise and easier to ignore."

That quieted some of them.

Not enough.

A woman in the second gallery tier stood. Her hair was wrapped in ash cloth. "We know who died. Are you defining that too?"

Security lights warmed along the aisle.

Om lifted one hand. The lights cooled.

"No," he said. "The dead do not require this tribunal to define them."

The woman remained standing.

"Then define who left them."

There it was. The whole chamber leaned toward the sentence.

Ira stood at the Evidence Review table behind junior counsel rail, case locked to her station, Vela's packet silent inside it like a held breath. She watched Om receive the question and saw him choose not to answer it yet.

"That is one of the questions this tribunal has authority to pursue," he said.

The woman laughed once. It was not humor. "One of?"

"Yes."

"What are the others?"

Om turned his display outward. The public wall lit with four headings:

CAUSE OF CINDER GATE EVACUATION FAILURE

COMMAND RESPONSIBILITY FOR TRANSIT WINDOW NARROWING

POSTWAR SUPPRESSION OR LOSS OF PRIMARY TELEMETRY

REPARATIVE OBLIGATIONS ARISING FROM ESTABLISHED FINDINGS

Ira felt the wrongness immediately because she had just seen the four clocks.

Cause of evacuation failure assumed failure.

Command responsibility assumed command alone.

Suppression or loss left room for accident where pattern might stand.

Reparative obligations arising from established findings delayed repair until after categories had done their narrowing work.

The headings were not lies. They were doors built too small.

A translation strip opened beneath the headings for the public gallery.

The tribunal offered four language channels: Formal Standard, Ash Belt Plain, Compact Legal, and survivor-access speech for people whose first legal education had come from ration lines rather than schools. A clerk selected Ash Belt Plain.

The wall softened.

WHY THE GATE FAILED.

WHO GAVE THE NARROWING ORDER.

WHAT RECORDS WENT MISSING.

WHAT CAN BE REPAIRED AFTER PROOF.

The chamber accepted the simpler words faster than it had accepted Om's voice.

Ira did not.

Translation was supposed to widen access. Here it had narrowed the wound with better manners. Failure became the first word again. Order became singular again. Missing records sounded like lost objects rather than removed structures. Repair waited politely behind proof while the people needing repair stood behind glass.

She raised a private note to Evidence Review:

PUBLIC TRANSLATION PRESERVATION REQUESTED. ACCESS LANGUAGE MAY ALTER SCOPE.

Senior Advocate Meren looked at it, looked at Ira, and approved without smiling.

Good. The lie had entered the room wearing plain speech. Now it had an exhibit number waiting for it.

At the counsel table, Nadir Pell sat with military logistics representatives. Ira had seen his photograph in the preparatory files: younger then, jaw hard under a convoy helmet, eyes narrowed against emergency light. In person he looked less severe and more dangerous. Age had loosened his face without softening his attention. He was not watching Om. He was watching the gallery, measuring which sentences landed where.

Routeholder counsel occupied the table to Pell's right. Their lead advocate, Marun Kett, wore mourning black cut in expensive cloth. A minor obscenity. Behind him sat observers from three houses that had survived Cinder with their fleets intact and their public sorrow well maintained.

Survivor counsel sat opposite them: two exhausted lawyers from Ash mutual-aid courts, one station union advocate, and an empty chair reserved for witnesses whose communities refused formal representation by anyone not personally harmed. The empty chair had already become the most honest object in the room.

Om continued. "The tribunal will not presume criminality from survival. It will not presume innocence from necessity. It will not convert suffering into proof without evidence, and it will not convert missing evidence into acquittal without inquiry."

That line was better.

Ira wrote it down because good lines were sometimes used to hide bad structures.

"Opening procedural matters," Om said. "First, the docket title."

The room shifted.

Ira's pen stopped.

Om looked toward the clerks. "The official docket is currently styled Cinder Gate Failure Inquiry."

Marun Kett rose before survivor counsel could. "Routeholder parties object to restyling before opening submissions. The term is established across prior inquiries, insurance settlements, memorial filings, and Compact archive indices."

Survivor counsel rose in overlapping indignation.

Om let them stand for two seconds.

"Sit," he said.

They sat. Even Kett.

"The tribunal has not ordered restyling. It has named the matter for opening argument."

Kett inclined his head with polished irritation. "Then routeholder parties reserve objection to any implication that established terminology prejudices the court."

"Reserved."

The woman in the gallery shouted, "Established by whom?"

This time the security lights did not warm. Someone had learned.

Om looked up. "That question is also reserved."

Ira glanced at her locked case. The packet's first demand had not been admitted, but its shadow had entered the chamber. Docket title. Gate failure. A phrase pretending to be neutral.

Vela would approve, probably without smiling.

"Second," Om said, "evidence handling."

The clerks displayed the ordinary rules: chain of custody, authenticated records, protected witness review, wartime classification challenge process, continuity-person testimony standards, alien evidence accommodation, emergency seal protocols. Each rule appeared with a reference code and an appeal route. The wall looked impressively fair.

Then Om added a temporary order.

"No party may summarize missing telemetry, corrupt files, untranslated records, nonverbal witness material, or continuity gaps as absence of evidence until the tribunal has ruled on the nature of the gap."

The chamber did not understand all of that at once.

Ira did.

So did Nadir Pell.

His eyes moved from Om to Evidence Review.

Not to the senior advocate. To Ira's case.

She kept her face still.

Kett rose again. "Routeholder parties request clarification. Is the tribunal creating an adverse inference from missing records before authentication?"

"No," Om said. "It is preventing premature comfort."

"That is not a legal category."

"It is now a handling warning. Sit down."

Kett sat more slowly this time.

Survivor counsel looked briefly delighted. Ira wished they would not. A good handling order was not victory. It was a clean table before the knives arrived.

The knives arrived quickly.

Military counsel requested that "untranslated records" be limited to nonhuman signals and excluded from all human operational logs until authentication. Routeholder counsel requested that "continuity gaps" be limited to damaged digital chains and excluded from settlement indexes. Survivor counsel requested that "nonverbal witness material" include memorial tags, missing-person walls, old queue tokens, and survivor gestures recorded by public cameras.

Every request sounded reasonable if heard alone. Together they made a map of fear.

Om took them in sequence.

"Untranslated records include any record whose material meaning has not yet been rendered into tribunal language," he said. "That may include nonhuman signal, damaged machine output, coded routeholder risk categories, incomplete child audio, or a witness gesture whose interpretation is disputed."

The gallery stirred at witness gesture.

Ira did not look up toward the second tier. She thought of the two fingers held against the glass, the child with the memorial tag, the black-line steward stepping between grief and extraction. Nonverbal witness material. The phrase had looked ceremonial on the wall. Now it had teeth.

Kett rose halfway. "Magistrate, routeholder risk categories are not untranslated records. They are commercial terms of art."

"Then you should be able to translate them without difficulty."

"Their meaning depends on contract context."

"Then they are not self-executing neutral facts."

Kett sat down again, not defeated, only warned.

Military counsel took the space he left. "Magistrate, incomplete child audio is an inflammatory example before admissibility."

"Correct," Om said.

The answer disoriented the room.

He continued, "Inflammatory does not mean invented. Incomplete does not mean useless. The tribunal will neither play damaged audio for emotional force nor bury it because it is capable of emotion. Its status will be decided under seal before public use."

Ira felt the locked case at her station as if it had heard him.

Survivor counsel rose. "Then memorial material should receive the same protection."

"No," Om said.

The advocate's face tightened. "Magistrate--"

"Memorial material is not evidence by devotion alone. It may become evidence if it bears time, place, chain, relation, or contradiction. It will not become evidence because a tribunal is afraid to disrespect grief."

Behind the glass, someone shouted. The dampers flattened the words but not the wound in them.

Om looked toward the gallery. "I am not protecting the powerful by saying this. I am protecting the dead from being used badly by those who love them."

That angered them more honestly than any insult could have.

Ira wrote the sentence down, then crossed out the first version because her hand had made protect look like possess.

A clerk at the public wall updated the temporary order in real time. New subclauses appeared beneath Om's first warning:

NO GAP MAY BE RENAMED AS ABSENCE BEFORE GAP STATUS REVIEW.

NO DAMAGED RECORD MAY BE OFFERED FOR EMOTIONAL FORCE BEFORE AUTHENTICATION.

NO CATEGORY MAY BE TREATED AS NEUTRAL UNTIL ITS OPERATIONAL EFFECT IS TESTED.

NO MEMORIAL OBJECT MAY BE DISMISSED OR ADMITTED SOLELY BECAUSE IT IS MOURNED.

The wall looked less fair now and more dangerous, which meant it had become more honest.

Nadir Pell read the subclauses without moving his head. Only his eyes changed. The third line had found him.

No category may be treated as neutral.

Ira saw routeholder counsel notice his reaction and fail to hide noticing. A small triangular current moved among the tables: Pell to Kett, Kett to military counsel, military counsel to Om, Om to nothing visible. Procedure had made a corridor and everyone was checking who had stepped into it first.

At the Evidence Review table, Senior Advocate Meren leaned close enough that only Ira could hear.

"Do not smile when they panic."

"I wasn't."

"You were thinking loudly."

Ira kept her eyes on the wall. "Is that a disciplinary category?"

"In this room, yes."

The answer steadied her more than kindness would have. Meren was not warm, but she was useful in the particular way courts required: she could tell the difference between courage and leakage.

Another message appeared on Ira's station, this one properly signed by Evidence Scheduling:

TEMPORARY ORDER 3-B ENTERED. SUPPORTING SEALED MATERIALS TO BE INDEXED AFTER RECESS.

Supporting sealed materials.

Not admitted. Not public. Not safe.

But indexed.

The distinction mattered. A record not indexed could disappear into delay. A record indexed under seal had a number, and a number had to be killed in public or explained in private. Ira touched the edge of her notepad and forced herself not to touch the case.

Vela's instruction returned with an unpleasant precision: Sable Array's internal clocks copied before someone discovers they matter.

Someone had just discovered that categories mattered.

The chamber had not seen Clock B. It had not heard the child in the duct. It did not know about green ships, gray dependents, black station-locals, or the way a color could become a law before the law admitted its own existence.

Yet the air had changed around those hidden things.

That was what procedure did when it worked. It altered the pressure around a truth before naming it.

"Third," Om said, "scope."

This was where the trap tightened.

"This tribunal's opening phase concerns the evacuation window between 17:41 and 17:52 station time on the date recorded as Cinder Gate Abandonment. It does not yet concern every postwar settlement built from that day's consequences. It does not yet concern all salvage distributions, all route privileges, all Compact legitimacy claims, or all later uses of wartime category law."

The gallery erupted.

Security lights flashed amber. Sound dampers lowered the volume but did not erase it. That was wise. Erasure would have made the chamber a battlefield. Dampening made it furious but breathable.

Ira watched Tamsin Vale in the witness waiting tier. The salvage captain was easy to identify because she wore a working coat among formal clothes and kept her hands visible on her knees. Her face did not change when Om narrowed scope. That told Ira the woman had expected betrayal and was reserving judgment only on its efficiency.

The survivor advocate from Cinder Nine stood. "Magistrate, the abandonment cannot be separated from postwar salvage and route privilege. The institutions at issue profited from the official story."

"Agreed," Om said.

The advocate paused. "Then why exclude them?"

"I did not exclude them. I said not yet."

"People die during not yet."

"They also die when courts collapse under everything true at once."

The answer struck the chamber with uncomfortable accuracy. Ira hated it. She also knew evidence review rooms full of broken proceedings where every truth had arrived in a crowd and no finding had survived appeal.

Om leaned forward.

"Listen carefully. The opening phase establishes whether the official sequence of the evacuation window can stand. If it cannot, then every dependent settlement, salvage claim, route privilege, insurance settlement, memorial index, and legal category built on that sequence becomes reachable by this tribunal or by proceedings this tribunal can compel. If we begin with the entire ash field, every powerful party in this chamber will spend the next six months arguing perimeter. I intend to begin with the match."

Silence spread unevenly.

The memorial tag in the gallery tapped once.

Then stopped.

Om let the explanation settle, then turned to the counsel tables. "Opening submissions will therefore address only the stated sequence, the authority to narrow the transit window, the status of primary telemetry, and the preservation of categories used to determine boarding priority."

Categories.

Ira wrote the word in the margin.

Unbonded dependent.

Cargo had manifests.

Children did not.

Nadir Pell rose.

He did not adjust his jacket or perform reluctance. He simply stood, and somehow the room recognized that an old functionary had become dangerous by refusing drama.

"Magistrate," Pell said, "I request permission to address the tribunal before party submissions."

Kett turned sharply. Military counsel whispered. Survivor counsel objected by standing halfway.

Om studied Pell. "On what basis?"

"I was logistics liaison to Cinder Gate military command during the evacuation window. My name appears in several category preservation records. I do not intend to let counsel discover that after I have listened in silence."

The chamber changed.

Ira looked at Om, expecting refusal. A represented witness did not simply insert himself before openings. Procedure had lanes. Pell had stepped out of all of them.

Om asked, "Are you making a statement against advice?"

Military counsel stood fully now. "Magistrate, Officer Pell is represented and should not be questioned outside prepared order."

Pell did not look at her. "Former officer."

"Mr. Pell," counsel said sharply.

"Former," he repeated.

The gallery found its anger again, but this time it had focus.

Om said, "You may make a limited procedural disclosure. No argument. No characterization of necessity. No facts beyond your own status in relation to the records."

Pell bowed his head once. "Accepted."

He turned toward the public gallery rather than the bench.

"My name is Nadir Pell. During the Cinder evacuation window, I transmitted category revisions from routeholder risk models to military command review. I did not command the gate. I did not fire on the gate. I did not order any station abandoned."

The gallery noise rose.

Pell continued through it.

"I also did not object."

That cut deeper than the denials.

Ira felt it land.

Pell turned back to Om. "That is my procedural status."

Om's face revealed nothing. "Entered."

Military counsel looked furious. Routeholder counsel looked worse: calculating.

Ira understood why. Pell had not confessed to crime. He had done something less controllable. He had placed himself in the chain before anyone could turn him into a sealed box.

Om ordered a ten-minute recess before opening submissions.

The chamber protested the delay. The press deck lit with requests. Counsel converged around their tables. Behind pressure glass, survivors argued with one another, some enraged that Pell had spoken, others shaken by the shape of what he had admitted.

Ira stayed still until Om's clerk signaled Evidence Review to withdraw.

As she lifted the case, a message arrived on her station from an internal tribunal channel:

SUBROOM THREE. NOW.

No signature.

Then a second line:

BRING THE CLOCKS.

Ira looked toward Magistrate Om.

He was still on the dais, listening to three objections at once.

The message had not come from him.

Across the chamber, Nadir Pell met her eyes.

He did not nod.

He looked, for one unguarded second, afraid of what she carried.

Chapter 4: Vela Cho Brings a Salt-Sealed Record

The message did not repeat.

That bothered Ira more than if it had. Panic repeated itself. Procedure confirmed itself. Traps often trusted the first hook.

She carried the case out of the tribunal chamber under Evidence Review escort, feeling Pell's fear at her back like heat from a door left open. The recess had turned the outer corridor into a pressure channel. Counsel moved in hard little clusters. Press drones hummed against their leash fields. Survivors behind the gallery exits shouted questions through dampened glass. Station militia stood with their hands deliberately away from weapons.

Subroom Three lay twelve meters away.

It felt farther because the corridor now knew she was carrying something.

The Evidence Review escort was a clerk named Bayo, narrow-shouldered and young enough to look insulted by his own seriousness. He kept glancing at the case.

"You received an internal call?" he asked.

"Yes."

"It wasn't in my routing."

"Then it wasn't for you."

"All evidence movement is for me."

Ira stopped.

Bayo stopped one step later and flushed.

She said, "That sentence is how records get buried by helpful people."

His embarrassment hardened into resentment. "Associate Advocate, I am trying to maintain chain of custody."

"Good. Maintain it by noting that I received an unsigned internal request, declined to open the case in corridor, and proceeded to the nearest sealed subroom with escort."

He blinked.

"Write that," she said.

Bayo wrote it.

Ira resumed walking.

Subroom Three opened to her credential and Om's standing seal. No one waited inside.

That was worse too.

The displays were dark except for the clock streams still cached under seal. Ira placed the case on the table, locked it to the surface, and looked at the room corners. Four visible sensors. Two thermal. One pressure seam near the floor. Standard tribunal equipment, unless Sable's old military listening architecture had been left in place beneath the legal skin.

"Run a room integrity check," she told Bayo.

"You don't have authority to order--"

"Then request one."

"On what basis?"

"Unsigned internal evidence instruction during active recess."

That worked. Bureaucracy liked phrases it could hold without understanding.

Bayo requested the check. The room sealed harder. A thin blue line ran around the door, vents, display edges, and floor seam. The result returned:

NO ACTIVE INTRUSION DETECTED.

Ira did not trust the word active.

The wall display lit before she could say so.

Vela Cho appeared again, clearer this time. Her relay had shifted to a higher-priority channel, which meant someone had either authorized her or failed to stop her quickly enough.

"Do not open the case yet," Vela said.

Bayo made a small sound of professional pain.

Ira folded her hands. "Did you send the message?"

"No."

"Do you know who did?"

"I know who could have."

"That is not the same thing."

"No. That is why you should be worried."

Bayo stepped toward the wall. "Archive Specialist Cho, this channel is not listed for Subroom Three recess communication."

"Correct."

"Please identify your authority."

Vela looked at him. "Shoals Compact emergency archive exception, Meridian Remnant Tribunal provisional accommodation, and a personal willingness to be sanctioned after useful damage."

Bayo hesitated. "I can enter the first two."

"Enter the third in remarks."

To Ira's surprise, he did.

The case warmed. Not from inside. From the table lock. A request pulsed across the evidence seal:

REMOTE HANDLING PROTOCOL AVAILABLE

SOURCE: VELA CHO

ATTACHMENT: SALT-SEALED RECORD

"Salt-sealed?" Ira asked.

Vela's expression changed by a fraction. "The Shoals Compact does not permit certain records to travel as clean files. The seal carries handling conditions from the relation that produced the warning."

"This is Orison legal material?"

"No. It is human war-crime material traveling under Orison-influenced protection because human systems have already failed it."

Bayo whispered, "That is not going in remarks."

"It should," Ira said.

He looked wounded but wrote a cleaner version:

Record uses nonstandard protection because prior human handling is disputed.

"Acceptable," Vela said.

Bayo added a classification field, then stopped with his fingers above the terminal.

"Foreign-origin evidence?" he asked.

"No," Vela said.

"Protected human evidence with alien handling marks?"

"Closer."

"Classified military remnant?"

"No."

"Glassward continuity material?"

"Not yet."

Bayo looked at Ira as if she had invited a weather system into his filing cabinet.

"Associate Advocate," he said, "if I select no class, the system will assign one at intake."

"Which one?"

"Whichever has the highest containment value."

"Meaning?"

"Usually classified military remnant."

Vela's image sharpened. "Do not let Sable name this record military."

Bayo did not enjoy being addressed like a door about to close. "I cannot invent a category."

"Then create a provisional hold," Ira said.

"On whose authority?"

Ira almost said Vela's. That would fail. She almost said Om's standing seal. That would drag him into the packet before his disclosure. She almost said her own, and felt the arrogance of it before the room punished her.

Instead she looked at the log Bayo had already made.

"On the authority of uncertainty you personally observed," she said. "Unsigned internal evidence instruction. Nonstandard protection. Prior human handling disputed. Active recess. Room integrity requested. Source channel contested. If the system assigns this to military by default, you will have converted uncertainty into custody."

Bayo's face went very still.

That had reached him. Not emotionally. Professionally, which might be stronger.

"Provisional hold for class determination," he said.

"With automatic assignment disabled," Ira added.

"That requires a reason."

"Default custody would prejudice the contested handling history."

He entered it.

The system resisted, politely.

DEFAULT CLASS ASSIGNMENT RECOMMENDED: MILITARY REMNANT / HIGH CONTAINMENT.

Bayo's jaw tightened. "It is doing what I said."

"Override," Ira said.

"I need a second credential."

Vela said, "Use mine."

"Your credential is external."

"Then the system can learn manners."

It could not. The terminal rejected her seal with a red border and a gentle explanatory note about nonparty archive actors. Ira read the note twice because gentle administrative language often hid the actual weapon.

NONPARTY ARCHIVE ACTOR MAY SUPPLY MATERIAL BUT MAY NOT CONTROL TRIBUNAL CUSTODY CLASS.

"There," Bayo said, too softly to be triumphant. "That is the wall."

Ira looked at the door. Om was not there. Meren was in the chamber. Evidence Review senior staff were likely buried under objections. The unsigned message had asked for the clocks now, not after a supervisory committee found its courage.

She placed her credential beside Bayo's on the table reader.

"What are you doing?" he asked.

"Making a bad record visible."

"That is not a procedural action."

"It is if you write it down."

Bayo stared at her.

Ira spoke slowly enough that he could type if he chose to help her rather than survive her. "Associate Senn requests provisional hold before class assignment. Clerk Bayo states second credential required. No second internal credential available within recess window. External archive seal rejected by system. Associate Senn submits evidence review credential as contested second authority solely to prevent automatic military custody pending magistrate review."

Bayo did not move for two breaths.

Then he typed.

The system accepted the credential with a yellow warning:

TEMPORARY HOLD ENTERED / REVIEW REQUIRED / AUTHORITY CONTESTABLE.

Ira let out a breath she had not meant to store.

"Authority contestable," Bayo said.

"Better than ownership invisible."

Vela nodded. "Now you understand salt."

Ira looked at her. "Because it preserves by making the surface difficult?"

"Because it makes tampering leave taste."

Bayo wrote that down, then seemed embarrassed and deleted it from the formal field. He kept it in remarks.

Ira decided she might forgive him for being young if he would continue being exact.

The record's provisional hold number appeared:

PH-8-CINDER-SALT-001.

Not admitted. Not understood. Not safe.

But no longer waiting to be renamed by the nearest powerful system.

Ira touched the table control but did not open the case. "What are the conditions?"

The display produced them one by one.

DO NOT SUMMARIZE SILENCE.

DO NOT TREAT WARNING AS OWNERSHIP.

DO NOT CONVERT DAMAGED WITNESS INTO SINGULAR PROOF.

DO NOT EXTRACT ALIEN HANDLING MARKS FOR HUMAN AUTHORITY CLAIMS.

DO NOT PLAY CHILD AUDIO IN PUBLIC SESSION WITHOUT WITNESS-PROTECTION REVIEW.

DO NOT LET DOCKET LANGUAGE PREJUDGE CAUSATION.

Bayo read them with increasing distress. "This is not a normal evidence seal."

"No," Vela said. "Normal is the problem under review."

Ira studied the third line longest.

Damaged witness.

"Is the child alive?" she asked.

Vela did not answer quickly enough.

Bayo stopped writing.

"There is a continuity derivative," Vela said at last.

"That is not an answer."

"It is the only accurate one I can give before Glassward custody opens."

"Name?"

"Sera Venn-3."

The name entered the room softly and changed it. Not because Ira recognized it. She did not. Because Vela said it as if the name had more than one owner and none at all.

"Derived from the maintenance child?" Ira asked.

"From a child believed to have been in Duct M-14. From later emergency memory salvage. From at least two postwar custody procedures. From one unauthorized preservation act. From a person the first inquiries treated as audio artifact."

Ira sat down.

She had not meant to.

The tribunal bells had stopped. Recess time moved differently: less formal, more dangerous.

"Does Magistrate Om know?"

"He knows there is a Glassward witness issue. He does not know the full chain."

"Why not?"

Vela's face hardened. "Because I do not know whether his old signature makes him witness, judge, or evidence."

Bayo looked toward the door as if hoping a senior clerk would arrive and remove his ears.

Ira thought of Om's insufficient surprise. Clerk to the wartime inquiry panel. A man who knew procedure could both preserve and bury.

"Why send this to me?" she asked.

"Because you are low enough in authority to notice the table legs."

"That is not reassuring."

"It was not meant to be."

The table chimed. Room integrity updated:

PASSIVE LEGACY ACOUSTIC PATHWAYS PRESENT.

Bayo went pale. "What does that mean?"

Ira looked up at the old listening spine behind the ceiling panels.

"It means the room can hear even when nothing is actively listening."

The integrity report unfolded another layer before anyone touched it.

LEGACY PATHWAY MAP: PARTIAL.

SUBROOM THREE WALL VOID TO SERVICE RIB 4.

SERVICE RIB 4 TO OLD SIGNAL SPINE.

OLD SIGNAL SPINE TO DECOMMISSIONED ENCRYPTION BAY.

STATUS: PHYSICAL CONTINUITY CONFIRMED. ACTIVE RECEIVER UNKNOWN.

Bayo read the path aloud once, then covered his mouth as if the words themselves had broken protocol.

"That bay is supposed to be welded shut," he said.

"Supposed to be is not a custody status," Vela said.

Ira looked at the map. A room could be sealed by every modern rule and still speak through an old military scar no one had wanted to pay to remove. It was the whole tribunal in miniature: civilian authority hung on walls, wartime infrastructure behind them, everyone hoping the difference mattered because the signs had changed.

"Log the pathway," Ira said.

Bayo's fingers hesitated.

"As what?"

"As a witness to handling risk."

He typed that, then added without prompting:

Legacy pathway may carry evidence exposure without visible breach.

Ira decided not to thank him. The exactness was better left undisturbed.

Vela cursed under her breath. "Do not open the salt seal in that room."

The door light changed from blue to amber.

Someone had requested entry.

Bayo checked the panel. "Magistrate Om."

Ira looked toward the archive table.

Vela said, "Ask him the handling question before you let him in."

Ira stood. "Magistrate, state your purpose for entry."

Silence from the other side.

Bayo looked scandalized. "You cannot--"

Ira held up one hand.

Om's voice came through the door speaker, calm and close. "To prevent you from opening a protected record in a compromised room."

Ira exhaled.

Vela nodded once.

"State the compromise," Ira said.

This time the pause was shorter.

"Passive legacy acoustic pathways. Sable was built to listen through metal. Legal conversion did not remove every military bone."

Ira opened the door.

Om entered alone. He looked at Vela's display, then at Bayo, then at the locked case.

"Good," he said.

"Good that the room is compromised?" Ira asked.

"Good that you asked."

Bayo recovered enough to say, "Magistrate, this creates chain complications."

"Everything worth hearing does."

Om ordered the case transferred to a mobile silence box, a device Ira had only seen in training modules. It looked disappointingly like a dented gray cabinet on mag wheels. Its interior generated counter-vibration, thermal masking, and localized recorder blindness. The Ash Belt called such devices confession lockers. Courts called them protected review environments. Both names were accurate in different dishonest ways.

They moved the case without opening it. Bayo logged each step. Vela remained on display until the silence box sealed, then transferred to its internal channel. Her face appeared on the box's small screen in green monochrome.

Om placed his artificial hand on the lid.

"Before we proceed, Associate Senn, I need you to understand your position."

"I am listening."

"You are not counsel of record. You are not judge. You are not witness. You are evidence review staff with temporary advocacy authority. That makes you useful and vulnerable."

"To whom?"

"Everyone."

Vela said, "Including you, Magistrate."

"Yes."

He did not resent the sentence. Ira marked that carefully.

Om continued, "If this record enters ordinary discovery, routeholder counsel will try to sever the Orison handling marks, military counsel will challenge Glassward continuity standing, survivor counsel may try to play the child audio in public before protection review, and my own tribunal clerks will attempt to summarize the packet into an admissible abstract."

Bayo whispered, "We would not."

Om looked at him with tired kindness. "You would. You would call it helping."

Bayo flushed again, but this time he did not argue.

Ira touched the silence box. "Then what do we do?"

"We create a special handling motion before recess ends."

Vela said, "With the conditions visible."

"Some conditions visible," Om said.

Ira felt the old narrowing return. "Which ones hidden?"

"Sera Venn-3's identity until Glassward custody is represented."

"And your old signature?"

The silence box hummed.

Bayo stared at the floor.

Vela watched Om without blinking.

Om answered, "Not hidden. Deferred until relevance is established."

Ira shook her head. "That sounds like hidden with a robe on."

The line escaped before she could discipline it.

Om's mouth tightened.

Vela's face did something that might have become a smile in a less dangerous room.

The magistrate said, "Correct. Then we will not defer it that way."

Ira went still.

Om turned to Bayo. "Enter that Magistrate Om disclosed possible historical involvement in prior Cinder Gate evidence handling through service as clerk to the first wartime inquiry panel. Scope and effect undetermined. Disclosure made before review of salt-sealed packet."

Bayo typed fast.

"Read it back," Om said.

Bayo read it back.

"Enter it," Om said.

The record accepted the line.

It did not absolve him. It did make the room less false.

Vela said, "Now the salt seal can open."

Om looked to Ira. "Your credential first."

"Mine?"

"The packet was sent to you."

"By whom?"

"That is one of the things we are about to learn."

Ira placed her palm on the silence box.

The seal opened.

The box screen filled with a dark field threaded by white timing marks, Orison pressure annotations, Glassward custody warnings, and one human phrase written in an old maintenance font:

THE GREEN SHIPS MOVED BEFORE THE SIREN.

Bayo made a soft, involuntary sound.

Vela closed her eyes.

Om did not move.

Ira read the sentence until it stopped being a sentence and became a door.

"Who wrote that?" she asked.

The salt-sealed record answered with a custody label:

SERA VENN-3 / SOURCE CHILD UNCONFIRMED / STANDING DISPUTED / DO NOT MERGE FOR CLARITY

The recess bell rang outside.

They had three minutes before the tribunal resumed.

Ira looked at Om. "We need the handling motion now."

"Draft it," he said.

"Me?"

"You objected to my robe. Earn the sentence."

Ira pulled the nearest terminal toward her and began to write while Vela dictated limits, Bayo logged chain details, and Magistrate Om stood beside the silence box with his compromised past entered into the record.

Outside, the chamber waited for procedure.

Inside, the first true witness had arrived as a warning not to make her simple.

Chapter 5: The Salvage Captain's Hands

Tamsin Vale refused the oath gloves.

The refusal took longer to process than her arrival.

She entered the tribunal chamber through the witness corridor in a working coat patched at both elbows and sealed at the cuffs with hand-stitched pressure tape. Her hair was gray at the temples and cut short enough not to catch in machinery. She wore no memorial tag, no station colors, no advocate's pin. Her boots left a faint trace of dock grit on the polished tribunal floor, and three clerks noticed at once because legal rooms trained people to care about the wrong residue.

The oath officer approached with the gloves on a tray.

Tamsin looked at them.

"No."

The officer paused. "Captain Vale, the gloves register biometric affirmation and stress markers. They are standard for live testimony."

"I know what they do."

"Then please place your hands--"

"No."

The gallery stirred behind the pressure glass. Survivors knew a meaningful no before courts did.

Magistrate Om leaned forward. "Captain Vale, the tribunal requires a reliable oath record."

Tamsin raised both hands.

They were ugly hands, and the chamber knew it should not think that and thought it anyway. The fingers were thickened by vacuum scar and old crush repairs. Two nails were missing. A translucent graft covered the back of her left hand from knuckle to wrist, showing the faint movement of tendon replacements beneath. Fine black lines marked embedded tool interfaces, some legal, some probably not. These were not the hands of a ceremonial salvage captain who sat in a command chair and spoke through drones. These hands had gone into walls after heat.

"These are the record," she said.

The oath officer looked helplessly toward the bench.

Om asked, "Do the gloves cause medical risk?"

"No."

"Do you object to biometric capture?"

"No."

"Then state the objection."

Tamsin's jaw moved once. "They make hands clean."

The sentence reached the gallery faster than the court.

Ira sat at Evidence Review with the salt-sealed handling motion still warm in the docket. Om had returned from recess and entered it before opening submissions could resume. Routeholder counsel had objected. Military counsel had objected. Survivor counsel had objected to only parts of it and then looked annoyed that caution had made them sound aligned with their enemies. Om had accepted the motion provisionally, which meant Vela's record could not yet be used as proof but could shape handling.

Do not summarize silence.

Do not play child audio in public session without witness-protection review.

Do not convert damaged witness into singular proof.

Now Tamsin Vale stood under oath procedure and refused gloves because they made hands clean.

Ira wrote the sentence down.

Om said, "The tribunal can accept spoken affirmation with visual biometric capture."

The oath officer looked distressed. "Magistrate, protocol--"

"Protocol can record the deviation it is currently surviving."

Tamsin's mouth tightened. Not a smile. Recognition, perhaps.

The oath officer withdrew the gloves and activated the visual field. A ring of pale light formed around Tamsin's hands and face. She did not flinch. The system scanned scars, pulse, pupil rhythm, voice base, microtremor. It wanted her body as confidence score. She let it look without giving it the comfort of cooperation.

The visual field tried to standardize her.

It tagged the missing nails as trauma artifacts. It tagged the tool-interface lines as occupational implants. It tagged the tendon graft as surgical repair of uncertain date. It tagged the blackened crescent under her right thumb as contaminant exposure and requested permission to smooth it from the public view.

Tamsin saw the request appear on the witness display.

"No smoothing," she said.

The oath officer froze again. "Captain Vale, dignity protocol allows--"

"I know what dignity protocol allows."

Om looked to Evidence Review. "Associate Senn?"

Ira had not expected to be addressed. Her terminal showed the same request with three options: public blur, private retention, full witness consent. The default was blur. The system always preferred clean skin when given a choice.

"Full witness consent is available," Ira said. "If Captain Vale wants the mark visible, the tribunal can retain it."

Kett rose. "Routeholder parties object to unnecessary display of injuries likely to inflame the gallery."

Tamsin looked at him. "It is not an injury."

"Then what is it?" Kett asked before his own caution could stop him.

"Dock scorch from hatch G-12."

The chamber held the answer.

Tamsin turned her hand slightly. The crescent caught the light, matte and ugly.

"The hatch wheel was hot enough to burn through the outer glove. Not hot enough to stop me. Your dignity protocol calls that a contaminant because it cannot tell the difference between dirt and place."

Ira selected full witness consent.

The public display kept the mark.

A small thing. A legal thing. The sort of thing that looked trivial until later, when an advocate tried to say the witness had only memory and no body in the record.

The oath system added a line:

VISIBLE SCAR/CONTAMINANT RETAINED BY WITNESS REQUEST.

Tamsin read it and shook her head. "Not contaminant."

The oath officer looked desperate. "The field category is fixed."

"Then add a remark."

Om said, "Add a remark."

The officer typed.

WITNESS IDENTIFIES MARK AS DOCK SCORCH FROM HATCH G-12.

Kett sat down as if he had chosen to conserve strength.

Ira watched the line settle into the record and understood why Tamsin had refused the gloves. The gloves would have measured grip strength, pulse, stress, skin conductivity. They would have converted every scar into a reliability adjustment. They would not have let a burn name a hatch.

Meren leaned toward Ira. "That scorch mark now has more standing than half the prepared affidavits."

"Because it is physical?"

"Because counsel objected to it and lost."

Ira looked at her.

Meren did not smile. "Courts remember what people try to hide."

In the gallery, the ash-cloth woman lifted her own right hand and turned it toward the glass. A pale crescent marked the base of her thumb too, smaller, older, perhaps from a different hatch or the same kind of heat. Two people near her noticed. Then five. Then a row.

Security watched for coordinated gesture.

Om watched for meaning.

The woman did not shout. She only held the hand there long enough for the public cameras to decide whether a scar was speech.

Ira heard Vela's handling condition inside her head: do not convert damaged witness into singular proof.

One hand did not prove a timeline.

Several hands did not prove a timeline.

But they made it harder to pretend the hatch existed only in Tamsin Vale's shame.

"State your name," the officer said.

"Tamsin Vale."

"Occupation."

"Salvage captain. Licensed under Ash Belt mutual recovery charter, Cinderwake registry, temporary tribunal witness protection seal."

"Do you affirm that your testimony will be accurate to the limits of memory, record, and conscience?"

Tamsin looked toward the gallery before answering.

"No."

The officer closed his eyes.

Om said, "Explain."

"Memory lies to survive. Records lie to get paid. Conscience lies when it wants sleep."

Someone in the gallery said, "Let her talk."

Om did.

Tamsin returned her gaze to the bench. "I affirm I will not knowingly make any of those lies prettier."

Om nodded once. "Accepted."

Routeholder counsel rose immediately. "Objection. That is not a standard oath."

"Correct," Om said. "It is a stronger one."

Kett sat down with visible effort.

Ira saw Nadir Pell watching Tamsin's hands. Not her face. Her hands. His own were folded flat on the table as if he had ordered them not to remember anything.

Survivor counsel began direct examination.
