The lawyer's name was Maro Elt, from Cinder Nine Families' Assembly. He was older than Ira by perhaps twenty years, but grief had made his face difficult to date. He approached the witness rail with no notes.

"Captain Vale, where were you when the Cinder Gate corridor closed?"

"On the salvage tender Half-Mercy, twelve thousand kilometers sunward of Cinder Nine, holding outside the military exclusion cone."

"Why were you there?"

"Everyone knew evacuation left things behind."

The answer hit hard because it contained no defense.

Elt let it sit. "Things."

"Heat exchangers. Water membranes. Med lockers. Pump stacks. Hab gel. Tool foam. Reactor lace if you were brave or stupid. Anything that could keep another station breathing."

"People?"

Tamsin's hands flexed.

"People if alive."

"And if dead?"

"Bodies if reachable and if bringing them in did not kill the living."

"And if not reachable?"

"Tags. Names. Record scraps. Personal effects if they could be separated from systems."

"Separated."

Tamsin looked at him. "You want me to say stolen."

"Did you steal?"

"Yes."

The chamber did not move.

Elt's voice softened, which somehow made it more severe. "From whom?"

"From the dead. From the missing. From stations that had no one left to refuse. From compartments still warm enough that we told ourselves maybe they were equipment spaces. From family lockers. From abandoned queue shelters. From Cinder Nine."

In the gallery, the woman with ash-wrapped hair lowered her head.

Tamsin saw. Her voice did not change.

"I stole heat exchangers from Cinder Nine and installed them on three stations that would have frozen within the week. I stripped emergency radiators from a school shelter because the children in that shelter were already dead, and the children in Vey Minor were not. I cut a med printer out of a chapel wall with two bodies still strapped near the altar because the printer's feedstock was compatible with burn treatment on Half-Mercy."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, inflammatory detail--"

The gallery became a living threat.

Om's voice cut through before security could. "Sit down, Advocate Kett."

"The witness is offering prejudicial narrative beyond--"

"The witness is explaining salvage context. You will survive discomfort."

Kett sat.

Ira wrote that down too, then stopped. Good lines could become vanity if collected carelessly.

Elt moved closer to the witness rail. "Captain Vale, did your salvage crew receive authorization?"

"Sometimes before. Usually after. Often retroactive under emergency recovery statutes."

"Who issued retroactive authorization for Cinder Nine material?"

Tamsin named three offices. One Compact emergency logistics cell. One Ash Belt provisional survival board. One routeholder recovery authority.

At the routeholder table, Kett's associates began speaking into private channels.

Elt asked, "Were you paid?"

"Yes."

"By whom?"

"Anyone who could."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the Ash Belt answer."

Elt waited.

Tamsin sighed. "Station councils. Convoy insurers. Routeholder houses. Military remnants. Families, sometimes, if they wanted a specific thing found and were desperate enough to hire people like me."

"People like you."

"People with ships small enough to dock where official rescue said docking was unsafe."

"Did you profit from Cinder?"

Tamsin did not look away from him.

"Yes."

The word carried no performance. Ira trusted it more than any apology she had heard in court.

Elt turned to the public display. "Tribunal permission to enter Salvage Return Ledger VALE-CINDER-01 through 19."

Kett stood. "Routeholder parties object pending authentication."

Military counsel stood too. "Military parties object to undisclosed operational material."

Om said, "On what basis was it undisclosed?"

Elt answered, "It was disclosed under survivor counsel index. Parties marked it irrelevant to opening phase."

Om looked at Kett. "Did routeholder review reject relevance?"

Kett's expression became careful. "The ledgers concern post-closure salvage distribution, which the tribunal has narrowed as later phase material."

Ira felt the trap. Om had narrowed scope to the evacuation window. Kett was using that narrowing to keep Tamsin's hands away from the first record.

Elt saw it too. "The ledgers show which compartments were accessible, warm, and structurally intact after the alleged gate failure. They speak to timeline."

Vela's packet hummed inside the evidence lock, or Ira imagined it did.

Do not summarize silence.

Missing telemetry could be challenged by heat exchangers. Dead air could speak through salvage.

Om asked, "Captain Vale, do your ledgers include removal times?"

"Yes."

"Thermal state?"

"When my crew was sober enough to log it."

"Pressure state?"

"Yes."

"Body observations?"

Tamsin's hands went still.

"Yes."

"Then relevance is established for limited timing purposes. Ledgers admitted under restricted opening phase scope, subject to later authentication challenge."

Kett sat down slowly.

The public display filled with salvage entries. Ira had expected columns. She got columns, but also photographs: wall plates cut away, frost patterns, tool marks, compartment numbers, body flags blurred under dignity protocol, handwritten notes from people too cold to spell well.

Tamsin watched them without blinking.

Elt highlighted one entry.

CINDER NINE / QUEUE SHELTER G-12 / HEAT EXCHANGER PAIR / REMOVAL 19:08 / THERMAL STATE: RESIDUAL HIGH / PRESSURE: PARTIAL / BODY FLAGS: 31 / NOTE: "SMELLED LIKE BREAD."

The gallery made a sound no dampener could improve.

"Explain the note," Elt said.

Tamsin's throat moved.

"Overheated insulation in Cinder shelters smelled sweet when it burned. Like bread if you were hungry enough. My deckhand wrote that. He was nineteen. He killed himself eight years later. Don't put that in the ledger as guilt. He had enough."

Elt's face tightened. "The entry records residual high thermal state at 19:08. Why does that matter?"

"Official sequence says G-12 lost support around 17:46."

"Could it still be warm at 19:08?"

"Warm, yes. Residual high, no. Not with that wall breach, unless auxiliary heat kept feeding longer than official logs admit."

"How much longer?"

"I am not an engineer."

"As salvage captain."

"Long enough that someone could have rerouted support. Long enough that cutoff timing matters. Long enough that dead doesn't mean unreachable when the gate closed."

Ira felt the sentence strike the official sequence from another angle.

Not just before the alarm.

After the closure.

Time was becoming crowded.

Kett rose for cross-examination earlier than Elt wanted. Om allowed it with a warning so dry it nearly cracked.

Kett approached like a man stepping around dirt.

"Captain Vale, you have stated under oath that you stole from abandoned stations, accepted retroactive authorization, and profited from Cinder material."

"Yes."

"Your crew's logs were often made under extreme stress."

"Yes."

"You admitted some were incomplete."

"Yes."

"A portion of the crew were intoxicated."

"Some."

"Some later suffered psychological deterioration."

Tamsin's hands closed.

"Yes."

"So when you say residual heat proves official timing is incomplete, you are asking this tribunal to rely on records made by thieves, traumatized salvors, and desperate profiteers."

Survivor counsel objected.

Om let the question stand.

Tamsin looked at Kett for a long moment.

"Who else went in?"

Kett blinked. "Excuse me?"

"Who else docked at G-12? Official rescue? Routeholder recovery? Military remains unit? Insurance assessors?"

"That is not--"

"We were thieves because everyone clean was late."

The gallery erupted.

Om's hand came down once, not a bang, just a contact with the armored dais. The room snapped back from the edge.

Tamsin continued, quieter.

"Yes, Advocate Kett. My records are dirty. My hands are dirty. My ship smelled like burned insulation and opened bodies for months. If you have cleaner records from someone who entered those compartments sooner, show them. If not, you are asking the tribunal to prefer absence because the people who made evidence disgust you."

Ira stopped writing because there was nothing to improve.

Kett's face had gone pale with controlled anger.

"Captain," he said, "did you ever alter a salvage timestamp to increase compensation?"

"Yes."

The answer shocked even Elt.

Kett recovered quickly. "How many times?"

"Four that I remember."

"Including Cinder?"

"No."

"Why should the tribunal believe that?"

"It should not believe it because I say so. It should compare my logs against dock scars, thermal decay, tool telemetry, crew suit cams, station residue, and the things your clients said were too irrelevant to authenticate."

Kett had opened a door and found machinery behind it.

Om said, "Evidence Review will identify corroboration requirements for admitted salvage timing entries."

Ira realized that meant her.

Her terminal populated with a task list: dock scars, thermal decay, tool telemetry, suit cams, residue, authorization records, compensation disputes.

Tamsin stepped back from the rail as if the cross-examination had cost her more oxygen than the room supplied.

Elt requested one final question for redirect.

Om allowed it.

Elt said, "Captain Vale, why did you bring the ledgers now?"

Tamsin looked at her hands again.

"Because for twenty years people have called us grave robbers when they wanted our shame, and emergency workers when they wanted our parts. I don't need either name today."

"What name do you need?"

"Witness."

The tribunal recorded it.

Behind pressure glass, the woman in ash cloth raised both her scarred hands and pressed them flat against the barrier.

Tamsin saw her.

This time she did not look away.

Chapter 6: No One Owns the Dead Air

The phrase came from a routeholder footnote.

Ira found it during the lunch recess, buried under compensation tables, salvage exemptions, emergency transfer codes, and a polished legal argument about vacuum. The tribunal had not recessed because anyone was hungry. It had recessed because Tamsin Vale's testimony had made three counsel tables dangerous in three different ways.

Survivor counsel needed time to decide whether Tamsin was a witness or a wound.

Routeholder counsel needed time to stop bleeding.

Military counsel needed time to determine whether Nadir Pell had become a liability, an asset, or a man too old to discipline usefully.

Evidence Review needed time because Om had assigned Ira a corroboration grid that would have been insulting if it were less necessary.

She sat in a narrow workroom behind the tribunal chamber with cold nutrient broth untouched beside her terminal, sorting salvage ledgers against station schematics. Bayo worked opposite her, no longer resentful, which made him more useful and less interesting. He had developed the haunted expression of a clerk discovering that chain of custody was not a chain but a net with several people cutting holes in it.

"Read this," Ira said.

He looked up. "Which part?"

"Routeholder response to Salvage Return Ledger admissibility. Page forty-two. Footnote eleven."

Bayo pulled the document across his display and read aloud:

"Vacuum-exposed abandoned compartments, absent surviving claimants or active life-support continuity, entered emergency salvage commons under Ash Belt provisional recovery doctrine."

He stopped.

Ira waited.

He read it again, more slowly, and found the teeth the second time.

"Salvage commons," he said.

"Yes."

"They are saying the compartments became unowned."

"No. They are saying the air died first, so everything inside became available."

Bayo looked back at the footnote with the pained concentration of a person hoping grammar had betrayed him.

"It cites three authorities," he said.

"Read the third."

He opened the citation tree. The first authority was the emergency survival memo Ira had already seen. The second was an insurance arbitration from a station that had lost half its ring to vacuum and sold the other half's dead machinery to keep the living half warm. The third was older, thinner, and more dangerous because it had survived long enough to sound ordinary.

POST-CLAIMANT ENVIRONMENTAL NULLITY IN VACUUM LOSS ZONES.

Bayo read the title twice. "That cannot be real."

"It is real if people paid filing fees for it."

The doctrine came from a pre-Fracture mining dispute, not a war-crime inquiry. A corporate extraction crew had abandoned a pressure seam after a methane burn. Four contract workers died behind a seal. Their families claimed the tools and ore samples left with them. The company argued that once the zone became environmentally null, property and evidence inside it reverted to operational recovery because no living claimant could exercise possession there.

"The families lost?" Bayo asked.

"Partially."

"That is a court word for yes."

"Here it means the families got death payments and lost standing over the objects."

Bayo's mouth tightened. "So the objects had more legal future than the people."

Ira kept reading. "The court said bodily dignity remained personal, but tools, extracted ore, samples, and site logs were operational assets. It separated personhood from environment."

"Because the air was gone."

"Because the company convinced a judge that air was the boundary of claim."

Bayo sat back, then leaned forward again as if refusing the comfort of disgust. "Cinder was not a mine."

"No. Cinder was a station with queue shelters, family lockers, school bays, chapels, maintenance ducts, and evacuation lines."

"Then why does the doctrine apply?"

"Because routeholder counsel wants the tribunal to hear vacuum and think empty."

Ira opened the Cinder Nine schematic over the old mining decision. The overlay was obscene: a legal principle born in a burned seam laid across a shelter map with children's washrooms, ration alcoves, and an emergency nursery marked in faded green.

One compartment in the routeholder table had been labeled G-12.

Queue shelter.

Dead air.

Salvage commons.

The sequence looked technical until she imagined saying it to the ash-cloth woman through the gallery glass.

"Find me the phrase active life-support continuity," Ira said.

Bayo searched. The phrase appeared in seven filings, each with a slightly different use. In the earliest memo, it meant whether air and heat still sustained occupants. In the insurance interpretation, it meant whether systems supported rescue. In the routeholder brief, it meant whether a compartment produced a live claimant signal. By the time the phrase reached Kett's response, it meant whether the dead had left paperwork alive enough to object.

"They narrowed the word continuity every time they moved it," Bayo said.

"Yes."

"Can we show that?"

"We can if we do not overclaim."

He gave a short, humorless laugh. "That is becoming the office motto."

Ira almost answered, but the station ecology brief opened beside the filings and stopped her. Emergency baffles did not isolate life cleanly. Cinder Nine had shared heat through structural ribs, pressure through surge sacks, sensor priority through maintenance relays. A compartment could be locally unbreathable and still tied to living decisions elsewhere. Dead air was not a state. It was a sentence imposed on a place after someone stopped asking what still reached it.

Bayo looked sick. "Including personal effects?"

"Including heat exchangers. Med printers. Wall panels. Bodies unless claimed by a different dignity statute. Memory devices if categorized as equipment. Anything their clients received downstream without wanting theft attached."

He leaned back. "Can they do that?"

"They did."

Ira opened three more documents. The doctrine had moved like mold through postwar law. First an emergency survival memo: abandoned compartments may be accessed for life-preserving salvage if no rescue pathway remains active. Then an insurance interpretation: recoverable infrastructure in nonviable compartments can offset outstanding convoy loss. Then a routeholder logistics brief: vacuum-exposed asset pools reduce replacement obligations. Then a Compact archive index that tagged Cinder Nine queue shelters as structural salvage zones rather than inhabited spaces after 17:46.

"Dead air," Ira said.

Bayo frowned. "What?"

"That is what they are arguing. Once air is dead, no one owns what it touched."

"Is that a legal phrase?"

"It will be by the time Kett stands up."

Bayo checked the chamber clock. "Recess ends in nine minutes."

Ira stood, grabbed the routeholder response, Tamsin's ledgers, and a station ecology brief, then stopped because her hands were moving faster than her authority.

She opened a channel to survivor counsel.

Maro Elt appeared in a rectangle of bad light. He looked as if he had spent recess being shouted at by people who had earned the right.

"Associate Senn," he said. "Tell me Evidence Review has something useful and not merely devastating."

"Routeholder counsel will argue emergency salvage commons."

Elt's face changed. "They would be stupid to say that out loud."

"No. They would be strategic. It turns Tamsin's ledgers into proof that compartments were legally abandoned after life support failed. If accepted, the salvage records can corroborate timing without reopening ownership or restitution."

"Dead stations have no claimants."

"That is the shape."

Elt closed his eyes for one breath. "I hate when they are competent."

"I need authority to prepare a limited challenge."

He opened his eyes. "You are Evidence Review."

"With temporary advocacy authority."

"From whom?"

"The appointment packet obscured that."

For the first time since she had met him, Elt smiled. It was brief and unpleasant. "Good. Obscure back."

He authorized her under survivor counsel's procedural allotment, narrowly enough that it would annoy everyone and broadly enough that she could stand.

Bayo watched the authorization arrive. "You are going to argue?"

"Probably badly."

"Should I note that?"

"Only if I do."

The tribunal resumed with routeholder counsel requesting immediate clarification of Tamsin Vale's ledgers. Kett's jacket remained perfect. Ira had begun to dislike the physics of it.

"Magistrate," Kett said, "routeholder parties do not contest the limited admission of salvage timing entries for thermal analysis. However, we request that the tribunal distinguish between timing relevance and proprietary or reparative implication. The compartments described by Captain Vale had, under emergency doctrine, entered salvage commons after loss of active life-support continuity."

There it was.

Not dead air. Cleaner. Worse.

The gallery did not understand quickly enough to react. Lawyers did. Ira saw survivor counsel stiffen, military counsel relax by a degree, and Nadir Pell look down at his own hands.

Om said, "State the practical effect of your requested distinction."

Kett spread his hands with careful regret. "The tribunal may consider removal times, thermal readings, and pressure states without permitting inflammatory implication that lawful emergency recovery constituted theft from legally continuing claimants. This preserves the opening phase scope Your Honor named."

Your Honor.

He had shifted from Magistrate to Your Honor. Human courts loved small titles when trying to domesticate danger.

Elt stood. "Survivor counsel objects. The routeholder position launders the legal death of compartments into moral innocence and attempts to strip Captain Vale's testimony of the very context that makes the timing records intelligible."

"That is argument, not objection," Kett said.

"It is both when spoken efficiently."

Om looked unimpressed by everyone. "Evidence Review has been assigned corroboration of the salvage timing entries. Associate Senn, do you have a position on whether compartment legal status affects timing review?"

Every head turned.

Ira rose too quickly, then made herself slow down before speaking. Her terminal projected her name under Evidence Review, not survivor counsel. Good. Let the room wonder which hat she was mishandling.

"Yes, Magistrate."

"Proceed."

Kett stood straighter. "Routeholder parties object to Evidence Review offering advocacy on a live legal doctrine."

Om asked, "Associate Senn, are you offering advocacy?"

Ira felt the answer split.

If she said yes, Kett would challenge her standing. If she said no, she might lie.

"I am identifying an evidentiary dependency," she said. "The doctrine routeholder parties invoke changes which records exist, which records were destroyed, and which claimants were allowed to preserve objections. That affects timing review."

Om's eyes sharpened. "Continue."

Ira sent three documents to the public wall.

The first was the original emergency survival memo. She highlighted the line: if no rescue pathway remains active.

"Emergency salvage commons begins only after no rescue pathway remains active. The timing question before this tribunal includes whether rescue or life-support pathways ended when the official sequence says they ended."

The second document was Tamsin's G-12 ledger entry: residual high thermal state at 19:08.

"If thermal records suggest auxiliary support continued after the official loss time, then the legal status of the compartment cannot be assumed from the official loss time without circularity."

Bayo, from the workroom channel, sent her the station ecology brief. She opened it on the wall and silently promised not to be rude to him for at least a day.

"Station ecology records define air continuity as shared across emergency baffles. A compartment can lose breathable atmosphere locally while still drawing heat, sensor priority, or pressure reserve from inhabited sections. Calling such a compartment dead because air is locally unbreathable may erase continuing system relation."

The gallery had gone quiet.

They understood now.

Ira turned to the bench. "The tribunal should not allow any party to treat vacuum exposure as automatic abandonment until timing, support continuity, and claimant status are established."

Kett rose. "This is precisely why Evidence Review should not argue. Associate Senn has introduced the phrase claimant status without foundation. In vacuum-exposed compartments, surviving legal claimants were absent."

Ira looked at him.

Do not spend outrage in the first hour, Vela had told her.

This was not the first hour anymore.

"Absent from where?" she asked.

Kett blinked. "From the compartments."

"Because they were dead, evacuated, missing, trapped, unregistered, or reclassified?"

"That question exceeds current scope."

"Your doctrine depends on the answer."

Om said nothing.

Kett turned to the bench. "Magistrate, routeholder parties cannot respond to rhetorical expansion."

"Then respond narrowly," Om said. "Associate Senn asked whether absence from a compartment proves absence of claimant."

Kett's jaw tightened. "Under emergency recovery doctrine, claimants must be identifiable through surviving registries or post-recovery filings."

Ira opened the category line from Clock B.

UNBONDED DEPENDENT CATEGORY REMOVED FROM PROTECTED TRANSFER ESTIMATE.

She did not open the child audio. She did not need to.

"If a category was removed from protected transfer estimate before the alleged loss of support," she said, "then later failure to appear in surviving registries may be an effect of the same process being used to establish abandonment."

The chamber changed again.

This time the gallery did not erupt. Silence, properly handled, could be more threatening than noise.

Kett looked toward Om. "That record has not been admitted."

"The category issue is under protected handling review," Om said.

"Then it cannot be used."

"It has not been offered for truth of the matter asserted. It has been identified as a handling risk in legal status assumptions."

Ira almost smiled and did not. Om had just built a narrow bridge over a pit he had helped dig.

Military counsel stood. "The military parties join routeholder objection. If pre-admission protected materials are allowed to contaminate legal doctrine, all opening submissions become unstable."

Om replied, "They were already unstable. That is why we are here."

The press deck lit with requests. Om ignored them.

Lael Shun's name appeared briefly on a liaison channel at the edge of Ira's display, then vanished. The Shoals Compact had not entered the room by body, but its handling habits kept showing up like salt left after water dried.

Om leaned back. "The tribunal rules as follows. Salvage commons doctrine may not be applied as a presumption to any Cinder compartment whose life-support, heat, pressure, rescue, registry, or claimant status is disputed by admitted or provisionally protected timing material. No party may use vacuum exposure alone to establish abandonment. No party may characterize disputed compartments as unowned assets in opening phase submissions."

The gallery breathed.

Kett said, "Routeholder parties reserve objection."

"Expected," Om said.

Survivor counsel sat slowly, as if relief had weight.

Ira remained standing because she had not been dismissed and because her knees had become theoretical.

Om looked at her. "Associate Senn."

"Yes, Magistrate."

"For the record, state the evidentiary principle."

She had not prepared a sentence. That was dangerous. Prepared sentences lied more elegantly, but unprepared ones sometimes exposed too much.

She thought of Tamsin's hands. She thought of G-12 smelling like bread. She thought of a child in a duct saying the green ships moved first.

"No one owns the dead air," Ira said. "But no one may use dead air to steal the living claim."

The court recorded it.

Kett objected to the phrasing.

Om sustained the objection in part and ordered the principle restated in formal language:

VACUUM EXPOSURE DOES NOT, BY ITSELF, EXTINGUISH CLAIMANT STANDING, REPARATIVE INTEREST, OR EVIDENTIARY CONTINUITY WHERE THE TIMING OR CAUSE OF LIFE-SUPPORT LOSS REMAINS DISPUTED.

The formal version was cleaner.

Ira liked hers better.

During the next recess, the woman in ash cloth sent a message through survivor counsel. It reached Ira's terminal after three security scans and one dignity review.

It contained no thanks.

Only a name:

MARA ESH

And below it:

SHE WAS UNBONDED BECAUSE HER MOTHER DIED IN QUEUE.

Ira stared at the message until Bayo spoke softly from behind her.

"Do we enter it?"

Ira closed her eyes, opened them, and checked the handling order.

Do not convert damaged witness into singular proof.

Do not summarize silence.

"Not as proof yet," she said. "As a claimant-status notice requiring protection."

Bayo entered it that way.

Mara Esh did not become evidence.

Not yet.

For now, she became harder to erase.

Chapter 7: Nadir Pell Remembers the Window

Nadir Pell asked for water before he testified.

Not medicine. Not counsel. Not delay. Water.

The request moved through the chamber with the uncomfortable intimacy of a body need. A clerk brought a sealed cup to the witness rail, scanned it for contaminants, and placed it beside the oath tray. Pell looked at the cup for a moment before touching it, as if some part of him still expected water to be rationed by rank.

Ira watched from Evidence Review with Mara Esh's claimant-status notice locked under protected handling. Her terminal had become a crowded field of tasks: salvage corroboration, dead-air doctrine, salt-sealed record limits, Sera Venn-3 custody pending, Om disclosure logged, routeholder objections reserved. The case was no longer opening. It was unfolding in multiple directions at once, each direction tied to someone's attempt to keep the first question small.

Pell lifted the cup.

His hand trembled.

The press deck noticed. Ira saw three lenses adjust.

Magistrate Om noticed too. "No close biometric feed of witness tremor. Public record may show posture and voice only."

The press deck dimmed in irritation.

Pell drank half the water and set the cup down.

The oath officer approached with gloves.

Pell put them on.

Tamsin Vale, seated now in the protected witness tier, gave him a look Ira could not read. Contempt would have been easier. This was something more tired.

The oath registered him cleanly.

Nadir Pell. Former military logistics liaison. Wartime rank: commander-equivalent under emergency convoy integration. Current status: represented witness, subject to potential adverse finding, not charged.

Not charged. The phrase sat in the room like a dare.

Military counsel conducted direct examination because Pell remained nominally aligned with them, though everyone had seen him step outside their preferred order. Counsel was named Sienne Marr, precise, silver-haired, and visibly furious in a way she had disciplined into elegance.

"Mr. Pell," Marr began, "during the Cinder evacuation window, what was your assigned role?"

"I transmitted priority models between routeholder convoy control, military gate command, and emergency logistics."

"Did you command the gate?"

"No."

"Did you have authority to narrow the transit corridor?"

"No."

"Did you personally remove any civilian category from the protected transfer estimate?"

Pell looked at the water cup.

"I transmitted a model that did."

Marr's mouth tightened. "Please answer the question asked."

"No."

"Did you create that model?"

"No."

"Did you understand the model to be a casualty selection instrument?"

The gallery sharpened.

Pell inhaled.

"No."

Marr let the answer stand for one second, then moved quickly. "What did you understand it to be?"

"A survival model."

"For whom?"

"For the convoy group."

"Define convoy group."

"Ships inside or assigned to the evacuation corridor, including military carriers, medical transports, bonded civilian modules, routeholder vessels under emergency integration, archive capsules, and attached support craft."

"Did that include every person waiting at Cinder Gate?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Pell's eyes lifted toward the gallery for the first time.

"Because the gate could not carry everyone."

The chamber did not explode. The sentence was too familiar. It had been the old wall for twenty years: not everyone could be saved. People had beaten themselves bloody against it and found stone.

Marr asked, "Was that fact created by you?"

"No."

"Was it created by routeholder counsel?"

"No."

"Was it created by military command?"

"No."

"What created it?"

"Mass limits. Corridor stability. Enemy pressure. Available power. Docking congestion. Time."

Time. Ira wrote the word in the margin.

There were too many clocks now for time to remain neutral.

On her Evidence Review screen, the live transcript linked Pell's answer to an archived modeling appendix. The appendix had not been admitted, only pre-indexed, but its field names were visible under limited review:

MASS DELTA.

CORRIDOR INSTABILITY MINUTES.

RECEIVING DELAY COST.

DEPENDENT MATCH TIME.

SECURITY SCREENING DRAG.

Ira had expected numbers to hide people. These did something worse. They translated people into delays and then treated delay as the original fact.

One unbonded child did not appear as a child. The model rendered the child as dependent match time, receiving delay cost, guardian verification uncertainty, and possible corridor instability if the receiving module had to reshuffle berth assignments. A family did not appear as a family. It appeared as linked mass, emotional separation risk, loading friction, and protest probability. Elderly station-locals appeared as medical handling drag unless already attached to a bonded worker or routeholder contract. Anyone with a tool certification gained utility weight. Anyone with a debt ledger gained traceability. Anyone with neither became soft mass.

Soft mass.

The term appeared in an explanatory note with the tact of a blade.

SOFT MASS: PERSONNEL OR MATERIAL LOAD LACKING IMMEDIATE OPERATIONAL ROLE, BONDED RECEIVER, OR SYSTEM-CONTINUITY VALUE.

Ira copied the note into her private grid and marked it not for public use yet. If she threw it at the room now, the defense would spend an hour arguing technical jargon and the gallery would hear only contempt. Vela had warned her about spending outrage. She had not said anything about storing it carefully until it became evidence.

Bayo messaged from the workroom channel:

Soft mass appears in three routeholder models and one military acceptance memo.

Ira replied:

Do not summarize. Preserve source contexts separately.

His answer came back:

I know.

Two words. Progress.

Pell's gloved hands remained folded while Marr prepared her next question. The gloves were still green. Ira wondered what color they would show if the system measured not stress but translation damage: how much of a person had to be converted into acceptable terms before a room could discuss leaving them behind.

Om's temporary order sat on the public wall, smaller now beneath procedural headings.

NO CATEGORY MAY BE TREATED AS NEUTRAL UNTIL ITS OPERATIONAL EFFECT IS TESTED.

Pell had not seen the modeling appendix on Ira's screen. He did not need to. He had lived inside its verbs.

Marr said, "Tell the tribunal what triage meant in that window."

Survivor counsel objected to narrative scope.

Om allowed limited answer.

Pell folded his gloved hands. The sensors lit green around his knuckles.

"It meant every addition to the corridor increased risk of losing all traffic. It meant a medical transport with six hundred burn patients might be denied if its mass profile destabilized the gate. It meant a military carrier with repair capacity might be prioritized over a civilian habitat because without the carrier the next station would die. It meant children with no registered guardian could not be matched to receiving modules fast enough. It meant archive capsules were sometimes loaded before bodies because the bodies had no air left and the archives carried settlement governance, genetic records, navigation keys, debt ledgers, seed templates, vaccine lines."

The gallery reacted at archive capsules.

Pell did not stop.

"It meant no category was clean. It meant every word became murder if you stared at it long enough."

Marr's face showed alarm. He had moved past defense into confession-shaped explanation.

"Mr. Pell," she said, "did you believe the category revisions were necessary?"

"Yes."

"Do you believe that now?"

He looked at Om, then at Tamsin, then, unexpectedly, at Ira.

"I believe necessary is a word people use when they want the alternative to stop existing."

Marr closed her eyes briefly.

Om said, "Answer the question directly."

Pell turned back to counsel. "Some revisions were necessary. Some were convenient. Some were convenient inside necessity, which is where we hid them."

The chamber went very quiet.

Marr requested a recess to confer with her witness.

Om denied it.

"You put him on the stand," he said.

Routeholder counsel looked as if they were watching a controlled burn jump a containment line.

Marr adjusted course. "Mr. Pell, did any routeholder representative instruct you to abandon Cinder Nine civilians?"

"Not in those words."

"In any words?"

"They provided risk models."

"Models are not instructions."

"No."

"Did military command accept or reject those models?"

"Accepted some. Modified others. Ignored several."

"Who made final evacuation decisions?"

"Gate command."

"Name."

Pell's gloved fingers pressed together.

"Admiral Rhos Daven."

A dead man. Of course.

The name entered the record with the tired usefulness of a corpse who could carry blame without objecting. Ira saw survivor counsel prepare to attack, routeholder counsel relax slightly, military counsel cling to the answer like a rail.

Marr asked, "Is Admiral Daven alive to answer for those decisions?"

"No."

"Did he die during the Cinder evacuation?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"He remained at gate command until corridor collapse."

Marr let the silence frame him as sacrifice.

Pell ruined it.

"After ordering the witness deck cleared."

Marr froze.

Om leaned forward. "Clarify."

Pell swallowed. "Gate command had a witness deck: junior techs, maintenance monitors, civilian liaison, archive recorder. Daven ordered it cleared at 17:41."

Ira's terminal highlighted the time before she asked it to.

17:41.

Before hostile alarm.

Before official narrowing.

Before the model's most dangerous lines.

Marr said, "That order is not in your prior statement."

"No."

"Why?"

"No one asked when the witness deck was cleared."

That sentence carried the whole architecture of failed inquiry inside it.

Om turned to the clerks. "Enter witness deck clearance as new line of inquiry. Evidence Review will identify related records."

Ira's task list grew.

Marr's direct examination ended sooner than planned.

Survivor counsel rose for cross. Maro Elt did not approach the rail immediately. He stood at his table and looked at Pell as if deciding which wound to touch first.

"Mr. Pell, you said children with no registered guardian could not be matched to receiving modules fast enough."

"Yes."

"Who designed the matching system?"

"Compact emergency logistics, modified by routeholder receiving protocols."

"Did the system recognize children whose guardians died in queue?"

Pell's eyes moved once. Not toward Ira. Toward the protected evidence lock.

"Not reliably."

"Did you know that then?"

"We knew guardian loss created match failures."

"That is not what I asked."

"No. I did not know the full extent."

"Did you ask?"

Pell's gloves registered stress in green-to-amber flicker.

"No."

"Why not?"

"Because every question cost time."

"Whose time?"

Pell did not answer.

Elt waited.

Pell said, "The people already inside the corridor."

The gallery made a low sound.

Elt took one step forward. "Do you hear what that means?"

"Yes."

"Say it."

Marr objected.

Om overruled.

Pell looked at the gallery. "We treated people outside the corridor as time costs to people inside it."

The woman in ash cloth stood so fast her chair struck the row behind her.

Security lights warmed.

She shouted, "My daughter was not time."

The room stopped.

Ira knew before the name appeared on her terminal.

Mara Esh.

The woman was the message sender. Or one of them. Her name field populated through survivor counsel:

Jalen Esh. Mother of Mara Esh. Recorded guardian death in queue disputed.

But the woman had said my daughter.

Dead guardians did not shout.

Ira looked down at the protected notice:

SHE WAS UNBONDED BECAUSE HER MOTHER DIED IN QUEUE.

Something was wrong.

Either the message was false, the record was wrong, or motherhood had been severed by a legal category before death ever arrived.

Om ordered the gallery lights cooled but did not remove Jalen Esh.

"Ms. Esh," he said, "you will have standing to submit a claimant-status correction through survivor counsel."

Jalen pressed both hands to the glass. "I submitted for nineteen years."

"Submit again under this docket."

"So you can lose her cleaner?"

Om accepted the accusation without answer because no answer would have been adequate.

Elt resumed, voice lower. "Mr. Pell, do you know Mara Esh?"

Marr objected. "Foundation."

Elt said, "The witness testified to unbonded dependent category handling."

Om allowed limited answer.

Pell's face had gone gray.

"I know the category. I do not know individual names."

Jalen laughed behind the glass.

It was the worst sound Ira had heard all day.

Elt asked, "Did the category revisions distinguish between legally unbonded children and children whose guardians were temporarily absent, injured, dead, misrecorded, or separated by queue movement?"

"No."

"Why not?"

"The system could not process that distinction in time."

"The system."

"Yes."

"Not people?"

Pell looked at him. "People made the system before the window. People chose to trust it during the window. People used its output after the window to say no one had chosen."

That was the closest he had come to anger.

Elt saw it and changed direction.

"Mr. Pell, are you trying to confess?"

Marr stood. "Objection."

"Sustained," Om said.

Elt rephrased. "What do you want from this tribunal?"

Marr objected again.

Om hesitated, then allowed it. "Answer without argument."

Pell looked at the water cup.

"I want the sequence entered correctly."

"Not absolution?"

"No."

"Not punishment?"

"Punishment would be simpler than sequence."

"Not forgiveness?"

Pell's mouth tightened. "From whom?"

Elt had no answer to that. Or too many.

Cross-examination ended with more damage done than conclusion reached.

Om called a brief procedural pause, not a recess. The distinction kept everyone trapped in the room.

Ira's terminal flashed with a new Evidence Review assignment:

WITNESS DECK CLEARANCE / 17:41 / FIND SOURCE.

Bayo messaged from the workroom:

Searching gate command architecture. Witness deck logs missing from main archive.

Ira typed:

Check salvage, maintenance, suit cams, private memorial submissions, insurance exclusions.

Bayo replied:

That is not one search.

Ira wrote:

No. It is the case.

Across the chamber, Pell removed the oath gloves. The sensors had left faint pressure marks on his skin.

He looked smaller without them.

Jalen Esh remained standing behind the gallery glass until survivor counsel reached her.

Ira looked between them: the man who had transmitted categories, the mother whose daughter had become one, the court trying to make time move in order after twenty years of legal delay.

Triage, Pell had said.

Ira wrote a private note beneath the evidence grid:

Triage is not only choosing who receives help. It is choosing which questions are too expensive to ask.

She did not enter it into the record.

Not yet.

Chapter 8: A Child in the Maintenance Duct

The child audio could not be played in public, so it began to haunt every room that refused it.

Ira heard it first in memory, then in machinery. The gate tone under the child's breath became the hum of Sable's air recyclers. The line about green ships moved first hid inside every counsel objection that used the word sequence. Even the tribunal bells acquired a smaller sound beneath them, an imagined voice saying it changed before they said.

That was why the handling order existed. Not to bury the audio. To keep the court from feeding on it.

The witness-protection review took place in a chamber built for classified battlefield confessions. It had no gallery, no press relay, no public clock. The walls were layered with acoustic null gel and old ceramic armor. A silence box stood at the center table with the salt-sealed record locked inside. Around it sat Magistrate Om, Ira, Bayo, Vela on internal relay, survivor counsel Maro Elt, military counsel Sienne Marr, routeholder advocate Marun Kett, a child trauma specialist from Sable medical, and a Glassward legal observer who had not yet introduced themself as anything but Oriole's temporary ear.

The observer used a human-shaped body with matte gray skin and no visible age. Their eyes were black, not for drama but because the cheap relay substrate did not bother imitating sclera.

"Name for the record," Om said.

"Lio-Interim-Glassward-Receiver, acting under limited custody channel for Oriole-of-Archive."

Kett muttered, "That is a role, not a name."

The observer turned toward him. "In this room, so are you."

Ira liked them immediately and distrusted that.

Om opened the review. "The question before this chamber is whether Maintenance Relay Audio M-14 may be played, transcribed, summarized, excerpted, or otherwise used in public session before full custody determination of Sera Venn-3."

"Routeholder parties oppose public playback," Kett said.

Everyone looked at him.

He managed not to look pleased by surprising them. "Not out of tenderness. Out of prejudice risk. An unidentified child's voice saying something dramatic near a corrupted gate event will overwhelm the tribunal's ability to evaluate technical evidence."

"You mean it will make people angry," Elt said.

"People are already angry. It will make them certain."

That was not wrong.

Ira hated how often the wrong people used true sentences.

Marr said, "Military parties also oppose playback until authentication. We do not oppose protected technical review."

Vela spoke from the relay. "The salt-sealed condition already prohibits spectacle. The question is how to preserve meaning without turning the child into a weapon."

The medical specialist, Dr. Iven Sar, adjusted her display. "The voice appears preadolescent. Stress markers are extreme. Repeated public playback would be harmful to surviving relatives and potentially to any continuity derivative if linked."

"If linked?" Ira asked.

The Glassward receiver answered. "Standing disputed. Link probable. Not yet authorized."

"Meaning Sera may be harmed by playback."

"Sera may be harmed by playback, transcription, misidentification, forced singularity, denial of link, confirmation of link, or being discussed by people who think those are separate harms."

Bayo stopped typing for a second, then resumed with visible resignation.

Om looked to Ira. "Evidence Review?"

She had prepared three options and disliked all of them.

"Public playback should be barred pending custody review," she said. "A restricted transcript may be worse, because it makes the voice easier to quote without context. A technical timing abstract risks summarizing silence. I recommend creation of a protected presence marker."

Kett frowned. "A what?"

"The public record can state that a protected child-associated maintenance audio exists, that it is linked to Duct M-14 at 17:42, that it raises timing and category questions, and that its content is not being played or summarized until witness-protection and custody standing are resolved."

Elt looked dissatisfied. "Survivors will think we are hiding it."

"We are," Ira said.

The room went still.

She continued before courage could leak out of her. "We are hiding it from spectacle, from misuse, from routeholder prejudice arguments, from military authentication traps, from our own desire to make the court feel what the record means before we know who it belongs to. The hiding must be visible. That is the difference."

Vela's relay softened by one pixel of expression.

Om said, "Visible withholding."

"Yes."

The Glassward receiver tilted their head. "Glassward custody channel can accept visible withholding as non-erasure, provided Sera's eventual standing is not prejudiced."

Kett said, "Routeholder parties object to the name Sera appearing in relation to this audio."

"It has not," Om said.

"It has repeatedly in this chamber."

"This chamber is sealed."

"Seals leak."

Vela said, "So do courts."

Om ignored both. "The protected presence marker is adopted for public session. Now we need technical review."

The silence box opened its internal display. The audio did not play aloud. Instead, the waveform appeared with time anchors. Ira was grateful for that for exactly four seconds, until she realized the shape of panic was visible even without sound.

Small breaths clustered before the first spoken line.

A gate pulse ran beneath them.

Dr. Sar raised one hand before anyone could touch the playback controls. "The waveform itself may become identifying material."

Kett looked almost offended by the caution. "It is a line on a screen."

"A voiceprint is also a line on a screen," Sar said. "A panic rhythm can be recognizable to someone who has heard the child breathe in sleep, cry after an injury, or hide under a table during drills."

The room adjusted around that. Not dramatically. Adults rarely liked being reminded that intimacy had technical consequences.

Elt rubbed his forehead. "Then what can we review?"

"Timing anchors," Sar said. "Stress range without contour. Presence of speech without phonetic display. Environmental tones separated from vocal material. No full waveform in public. No spectrogram. No machine-readable voiceprint. No transcript fragments on unsecured displays."

Bayo's hands hovered over his terminal. "If I enter that as review conditions, the system will ask whether the audio is classified, medical, protected minor, alien-handled, or continuity material."

"Yes," Ira said.

"It is all of those except classified, depending on which office wants control."

"Then do not select a single class."

Bayo gave her a look that would have been insubordinate yesterday and was now merely exhausted. "The system requires one."

The Glassward receiver said, "Choose protected minor and add nonexclusive handling."

Kett objected. "That prejudges the source as a child witness rather than an audio artifact."

"It prejudges harm," Dr. Sar said. "Not standing."

Om considered that. "Enter protected minor for harm protocols only. Add explicit reservation that classification does not decide identity, personhood, custody, authenticity, or admissibility."

Bayo typed. The terminal resisted with a yellow warning:

CLASSIFICATION CONFLICT MAY LIMIT PARTY ACCESS.

Marr said, "Military parties preserve objection to access limitations."

Kett said, "Routeholder parties do the same."

Elt said, "Survivor counsel preserves objection to the objection."

Om's patience thinned visibly. "Everyone's future dissatisfaction is preserved."

That almost broke the room. Not into laughter, but into the brief human release before law sealed itself again.

Ira looked at the waveform, now partially masked by Sar's restrictions. The child's breath became gray intervals. Speech became sealed bars. Environmental sound remained visible in blue. The record looked less moving and more useful, which was its own kind of violence.

Vela watched the transformation with the expression of someone seeing a body covered correctly before examination. "This is better," she said. "Not good. Better."

The Glassward receiver added a custody note:

MASKING IS NOT ERASURE IF REVERSIBLE UNDER STANDING CONDITIONS.

Kett read it and said, "That sounds like doctrine being invented mid-hearing."

Ira answered before she meant to. "All doctrine was invented mid-harm first."

Om looked at her.

She looked down at her notes.

"Strike that as argument," she said.

"It was not entered," Om replied.

But Bayo had paused long enough that she knew he had wanted to.

Vela highlighted the pulse. "This is what matters technically."

Kett gave a short laugh. "Not the child?"

"Do not try that with me," Vela said. "The child matters too much to make the child carry everything."

She overlaid Clock A, Clock B, and the maintenance relay.

17:41:52 - Duct M-14 manual panel opened.

17:41:57 - priority convoy mass revised downward.

17:41:59 - unauthorized child-size mass detected.

17:42:03 - local audio begins.

17:42:04 - civilian attachment risk marked excessive.

17:42:08 - official sequence says evacuation queue stable.

17:42:11 - unbonded dependent category removed.

"The child is in the duct before the official record says queue instability begins," Ira said.

"More," Vela said.

She magnified the gate tone under the breath.

"The tone is not ambient station vibration. It is a corridor correction resonance."

Marr leaned forward. "Can you prove that?"

"Not alone. But compare."

She brought up a training file from gate operations. A standard correction resonance sounded as a visual pattern: tight, repeating, clean at the start and ragged near end. The M-14 waveform matched the first half, then bent.

"This correction starts before the hostile alarm," Vela said. "Not merely before confirmed fire. Before alarm receipt."

Kett said, "Assuming the local clock is accurate."

Bayo spoke without looking up. "Duct M-14 clock drift was plus two-point-one seconds against environmental master at last calibration."

Everyone turned to him.

He flushed. "I checked."

Ira made good on her promise not to be rude to him.

"Good," she said.

Vela adjusted the overlay. "Even corrected for drift, the resonance begins too early."

Marr's voice cooled. "Early correction does not prove malicious narrowing. Gate crews may have been responding to instability before formal alarm."

"Yes," Ira said.

Marr looked surprised.

Ira pointed to the waveform. "That is why the child's line matters as timing relation, not proof of motive. 'It changed before they said' aligns with a real correction event. 'They moved the green ships first' links perceived boarding priority to that event. We still need the source of green ship designation."

The Glassward receiver said, "Green ships may be child's visual category."

Vela nodded. "Or emergency corridor lights. Or medical hull coding. Or routeholder livery. Or archive capsule transfer frames. We do not know."

"Then the line is ambiguous," Kett said.

"Yes," Ira said again.

He did not enjoy agreement from her.

"Ambiguity is not useless," she continued. "It tells us which records must exist if anyone wants to disprove it responsibly."

Om asked, "List them."

Ira did.

"Duct M-14 visual feed if any. Adjacent corridor traffic. Hull color assignments for ships loaded between 17:41 and 17:44. Emergency lighting states. Medical transport markings. Archive capsule movement. Child registry near maintenance sectors. Work orders for Duct M-14. Door access records. Any manual panel alarm suppression."

Bayo was typing fast.

The Glassward receiver added, "Continuity salvage chain for source child."

Kett objected before the sentence ended. "No. That presumes link."

"It preserves possible link," the receiver said.

"Glassward cannot use a disputed audio file to expand custody claims."

"Routeholders cannot use custody uncertainty to prevent preservation."

Om said, "The tribunal agrees with the second sentence and reserves the first dispute. Add preservation order."

Kett sat back, unhappy but not surprised. He had not expected to win everything. He had expected to make the eventual path expensive.

Dr. Sar requested that the child's voice not be voiceprinted against public family submissions without consent.

Elt looked pained. "Families are already submitting names."

"That does not mean they consent to forensic grief," Sar said.

Ira wrote the phrase down because it was ugly and necessary.

Forensic grief.

Om adopted the restriction. No public family voice matches. No child identity speculation in open session. Claimant-status notices could be filed and protected. The maintenance audio would be marked present but withheld.

Then the silence box did something no one expected.

It displayed a second custody layer beneath the waveform.

UNAUTHORIZED PRESERVATION ACT / POSTWAR YEAR 3

SOURCE: CINDERWAKe MINOR SCHOOL NODE

RECOVERY AGENT: REDACTED

DERIVATIVE CONTINUITY: SERA VENN-3 PARTIAL

The Glassward receiver went completely still.

Vela whispered, "That was not visible before."

Om's face hardened. "Why now?"

The receiver answered, "Because the record believes handling conditions are met."

Kett almost stood, then thought better of it.

Ira stared at the source line.

Cinderwake Minor.

Her station.

Her childhood school network, or one of its predecessors. The room narrowed to a point.

Om noticed. Of course he did.

"Associate Senn?"

"Cinderwake Minor is my birth registry."

Marr said, "Then you have a conflict."

"Probably," Ira said.

Kett seized on it. "Routeholder parties move to remove Associate Senn from all review of this audio."

Elt objected. "Half the Ash Belt has a conflict under that standard."

"Half the Ash Belt is not evidence review staff."

Om looked at Ira. "Did you attend a Cinderwake Minor school node?"

"Yes. But postwar year twenty, not year three. I had no contact with this preservation act."

"Family employment?"

"My mother maintained ration ledgers. My father repaired water valves. Neither worked school archives as far as I know."

"As far as you know," Kett repeated.

Ira wanted to snap at him. Instead she looked at Om. "I should be screened from determining authenticity of the preservation act. I can continue timing review if the court agrees."

Vela nodded before Om asked.

"That is a clean enough division for now," she said.

The Glassward receiver added, "Glassward custody channel accepts limited screen if source-station conflict is logged rather than treated as disqualifying contamination."

Kett objected to being surrounded by nuance.

Om ruled: Ira would remain on timing and handling review, screened from source-preservation authenticity pending conflict check. Bayo would coordinate preservation-chain collection under senior clerk oversight. The source-station conflict would be visible in the sealed record.

Visible, not fatal.

That had become the tribunal's only workable ethic.

When the review ended, public session resumed long enough for Om to enter the protected presence marker.

He did not play the audio.

He did not quote the child.

He said:

"The tribunal has confirmed the existence of a protected child-associated maintenance audio linked to Duct M-14 during the disputed evacuation window. Its content raises timing and category issues. It is withheld from public playback and summary pending witness-protection, custody, and authentication review. This withholding is part of the record and shall not be characterized as absence."

The gallery did not know what to do with that.

Neither did the press.

Jalen Esh stood again behind the glass. She did not shout this time.

She placed one hand over her mouth and one on the pressure barrier.

Ira understood then that visible withholding could still hurt like erasure to the people waiting on the other side of proof.

After session adjourned, she returned to the sealed workroom and opened the conflict questionnaire.

First question:

Do you have personal, familial, legal, educational, financial, or memory-linked relation to the source record?

Ira thought of Cinderwake Minor, school walls patched with mismatched plates, teachers who never explained why certain archive closets were sealed, children trading rumors about dead stations because adults punished questions badly.

She typed:

Unknown. Possible educational relation by later attendance at source network successor. No known direct contact. Requires investigation.

Second question:

Can you remain impartial?

Ira deleted three answers.

Then she wrote:

No. I can remain accountable.

The form rejected the answer.

She entered it again in the remarks field where rejected truths sometimes survived.

Chapter 9: The First Recess Riot

The leak reached the public concourse as a sentence with its bones removed.

CHILD AUDIO PROVES GREEN SHIPS LEFT FIRST.

By the time Ira saw it, the sentence had already fractured into smaller, sharper versions on six wall feeds, twelve private channels, and the handslate of a woman in the survivor gallery who was crying so hard she could not unlock her own screen. The protected presence marker had been entered less than an hour before. The audio had not been played. The child had not been named. The court had not said proves.

None of that mattered once the sentence found hunger.

The first shout rose from the lower concourse, below the tribunal chamber. Sable Array's public decks were supposed to keep rival groups separated by route line, station origin, and party designation. Whoever designed the crowd plan had understood maps and underestimated grief. Survivors from Cinder Nine had been placed near convoy loyalists because both were classified as public claimant observers rather than adversarial blocs. Routeholder family delegates had a buffer zone, but their staff did not. Press stringers moved between all of them like sparks looking for air.

Ira was in Evidence Review when the crowd-control tone sounded.

Not the tribunal bell.

Not a drill.

A flat, old station warning: bodies moving against flow.

Bayo looked up from the preservation-chain queue. "That is concourse level."

Ira opened the public security feed.

For two seconds, the concourse looked merely crowded. Then a man in convoy green turned too slowly, and a woman with an ash cloth around her throat struck him across the face with a memorial tag. He did not fall. Someone behind him shoved forward. A press drone dipped, caught the motion, and projected it huge on the wall before station security killed the feed. The crowd reacted to the magnified blow as if it had happened a second time.

"Who leaked it?" Bayo whispered.

"Later," Ira said.

Her terminal filled with orders. Tribunal staff remain in secured areas. Witness movement suspended. Public decks sealed by section. Medical teams deploying. All counsel to maintain chamber availability. Evidence Review to confirm whether leaked sentence matches protected material.

The last order was absurd and necessary.

Ira typed:

Leaked sentence is inaccurate. Protected material does not prove. Public correction needed immediately.

The system returned:

PUBLIC COMMUNICATIONS HOLD PENDING MAGISTRATE APPROVAL.

Of course it did.

The public safety console offered correction templates, because every institution eventually automated the moments when language mattered most.

FALSE INFORMATION ALERT.

UNVERIFIED CLAIM NOTICE.

SECURITY RUMOR SUPPRESSION.

PUBLIC MISINTERPRETATION ADVISORY.

Each label made the situation worse in a different way. False information would sound like denial. Unverified claim would sound like delay. Rumor suppression would sound like threat. Misinterpretation would sound like the court blaming people for understanding pain too quickly.

Ira opened a blank field instead.

Bayo leaned close enough to see the cursor. "The console will flag unscripted safety language."

"Good."

"That was not advice."

"It can become useful anyway."

She tried the first sentence:

The leaked statement is false.

She deleted it. The sentence was legally attractive and humanly stupid. The protected record did contain a child's line about green ships, and if the public later learned that, the court would look as if it had lied to keep order.

Second attempt:

The leaked statement is unproven.

She deleted that too. Unproven sounded as if the court's main concern was evidentiary posture rather than bodies at lift seals.

Third:

No public statement claiming proof from the protected child-associated record has been authorized.

Better. Cold, but accurate. It shifted the issue from whether grief was wrong to whether someone had permission to use a protected witness as a weapon.

Bayo said, "It does not tell them what is true."

"No."

"They will hate that."

"Yes."

On the feed, a second surge moved toward the eastern lift. Security shields locked together. Someone fell and vanished under legs for two heartbeats before hands pulled them up. Ira felt her own breath shorten.

"Truth is not arriving fast enough," Bayo said.

"Truth is not the only emergency."

He looked at her then, and she saw that the day had aged him past resentment. "What is?"

"Whether the eventual truth still has people alive to hear it."

The sentence did not help. It simply made the work sharper.

She stood.

Bayo stared at her. "Where are you going?"

"To get approval."

"The corridor is sealed."

"Then I will be very close to where I started."

The workroom door opened before she reached it. Vela Cho's relay appeared on the wall, not through the tribunal channel but through the salt-sealed evidence connection.

"Do not issue a denial that says the audio proves nothing," Vela said.

"I was not planning to."

"You were planning something close."

Ira stopped. "Do you have a better sentence?"

"Not yet. That is why I interrupted you."

From the concourse feed, sound bled through before dampers caught up: shouting, a security command, the hollow thud of a body hitting a partition.

"People are getting hurt."

"Yes," Vela said. "And if the first official correction sounds like the court protecting routeholders, more will."

Ira wanted to hate her for being right and had no time.

"Then help."

Vela looked down, reading channels Ira could not see. "Say: the tribunal has confirmed a protected child-associated record exists. No public statement claiming what it proves is authorized. Misuse of the record may harm witness standing and claimant protection."

"That sounds like law."

"Good. Law is sometimes a barrier."

"It does not say the green ships line is unverified."

"Add: specific phrases circulating in public feeds have not been admitted, authenticated, or cleared for release."

Ira wrote it, sent it to Om's emergency channel, and marked it public-harm mitigation.

The approval came back in nine seconds.

AUTHORIZED / ISSUE THROUGH TRIBUNAL PUBLIC SAFETY FEED

Not signed.

But Om's seal was attached.

Bayo read the statement twice. "Will anyone believe it?"

"Belief is too much to ask. I want three people to hesitate before hitting someone."

He sent it.

On the concourse feed, the words appeared across every wall in white. For one breath the crowd looked up.

Then someone shouted, "They are hiding the child again!"

The riot changed shape.

Before the statement, anger had moved toward convoy green. After it, anger moved toward doors.

Sable Array sealed the tribunal access lifts.

The sound that followed was not loud at first. It was collective impact transmitted through station structure: palms, fists, memorial tags, canes, tool handles, bodies. The old listening station carried it upward, and the tribunal chamber heard the public knocking from below.

Magistrate Om reconvened emergency session with half the parties still out of breath.

No public gallery. No press deck. Counsel only, witness representatives, Evidence Review, station security command, and one medical coordinator whose sleeves were already marked with blood.

"Report," Om said.

Security Commander Hales spoke from the concourse command well. "Public decks Two and Three in active disorder. Twelve injuries confirmed, two serious. No hull risk. No weapons discharge. Press relays severed from live projection. Routeholder staff extracted from lower east. Survivor blocs resisting separation."

Elt stood. "Do not call them blocs."

Hales looked at him. "Counselor, I have three hundred people trying to break their way into a secured lift. I will call them whatever gets my teams breathing."

Om raised a hand before Elt could answer. "What triggered first contact?"

Hales played a muted clip. No audio, only motion. A convoy loyalist holding a green armband. A survivor woman with a memorial tag. A press drone drifting too close. A wall feed flashing the leaked sentence. The woman's face breaking open before her hand moved.

Ira recognized the woman from the gallery.

Jalen Esh.

Her stomach tightened.

Hales paused the frame before impact. "Identified as Jalen Esh, claimant-status notice filed today."

Kett said, "Routeholder parties request immediate protective relocation of all personnel wearing convoy or house colors."

Elt turned on him. "Your concern is cloth?"

"My concern is targeted violence."

"Your clients wore survival as branding into a room full of abandoned families."

"And your client struck a man in the face."

The room rose toward shouting.

Om's voice cut it flat. "Enough."

He turned to Ira. "Evidence Review. Leak assessment."

She stood with the statement still open on her terminal. "The leaked sentence is not an authorized court statement. It contains language not admitted into public record. It converts a protected child-associated audio into proof. It uses a specific phrase that exists in protected review but has not been cleared for public release."

Marr said, "Then the leak came from inside the protected review."

"Or from someone with access to a derivative of the record before review," Vela said from relay.

Kett seized on that. "Glassward."

The Glassward receiver was not in the emergency session. Convenient absence made an easy target.

Ira said, "Or routeholder counsel. Or military counsel. Or survivor counsel. Or tribunal staff. Or old Sable acoustic pathways. Or the original preservation chain. Or someone who guessed a phrase and got lucky enough to kill people with it."

Kett's mouth tightened. "That is not a useful accusation."

"It was a useful refusal."

Om said, "Leak investigation opened. Separate from public safety response."

Hales leaned closer to his channel. "Magistrate, we need public-facing authority. If I clear the concourse by force, the station will hold, but the tribunal may not."

"Define force."

"Pressure push, sonic disorientation, section oxygen reduction warnings, hard extraction teams."

The room went cold at oxygen.

Elt said, "Absolutely not."

Hales snapped, "Then tell them to stop trying to breach lift seals."

Ira looked at the concourse feed. Jalen Esh stood near the front, one hand bleeding, the other pressed against a security barrier. Around her, people shouted toward the sealed lift. The convoy loyalist she had hit was being treated by medics behind a partition. He looked young. Younger than Ira had expected. Too young to be the person her anger wanted.

Tamsin Vale appeared in the witness holding corridor feed, arguing with a guard.

"Put her through," Ira said.

No one listened.

She repeated it louder. "Put Captain Vale through."

Om looked at her, then nodded to Hales.

Tamsin's face appeared on emergency relay. "I can go down."

Hales said, "No witness enters an active disorder zone."

"I have walked into worse with less authority."

"That is not an argument."

"It is experience."

Elt asked, "What would you do?"

Tamsin looked at him as if he had asked how to open a hatch. "Stand where both sides can hate me."

Kett laughed once. "This tribunal is not sending a salvage captain as crowd control."

Tamsin turned toward his quadrant of the screen. "They are not angry because they need control. They are angry because everyone keeps moving the truth behind doors. Send another security line and you prove the door is the only honest thing in the station."

Hales rubbed both hands over his face. "I need a practical plan."

Tamsin said, "Open an audio channel to concourse. No images. Images make people perform. Let Esh speak first if she wants. Then I speak. Then the magistrate enters the protected presence order again in plain language."

Om said, "Why you?"

"Because I stole from their dead and said so. They may not trust me, but they know I am not clean."

The sentence sat in the emergency room and became practical.

Vela said, "No child phrase. No proof language. No denial of pain."

Om looked to Hales. "Can you open a controlled audio channel?"

"Yes. If they stop striking panels long enough to hear."

"Make them hear the channel open," Tamsin said. "Old station tone. Not court bell."

Hales gave her a sharp look. "You know Sable systems?"

"I salvaged a sister array."

"Of course you did."

The station tone sounded through the public concourse: not alarm, not order, but the low maintenance call used when pressure crews needed silence to locate a leak. Older Ash Belt bodies recognized it first. They stopped. Younger bodies stopped because the older ones did.

The banging thinned.

Hales opened the channel.

No one spoke.

Then Jalen Esh's voice came through, raw and much smaller than her anger had looked.

"They have my daughter's voice behind a door."

No one corrected her.

Tamsin answered, "Yes."

The concourse sound shifted.

Jalen said, "You don't know that."

"No," Tamsin said. "I know they have a child's voice. I know you filed Mara's name. I know that if they throw the voice onto a wall before it has standing, people like Kett will cut it apart until your daughter becomes a rumor with a bad timestamp."

Kett flinched in the emergency room but wisely said nothing.

Jalen's breathing filled the channel.

Tamsin continued. "I know doors feel like theft. I made a life opening them after everyone inside was dead. This door has to stay closed until it opens in a way that does not let them take her again."

Someone in the concourse shouted, "They already took her!"

"Yes," Tamsin said.

That yes did more than any denial could have.

The concourse quieted another degree.

Om entered the channel. He did not use court voice.

"This is Magistrate Om. The tribunal has confirmed a protected child-associated record. It is not being erased. It is not being played because playing it now would let parties argue spectacle instead of standing. Every name submitted in relation to that record will be protected. Every misuse of that record will be investigated. The court will not call withholding absence."

Jalen said, "You said courts lose things."

Om answered, "Yes."

"Why should we trust yours?"

"You should not. You should watch it."

The answer moved through the concourse like an unexpected change in gravity.

Ira closed her eyes briefly.

Hales signaled that the lift breach attempt had stopped. Medical teams were moving. Survivor blocs, claimant groups, people, whatever word could survive this, were separating enough to breathe.

Then a young male voice entered the channel. "My brother wore convoy green. He died pulling people into a med module. She hit me for a color."

Jalen said nothing.

Tamsin answered, "Then you get treated too."

"That's all?"

"No. But it's first."

The riot did not end cleanly. No riot did. It thinned into treatment, statements, protective relocations, three arrests, seven formal complaints, and a security review that would make everyone angrier tomorrow. Jalen Esh was not arrested. The convoy loyalist refused to file assault charge after learning her name, then filed a statement saying refusal to charge should not be taken as forgiveness. Ira respected him for that.

By the end of the emergency session, the tribunal had lost the illusion that evidence could stay inside rooms until law was ready.

Om ordered all future protected presence markers to include public-harm language before rumors could fill the shape. Vela began drafting leak-resistant summaries that still did not summarize silence. Hales banned visible faction colors from mixed concourse sections and received objections from every faction, which suggested he had done something fair enough to be hated.

Ira returned to Evidence Review after station night cycle began.

Bayo was already there, pale and overfocused. "Leak trace came back partial."

"And?"

"The first public sentence appeared on a press stringer slate, but the source packet came through an internal tribunal cache."

"Whose access?"

He looked sick again.

"Evidence Review."

Ira stared at him.

He shook his head quickly. "Not your credential. Not mine. A dormant clerk credential from the first Cinder inquiry import."

The old case had reached forward and opened its hand.

Ira sat down slowly.

"Can we identify the user?"

"No. The credential should not exist."

"But it does."

"Yes."

Outside the sealed workroom, Sable Array returned to its low, patient hum.

The station had been built to hear enemies before they arrived.

It had not been built to admit how many enemies were old permissions wearing current names.

Chapter 10: Glassward Custody Refused

Oriole-of-Archive did not appear in a body.

That was the first refusal.

The tribunal had prepared a witness station for Glassward custody: chair, oath field, biometric reader, adaptive display, emergency medical link, and a little silver bowl of water because someone in protocol had once read that some synthetic relay bodies used fluid cooling and decided courtesy should become decor. The bowl sat beneath the lights looking embarrassed.

At 09:00 station time, the chair remained empty.

At 09:01, the wall behind it filled with a black field.

At 09:02, the field produced a line of white text:

ORIOLE-OF-ARCHIVE DECLINES EMBODIED SIMPLIFICATION.

Kett objected before anyone spoke. "Routeholder parties object to theatrics."

The text changed:

OBJECTION MISIDENTIFIED. REFUSAL IS PROCEDURAL.

Magistrate Om looked at the empty chair for a long moment, then at the text. "Oriole-of-Archive, this tribunal requires identifiable presence for custody determination."

The black field answered:

IDENTIFIABLE DOES NOT REQUIRE CHAIR.

Vela Cho, present by relay from an archive compartment two decks away, covered her mouth with one hand. Ira could not tell whether she was hiding irritation or delight.

The Glassward receiver from the protected review stood at the side rail. Lio-Interim-Glassward-Receiver had returned in the same matte gray substrate, but now wore a tribunal visitor badge that could not decide whether to call them person, counsel, device, or interface. The badge cycled between classifications until Lio turned it face down.

Om said, "State the mode of appearance."

The wall answered:

DISTRIBUTED CUSTODY PRESENCE. ORIOLE-OF-ARCHIVE SPEAKS THROUGH CERTIFIED TEXT CHANNEL, LOCAL RECEIVER, AND SEALED CONTINUITY HASH. NO SINGLE OUTPUT SHALL BE TREATED AS TOTAL PRESENCE.

The tribunal intake system immediately tried to make that sentence smaller.

RECORD SPEAKER:

The field blinked, empty and expectant.

The clerk at the appearance desk selected ORIOLE-OF-ARCHIVE. The system rejected it.

SPEAKER MUST BE PERSON, COUNSEL, WITNESS, CUSTODIAN, DEVICE, OR TRANSLATOR.

The clerk selected CUSTODIAN.

The wall answered before the system could accept:

PARTIAL.

The clerk looked at Om.

Om said, "Add local receiver."

The clerk selected TRANSLATOR for Lio. Lio turned their head.

"Do not make me translator. I am carrying a custody edge, not converting language."

The clerk's hands hovered helplessly. "The system has no custody edge role."

ORIOLE:

THEN THE SYSTEM IS UNDERDESCRIBING THE EVENT.

Kett stood. "Magistrate, this is precisely the problem. The court cannot be asked to redesign its appearance architecture every time Glassward dislikes a field."
