Lio said, "Your fields are not architecture. They are doors with expensive labels."

Om cut across the answer. "Enough. Appearance record will list Oriole-of-Archive as distributed custodian, Lio-Interim-Glassward-Receiver as local custody edge, certified text channel as statement surface, and sealed continuity hash as presence verification. No one category controls."

The system flashed amber.

NONSTANDARD APPEARANCE MAY LIMIT ENFORCEMENT.

Ira read the warning twice. Enforcement was the court's private gravity. Everything eventually fell toward it.

Marr rose. "Military parties need to know who can be sanctioned if the presence lies."

ORIOLE:

ORIOLE CAN BE SANCTIONED.

Marr asked, "How?"

The wall paused.

Lio answered aloud, and for the first time their voice carried something like distaste. "Access limitation. Custody privilege suspension. Archive quarantine. Adverse inference. Future standing denial. Removal from cooperative preservation channels."

"Not imprisonment," Kett said.

"Not for you either when your institution lies," Lio replied.

Om did not let that bloom. "The tribunal will not pretend all sanctions have the same body. It will identify enforceable consequences appropriate to mode of appearance."

Bayo whispered, "That sentence is going to ruin three forms."

Ira said, "Only three?"

"I was being hopeful."

The clerk entered the nonstandard appearance. The record accepted it reluctantly, creating a new temporary code:

APPEARANCE MODE: DISTRIBUTED / ENFORCEMENT RESERVED.

That was how the hearing began: not with testimony, but with the court admitting its chairs were too few.

Bayo, at Evidence Review, whispered, "I hate that I understand why that is properly formatted."

Ira did not look away from the wall. "You're growing."

"Please don't say that during custody review."

Om entered the appearance mode provisionally. Kett objected again. Marr reserved position. Survivor counsel accepted with conditions. The press deck, restored after riot restrictions, filed six requests to display Oriole's text feed live. Om denied all six.

"This hearing concerns custody, not spectacle," he said.

On the wall:

APPRECIATED.

Then:

NOT TRUSTED.

Om's mouth twitched once. "Likewise."

The custody issue had been framed by routeholder motion and military joinder: the tribunal should compel Glassward to produce a simple evidentiary copy of the continuity derivative associated with Maintenance Relay M-14. The motion called Sera Venn-3 a record object, an unstable personality fork, and a potential witness-material bundle. It avoided calling Sera a person while insisting the court could handle personhood later.

Glassward had responded with seventeen pages of refusal and one sentence in large type:

YOU CANNOT CROSS-EXAMINE A WOUND BY MAKING IT FLAT.

Now the wound's custodian had arrived as text, receiver, and hash.

Kett began the argument.

"Magistrate, the tribunal's core function is fact-finding. We are confronted with a disputed audio record, an alleged continuity derivative, and handling restrictions that have already contributed to public disorder. Routeholder parties do not ask this tribunal to decide Sera Venn-3's personhood today. We ask only that Glassward produce a stable, reviewable copy of all memory material derived from the Cinder source child, so parties may evaluate authenticity, alteration, and relevance."

The wall remained black.

Kett continued, warming into reasonableness. "No court can function if evidence custodians may withhold material by claiming that ordinary review harms the material. If Sera is evidence, produce the evidence. If Sera is a witness, produce the witness. If Sera is something else, define it in terms this tribunal can enforce."

ORIOLE-OF-ARCHIVE:

REFUSED.

Kett turned to Om. "That cannot be sufficient."

Lio spoke aloud for the first time. Their voice was calm and unadorned. "It is not meant to satisfy you. It is meant to stop you."

Om said, "Oriole will give reasons."

The wall paused.

Then text began appearing in measured lines.

SERA VENN-3 IS NOT A SIMPLE COPY.

SERA VENN-3 IS A DAMAGED CONTINUITY CHORUS DERIVED FROM EMERGENCY MEMORY SALVAGE, CHILD-SOURCE AUDIO, SCHOOL NODE PRESERVATION, POSTWAR CUSTODY INTERVENTION, AND THREE SUBSEQUENT STABILIZATION ACTS.

ANY EXPORT THAT MERGES THESE LINES FOR REVIEW CHANGES THE WITNESS.

ANY EXPORT THAT SEPARATES THESE LINES FOR REVIEW CHANGES THE WITNESS.

ANY EXPORT THAT ALLOWS ADVERSARIAL PARTIES TO QUERY RAW TRAUMA LAYERS WITHOUT STANDING REVIEW HARMS THE WITNESS.

THEREFORE SIMPLE COPY IS REFUSED.

The room held the words.

Marr rose. "Military parties do not seek raw trauma layers. We seek chain integrity."

Oriole answered:

CHAIN INTEGRITY HAS HISTORICALLY BEEN USED TO DENY BROKEN CHAINS EXIST.

Marr's face tightened. "That is a political statement."

Vela said, "It is also an archive statement."

Om turned to Ira. "Evidence Review, define the practical problem."

Ira stood slowly. She had spent the night reading Glassward continuity law until her eyes burned and every answer became plural.

"The practical problem is that Sera Venn-3 may be the only surviving continuity relation to the maintenance child, but the path that preserved Sera may have altered, fragmented, or layered the original memory. Compelling a simple copy might produce something reviewable but false to the current witness. Refusing all production prevents authentication. Both failures benefit parties who prefer the record unusable."

Kett said, "Evidence Review again takes advocacy position."

Ira did not look at him. "Evidence Review is tired of pretending usability is neutral."

Om let that pass, which meant he would make her pay for it later in precision.

Lio said, "Glassward can offer staged contact."

The wall displayed:

PROPOSED:

1. HASHED EXISTENCE CONFIRMATION.

2. CUSTODY CHAIN MAP WITH HARMFUL LAYERS SEALED.

3. TECHNICAL AUTHENTICITY REVIEW OF NON-TRAUMA TIMING MARKS.

4. STANDING HEARING FOR SERA VENN-3 AS DAMAGED WITNESS CHORUS.

5. ONLY AFTER STANDING, NEGOTIATED TESTIMONY CONDITIONS.

Kett rose before the list finished. "Unacceptable. That lets Glassward define the witness before parties can examine the evidence."

Lio turned toward him. "You are asking to examine the evidence before deciding whether the evidence is a person."

"I am asking to avoid circularity."

"You are asking to cut the circle through the body least able to object."

Survivor counsel murmured approval.

Om silenced it with a glance.

Marr approached more carefully. "If the tribunal accepts staged contact, what prevents a custodian from hiding alteration inside sealed harmful layers?"

Vela answered before Glassward could. "Independent hash comparison against the salt-sealed record, maintenance relay waveform, school node preservation marks, and non-cognitive metadata. You can authenticate edges before opening the wound."

"Edges can be forged."

"So can clean copies."

Ira saw Om watching Vela with the particular attention of a judge measuring whether expertise had become argument. Vela knew it too and sat back.

Om asked Oriole, "Can Sera Venn-3 express preference?"

The black field did not answer immediately.

When it did, the text came slower.

SERA EXPRESSES PREFERENCE INCONSISTENTLY.

SERA-LEGAL WANTS STANDING.

SERA-CHILD HIDES FROM COURT-SHAPES.

SERA-ARCHIVE WANTS THE SEQUENCE KNOWN.

SERA-THIRD DOES NOT ACCEPT THESE DIVISIONS.

NO PART IS SUFFICIENT ALONE.

NO PART MAY BE ERASED FOR CONSISTENCY.

The gallery was closed for custody hearing, but Ira imagined the public trying to understand that. She imagined Kett trying to exploit it. She imagined herself at twelve years old in Cinderwake Minor hearing that a person could be made of parts that disagreed and wondering whether that made them less person or more honest.

Om said, "The tribunal cannot take testimony from a contradiction."

ORIOLE:

IT DOES OFTEN.

This time Vela did smile, briefly and helplessly.

Om ignored her. "Legal testimony requires responsibility for statements."

Lio answered, "Then create responsibility appropriate to damaged continuity."

"Propose."

"Statement clusters. Each cluster marked by continuity layer, confidence, stress cost, and objection from other layers if present. No forced harmonization. Cross-examination through approved question forms. No direct repetition of source trauma for contradiction hunting. Right to halt if stability drops."

Kett shook his head. "That is not testimony. That is curated narrative."

Ira said, "So is every direct examination."

He turned on her. "Associate Senn, are you now counsel for Glassward?"

"No. I am someone who has watched counsel pretend their question order is reality."

Om said, "Enough."

He meant her more than Kett. Fair.

The magistrate called a ten-minute pause without ending the sealed session. Parties remained in place. No one left. The empty chair sat under the lights. The silver bowl of water reflected the black text field.

Ira expected Om to retreat into private consultation. Instead he leaned back and looked at the ceiling, as if asking Sable's old listening bones what they had heard from earlier wars.

Finally he said, "When I was clerk to the first inquiry, a maintenance technician tried to submit a damaged recorder."

The room changed around him.

Marr went still.

Kett's eyes sharpened.

Om continued, "The panel required a clean export. The technician said the device would not survive standard extraction. The panel ordered extraction anyway. The file opened. It contained eleven seconds of static and a timestamp. The technician said the record had been killed. The panel ruled the static unauthenticated."

No one spoke.

Ira felt the old case, the dormant credential, the leak, the child audio, all sliding toward one another.

Om looked at Oriole's black field. "Was that recorder part of this chain?"

The answer took eleven seconds.

YES.

Marr whispered something Ira could not hear.

Kett sat down as if standing had become unsafe.

Om's artificial hand closed against the bench. "Why was this not in your filing?"

ORIOLE:

BECAUSE YOUR COURT ASKED FOR SERA, NOT FOR THE FIRST DEATH OF THE RECORD.

That sentence struck harder than accusation.

Om accepted it visibly.

"Then the court asks now," he said.

The wall displayed a new line:

REQUEST RECEIVED.

Vela leaned forward. "Magistrate, that may open archival material beyond today's motion."

"Yes."

"It may implicate the first inquiry panel."

"Yes."

"It may implicate you."

"It already does."

Ira wrote the exchange exactly, hands steady for once.

Om ruled before anyone could reshape the moment.

"The motion to compel a simple copy is denied. Glassward will produce staged contact under tribunal supervision. Hashed existence confirmation and non-trauma metadata will be produced first. Custody chain map will include the damaged recorder extraction and the first inquiry panel's order. Harmful layers remain sealed pending standing hearing. The tribunal will schedule a damaged witness chorus standing review for Sera Venn-3."

Kett stood. "Routeholder parties reserve all objections and request immediate appeal certification."

"Denied for now."

"On what basis?"

"You cannot appeal your way into a witness's wound before the court decides whether it is a wound."

Kett looked as if he wanted to call that poetry and feared it might become law.

Marr rose slowly. "Military parties request access to the first inquiry extraction order."

Om said, "So do I."

ORIOLE:

ACCESS WILL BE STAGED. SOME OF YOU HAVE ALREADY PROVEN DANGEROUS TO RECORDS.

"Name who," Kett said.

ORIOLE:

NO.

"Convenient."

ORIOLE:

PROTECTIVE.

The session ended with no one satisfied, which was becoming the tribunal's most reliable measure of progress.

As parties gathered their materials, Lio approached Ira's table.

"Sera noticed your conflict answer," they said.

Ira stopped packing. "Sera can see sealed forms?"

"Sera can see forms about Sera when they are used to shape access."

"That was not explained."

"No."

"Is Sera offended?"

Lio considered the word. "Sera-legal approves. Sera-child does not know you. Sera-archive marked the phrase remain accountable. Sera-third objected to the form rejecting it."

Ira did not know what to do with that.

"Tell Sera..." She stopped. Every sentence seemed to reach too far.

Lio waited.

"Tell Sera I will not ask for a simple copy."

"That is already known."

"Then why tell me this?"

The receiver's black eyes reflected the tribunal lights. "Because knowing you are seen may alter how you handle power."

They left before Ira could answer.

On the wall, Oriole's black field vanished.

The empty chair remained.

For the first time, Ira understood it not as absence but as a space the court had failed to deserve.

Chapter 11: Ira Learns the Beneficiary Chain

Insurance ledgers had no shame.

That was their usefulness.

People lied in language first. Institutions lied in policy. Military witnesses lied by omission, routeholders by clean definitions, survivors by memory's necessary repairs, courts by calling delay neutrality. Ledgers lied too, but usually in more expensive ways. They carried numbers forward because numbers were how power remembered what it refused to mourn.

Ira spent the next station night inside Sable Array's restricted finance archive with Bayo, Vela on a low-priority relay, and three pots of bitter stimulant broth that tasted like heated wire. The archive room had once held targeting computers. Now it held compensation tables, convoy risk pools, routeholder continuity bonds, salvage offsets, casualty settlements, and postwar restructuring instruments. The walls were colder than the rest of the station. Money liked preservation conditions.

Bayo pulled the first ledger onto the central display.

"Cinder Gate Transit Loss Pool," he said. "Filed by Halcyon Mutual, underwritten by three routeholder houses, military emergency rider attached."

"Show revision history."

He did.

Rows unfolded by time. Before 17:42, the convoy loss estimate treated Cinder evacuation as a mixed civilian-military event with catastrophic liability if gate command failed to preserve queue order. After 17:42, the model changed. Civilian attachment risk increased. Unbonded dependents dropped out of protected transfer. Archive capsules gained priority weighting. Military repair capacity became a survival multiplier. Routeholder hulls carrying modular life-support received "continuity preservation credit."

Vela leaned closer through relay grain. "There. Freeze row."

Bayo froze it.

17:42:38 - TRANSIT PREMIUM ADJUSTED.

Ira had seen the line in Clock B. Here it had children: subsidiary calculations, beneficiary allocations, reserve releases, premium relief triggers.

"Translate," she said.

Bayo frowned. "It reduced immediate loss exposure for ships inside the narrowed corridor."

"More plainly."

Vela answered, "Once the model treated people outside the corridor as no longer protected transfer obligations, the underwriters owed less for leaving them."

The room seemed to get colder.

Ira scrolled down. "Who benefited?"

The display produced institution names, some familiar, some buried under merger histories.

House Vey-Kalt Logistics.

Sable Military Emergency Command Residual Fund.

Halcyon Mutual Transit.

Compact Archive Continuity Office.

Routeholder House Mar.

Ash Provisional Survival Board.

Three station councils that later received discounted infrastructure.

Four convoy guilds.

One Glassward preservation contractor.

"That is too many," Bayo said softly.

"Too many for what?"

"For a villain."

Ira kept reading. "Good."

He looked at her.

"A villain would be easier to bury."

They built the chain line by line. Not guilt yet. Benefit. That distinction mattered, and Ira hated that it mattered because it felt like kindness to the powerful. But law that could not distinguish benefit from command would be destroyed on appeal before it reached repair. She could already hear Kett: receiving discounted infrastructure after the fact does not prove participation in wartime selection. True. Insufficient. Dangerous if ignored.

So they mapped.

The first map was useless.

It looked dramatic and therefore suspicious: names radiating from Cinder Gate in red and white, institutions connected by bright lines, every benefit touching every death until the whole display resembled the kind of conspiracy chart that made serious courts stop listening. Bayo stared at it for three seconds and closed the view himself.

"No," he said.

Ira looked at him.

He flushed but held his ground. "That makes everything look equally caused by everything else."

Vela's relay gave a soft click of approval. "Good. Never trust a map that flatters your anger."

Bayo opened a new schema.

This one had layers.

LAYER ONE: WINDOW ACTIONS.

LAYER TWO: MODEL INPUTS.

LAYER THREE: LEGAL RECLASSIFICATIONS.

LAYER FOUR: IMMEDIATE MATERIAL BENEFITS.

LAYER FIVE: POSTWAR STRUCTURAL ADVANTAGES.

LAYER SIX: REPARATIVE OBLIGATION QUESTIONS.

The display became less satisfying and more dangerous. Lines had direction now. Some stopped early. Some split. Some looped back through settlement law years later. House Mar's route privilege did not point directly to a child in a duct; it pointed to survival credit, corridor inclusion, postwar capacity certification, and then to a route license renewal that cited continuity service during Cinder. Halcyon Mutual's reduced payout did not prove command influence; it proved that a category change had financial consequences within thirty-four seconds. The Ash Provisional Survival Board did not order the abandonment; it received membranes, power couplers, and legitimacy from the salvaged aftermath, then wrote repair policy that treated salvage commons as regrettable necessity.

"This is slower," Bayo said.

"Slower is admissible," Ira said.

Vela added, "And harder to mourn. That will matter."

They created confidence tags for every line.

DOCUMENTED TRANSFER.

INFERRED BENEFIT.

TEMPORAL PROXIMITY ONLY.

LEGAL DEPENDENCY.

MATERIAL DEPENDENCY.

CONTESTED CAUSALITY.

Bayo insisted on the last tag even when Ira wanted to avoid it. He was right. Contested causality was not weakness if marked honestly. It was where the case told future advocates where not to overstep.

At Vela's suggestion, they added a separate field:

REPAIR WITHOUT BLAME POSSIBLE.

Ira disliked it immediately.

"That sounds like absolution."

"It is not," Vela said. "Some benefits will produce obligations even where culpability cannot be proven. If you force every repair through blame, every party will defend against repair as if it were conviction."

Bayo looked between them. "So a station can owe because it survived, not because it killed."

"An institution can," Ira said. "A person needs more care."

"That sentence will be attacked."

"Most useful ones are."

They kept the field.

Who received reduced liability when unbonded dependents were removed?

Who received corridor stability credit when military repair capacity was prioritized?

Who received postwar route rights because their ships survived?

Who received salvage offsets for material taken from compartments later declared commons?

Who received archive legitimacy because capsules were loaded before bodies?

Who received political authority because they had records the dead could not contest?

At 03:17 station time, Bayo stopped typing and put both hands over his face.

Ira let him sit for ten seconds.

Then she said, "Break?"

"No." His voice was muffled. "If I stop, I will understand."

"You already understand."

"I understand it badly."

"That is usually first."

He lowered his hands. His eyes were red, whether from fatigue or the archive air. "My grandmother's station got a membrane shipment after Cinder. Family story says a routeholder captain took pity."

Vela said nothing.

Ira asked, "Which station?"

"Pellion Drift."

She searched the beneficiary list.

Pellion Drift appeared under emergency infrastructure stabilization, credited with heat membrane allocation funded by Cinder salvage offsets and convoy continuity surplus.

Bayo read the line and went very still.

"My mother was born because of those membranes," he said.

The archive room did not know how to hold that sentence. It gave them colder air.

Ira said, "That does not make your mother a crime."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"No."

He laughed once, badly.

Vela's relay crackled. "Beneficiary chain is not blood guilt. If you let it become blood guilt, Kett wins by making the doctrine monstrous."

Bayo looked at her. "What is it then?"

"Obligation."

Ira nodded. "Institutions can inherit obligations from benefits. People inherit histories. Those are not the same, but they touch."

Bayo returned to the ledger with shaking hands.

They marked Pellion Drift as downstream beneficiary, not culpable actor. The distinction felt thin. It was also the only way the map could survive contact with people.

At 04:02, Vela found the hidden column.

She did it by accident or instinct. Ira had learned not to ask which. An inactive display field sat to the right of the main allocation table, blank under normal view. Vela asked Bayo to expose deprecated columns. The archive resisted. Bayo used Evidence Review authority. The archive requested senior approval. Ira attached Om's standing order. The archive complained in three formats, then obeyed.

Column title:

SURVIVAL LEGITIMACY INDEX.

"That is obscene," Bayo said.

"It is also not an insurance term," Vela said.

Ira opened the metadata.

Created by Quiet Directorate advisory cell.

The name entered the room like a pressure drop.

The Quiet Directorate was officially dissolved, unofficially everywhere old emergency systems still knew how to make people disappear in the name of continuity. It had appeared in previous books as method more than institution: preemptive secrecy, survival math, archives shaped like weapons. Ira had hoped Cinder would have enough villains without them.

Hope was not evidence.

The Survival Legitimacy Index ranked which institutions were expected to be necessary for postwar order if the evacuation failed. Gate command. Military repair carriers. Archive capsules. Routeholder logistics ships. Certain station councils. Certain legal offices. Certain witnesses. Civilian queue sections ranked low where they lacked recognized governance value.

Unbonded dependents did not rank.

They were not zero.

They were absent.

Ira sat back.

Vela whispered, "This is not triage."

No. Triage valued the injured by need and chance. This valued institutions by future usefulness and then let human categories fall around that hidden shape.

Bayo said, "Does this prove selection?"

"It proves pre-selection logic existed inside the model," Ira said. "We need to show operational use."

"The premium adjustment."

"Part."

"The category removal."

"Part."

"Witness deck clearance."

Ira looked at him.

Bayo opened a new search. Witness deck clearance, 17:41, Quiet Directorate advisory cell, Survival Legitimacy Index. The archive returned nothing. Then, after he included deprecated routing fields, it returned one transfer.

17:40:55 - QD ADVISORY CELL SENDS OBSERVER STREAMLINE RECOMMENDATION TO GATE COMMAND.

17:41:09 - GATE COMMAND ACKNOWLEDGES.

17:41:31 - WITNESS DECK CLEARANCE ORDER DRAFTED.

17:41:44 - ORDER EXECUTED.

"Hostile alarm was 17:43:29," Bayo said.

Ira nodded.

Vela looked tired enough to become transparent. "They cleared witnesses before the official crisis."

"Before the official crisis," Ira said, "and after the survival legitimacy index."

The chain was no longer only beneficiary. It was beginning to show sequence.

They copied the records under sealed hash. Bayo made three redundant evidence packages: tribunal archive, salt-sealed handling queue, and independent cold store. Vela insisted on a fourth, uncropped metadata snapshot held outside Sable's legacy credential system. Ira approved it before asking whether she had authority. Sometimes correct process followed correct action, limping and annoyed.

At 05:20, Om arrived.

He wore no robe. Just a gray station tunic and fatigue that made him look less like a magistrate than an old man who had found a door in his house leading to a room he remembered ordering sealed.

"Show me," he said.

Ira showed him.

He did not interrupt. Not at the premium adjustment, not at the unbonded dependent removal, not at the salvage offsets, not at the downstream infrastructure list. When the Survival Legitimacy Index appeared, his artificial hand made a soft mechanical click.

When the witness deck streamline recommendation appeared, he closed his eyes.

Vela said, "You knew the first inquiry record was incomplete."

Om opened his eyes. "Yes."

"Did you know this existed?"

"No."

Ira believed him. That did not help.

Om asked, "Was my panel on the index?"

Bayo searched.

The first inquiry panel appeared under post-event legitimacy preservation.

Magistrate Om's name did not. The panel office did. The clerk pool did.

Om read the line. "We were not judging the index. We were part of it."

No one corrected him.

He turned to Ira. "How do you intend to enter this?"

"Carefully."

"That is not an answer."

"No. It is a warning."

He almost smiled and failed. "Try again."

Ira stood because she needed her body to know this was not only archive work.

"Not as proof of all culpability at once. If we enter the beneficiary chain whole, Kett will call it conspiracy fog. Marr will call it wartime contingency planning. Survivor counsel may overreach because the map finally shows what people have felt for twenty years. We need a sequence motion."

"Define."

"First, reopen scope around pre-alarm decision logic. Second, enter the Survival Legitimacy Index as a model field requiring authentication. Third, tie witness deck clearance to the advisory recommendation. Fourth, reserve beneficiary chain for later obligation phase but preserve it now so no institution can restructure around disclosure."

Vela nodded. "And attach Shoals handling principle."

Om looked at her. "Which one?"

"Obligation follows operational benefit. Not guilt. Obligation."

"That principle is not Ash Belt law."

Ira said, "Not yet."

Om gave her a long look. "You are becoming ambitious."

"No. Angry with footnotes."

Bayo made a sound that might have been a laugh and turned it into a cough.

Om ordered the records sealed for emergency procedural motion at next session. He also ordered notice to all parties that new finance-archive material had been identified, without naming the Survival Legitimacy Index until protective review completed. That compromise pleased no one in the room, which recommended it.

Before leaving, he looked again at the first inquiry panel line.

"I need to enter additional disclosure."

Vela said, "Yes."

Ira said nothing.

Om dictated:

Magistrate Om discloses that the first Cinder inquiry panel, for which he served as clerk, appears in newly identified finance-archive material under post-event legitimacy preservation. Magistrate Om had no prior knowledge of this classification. The tribunal will accept motions regarding scope of conflict after preservation orders are complete.

Bayo entered it.

The record accepted it.

Om looked older after that. Not absolved. Not destroyed. Entered.

When he left, station morning cycle began, though no one in the archive had slept enough to deserve morning.

Bayo stared at the beneficiary map. "My grandmother's station is still on there."

"Yes."

"Do I have to disclose?"

Ira thought about forms that rejected accountability when it did not fit the field.

"Yes," she said. "And you should not be removed for being born after someone else's compromised survival. But you should not hide from the touch."

He nodded slowly.

Vela's relay dimmed. "That sentence needs work before court."

"Most true things do," Ira said.

She saved the beneficiary chain under a working title:

OPERATIONAL SURVIVAL BENEFIT MAP.

Then she deleted the title and replaced it with something less clean:

WHO COULD KEEP MOVING AFTER CINDER.

The archive accepted the file name.

For once, the system did not object to accuracy.

Chapter 12: The Ash Belt Hates a Clean Villain

By morning, Admiral Rhos Daven had become useful again.

Dead men often did.

His face appeared on public feeds before the tribunal opened: formal portrait, wartime command still, memorial engraving, leaked casualty citation, a youth photograph from before the Fracture Wars when his hair had been black and his uniform collar too large. Press packages loved a face. They could put it beside a headline and pretend history had edges.

DAVEN CLEARED WITNESS DECK BEFORE CINDER ALARM.

DEAD ADMIRAL'S ORDER UNDER REVIEW.

WAS CINDER GATE ONE COMMANDER'S CRIME?

The third version spread fastest because it offered relief disguised as outrage.

Ira saw the feeds in the concourse on her way to Evidence Review and nearly walked into a closed security gate. Station staff had reorganized public movement after the riot. No visible faction colors in mixed areas. Survivor claimant lines separated from convoy family access. Press drones restricted to ceiling tracks. Security Commander Hales had posted the new rules with the expression of a man who knew everyone would accuse him of bias and had decided bias was less fatal than another breach attempt.

At the checkpoint, a woman from Cinder Nine spat near a screen showing Daven's face.

"Good," she said. "Let his bones answer."

An older man beside her shook his head. "Bones are cheap."

The woman turned on him. "He ordered it."

"Maybe."

"Maybe?"

"If they give you one dead admiral today, they keep every living office tomorrow."

The woman looked ready to strike him, then tiredness caught her wrist before anger did.

Ira kept walking.

The Ash Belt hated clean villains the way a body hated a foreign object: by inflaming around them until they either came out or became part of the scar. People wanted Daven guilty. They also knew guilt could be used as a disposal chute. The dead were perfect containers for institutions that preferred mourning to repair.

In Evidence Review, Bayo had already opened a file labeled DAVEN CONSOLIDATION RISK.

Ira stared at it. "You made a risk file before stimulant?"

"I have become unpleasant."

"Useful."

"That too."

The file contained public feed captures, counsel statements, routeholder briefing language, military memorial releases, and survivor channel arguments. Bayo had highlighted repeated phrases:

FINAL AUTHORITY.

SOLE COMMAND DISCRETION.

WARTIME JUDGMENT.

TRAGIC RESPONSIBILITY.

COMMAND SACRIFICE.

"They are building the box," Ira said.

"Yes."

"Who?"

"Everyone, differently."

Bayo opened a second panel labeled not actors but uses.

USE ONE: MILITARY CONTAINMENT.

USE TWO: ROUTEHOLDER DISTANCE.

USE THREE: SURVIVOR RELIEF.

USE FOUR: PRESS SIMPLIFICATION.

USE FIVE: TRIBUNAL ADMINISTRATIVE COMFORT.

Ira looked at the fifth line. "That one is unkind."

"It is also true."

He expanded it. The clerk traffic from the morning had already begun sorting Daven-related materials into a clean package: authority record, death certificate, final command citation, witness deck order, memorial index, surviving command architecture. The package had a neatness that made it attractive. One folder. One dead man. One procedural path. It promised hearings with clear witnesses, familiar military-law standards, and fewer living institutions filing emergency appeals before breakfast.

"It is efficient," Bayo said.

"So is an airlock."

"That metaphor is also unkind."

"Also true."

They marked each use with a handling risk.

MILITARY CONTAINMENT: turns Daven into honorable error and protects advisory uptake.

ROUTEHOLDER DISTANCE: turns models into passive inputs and protects financial benefit.

SURVIVOR RELIEF: turns grief toward a reachable name and risks premature closure.

PRESS SIMPLIFICATION: turns structure into portrait.

TRIBUNAL ADMINISTRATIVE COMFORT: turns complexity into manageable docket.

Bayo added a final column:

WHAT REMAINS TRUE.

That mattered. Daven was not an invented diversion. He had cleared the witness deck. He had held command authority. He had died at his post. Any resistance to clean villainy that minimized those truths would look like institutional defense, and worse, it would be false.

Under Daven, Ira typed:

TRUE INDIVIDUAL CULPABILITY MAY EXIST. DO NOT LET IT BECOME A WALL.

The system underlined MAY EXIST as insufficiently definite.

"It wants certainty," Bayo said.

"Everyone does today."

She left the phrase.

He was right. Military counsel wanted Daven as honorable but mistaken final authority. Routeholders wanted Daven as independent commander who accepted or altered models. Some survivor channels wanted Daven as murderer because a murderer could be named before lunch. Press wanted Daven because a face filled space better than a beneficiary map. Even tribunal clerks liked Daven because dead witnesses did not require accommodation.

Vela joined the relay with her hair still damp from a station rinse. "If you let this happen, the case becomes a memorial prosecution."

"Good morning to you too," Ira said.

"It is not."

Bayo sent Vela the consolidation file.

She read fast. "Predictable."

"Can we stop it?"

"Not by saying Daven was innocent."

"He wasn't."

"Probably not. But innocence is not the question."

Vela enlarged Bayo's uses panel and added a sixth line without asking:

USE SIX: SELF-EXEMPTION BY BENEFICIARIES.

Bayo went still.

Pellion Drift sat invisibly in the room again.

Vela saw his face soften with pain and did not apologize. "If Daven becomes total container, downstream beneficiaries get to mourn cleanly. That includes people you like. That includes people who should not be blamed. That is why the distinction has to be built before the argument begins."

Bayo nodded once, too sharply.

Ira said, "We need language that does not accuse survival."

"Yes," Vela said. "And does not let survival become solvent."

Ira looked at the public capture of Daven's portrait. She imagined his hand clearing the witness deck. She imagined him at gate command while the corridor narrowed. She imagined him dead, which was easy, and powerful people alive, which was harder because they had learned to spread themselves across offices and models.

"We need someone from the Belt to say it," she said.

Bayo looked up. "Say what?"

"That a clean villain is theft."

Vela's relay flickered. "Careful. If Evidence Review stages public moral framing, Kett will gut you."

"Then not Evidence Review."

"Who?"

Ira knew before she liked the answer.

Tamsin Vale refused her first request.

She was in witness quarters repairing the cuff seal on her coat with a tool she had definitely not declared at security. Ira stood in the doorway with Bayo behind her pretending not to see the tool.

"No," Tamsin said.

"You don't know what I'm asking."

"You have that look advocates get when they want someone dirtier than them to say the sentence they can't."

Ira closed her mouth.

Bayo became very interested in the corridor wall.

Tamsin threaded pressure fiber through the cuff. "What sentence?"

"The tribunal is being pulled toward Admiral Daven as the clean container for Cinder."

"He ordered enough."

"Yes."

"He cleared the witness deck."

"Yes."

"He narrowed the gate."

"Maybe. Probably. We are still proving sequence."

Tamsin cut the fiber with her teeth. "Then why shouldn't the Belt get his name?"

"It should. But not only his."

Tamsin looked up. "And you want me to tell people not to be satisfied."

"Yes."

"People who have had nothing for twenty years?"

"Yes."

"That is a cruel ask."

"I know."

"No, you don't."

Ira accepted that because it was true enough.

Tamsin stood and tested the cuff seal. "I spent ten years letting people call me grave robber because it was easier than explaining which station lived because I cut into whose wall. You know what I wanted most?"

"What?"

"One person I could hate without remainder."

Ira said nothing.

"Daven will do for some of them," Tamsin said. "For a while."

"And after?"

"After, the offices sleep."

The sentence had no heat in it. That made it stronger.

Tamsin looked past Ira toward the public corridor. "I will not make a speech."

"I was asking for testimony."

"Same danger, better clothes."

"A statement in response to salvage context. Not about Daven. About how the Ash Belt learned to distrust single-name blame."

Tamsin laughed once. "You do know how to put a hook in a person."

"Will you?"

"No."

Ira started to speak.

Tamsin held up one scarred hand. "I will answer if asked in court. I won't volunteer. Make your law do its own reaching."

That was fair. Annoyingly fair.

Ira returned to Evidence Review and drafted a question sequence survivor counsel could use if they chose. She sent it to Elt with a warning:

This may hurt your own immediate narrative.

Elt replied:

The immediate narrative is already trying to eat the case. Send.

The tribunal opened with military counsel's motion to enter Daven's final command authority record. Marr handled it with the solemn efficiency of someone placing a wreath exactly where it could block a door.

"Military parties submit that Admiral Rhos Daven held final command discretion during the Cinder evacuation window. Any narrowing order, witness deck clearance, or transit sequence adjustment required his authorization. This does not end inquiry, but it establishes command locus."

Command locus. A clean phrase. A polished coffin.

Kett rose to support limited admission. Of course he did.

"Routeholder parties agree that command locus is necessary to prevent speculative diffusion of responsibility across advisory, insurance, or support functions. Models advise. Command decides."

Survivor counsel objected.

Elt's voice was calm. Too calm. "Survivor parties do not oppose admission of Daven's authority record. We oppose its use as a disposal container."

Om asked, "Disposal container?"

Elt looked toward Tamsin in the witness tier. "We request brief recall of Captain Vale on Ash Belt salvage practice and single-name liability."

Kett objected. Marr objected. Om looked at Ira for half a second, which meant he knew exactly where the question had been born and would permit it only if it stood without her.

Elt made it stand.

"The witness's prior testimony established post-Cinder salvage practice, retroactive authorization, and the moral instability of naming actors after institutional abandonment. This question concerns how responsibility is preserved or lost in Ash Belt recovery systems."

Om allowed limited recall.

Tamsin returned to the witness rail, saw the oath gloves, and said, "Still no."

The oath officer sighed quietly.

Elt began. "Captain Vale, in salvage practice, when a station fails, do you assign responsibility to one broken part?"

Kett stood. "Objection. Metaphor."

"Overruled," Om said. "But keep it useful."

Tamsin answered. "No. One broken part may kill the system. But you trace why it broke, who ignored wear, who changed maintenance schedule, who bought cheap sealant, who silenced alarms, who benefited from delaying repair."

Elt nodded. "Why?"

"Because if you replace one part and leave the practice, the station kills again."

The gallery, reopened under tighter controls, listened with an attention deeper than approval.

Elt asked, "Did Ash Belt salvage courts ever name a single captain, engineer, or commander responsible for a failed station?"

"Yes."

"Was that always wrong?"

"No."

"What was the danger?"

Tamsin's eyes moved toward Daven's portrait on the evidence wall. "A name can be true and still be used to hide the machine."

There. The sentence reached the room without Evidence Review fingerprints.

Elt let it breathe.

Then he asked, "What happens when the machine is hidden?"

"People keep living inside it."

Marr rose for cross, careful and cold. "Captain Vale, are you suggesting Admiral Daven's final command authority is irrelevant?"

"No."

"Are you suggesting command decisions do not matter?"

"No."

"Then what are you suggesting?"

"That dead command is the easiest kind to punish and the easiest kind to use."

Marr's expression hardened. "Admiral Daven died at his post."

"So did a lot of people who didn't have posts."

The gallery murmured.

Om warned them with one glance.

Marr said, "You speak as if sacrifice has no meaning."

Tamsin leaned slightly toward the rail. "I speak as if sacrifice can be real and still not pay someone else's debt."

Marr had no fast answer.

Kett took cross next. He should not have. Ira knew it before he opened his mouth. His skill was precision, but Tamsin made precision look like a tool that had never touched heat.

"Captain Vale, you have profited from complex responsibility. The more institutions involved, the more retroactive authorizations shield your actions."

"Yes."

Kett paused. He had not expected agreement.

Tamsin continued, "Complexity saved me from being the only thief in the room. It did not make me clean."

"So your testimony today protects you."

"Some."

"And shifts blame toward institutions."

"Some."

"Including institutions that kept Ash Belt stations alive."

"Yes."

Kett smiled faintly. "Then perhaps the Belt does not hate clean villains. Perhaps it hates that complex responsibility might reach its own survival."

The chamber changed.

This was the dangerous truth beneath his maneuver. Bayo's grandmother. Pellion Drift membranes. Salvage offsets. Stations alive because someone else was not. The Ash Belt did not stand outside the beneficiary chain. It lived inside it.

Tamsin looked at him for a long time.

"Yes," she said.

Kett's smile thinned.

"We hate that most," she said. "That is why we know your clean villain is a lie. If Daven alone did it, we get to be innocent. If the chain did it, we have to find out who breathed because someone else stopped. You think that protects your clients because it hurts us too. It doesn't. It makes the record worth having."

Ira heard Bayo inhale behind her.

Kett sat down without asking another question.

Om entered Daven's command authority record, but with a limiting instruction:

ADMIRAL RHOS DAVEN'S FINAL COMMAND AUTHORITY IS RELEVANT TO ORDER TRANSMISSION, WITNESS DECK CLEARANCE, AND GATE NARROWING. IT SHALL NOT BE USED TO PRECLUDE INQUIRY INTO ADVISORY MODELS, BENEFICIARY STRUCTURES, CATEGORY DESIGN, POSTWAR SUPPRESSION, OR OPERATIONAL ADVANTAGE.

The formal language did not carry Tamsin's force, but it kept the door open.

That was law on its better days: not justice, not truth, but a door prevented from closing too soon.

After the session, Elt found Ira outside Evidence Review.

"You wrote those questions," he said.

"You asked them."

"That is not denial."

"No."

He leaned against the wall, suddenly looking as tired as everyone else. "I hated them."

"I know."

"My clients hated them more."

"I know."

"They were necessary."

"I know."

He laughed without humor. "You are becoming difficult."

"I think that was in the appointment packet."

"Obscured?"

"Heavily."

Elt pushed away from the wall. "Jalen Esh wants to file a supplemental statement. She says if the court gives her Daven alone, she will throw him back."

Ira felt the sentence settle somewhere deep.

"Good," she said.

"She also says she still wants his name burned into every door that closed."

"Also good."

Elt studied her. "Can the law hold both?"

Ira thought of the empty Glassward chair, Tamsin's hands, Bayo's grandmother, Om's disclosure, Daven's dead face on public feeds.

"It had better learn."

That evening, she opened the beneficiary map again.

At the top, she added a note:

DO NOT LET TRUE INDIVIDUAL CULPABILITY BECOME INSTITUTIONAL HIDING PLACE.

The archive accepted the note.

Then, after a pause, it flagged the line as argumentative.

Ira smiled for the first time all day.

"Correct," she told the system, and left it in.

Chapter 13: Vela Crops Nothing

Vela Cho's reconstruction room looked like a crime scene that had learned patience.

She had taken over Archive Bay Six after the Glassward custody ruling, evicting two tribunal media clerks, four automated digest engines, and a wall of summary templates with the quiet violence of someone who had spent her career watching good records die of helpfulness. The bay's main display no longer showed clean timelines. It showed fragments suspended in dark space: gate pulses, duct audio waveform, salvage photographs, witness deck clearance orders, insurance model revisions, thermal decay curves, routeholder risk tables, pressure baffle logs, and blank fields marked not missing but not yet safely read.

Ira arrived with Bayo and found Vela standing barefoot on the cold floor.

"Shoes?" Bayo asked before he could stop himself.

Vela did not look away from the display. "Archive floor resonance is wrong with station soles."

Bayo looked at Ira.

Ira shook her head. "Do not ask the obvious follow-up."

"I was not going to."

"You were."

Vela pointed to a chair without turning. "Sit if you need comfort. Stand if you need shame."

Bayo sat.

Ira stood.

The reconstruction filled three walls. The official Cinder sequence ran through the center as a white line because white was the color of institutional confidence. Around it, Vela had placed damaged records in their own colors but refused to align them neatly. Some floated above the line. Some crossed it. Some stopped before touching any human time index. One section was only a black rectangle labeled CHILD AUDIO WITHHELD / PRESENCE ACKNOWLEDGED. Another was a gap labeled WITNESS DECK STATIC AFTER FORCED EXTRACTION.

No one had smoothed the gap.

It hurt to look at.

"This is unusable in court," Bayo said.

"Yes," Vela said.

"Then why build it?"

"Because court usability is how we got the wrong story."

Ira stepped closer to the witness deck segment. "You found the static?"

"Oriole released hash-confirmed edge material last night. Not content. Edges. Enough to prove extraction damage."

She expanded a record. Eleven seconds of static became a block of gray noise, framed by metadata from the first inquiry. The extraction order carried a panel identifier, device serial, technician protest note, and result classification:

UNAUTHENTICATED / LOW VALUE / NO ACTION.

Vela had not cropped the technician protest note even though half of it was corrupted and the remaining text repeated itself:

standard pull will kill buffer / please / please / standard pull will / please / not designed for

Bayo whispered, "That is awful."

"Yes," Vela said. "Do not improve it."

Ira looked at the protest note until the repeated please became a person in the room.

"Can we identify the technician?"

"Maybe. Not today. Today we prove the record died under procedure."

"Procedure ordered by the first inquiry panel."

"Yes."

"Where Om was clerk."

"Yes."

Bayo shifted in his chair. "He disclosed."

Vela turned then. Her eyes were red from too little sleep and too much screen light. "Disclosure is not resurrection."

No one answered.

She returned to the display. "The court will want a clean sequence: at 17:40 advisory model, at 17:41 witness deck cleared, at 17:42 category removal, at 17:43 alarm, at 17:44 official narrowing. That sequence is true enough to be dangerous."

Ira frowned. "Dangerous how?"

"It makes the hidden order look clearer than it was. It gives motive and causation a straight spine. Kett will attack the uncertain joints. Marr will call it hindsight. Survivor counsel may turn it into a charge the evidence cannot yet carry. I need the tribunal to see the crookedness without mistaking crooked for weak."

Bayo said, "That sounds impossible."

"It is archive work."

Vela opened a second layer. The white official line bent. Not dramatically. Just enough to show dependence. Insurance model revisions did not cause witness deck clearance in any proven direct line yet. The Quiet Directorate advisory did not directly command Daven. The child audio did not prove green ships were routeholder vessels. Salvage heat did not prove living bodies remained in G-12. But each record made the official story less able to stand alone.

"This," Vela said, "is not a replacement story. It is a collapse map."

Ira studied the term. "A map of where the official story fails under load."

"Exactly."

"Courts like replacement stories."

"Courts like many harmful things."

Bayo raised one finger. "Magistrate Om will ask for admissible structure."

"That is why you are here." Vela pointed at him. "You know clerk instincts. How will they try to clean it?"

Bayo looked alarmed. "I am not senior enough to represent clerk instincts."

"All clerks represent clerk instincts. It is how institutions reproduce."

He sighed, stood, and approached the display. "They will make an exhibit table. Record, source, timestamp, reliability, relevance. Anything without timestamp goes in appendix. Anything emotionally prejudicial gets summarized. Anything ambiguous becomes note. Anything with multiple possible meanings becomes pending."

Vela nodded. "And by the time they are done?"

"The official line stays central. The damaged records become footnotes."

"Good. Stop that."

"How?"

Ira answered before Bayo had to. "Make failure of fit the exhibit."

Vela looked at her.

Ira continued, thinking aloud. "Not a table of records supporting a new sequence. A table of official claims and what each damaged record prevents the tribunal from assuming. Claim: hostile alarm caused narrowing. Prevented assumption: no pre-alarm correction. Claim: witness deck absence means no witness. Prevented assumption: no record existed. Claim: unbonded dependents were administratively unmatchable. Prevented assumption: category removal followed crisis rather than preceded it."

Bayo was already writing.

Vela's face changed in the smallest way. Approval, but not softness.

"Good," she said. "Negative structure."

"That sounds like a weapon," Bayo said.

"It is a shield with edges."

They worked for five hours.

Archive Bay Six became a machine for preserving difficulty. Every time Ira wrote a clean sentence, Vela asked what it erased. Every time Bayo built a column, Ira asked what it centered. Every time the system suggested auto-alignment, Vela rejected it with increasing personal contempt.

At one point the digest engine attempted to title the exhibit:

ALTERNATIVE CINDER GATE SEQUENCE.

Vela deleted it so hard the console chimed a warning.

She typed:

OFFICIAL SEQUENCE ASSUMPTION FAILURE MAP.

The system flagged the phrase as nonstandard.

Vela accepted the warning.

The final exhibit had five fields:

OFFICIAL CLAIM.

ASSUMPTION REQUIRED.

DAMAGED RECORD THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION.

WHAT CANNOT YET BE CONCLUDED.

PRESERVATION REQUIRED.

The fourth field mattered most. Vela insisted. Without it, the exhibit would become accusation pretending to be caution. With it, the exhibit became slower and harder to weaponize.

"The fourth field will make everyone angry," Bayo said.

"Good," Vela answered.

"I mean procedurally angry."

"Also good."

He pointed at the empty column. "Survivor counsel will say we are sanding down the evidence. Routeholders will say the first three columns prejudice them and the fourth does not cure it. Military counsel will say uncertainty proves the official sequence remains the only usable one. The press will ignore the fourth field entirely."

"Then write it so ignoring it leaves a visible scar," Vela said.

Bayo looked at Ira. "Can columns have scars?"

"In this room, apparently."

Vela stepped between them and the display. "Listen. Every corrupt summary does two things. It says more than the record can bear, and it hides what the record refuses to say. We will not do either. If a damaged record prevents an assumption, we say that. If it does not prove motive, we say that. If it does not name a murderer, we say that. If it does not absolve the people who want absolution, we say that by preserving the question."

Ira wrote the rule at the top of the exhibit:

PREVENTED ASSUMPTION IS NOT A FINAL FINDING.

Vela read it. "Too clean."

Ira tried again:

THIS MAP IDENTIFIES WHAT THE OFFICIAL SEQUENCE MAY NOT SAFELY ASSUME. IT DOES NOT REPLACE UNPROVEN ANSWERS WITH PREFERRED ONES.

"Better," Vela said.

Bayo added a subline:

UNCERTAINTY SHALL NOT BE CONVERTED INTO ACQUITTAL, ACCUSATION, OR DELAY WITHOUT SEPARATE RULING.

Vela gave him a look.

He straightened. "Too much?"

"No. Annoyingly good."

He looked pleased for half a second before remembering where they were.

The digest engine objected to the subline:

MULTIPLE LEGAL FUNCTIONS DETECTED. RECOMMEND SEPARATION.

Vela said, "Denied."

The engine asked for a reason.

She typed:

SEPARATION IS THE FAILURE UNDER REVIEW.

This time the warning stayed amber instead of red.

Ira wondered whether software could be intimidated. Then she decided almost everything could, if Vela Cho was tired enough.

Ira entered the child audio row:

OFFICIAL CLAIM: Queue stable until hostile alarm disrupted evacuation.

ASSUMPTION REQUIRED: No pre-alarm local witness perceived corridor or boarding change.

DAMAGED RECORD THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION: Protected child-associated maintenance audio linked to Duct M-14, withheld from public summary.

WHAT CANNOT YET BE CONCLUDED: Identity of child; meaning of green ships phrase; motive for any boarding change; legal standing of Sera Venn-3.

PRESERVATION REQUIRED: Protected presence marker; claimant-status notices; Glassward staged contact; no public playback.

Vela read it twice.

"Acceptable."

From her, that felt like a medal and a warning.

Bayo entered the witness deck row:

OFFICIAL CLAIM: Primary telemetry corruption left no reliable internal witness record.

ASSUMPTION REQUIRED: No witness deck record survived in usable form.

DAMAGED RECORD THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION: Hash-confirmed static from forced extraction; technician protest note; Glassward custody edge material.

WHAT CANNOT YET BE CONCLUDED: Original content of damaged recorder; whether extraction destruction was negligent, reckless, or intentional; full identity of technician.

PRESERVATION REQUIRED: First inquiry extraction order; device chain; panel minutes; Oriole staged release.

Ira entered the beneficiary row:

OFFICIAL CLAIM: Emergency triage decisions were driven by immediate gate instability.

ASSUMPTION REQUIRED: Priority categories were generated only in response to active crisis conditions.

DAMAGED RECORD THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION: Survival Legitimacy Index; pre-alarm premium adjustment; unbonded dependent removal; witness deck streamline recommendation.

WHAT CANNOT YET BE CONCLUDED: Direct command adoption; criminal intent; full institutional knowledge; individual culpability.

PRESERVATION REQUIRED: Finance archive seals; deprecated column exports; Quiet Directorate advisory metadata; beneficiary chain map.

Vela stopped at that one.

"Change damaged record," she said.

"Why?"

"These are not damaged in the same way. Some are hidden, not damaged. Hidden records and damaged records do different work."

Ira revised the field title:

RECORD CONDITION THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION.

Vela nodded. "Better."

The exhibit became less elegant and more accurate.

Magistrate Om arrived during the sixth hour with Sienne Marr, Maro Elt, and Kett. Ira had not known he was bringing counsel. Vela had, apparently, because she did not react. Bayo reacted enough for both of them and knocked over an empty broth cup.

Kett looked around Archive Bay Six as if it smelled illegal.

"This is an advocacy exhibit," he said before reading it.

Vela replied, "This is an anti-summary exhibit."

"That is not a category."

"Many useful things are born before categories."

Om said, "Enough. Show us."

Ira presented the map because Vela refused to perform neutrality and Bayo looked ready to melt. She walked them through the structure, each official claim paired not with contradiction but with a stopped assumption. Marr listened with controlled suspicion. Elt listened like someone being asked not to use a blade he badly needed. Kett listened as if memorizing appeal language.

At the child audio row, Kett said, "This allows prejudice without content."

Ira answered, "It allows preservation without spectacle."

"It suggests hidden content is damaging to my clients."

"Your clients are welcome to support authentication conditions that reveal it safely."

"And if the content does not support the implication?"

"Then the row's fourth field already says what cannot be concluded."

Kett looked at the field and found no easy grip.

At the witness deck row, Marr spoke. "The technician protest note may reflect excessive caution. Not every warning from a technician proves panel error."

Vela answered, "Correct. That is why the row says cannot yet conclude negligent, reckless, or intentional destruction."

"But the exhibit's emotional force points there."

Vela's eyes sharpened. "The emotional force belongs to the technician who wrote please three times before procedure killed the buffer. I am not cropping it to comfort your evidentiary posture."

Marr took the blow and did not return one.

At the Survival Legitimacy Index row, Om was silent for a long time.

Finally he said, "This structure may be admitted as a court aid, not proof."

Kett objected. "Even as aid, it reframes the entire docket."

"The docket requires reframing," Om said.

"Then parties require time."

"They will have it."

Elt frowned. "Delay favors institutions."

Om looked at him. "Premature collapse also favors them. Choose which way you prefer to lose."

Elt's mouth closed.

Vela said, "The map should be visible to public record."

Kett laughed in disbelief. "Absolutely not."

Marr said, "Public release risks another riot."

"Public concealment risks another leak," Ira said.

Om turned toward her. "Recommendation?"

She had one, which worried her.

"Public release of structure without sensitive content. Show official claims, assumption warnings, cannot-yet-conclude fields, and preservation requirements. Keep record identifiers sealed where needed. Make the shape visible before rumor fills it."

Vela nodded. Elt looked uncertain but interested. Marr looked unhappy. Kett looked opposed, which was almost background noise now.

Om ruled for delayed public structural release after parties reviewed wording for safety, not argument.

"Safety will become argument," Kett said.

"Everything does," Om replied. "We will still distinguish."

After counsel left, Vela finally sat down on the floor.

Ira had never seen her sit that way, abruptly, like power had been cut.

"Are you all right?" Bayo asked.

"No."

He looked at Ira, alarmed by the honesty.

Vela leaned her head against the display wall beneath the static segment. "The first time I built an archive reconstruction, I cropped out a panic gap because the senior curator said it distracted from the sequence. The report was praised for clarity. Three years later, the gap turned out to contain a pressure alarm that would have changed liability."

Ira sat beside her.

Bayo stayed standing, then seemed to decide shame was no longer necessary and sat too.

Vela continued, "People think bad archives are made by villains. Some are. Most are made by tired people improving readability."

Ira thought of every form that had rejected her best answer.

"You don't crop now," she said.

"No."

"Ever?"

Vela's laugh was small and bitter. "I crop lunch from my calendar. Nothing else."

They sat under the dead recorder's static until the archive lights dimmed for station evening.

On the wall, the assumption failure map remained ugly, incomplete, difficult, and alive.

For once, no one tried to make it easier to read.

Chapter 14: Magistrate Om's Old Signature

The signature came from a printer that should have been dead.

It emerged at 02:12 station time from a sealed hardcopy vault on Sable's lower archive ring, where first inquiry materials had been stored under the theory that physical paper could not be corrupted by network politics. The theory had ignored fire, mold, vacuum, clerks, and the human gift for filing dangerous things under harmless labels. Still, the vault survived. So did the printer, a prewar thermal-scribe unit that woke once a month to test its feed and complain in mechanical clicks.

Bayo found the index reference first:

FIRST CINDER INQUIRY / EMERGENCY EVIDENCE RULE / PANEL CLERK COUNTERSIGNATURE REQUIRED / HARDCOPY AUTHORITY ONLY.

Then he stared at the line until Ira asked whether he was breathing.

"I think this is Om."

Vela's relay sharpened. "Open image."

"It says hardcopy only."

"Of course it does."

Ira checked authority. The preservation order covered first inquiry extraction materials, panel minutes, and device chain. It did not explicitly cover hardcopy evidence rules countersigned by clerks.

"We need Om," she said.

Vela looked at her as if she had suggested asking a fire for permission to inspect ash. "We need the record preserved before he can recuse, seal, collapse, or do something noble and procedurally useless."

"He is the magistrate."

"He is also named in the possible chain."

Bayo spoke carefully. "If we open without him, Kett will say Evidence Review exceeded authority."

"Kett will say that if you drink water near the file," Vela said. "The question is whether he will be right enough."

Ira looked at the authorization language again. Panel minutes. Device chain. Extraction order. Emergency evidence rule might be part of extraction order authority if the rule governed the forced extraction that killed the buffer. Might. Courts lived and died inside might.

She opened a channel to Om's night office.

No response.

She opened the emergency disclosure channel.

No response.

Bayo checked station location. "His credential is in Quiet Room Two."

Quiet rooms were not sleep rooms. They were places where tribunal staff went when old war material made them unsafe to themselves or others. No recorders. No messages except medical emergency. No legal work.

Ira closed her eyes.

Vela said nothing. That was how Ira knew she was waiting to see which value would win.

"We preserve, do not inspect," Ira said. "Bayo, request the hardcopy vault produce sealed duplicate into evidence bag under existing preservation order. We do not view content until Om or conflict substitute is present."

Vela exhaled through her nose. "Conservative."

"Defensible."

"Those are not always friends."

"Tonight they are roommates."

Bayo issued the request.

The vault refused twice. Once because the printer was not assigned to active tribunal network. Once because the hardcopy authority required a clerk class that no longer existed. Bayo found a compatibility bridge through postwar archive migration rules. The vault accepted with the reluctance of a machine whose dead masters had not anticipated living persistence.

Before it accepted, the vault produced a historical warning:

HARDCOPY AUTHORITY MATERIAL MAY CONTAIN ACTIVE OBLIGATIONS NOT REPRESENTED IN DIGITAL INDEX.

Bayo read it aloud, then looked at Ira. "What does that mean?"

"It means paper can still order people around."

Vela said, "It means old procedure sometimes survives as a weapon because no one digitized the handle."

The warning listed three possible active obligations: nondisclosure, chain continuity, and command deference. Each belonged to a legal world that had supposedly ended with the first inquiry. The migration notes had marked them obsolete, but the vault had not. Obsolete was a digital opinion. The paper disagreed.

Bayo opened the compatibility bridge more carefully. "If I request content, I may trigger command deference."

"Define."

"It notifies the highest surviving authority named in the hardcopy chain."

Ira understood the trap a beat before he said it.

"Om," Bayo said.

Vela's relay hissed with static as she shifted channels. "Do not request content."

"I wasn't going to."

"Good. Continue not doing it."

Ira leaned over the authorization fields. Preservation request. No content rendering. No authority notification beyond sealed custody movement. No chain interpretation. No status conversion. The form had not been built for abstinence; every button wanted to know, classify, extract, notify, or obey.

"There is no preserve-only option," Bayo said.

"Make one."

"That is not how buttons work."

"It is how records survive buttons."

He created a manual exception note and attached the existing preservation order, Om's prior disclosure, the conflict-risk flag, and Vela's archive warning. The vault rejected the first version because manual exceptions required a purpose category. The available categories were review, litigation, classification, destruction hold, and transfer.

"Destruction hold," Vela said.

Bayo hesitated. "That implies risk of destruction."

"Correct."

"By whom?"

"By every process currently trying to make us open it wrong."

Ira nodded. "Use destruction hold for handling process risk, not physical decay."

Bayo entered:

DESTRUCTION HOLD: RISK ARISING FROM CONTENT ACCESS TRIGGERING OBSOLETE COMMAND DEFERENCE OR CHAIN OBLIGATION BEFORE CONFLICT REVIEW.

The vault considered that for a long time.

Then it accepted.

Ira felt no triumph. Only the unpleasant awareness that they had nearly asked a dead rule for permission to wake itself.

At 02:37, a sealed courier tube arrived.

No drone carried it. Sable did not trust drones in lower archive after the riot. A human runner brought it: teenage, shaved head, station uniform too large at the shoulders. She looked at Ira, Bayo, Vela's relay, then at the tube.

"This thing made the printer scream," she said.

"Printers do that," Bayo said.

"Not like it knew."

She left before anyone could make that less unsettling.

The courier tube sat on the table.

Ira did not touch it.

Vela did not tell her to.

For nearly a minute, no one moved.

Then the door opened and Magistrate Om entered in a plain robe thrown over sleep clothes.

His face was gray.

"Open it," he said.

Ira stood. "Magistrate, you were in Quiet Room Two."

"I left."

"Should you have?"

"No."

"Then we need a medical or conflict officer present."

His eyes narrowed. "Do not protect me from my record."

"I am protecting the record from you."

Bayo made a very small sound.

Om looked at Ira for a long moment, then nodded once.

"Correct. Call conflict officer."

The conflict officer on duty was a woman named San Pellin, no relation to Nadir Pell despite the unfortunate convergence of syllables. She arrived with her hair flattened on one side and the expression of someone who had been woken to prevent a tribunal from eating itself.

"State issue," she said.

Ira did.

Pellin listened, read the preservation order, confirmed Om's presence, and ruled that the tube could be opened under her authority, with Om permitted to observe but not direct handling. Om accepted the limitation.

No one looked relieved.

Bayo opened the tube.

Inside was one sheet of dark archival paper and a custody slip. The paper had curled at the edges. Thermal-scribe text shimmered faintly where age had changed the compounds. At the bottom sat three signatures: panel chair, military evidence officer, clerk countersignature.

Ira knew Om's current signature from orders.

The old one was younger but unmistakable.

OMARREN VALE OM, CLERK AUTHORITY SECOND CLASS.

Vela said, "Read the rule."

Ira did.

EMERGENCY EVIDENCE RULE 17-C:

During active wartime inquiry, damaged recording devices, unstable memory buffers, partial sensor packages, unofficial maintenance logs, nonstandard civilian submissions, and related materials may be subjected to standard extraction without preserving native operating conditions where panel determines delay creates risk to military, evacuation, or continuity interests.

Materials failing standard extraction shall not be presumed destroyed by process absent independent proof.

Panel may classify failed extraction output as unauthenticated low-value material.

Clerk certification confirms procedural sufficiency.

The room became airless.

Vela's voice was very quiet. "That rule killed the recorder."

Om stared at the signature. "Yes."

Conflict Officer Pellin asked, "Magistrate, did you draft this rule?"

"No."

"Did you review it?"

"Yes."

"Did you understand it would allow standard extraction over technician objection?"

He did not answer quickly.

Ira watched his face and saw not performance but search. Memory was not a drawer. It was a salvage field. He was walking through it under everyone's light.

"I understood that it allowed panels to proceed without delay," he said. "I understood technician objections could be overruled. I did not understand enough about native operating conditions to know what standard extraction would destroy."

Vela's expression hardened. "Did the technician tell the panel?"

"Yes."

"Then you understood and chose not to know the importance."

Om closed his eyes.

Conflict Officer Pellin said, "Archive Specialist Cho, this is not cross-examination."

"No. It is a sentence that should exist somewhere."

Pellin looked at Ira. "Enter as archive specialist observation, not finding."

Ira did.

Om opened his eyes. "I certified procedural sufficiency."

"Why?" Ira asked.

Pellin turned slightly. "Associate Senn--"

Om lifted a hand. "No. I can answer if not compelled."

Pellin allowed it with a nod.

Om looked at the paper. "Because I believed procedure was how we kept vengeance from replacing truth. The panel was under pressure. Military command wanted speed. Survivors wanted the recorder admitted. Technicians wanted preservation delay. The chair wanted a rule. I checked authority, form, emergency scope, signature order. It was sufficient."

"And wrong," Vela said.

"And wrong," Om said.

The words did not balance. They did not repair. They only entered.

Bayo asked, barely above whisper, "Does this mean you recuse?"

Pellin answered before Om could. "That depends on scope."

Om looked at her. "No."

"Magistrate--"

"If I recuse before preservation orders are complete, every party with an interest in collapse will race to freeze this tribunal around my conflict. If I do not recuse, every party will claim I am protecting myself. Both arguments have merit."

Pellin said, "That is not a decision."

"No. It is the room."

Ira looked at the rule again. Materials failing standard extraction shall not be presumed destroyed by process absent independent proof. The sentence was a trapdoor. It made destruction impossible to prove using the thing destroyed. It gave procedure the right to kill records and then call death unauthenticated.

"We have independent proof now," she said.

Everyone turned.

"The technician protest note. Oriole's hash-confirmed edge material. Device chain. Standard extraction order. This rule. The static output. Maybe not enough to prove intent, but enough to prove process destruction as a live issue."

Vela nodded slowly.

Om said, "Then enter it that way."

Pellin intervened. "Not under Magistrate Om's instruction. Evidence Review may propose entry. Conflict office will approve handling. Magistrate Om may not direct use of this document until conflict motion is heard."

Om accepted it immediately.

That helped. Ira wished it did not.

They built the entry:

Emergency Evidence Rule 17-C, countersigned by then-clerk Om, authorized standard extraction of damaged records over preservation concerns during first Cinder inquiry. The rule may have contributed to destruction or devaluation of witness deck recorder. It is entered for preservation, conflict review, and assumption failure analysis. No finding yet as to intent, negligence, or individual culpability.

Vela insisted on adding the technician protest note as linked material.

Pellin allowed it.

Bayo sealed the package.

Om remained standing by the table, looking at his younger signature.

Ira had expected anger at him to arrive cleanly. It did not. What she felt was worse: a sense of how small a person could be inside a large wrong and still be responsible for the pressure of their hand.

Om said, "My name should be removed from active rulings on first inquiry evidence."

Pellin nodded. "Yes."

"But not from the whole tribunal."

"I will not decide that at this table."

"No."

He turned to Ira. "You will receive an interim conflict protocol by morning."

"From whom?"

"Not me."

"Good."

The answer almost hurt him. Good, she thought. Let it.

Vela's relay dimmed, then brightened again. "Magistrate."

He looked at her.

"I said disclosure is not resurrection."

"You did."

"This still is not."

"No."

"But it is not burial."

Om bowed his head once.

After he left, Bayo sat down hard.

"I keep thinking there is a bottom," he said.

Ira looked at the sealed evidence package.

"There may be," she said. "But not because the fall gets tired."

Pellin gave her a look. "Do not put that in a motion."

"I won't."

Vela said, "She will put something worse in remarks."

Ira almost smiled, then did not.

At 04:10 station time, she added Emergency Evidence Rule 17-C to the assumption failure map.

OFFICIAL CLAIM: First inquiry found witness deck recorder unauthenticated and low value.

ASSUMPTION REQUIRED: Recorder failure reflects evidentiary weakness rather than extraction process.

RECORD CONDITION THAT PREVENTS ASSUMPTION: Technician protest note; forced standard extraction; Emergency Evidence Rule 17-C; static output; Glassward edge confirmation.

WHAT CANNOT YET BE CONCLUDED: Whether panel intended destruction; whether clerk certification reflected knowledge of risk; whether recoverable content remains.

PRESERVATION REQUIRED: Conflict protocol; native-condition reconstruction attempt; Oriole staged release; panel minutes; testimony from surviving technician if identifiable.

The system accepted the row.

Then it flagged:

CONFLICT: PRESIDING MAGISTRATE NAMED IN SOURCE MATERIAL.

For once, the warning was neither obstruction nor comfort.

It was simply true.

Chapter 15: The Routeholder Prayer

The routeholders held their memorial in a dock chapel with too much glass.

That was Ira's first note and she disliked herself for making it. Glass was not a crime. Light was not a crime. Beauty had survived the Fracture Wars too, though the Ash Belt often treated it as evidence tampering. Still, the chapel's panoramic curve faced the intact convoy berths rather than the station gravefield. Every line of sight had been chosen. The dead could be invoked without being allowed to interrupt the view.

Sable Array had permitted the ceremony under equal-access rules after survivor groups held three unauthorized vigils in pressure corridors. Routeholder houses and convoy families argued that their dead were also Cinder dead, and they were right. That was the problem with rights inside a room full of unequal power: true claims could still be staged as shields.

Ira attended because Evidence Review needed public context after the riot.

That was the official reason.

The private reason was worse. She wanted to see how innocence was built.

Bayo came with her, carrying a tablet and the expression of a man trying not to look like tribunal staff in a chapel where everyone hated tribunals differently. Vela refused to attend in person but opened a passive relay in Ira's ear.

"Describe only what matters," Vela said.

"Everything will claim to matter."

"Then begin with what the room wants you to crop."

The room wanted her to crop the security.

House guards stood at the chapel entrance in formal gray, unarmed by visible law and armed by posture. Station security stayed farther back, resenting the redundancy. Convoy families wore green bands despite the mixed-area ban because the chapel had been temporarily classified as dedicated memorial space. Survivor observers were allowed in the rear section after a negotiation that had produced seven rules and no trust.

At the front, beneath the glass, a routeholder chaplain arranged ninety-one small engine bearings in a spiral.

The bearings represented ships that cleared Cinder Gate.

Not people. Ships.

Ira wrote that down.

Bayo leaned close. "Should I be logging?"

"Yes."

"As evidence?"

"As context."

"That is how everything becomes evidence."

"Good. You are learning."

The ceremony began with a low chime modeled on an old docking tone. People bowed their heads. Ira did not, then realized refusal could become performance and lowered her eyes to her notes.

The chaplain was a woman from House Vey-Kalt, old enough to remember Cinder and polished enough to have been selected for broadcast restraint. Her robe carried no house crest, only a thread pattern that matched convoy route maps if you knew what to look for. The press deck had been barred, but private family recordings were permitted. That meant the ceremony would spread with plausible intimacy before becoming public statement.

"We gather," the chaplain said, "not to contest the court, but to remember what no court can hold: the terror of passage, the burden of command, the mercy of those who kept ships moving when stillness meant death."

Vela's voice in Ira's ear: "There. Kept ships moving."

Ira marked it.

The chaplain continued. "At Cinder, there were no clean choices. Every captain who crossed carried not triumph but remainder. Every engineer who held a seal, every pilot who obeyed a narrowing call, every logistics officer who counted mass while people screamed, every medical crew who received bodies they could not save, bore the ash forward so that some part of the Reach might live."

Bayo whispered, "That is well written."

"Yes."

"I hate that."

"Also learning."
