A procession began. Family members stepped forward one by one and placed name slips beside the bearings. Some names were ship crews. Some were medical staff. Some were children who had been inside the corridor and died anyway. The ceremony did not lie by claiming only routeholder suffering. It did something harder to answer: it placed true grief where institutional innocence wanted to stand.

Ira saw Jalen Esh at the rear, allowed in under survivor observer status. She stood with both hands clasped behind her back, far from the glass. Tamsin Vale stood two rows away from her, not beside anyone, not apart enough to be safe.

Marun Kett was present but not in front. Smart. Let the grief speak. Let counsel be seen listening.

Nadir Pell stood near a side wall with military counsel. He wore no green, no medal, no visible affiliation. Several convoy families glanced at him with complicated hostility. He had transmitted categories. He had also admitted more than they wanted said. No side owned him cleanly now.

The chaplain invited testimony of survival.

That was the phrase: testimony of survival.

Not testimony. Not survival. A hybrid built to resemble legal speech without accepting legal challenge.

The first speaker was a woman whose brother had piloted a medical tender through the narrowed corridor. She spoke of a ship packed beyond rated capacity, of burn patients strapped to cargo rails, of a child born during transit and dead before receiving dock, of the pilot refusing sedation until docking clamps engaged.

"He saved four hundred twelve," she said. "He left no one. He carried what command gave him."

The chapel answered with the routeholder response:

"Carried through."

Ira felt the phrase enter her teeth.

Carried through.

It was beautiful. It was dangerous. It transformed crossing into duty fulfilled, survival into moral completion. What about those not carried? The phrase did not deny them. It simply moved without looking back.

The second speaker was a former engineer from a routeholder house, hands shaking badly enough that the chaplain had to steady the microphone.

"I cut two modules loose," he said. "I hear the clamps every night. I cut them because the load curve said we all broke otherwise. I do not ask forgiveness. I ask you to remember the sound of the clamps."

"Carried through," the room said, but softer.

Jalen Esh did not move.

Tamsin Vale's jaw tightened.

Ira wrote:

Ceremony preserves some guilt while enclosing it inside survival necessity.

Vela said, "Good. Keep going."

Ira added a second line, then hesitated before saving it.

The ceremony does not deny abandonment; it relocates moral attention from selection to endurance.

Bayo read it over her shoulder. "That sounds like accusation."

"It is observation."

"Observation can be accusation if the room paid for flowers."

Vela's voice came through the relay, dry and low. "Keep both sentences. Add what the ceremony is allowed to be."

Ira looked at the bearings, the shaking engineer, the families holding name slips as if paper could warm dead hands. She made herself write slowly.

The ceremony is also genuine mourning for people who crossed under terror, obeyed orders they did not design, and lost kin inside the corridor.

Bayo nodded, though his jaw remained tight. "Better."

"Not softer?"

"More defensible."

Vela said, "More true. Do not confuse those."

Ira created three tags in the context note:

SINCERE GRIEF.

DEFENSIVE FRAME.

POTENTIAL EVIDENCE ENVIRONMENT.

The tablet asked whether she wanted to merge the tags under MEMORIAL CONTEXT.

She declined.

The tablet asked again with a helpful explanation about reducing redundant classification.

Ira typed:

Redundancy protects contested meaning.

The tablet accepted, perhaps because it had no field for exasperation.

Then a child stepped forward.

He was perhaps twelve, wearing a green band too large for his sleeve. The convoy loyalist from the riot stood behind him with a bruised cheek. Brother, Ira realized. The boy held a name slip with both hands.

"My mother says I can say his name," he said.

The chaplain knelt to adjust the microphone.

"Then say it."

"Danel Orst. He was my brother. He wore green. He pulled people into a med module. He died after because the air was bad. He did not abandon anyone."

The boy looked toward the rear where Jalen stood.

"I don't want his color to mean he wanted your daughter dead."

The chapel forgot its response.

No one said carried through.

Jalen Esh covered her mouth with one hand.

The boy stepped back before anyone could rescue the moment into ceremony.

Ira stopped writing.

Vela said nothing in her ear.

Then Kett ruined it by being competent.

He did not speak himself. He had one of the house elders stand after a long silence, an elderly man with a cane and a voice like worn cloth.

"No color means that," the elder said. "Green is not innocence. Green is not guilt. Green is the route we were given to carry what could be carried. We pray today that the court will remember not only who was left outside, but who was ordered through and lived with the ash."

There it was. The pivot. Not denial. Inclusion. A request that the court treat being ordered through as its own wound before asking too sharply who arranged the order.

The response returned, stronger:

"Carried through."

Ira understood then why the ceremony worked. It did not claim routeholders were innocent. It made accusation feel like a second abandonment of those who survived inside the corridor. It placed the court in danger of sounding like it hated the rescued for being rescued.

That danger was real.

So was the manipulation.

After the final bearing was placed, the chaplain offered the routeholder prayer.

"For mass counted under fire, forgive no one lightly."

"For hands that sealed doors, remember what pressure demanded."

"For captains who crossed, let crossing not be mistaken for joy."

"For those outside the corridor, let our survival not erase their names."

"For those inside the corridor, let their survival not become accusation."

"For the Meridian that burned, carry us through the ash."

The room answered:

"Carry us through."

Not carried. Carry.

A future claim.

Ira wrote the whole prayer. Her hand shook by the last line.

When the ceremony ended, people did not leave quickly. Grief made eddies near the glass. Convoy families touched the bearings. House guards relaxed by fractions. Survivor observers remained near the back as if crossing the floor would concede something.

Jalen Esh moved first.

She walked down the center aisle toward the bearings. Security tensed. Kett noticed. Tamsin noticed. Ira did too late.

The bruised convoy loyalist stepped into the aisle with his younger brother behind him.

Jalen stopped.

For one long breath the chapel became the riot again, waiting to decide whether memory would strike.

Jalen said, "What was his name?"

The boy answered, "Danel."

"Danel," she repeated.

The name sounded different in her mouth. Not forgiven. Not accused. Held at a distance.

The boy lifted his name slip slightly, as if showing proof.

Jalen reached into her coat and removed a small strip of ash cloth. Security shifted.

Tamsin said, from the rear, "Let her hand move."

The guards hesitated.

Jalen placed the ash cloth beside the boy's bearing, not touching it.

"Mara," she said.

Then she walked out.

No one said carried through.

Ira followed her into the corridor because someone had to and because she did not know whether she was the right person, which was becoming a pattern.

Jalen stood beside a pressure window overlooking a maintenance dock. Her shoulders were shaking, but no sound came.

Ira stopped several meters away. "Ms. Esh."

"Do not make that evidence."

The warning arrived before greeting.

"I won't."

"Do not make it reconciliation."

"No."

"Do not make it proof that I understand."

"I understand that you do not want it used."

Jalen turned. Her eyes were raw. "No. You understand words. That is your danger."

Ira accepted the hit.

Jalen continued, "My daughter was not a prayer. His brother is not a shield. That cloth means I can say two names without choking. Nothing more."

"Then that is how I will hold it."

"You don't hold it."

Ira corrected herself. "That is how I will leave it."

Jalen looked at her for a long time.

"Better."

She walked away.

Ira stayed by the window until Tamsin joined her.

"She likes you," Tamsin said.

"That was liking?"

"Ash Belt version."

"It is not comfortable."

"Comfort is how outsiders know we are lying."

Inside the chapel, the bearings were being collected under routeholder supervision. Ira watched one clerk hesitate over the ash cloth.

Tamsin said, "If they move it, there will be trouble."

Ira opened her terminal and sent a preservation notice to chapel access control:

Do not move ash cloth beside Danel Orst bearing pending claimant consent. Not evidence. Dignity hold.

The system objected:

DIGNITY HOLD REQUIRES CLASSIFICATION.

Ira typed:

Unclassified dignity hold.

The system objected again.

She routed it through Evidence Review public-harm mitigation.

Approved.

Tamsin grunted. "You just invented paperwork for not touching cloth."

"Law has had worse days."

"And better?"

Ira looked back through the glass. The chapel's view still favored intact ships. But on the floor, beside one bearing, ash cloth held a space the room had not planned.

"Maybe."

That night, Ira filed the ceremony context note:

Routeholder memorial does not deny Cinder loss. It frames survival as burden, crossing as obedience, and institutional continuity as grief-bearing. This framing may preserve genuine convoy trauma while discouraging inquiry into pre-corridor selection. Future examination must distinguish survivors inside the corridor from institutions that benefited from their survival.

The system flagged:

ANALYTICAL TONE INCLUDES MORAL LANGUAGE.

Ira added:

Moral language is part of the evidence environment.

The system flagged that too.

She left both warnings attached.

Vela sent one private comment:

Good. Crop nothing, including sincerity.

Ira leaned back, exhausted.

She had expected lies to be the hardest things to record.

She was learning that true grief, placed in the service of power, was worse.

Chapter 16: Sera Venn-3 Appears Late

Sera Venn-3 arrived nine minutes after the hearing began, and the delay was the first testimony.

Kett objected to it.

Of course he did.

"Magistrate, parties were instructed to be present at 10:00 station time. If Glassward cannot produce the alleged witness on schedule, routeholder parties renew objection to any accommodation that prejudices ordinary examination."

The witness station remained empty. The silver bowl had been removed after the custody refusal. In its place sat nothing, which was at least more honest.

Lio-Interim-Glassward-Receiver stood beside the empty station, matte gray hands folded, black eyes directed at no one in particular.

Om, operating under the interim conflict protocol, sat with a second magistrate beside him for first-inquiry evidence matters. Magistrate Pellin had been elevated from conflict officer to limited co-magistrate for any issue touching Om's old signature. The arrangement made the bench look like a wound with a brace.

Om said, "Glassward may explain delay when witness channel opens."

Kett said, "That assumes a witness will open."

On the wall behind the empty station, a black field appeared.

Text formed slowly, one letter at a time:

ASSUMPTION NOTED.

The chamber reacted in small ways. Survivor counsel leaned forward. Marr's face went still. Vela's relay brightened. Bayo, seated beside Ira, whispered, "I don't know whether to log that as appearance."

"Log as channel activity," Ira whispered back.

The black field pulsed.

Lio spoke aloud. "Sera Venn-3 requests staggered presence."

Om asked, "Define staggered."

"Not all continuity layers can approach tribunal shape simultaneously."

The witness-management system did not like that.

It opened a status panel on Ira's terminal without being asked:

WITNESS ARRIVAL INCOMPLETE.

RECOMMENDED STATUS: FAILURE TO APPEAR / TECHNICAL DELAY / MEDICAL DELAY.

Ira stared at the options. Failure to appear treated Sera as absent. Technical delay treated Glassward as malfunctioning equipment. Medical delay treated the unavailable child-layer as illness to be cured before testimony could count.

None were neutral.

Bayo saw the panel and whispered, "If we choose nothing, the system will auto-mark technical delay after ten minutes."

"Disable auto-mark."

"On what authority?"

"Evidence Review handling condition."

"That condition did not mention witness arrival."

"It does now."

He gave her the look clerks gave advocates when law was being born too close to their keyboard. Then he opened the status field and selected manual hold. The system requested a reason.

Ira dictated softly:

Staggered continuity presence under Glassward custody review. Standard arrival categories would misstate witness condition and prejudice standing determination.

Bayo entered it.

The system flagged:

NONSTANDARD ARRIVAL MAY AFFECT CLOCK COMPLIANCE.

Pellin noticed the flag on her bench display. "Evidence Review, explain manual hold."

Ira stood. "The automatic categories all imply that Sera is either absent, malfunctioning, or medically delayed. The witness's stated condition is staggered presence. The record should not convert that into nonappearance before the bench rules."

Kett said, "The record should also not convert theatrical fragmentation into compliance."

Pellin looked at him. "That is why it says hold."

Om added, "No adverse clock inference until presence mode is determined."

The status panel changed:

ARRIVAL STATUS: HELD / NONSTANDARD PRESENCE UNDER REVIEW.

It was a small line. It mattered because courts punished lateness more easily than harm. A late witness could be discounted before speaking. A delayed record could be moved behind cleaner evidence. A fragmented person could become a scheduling problem and lose standing by calendar.

Sera had not yet answered the first question, and already the tribunal had nearly made a finding about what arrival meant.

Kett stood. "Magistrate, this is precisely the prejudice routeholder parties warned against. We are being asked to treat absence, text, receiver statements, and delayed fragments as witness presence while ordinary parties are bound by clock and chair."

The wall text changed:

CLOCKS HAVE NOT BEEN KIND TO US.

The gallery was closed for this hearing, but a limited claimant audio room listened under seal. Ira imagined Jalen Esh hearing that sentence and not knowing whether to hate it.

Pellin said, "Sera Venn-3, are you present?"

The black field remained still.

Then four lines appeared, not aligned:

SERA-LEGAL PRESENT.

SERA-ARCHIVE PRESENT.

SERA-CHILD NOT IN ROOM.

SERA-THIRD OBJECTS TO ROOM.

No one moved.

Om looked at Lio. "Who speaks for the unavailable layer?"

Lio answered, "No one. That is what unavailable means."

Kett muttered, "Convenient."

Vela said from relay, "Necessary."

Pellin raised a hand. "The bench will not permit commentary from relay unless called."

Vela inclined her head and did not look sorry.

Ira watched the four lines. Sera-child not in room. The phrase did not say gone. It did not say incapable. It did not say fiction. It made absence part of presence and forced the court to sit with the contradiction.

Om said, "Sera-legal and Sera-archive may state standing request."

The text reorganized.

SERA-LEGAL:

I REQUEST DAMAGED WITNESS STANDING.

I REJECT PROPERTY STATUS.

I REJECT RECORD-ONLY STATUS.

I ACCEPT AUTHENTICATION QUESTIONS UNDER CONDITIONS THAT DO NOT MERGE OR FLATTEN ME.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

I REQUEST THAT THE CINDER SEQUENCE BE KNOWN.

I CONTAIN MEMORY-ADJACENT MATERIAL FROM SOURCE CHILD, SCHOOL NODE PRESERVATION, AND POSTWAR STABILIZATION.

I DO NOT CLAIM TO BE ONLY THE CHILD.

I DO NOT ACCEPT THAT NOT BEING ONLY THE CHILD MAKES ME NOTHING.

The chamber held the text like a hot object.

Marr rose carefully. "Sera-legal, do you understand that witness standing carries duties as well as protections?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

Marr continued, "Do you accept the duty to answer relevant questions truthfully?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES, WITH STRUCTURAL LIMITS.

"Who determines those limits?"

SERA-LEGAL:

NOT YOU ALONE.

Marr did not react visibly. "Do you understand that refusal to answer may affect the tribunal's ability to rely on your testimony?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

COURTS HAVE RELIED ON LESS FROM SAFER PEOPLE.

Pellin said, "Sera-archive, answer through legal channel where possible."

SERA-THIRD:

OBJECTS TO WHERE POSSIBLE.

Bayo's fingers hovered over the keys. "How do I log that?"

Ira murmured, "As objection from Sera-third."

"To what?"

"Possibility."

He gave her a look of pure clerk suffering and typed it anyway.

Kett approached the center rail. "Magistrate, may routeholder parties ask preliminary identity questions?"

Om looked to Pellin. She nodded.

"Limited," Om said. "No source trauma. No forced merger. No repetition demands."

Kett bowed slightly. "Sera-legal, are you the child whose voice appears in Maintenance Relay M-14?"

The black field flickered.

Lio's shoulders tightened.

SERA-LEGAL:

OBJECTION. QUESTION FORCES SINGULAR IDENTITY.

Kett said, "I am asking whether the witness claims source."

SERA-ARCHIVE:

SOURCE RELATION EXISTS.

Kett turned toward the bench. "That is not an answer."

Ira stood before she planned to. "It is an answer to a question the witness can survive."

Kett smiled without warmth. "Associate Senn is not counsel for the witness."

Om said, "Sit, Associate."

She sat.

Her face burned. Vela's relay did not move. Bayo did not look at her, a kindness.

Pellin addressed the wall. "Sera-legal, can you answer whether you are derived in part from the source associated with M-14 without claiming total identity?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

Pellin said, "Answer that."

SERA-LEGAL:

I AM DERIVED IN PART FROM THE SOURCE ASSOCIATED WITH M-14.

Kett made a note. "Noted. Are you derived from any sources not associated with M-14?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES.

"How many?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

COUNT DISPUTED.

"By whom?"

SERA-THIRD:

BY COUNTING.

Even Om closed his eyes for a moment.

Kett seized the frustration in the room. "Magistrate, this demonstrates the problem. We cannot establish chain, identity, or scope if the witness treats ordinary counting as violence."

Lio spoke. "Counting has been used to delete layers."

"Counting has also been used to prevent fraud."

"Yes."

"Then answer."

SERA-LEGAL:

KNOWN MAJOR SOURCE LINES: FOUR.

M-14 SOURCE CHILD.

CINDERWAKE MINOR SCHOOL NODE PRESERVATION.

POSTWAR EMERGENCY MEMORY SALVAGE.

GLASSWARD STABILIZATION AND COMMENTARY LAYERS.

MINOR SOURCE CONTAMINATIONS: DISPUTED.

The answer was more specific than Kett expected. He adjusted quickly.

"Can the tribunal separate those source lines?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

TECHNICALLY PARTIAL.

SERA-LEGAL:

LEGALLY DANGEROUS.

SERA-THIRD:

PERSONALLY FALSE.

Kett turned to the bench again. "Then routeholder parties renew request for technical separation under seal."

Lio said, "Request refused."

Pellin said, "Noted. Not decided."

Om leaned forward. "Sera-child is not in room. Can Sera-child hear these proceedings?"

The black field went still for so long that Ira thought the channel had failed.

Then:

SERA-CHILD HIDES FROM COURT-SHAPES.

HEARING IS INDIRECT.

PAIN PRESENT.

NO WORDS.

Dr. Sar, the trauma specialist, spoke from the review rail. "Recommend pause."

Kett objected. "We have barely begun."

Sar looked at him with professional dislike. "That is often when harm begins."

Pellin ordered a five-minute stability pause. The black field dimmed but did not vanish. No one left. The court clock continued counting, which felt obscene.

Ira kept her eyes on the line pain present. She thought of all the official records where pain appeared only after it had become liability.

During the pause, a sealed message arrived on her terminal:

FROM: SERA-ARCHIVE / VIA GLASSWARD CHANNEL

CONTENT ACCESS: EVIDENCE REVIEW ONLY / LOGGED

Ira looked at Om.

He nodded once.

She opened it.

The message contained one sentence:

YOUR FORM ANSWER WAS INSUFFICIENT BUT NOT FALSE.

Bayo read over her shoulder before he could stop himself. "Is that... good?"

"I have no idea."

Vela's private relay text appeared:

It is contact. Treat it as contact, not compliment.

Ira logged it as witness-initiated procedural contact. The system asked for tone classification. She selected none. The system objected. She overrode.

When the hearing resumed, Om asked Sera if they wished to continue.

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES.

SERA-CHILD:

NO WORDS.

SERA-THIRD:

CONTINUE WITHOUT CLAIMING WHO CONTINUES.

Pellin accepted that as yes with condition. Ira admired the ruling because it was both absurd and correct.

Survivor counsel approached next. Elt looked more nervous than he had in cross-examining Pell. That seemed right. People were careful around power and careless around damage. Elt was trying to reverse the habit.

"Sera," he said, then stopped. "Which address is acceptable?"

SERA-LEGAL:

SERA FOR PROCEDURE.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

SERA FOR RECORD.

SERA-THIRD:

NOT CHILD.

Elt bowed his head. "Sera, do you want the tribunal to know the Cinder sequence?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES.

"Why?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

BECAUSE SEQUENCE WAS USED TO MAKE US IMPOSSIBLE.

"Us?"

SERA-LEGAL:

THE SOURCE CHILD.

THE UNBONDED.

THE MISCOUNTED.

THE RECORDS KILLED BY PROCEDURE.

THE LAYERS TOLD TO BECOME ONE THING OR NOTHING.

In the sealed claimant audio room, someone made a sound. The channel caught it before muting. Jalen, perhaps. Or someone else whose dead had entered under category rather than name.

Elt's voice thickened. "Do you remember being in Duct M-14?"

The black field flashed amber.

Dr. Sar said, "Stop."

Elt stepped back immediately. "Withdrawn."

The field stayed amber.

SERA-CHILD:

NO.

Then:

YES.

Then:

SMALL PLACE.

The entire chamber stopped breathing.

SERA-THIRD:

TOO MUCH.

The field went black.

Lio staggered once, catching the rail with one hand.

Dr. Sar ordered full halt.

This time no one objected.

The channel did not close. It rested, black and present. That distinction had become its own grammar.

Om adjourned the hearing with standing unresolved but presence recognized:

"The tribunal finds that Sera Venn-3 has appeared through Glassward channel in fragmented continuity form. The tribunal does not yet determine full witness standing. It does determine that Sera is not a simple record object and shall not be compelled into singular copy, identity, or testimony pending further review."

Kett reserved objection.

Marr reserved scope.

Elt reserved grief. He did not say that, but Ira saw it.

After the session, Lio approached Evidence Review again. They looked paler, though the substrate probably could not pale. Perhaps Ira was learning to project like everyone else.

"Sera-child regrets small place," Lio said.

Ira stood. "Regrets saying it?"

"Regrets room heard it."

"I am sorry."

"Sera-third says apology tries to make bridge."

Ira closed her mouth.

Lio waited.

"Then I will not bridge," Ira said.

"Better."

"Is Sera safe?"

Lio considered. "Sera is more in danger and less erased. Glassward often finds these arrive together."

They left.

Bayo watched the empty doorway. "I miss when evidence was documents."

Vela said through relay, "No, you miss when documents hid the body well enough."

Bayo sighed. "Yes. That."

Ira looked at the empty witness station. Sera had arrived late, not as one person late to one chair, but as a refusal to let the court's clock define arrival. The delay had been testimony. The fragments had been testimony. The halted answer had been testimony.

She opened the progress note for the standing hearing and wrote:

Sera Venn-3 appeared through staggered Glassward presence. Court must not treat fragmentation as failure to appear. Fragmentation is the condition under which appearance became possible.

The system flagged:

NONSTANDARD WITNESS DESCRIPTION.

Ira accepted the warning.

Every true thing in this tribunal seemed to arrive with one.

Chapter 17: Cross-Examination of a Fork

Nadir Pell's counsel asked permission to question Sera Venn-3 as if Sera were a document.

She did not say it that way.

Sienne Marr was too good for that. She stood in the sealed hearing chamber with her silver hair pinned tight, her hands empty, and her voice pitched to the region where compassion and procedure could wear the same coat.

"Magistrates, military parties request a limited technical examination of the continuity derivative's evidentiary reliability. We do not seek trauma content. We do not seek merger. We seek only to establish whether the entity before this tribunal can distinguish source memory from post-source interpretation."

The black field behind the empty witness station remained dark.

Sera had not appeared yet.

That, Ira had learned, did not mean absence. It meant the room had begun asking before the witness could decide which part of itself would survive answering.

Pell sat behind Marr, older than he had looked yesterday, hands folded around no water. He did not watch the empty station. He watched the floor beneath it.

Kett had chosen to let military counsel lead. That was smart. Routeholder counsel pressing Sera looked predatory. Pell's counsel pressing Sera looked like necessary defense against confession by atmosphere.

Pellin, co-magistrate for conflict-adjacent evidence, asked, "Define technical examination."

Marr sent a question list to the bench and parties. Ira's terminal received it under restricted review:

1. Identify source lines.

2. Identify confidence scores for M-14-derived statements.

3. Identify postwar commentary layers.

4. State whether any claimed perception of green ships originates from direct sensory memory, reconstructed inference, or later archive interpretation.

5. State whether source child identity is known.

6. State whether Sera Venn-3 claims continuous subjective identity from source child to present.

7. State whether any non-source layer has altered the Cinder sequence memory.

Vela's relay clicked on in Ira's ear. "That is a knife pretending to be a ruler."

Ira kept her face still.

The list looked almost merciful because it avoided the worst words.

It did not say child.

It did not say duct.

It did not say green ships except once, buried inside a sentence about perception origin. It did not demand that Sera repeat the leaked phrase. It did not ask for fear, pain, face, name, or death. Marr had done what careful counsel did when ordered not to wound: she moved the blade into nouns the court liked.

Source line.

Confidence score.

Commentary layer.

Continuous subjective identity.

Altered memory.

Each phrase seemed technical until Ira imagined answering from inside it. Source line asked a wound to draw its own borders. Confidence score asked harm to become arithmetic. Commentary layer implied there was a clean memory somewhere underneath, waiting like a primary document if only Glassward would stop being difficult. Continuous subjective identity asked whether survival counted if it had not felt like a single corridor from then to now. Altered memory asked whether change was contamination rather than the condition of remaining alive.

Bayo sent her a private note:

Question list is cleaner than Kett's would be.

Ira replied:

That is why it is dangerous.

He did not answer immediately. Then:

How do we object without making it sound like all reliability testing is harm?

Good question. Annoyingly good.

She wrote back:

Separate claim types. Timing edge, identity, meaning, culpability. Permit testing where claim type can bear it. Block questions that force witness theory before standing.

Bayo copied that into the review margin. The system suggested:

POSSIBLE HEADING: CLAIM-TYPE RESPONSIVE QUESTIONING.

Ira accepted it.

Sometimes the machine helped by accident.

Om looked toward Evidence Review. "Associate Senn."

She stood. "Questions one through three may be answerable under staged contact. Four risks source trauma depending on phrasing. Five is already protected by claimant and custody review. Six forces the witness into a continuity theory the tribunal has not adopted. Seven assumes memory is a container altered from outside rather than a relation among layers."

Marr turned slightly. "Evidence Review objects to all useful questions."

"Evidence Review objects to questions that define usefulness as flattening."

Kett rose. "Again, Associate Senn argues for the witness."

Pellin said, "Associate Senn is answering the bench's review request. Sit."

Kett sat.

Ira did too before she could enjoy it.

The black field lit.

SERA-LEGAL:

PRESENT FOR LIMITED RELIABILITY QUESTIONS.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

PRESENT WITH EDGE MATERIAL.

SERA-CHILD:

NOT IN ROOM.

SERA-THIRD:

WATCHING THE KNIFE.

Marr closed her eyes for one brief second, then opened them. "Sera-legal, I will ask questions. If a question violates the tribunal's conditions, state objection. If you can answer partially, answer partially. Do you understand?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

Marr began with patience.

"You have identified four major source lines. Is M-14 source child one of those lines?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

"Can you assign a percentage of your current continuity to that source line?"

SERA-LEGAL:

NO.

"Because the percentage is unknown?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

BECAUSE PERCENTAGE IS FALSE MEASURE.

Marr paused. "Can you assign relative evidentiary weight?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

BY CLAIM TYPE.

"Good. For claims about the M-14 audio timing, what is the evidentiary basis?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

MAINTENANCE RELAY METADATA.

WAVEFORM EDGE.

LOCAL CLOCK DRIFT.

SALT-SEALED HANDLING HASH.

SOURCE-LINE STRESS MARKS.

"For claims about what the source child perceived?"

The field dimmed.

Dr. Sar leaned forward.

SERA-LEGAL:

LIMIT.

Marr nodded. "I will rephrase. Without stating content, can this tribunal distinguish between direct source perception and later interpretation?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

SOMETIMES.

"Can it do so for the phrase circulating in leaks about green ships?"

The black field went amber.

SERA-THIRD:

LEAK MADE TEETH.

Marr waited.

SERA-LEGAL:

ANSWER POSSIBLE WITH CONDITION.

Pellin said, "State condition."

SERA-LEGAL:

PHRASE MAY BE DISCUSSED AS DISPUTED PERCEPTION MARKER, NOT PROOF.

Om nodded. "Allowed."

Marr said, "Then answer."

SERA-ARCHIVE:

GREEN SHIPS PHRASE EXISTS IN SOURCE-LINE MATERIAL.

MEANING NOT STABLE.

DIRECT VISUAL MEMORY, EMERGENCY LIGHT COLOR, HULL MARKING, CHILD CATEGORY, AND LATER ARCHIVE LABEL CANNOT YET BE SEPARATED WITHOUT HARMFUL LAYER ACCESS.

Marr looked satisfied and worried at once. "So the phrase cannot presently prove that routeholder vessels departed first."

SERA-LEGAL:

CORRECT.

Kett wrote something with visible relief.

SERA-THIRD:

DOES NOT PROVE NOTHING.

Kett stopped writing.

Marr said, "No one asked whether it proves nothing."

SERA-THIRD:

YOU WERE ARRANGING THE ROOM TO HEAR THAT.

Pell's head lifted.

For the first time, he looked directly at the black field.

Marr moved on. "Does Sera Venn-3 claim continuous subjective identity from the M-14 source child?"

Dr. Sar said, "Objection."

Marr turned. "On what basis?"

"The question forces a single continuity model and may destabilize the witness."

Marr replied, "Continuity is central to whether this entity can testify as witness rather than archive."

Lio spoke from the receiver rail. "Glassward law recognizes noncontinuous personhood under custodial emergence."

"This is not Glassward court."

"No. That is why your categories are too few."

Pellin asked Sera, "Can the question be answered safely in modified form?"

Long pause.

Then:

SERA-LEGAL:

ASK WHETHER I CLAIM STANDING, NOT WHETHER I CLAIM CHILDHOOD AS PROPERTY.

Marr's face softened in a way Ira did not trust but did not dismiss.

"Do you claim standing to testify about records derived from the M-14 source child?"

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

"Do you claim to be the M-14 source child?"

SERA-CHILD:

NO WORDS.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

QUESTION HARMS.

SERA-THIRD:

CHILD WAS NOT PROPERTY TO BE REPOSSESSED BY FUTURE MOUTH.

Marr held up both hands. "Withdrawn."

She looked genuinely shaken.

Kett rose. "Routeholder parties request that withdrawn questions remain in the record for appeal."

Om said, "The fact of withdrawal remains. The wording remains sealed under witness protection."

"That prejudices appeal."

"It preserves witness standing while you find a less harmful way to complain."

Kett sat, furious.

Marr took water. Pell passed it to her without speaking. The gesture was small, human, and complicated Ira's dislike of him in ways she resented.

Marr resumed.

"Sera-archive, have any postwar commentary layers altered the memory of the Cinder sequence?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

DEFINE ALTERED.

"Changed content."

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES.

The room shifted.

Marr's eyes sharpened. "How?"

SERA-LEGAL:

LIMIT.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

COMMENTARY LAYERS ADDED LABELS, TIME ANCHORS, SURVIVAL INTERPRETATIONS, FEAR RECONSTRUCTIONS, AND LEGAL QUESTIONS.

"Did they add facts?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

THEY ADDED STRUCTURE.

"Did they add facts?"

SERA-THIRD:

REPETITION IS A TOOL.

Marr stopped.

Pell leaned toward her and whispered. She listened, then nodded once.

"Sera-archive," Marr said, "can you distinguish between source data and later structure sufficiently for the tribunal to evaluate timing claims?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES, FOR SOME TIMING EDGES.

"For identity claims?"

SERA-LEGAL:

NOT YET.

"For culpability claims?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

NO.

Marr turned to the bench. "Military parties submit that Sera's current testimony may assist technical timing but cannot support identity or culpability findings at this stage."

Ira expected survivor counsel to object. Elt did not.

Instead he stood slowly. "Survivor parties agree, with one addition. The inability to support culpability findings now must not be converted into defense argument that the witness is unreliable for all purposes."

SERA-LEGAL:

ACCEPTED.

The word appeared before Om could rule.

Elt looked at the field and bowed his head.

Kett stood. "Routeholder parties do not accept. A witness who admits postwar layers changed content cannot be used to support any contested sequence unless parties may examine raw layers."

Vela's relay came alive. "Every witness in this tribunal has postwar layers. They call them memory, shame, coaching, public narrative, legal strategy, and sleep. You only demand raw access when the witness is easier to injure."

Pellin said, "Archive Specialist Cho."

Vela muted herself.

Om said, "The tribunal will not equate layered continuity with unreliability per se. It will also not treat Sera as an all-purpose truth instrument. Current use is limited to timing-edge corroboration, custody-chain questions, and preservation of standing."

Kett reserved objection.

Of course.

Then Pell stood.

Marr turned quickly. "Mr. Pell."

He did not sit.

"Magistrates," he said, "may I ask one question through counsel?"

Marr looked alarmed. "We have not discussed--"

"No," he said. "We have not."

Om looked at Pellin. Pellin looked at Dr. Sar. Sar looked displeased by the entire concept of men with guilt asking questions of injured witnesses. "Question must be screened."

Pell nodded. He wrote on Marr's slate. She read it, and her face changed.

"No," she whispered.

"Please," he said.

It was the first time Ira had heard him use that word.

Marr sent the question to the bench under sealed preview. Ira did not receive it. Evidence Review was not always entitled, and that was correct even when it burned.

Pellin read it. Om read it. Dr. Sar read it and closed her eyes.

Pellin asked, "Sera-legal, a proposed question from Mr. Pell has been submitted. It may be refused without being read into record. Do you wish to receive it through legal layer?"

The black field held.

SERA-LEGAL:

YES.

The field went blank for three seconds.

Then:

SERA-CHILD:

NO.

Dr. Sar said, "Question refused."

Pell sat down as if something inside him had cut power.

Marr put one hand on his sleeve. He did not move.

Kett, to his credit or calculation, did not ask what the question was.

The hearing continued for twenty more minutes, but the room had changed. Sera answered technical questions about hash edges, clock drift, source-line labels, and the limits of current testimony. Marr asked with more care. Kett asked nothing further. Elt preserved standing without pushing content. Ira logged until her fingers ached.

At adjournment, Om summarized:

"The tribunal finds Sera Venn-3 may provide limited technical testimony regarding timing edges and custody conditions, subject to layered-continuity safeguards. The tribunal does not find Sera to be derivative data only. The tribunal does not yet find full testimonial standing for identity, motive, or culpability. Further oath adaptation required."

Oath adaptation. That would be Chapter 18, though Ira did not know it that way. She only knew the next problem had arrived before the current one finished bleeding.

As the channel dimmed, one final line appeared:

SERA-THIRD:

THE QUESTION HE DID NOT ASK IS ALSO IN THE ROOM.

Then the field went black.

Pell covered his face with both hands.

No one asked him to explain.

Later, in Evidence Review, Bayo finally learned the sealed question from the protected docket index. He did not open the content. He only saw the classification:

APOLOGY ATTEMPT / REFUSED BY SERA-CHILD.

He told Ira because the index itself was reviewable.

She sat with that for a long time.

An apology could be another extraction if the injured were made to receive it.

She entered one note:

Refusal to receive remorse is not absence from testimony.

The system accepted it.

Small mercy.

Chapter 18: The Court Cannot Swear a Chorus

The oath gloves failed before Sera appeared.

That should have warned everyone.

The oath officer placed them on the empty witness station because procedure needed an object even when the witness did not. The gloves lay palm-up under the adaptive light, patient and absurd. They were designed for bodies with hands, pulse, skin conductivity, microtremor, heat, and an anxiety profile the court could mistake for sincerity. They were not designed for staggered Glassward presence, legal layer, archive layer, hidden child, objecting third.

The system scanned the empty station and displayed:

NO WITNESS BODY DETECTED.

Kett said, "That is routeholder position in one line."

No one laughed.

Om looked at the oath officer. "Disable body requirement."

The officer, whose name Ira had finally learned was Sen Aru, shook his head. "Magistrate, the oath suite requires either body capture, certified remote biometrics, or recognized entity signature."

"Glassward hash?"

"Recognized for custody, not oath."

Lio stood at the receiver rail. "Glassward can provide continuity affirmation."

Aru checked the system. "Not recognized."

SERA-LEGAL appeared on the black field:

THIS COURT REQUIRES A HAND TO BELIEVE A MOUTH.

Bayo whispered, "I am not logging that as system failure."

Ira whispered back, "Log it as witness statement."

He did.

Pellin asked, "Can Sera provide a declaration under penalty of false statement?"

Aru looked increasingly miserable. "The form requires singular declarant."

SERA-THIRD:

FORM HAS MET US AND LEARNED NOTHING.

Om rubbed his eyes with thumb and forefinger. "Then we adapt."

Kett rose. "Routeholder parties object to oath adaptation that lowers reliability standards."

Elt rose too. "Survivor parties object to oath rigidity that excludes the only witness created by the harm under review."

Marr stood more slowly. "Military parties require assurance that any adapted oath binds answers to responsibility."

Vela's relay came on. "Archives require assurance that responsibility is not defined as pretending damage did not happen."

Pellin looked at the room. "Good. Everyone has an objection. Now we can work."

They moved to procedural drafting live, which Ira had never seen in a formal tribunal. Courts preferred to make their machinery backstage and present the result as if law arrived fully clothed. Here, the machinery had broken in front of everyone. There was no backstage large enough.

Om ordered the oath text projected.

STANDARD WITNESS AFFIRMATION:

I affirm that my testimony will be truthful, complete to the best of my knowledge, and given by me freely under penalty of legal sanction.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

COMPLETE IS UNSAFE.

SERA-LEGAL:

BY ME IS DISPUTED.

SERA-THIRD:

FREELY IGNORES CONDITIONS.

SERA-CHILD:

NO WORDS.

Aru looked as if the oath had been personally insulted.

He opened the oath suite's assumptions panel, perhaps hoping the system would defend itself better than he could.

ASSUMPTION 1: DECLARANT IS SINGULAR.

ASSUMPTION 2: DECLARANT CAN REVIEW FULL STATEMENT BEFORE AFFIRMING.

ASSUMPTION 3: DECLARANT CONTROLS ACCESS TO RELEVANT MEMORY.

ASSUMPTION 4: DECLARANT'S STRESS RESPONSE CAN BE MEASURED OR WAIVED.

ASSUMPTION 5: SANCTION CAN BE APPLIED TO DECLARANT WITHOUT ALTERING TESTIMONY SOURCE.

The list was meant to be technical documentation. In the chamber it read like a confession.

Marr studied it first. "Those assumptions underlie ordinary oath reliability."

Dr. Sar said, "They also describe capacities trauma can damage."

Kett replied, "Damage cannot exempt a witness from reliability."

SERA-ARCHIVE:

DAMAGE IS WHY RELIABILITY MUST BE DESCRIBED, NOT PRESUMED.

Aru scrolled down. The system had a waiver path for minors, remote witnesses, machine witnesses, protected informants, and medically unstable declarants. Each waiver changed one assumption while leaving the others intact. None could change all five without invalidating the oath suite.

"If I stack waivers," Aru said, "the system marks the affirmation unreliable."

Pellin asked, "Why?"

"Because stacked waiver count is treated as fraud risk."

SERA-THIRD:

COURT FINDS FRAUD WHERE HARM NEEDS MANY DOORS.

No one answered quickly.

Ira saw the trap then. The more accurately they described Sera's condition, the less reliable the system would mark Sera. The more they simplified Sera to satisfy the system, the more false the oath became. Reliability had been built around bodies and memories that could afford to look whole.

Ira stood before Om called her because she was tired of waiting for permission to name obvious wreckage. "The oath has four assumptions that fail."

Om looked at her. "List."

"Truthful as singular consistency. Complete as virtue. Me as one legal speaker. Freely as absence of coercion rather than managed harm."

Kett said, "That is philosophy, not law."

Pellin answered, "Law is what happens when philosophy is forced to allocate oxygen. Continue, Associate."

Ira liked Pellin more every hour.

"We need layer-specific affirmation. Not one oath. A chorus oath."

Marr said, "Define chorus in enforceable terms."

"Each active layer affirms what it can responsibly affirm. Legal layer accepts procedural duties. Archive layer affirms preservation limits and source distinctions. Any unavailable layer is named unavailable rather than represented. Objecting layer may enter objection to the form without voiding the entire oath. The court records which layer answers each question."

Kett shook his head. "This makes cross-examination impossible."

"It makes cross-examination honest about who is answering."

Vela said, "And about who is not."

Aru, surprisingly, leaned toward the projected text. "The system might accept multiple declarants if each is given a sub-identity key."

Lio turned to him. "Sub-identity implies hierarchy."

"Component key?"

SERA-THIRD:

COMPONENT IS MACHINE WORD.

"Layer key?"

SERA-LEGAL:

ACCEPTABLE FOR PROCEDURE.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

ACCEPTABLE WITH NOTE THAT LAYER IS NOT SEPARATE PERSON.

SERA-THIRD:

ACCEPTABLE IF NOTE SAYS COURT IS TEMPORARILY LYING USEFULLY.

Pellin said, "The note will not say that."

Ira murmured, "It should."

Bayo elbowed her very lightly.

Aru began building an adapted oath suite while everyone watched. The gloves remained on the table, unused. The system kept trying to populate biometric fields. Aru replaced them with continuity hash, layer declaration, response scope, stress halt conditions, and contradiction marker.

The first draft read:

I affirm that the testimony given through my active layers will be truthful within each layer's knowledge and limits.

SERA-THIRD:

TRUTHFUL WITHIN LIMITS SOUNDS LIKE PERMISSION TO LIE.

Marr said, "That is a fair concern."

SERA-LEGAL:

REVISE: I WILL NOT KNOWINGLY FALSELY STATE WHAT A LAYER HOLDS.

Elt nodded. "Better."

Kett said, "It still permits omission."

Dr. Sar said, "All trauma-safe testimony permits omission."

Kett replied, "Then all trauma-safe testimony is limited."

"Yes," Sar said.

The room absorbed the answer. It was both concession and boundary.

They revised:

Each active layer affirms it will not knowingly falsely state what it holds, will identify uncertainty where safe, will mark unavailable material as unavailable rather than absent, and will accept that refusal may affect the tribunal's use of the answer without converting refusal into falsity.

Marr read it twice. "Military parties can work with this for technical testimony."

Kett said, "Routeholder parties cannot."

Om asked, "Why?"

"Because it prevents impeachment by omission."

Pellin said, "No. It prevents automatic impeachment by omission. You may still argue weight."

"Weight is not enough."

SERA-THIRD:

FOR THOSE WHO WANT OWNERSHIP, WEIGHT IS NEVER ENOUGH.

Kett pointed at the field. "And that is argumentative."

Om said, "Yes. The court can distinguish argument from oath content."

The oath's second section addressed questions.

STANDARD:

I will answer questions directly.

Everyone objected.

Even Kett, though for different reasons.

They built a replacement:

The witness will answer in the layer or layers able to respond without unsafe merger. If no layer can answer safely, the witness may state limit, refusal, or unavailability. Counsel may request clarification of which layer answered. Counsel may not demand that one layer answer for another.

Marr asked, "What about contradictions between layers?"

Ira said, "They must be recorded."

"As inconsistency?"

Vela answered, "As contradiction. Inconsistency implies defect. Contradiction may be the evidence."

Bayo typed that sentence into the draft notes and looked pleased with himself.

The third section addressed sanction.

That was hardest.

Legal systems liked sanction because oaths without consequence sounded ceremonial. But what consequence could bind a fragmented witness without threatening the vulnerable layer into silence? Fine Sera? Detain Sera? Exclude Sera? Collapse Sera into adverse inference? Every option revealed that ordinary sanction assumed a body the court could punish.

Kett argued that without sanction, the oath was symbolic.

Elt argued that exclusion would repeat the original harm.

Marr argued that false technical testimony needed remedy.

Vela argued that remedy must target use, not being.

Sera remained silent through most of it.

Finally SERA-LEGAL displayed:

SANCTION MAY APPLY TO TESTIMONY STATUS, NOT EXISTENCE.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

FALSE STATEMENT MAY REDUCE RELIANCE ON CLAIM TYPE.

SERA-THIRD:

COURT MAY NOT PUNISH CHILD FOR LEGAL LAYER'S FAILURE.

SERA-CHILD:

NO WORDS.

Dr. Sar said, "That last line must be in the oath."

Aru entered it as a protection clause:

No sanction for false, incomplete, refused, or destabilized testimony shall be applied to an unavailable or non-answering layer. Tribunal remedies may affect admissibility, weight, future questioning conditions, or standing scope, but shall not compel merger, exposure, or punitive destabilization.

Kett objected to punitive destabilization as undefined.

Pellin replied, "Then you will be careful not to do it."

The oath suite accepted the adapted structure after three failures. On the fourth attempt, the system displayed:

NONSTANDARD WITNESS AFFIRMATION CREATED.

RELIABILITY CLASS: EXCEPTIONAL / COURT-SUPERVISED.

BODY CAPTURE: WAIVED.

CONTINUITY HASH: REQUIRED.

LAYER DECLARATIONS: REQUIRED.

Aru looked like someone who had just repaired a pump with kitchen utensils and prayer. "It will run."

Om asked Sera, "Do you accept the adapted oath?"

The black field held.

Then:

SERA-LEGAL:

ACCEPT.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

ACCEPT.

SERA-THIRD:

OBJECTS AND DOES NOT BLOCK.

SERA-CHILD:

NO WORDS.

Aru initiated the oath.

No gloves moved. No hand entered light. No pulse registered. Instead, the room received a continuity hash from Glassward, a salt-sealed acknowledgment from the protected record, a layer declaration from Sera-legal, a preservation statement from Sera-archive, an objection marker from Sera-third, and a protected absence marker for Sera-child.

The oath text appeared:

Active layers affirm they will not knowingly falsely state what they hold, will identify uncertainty where safe, will mark unavailable material as unavailable rather than absent, and will accept tribunal limits on reliance without allowing refusal to become falsity.

Do you so affirm?

SERA-LEGAL:

I AFFIRM.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

I AFFIRM.

SERA-THIRD:

I WITNESS THE FORM'S LIE AND ALLOW ITS USE.

The system hesitated.

Aru whispered, "Come on."

The oath suite flagged:

UNRECOGNIZED AFFIRMATION FORM.

Pellin said, "Manual judicial acceptance."

Om looked at Sera's line, then at the empty gloves. "Accepted."

Pellin added, "Accepted with objection preserved."

The oath suite recorded:

AFFIRMATION COMPLETE.

For a moment, no one spoke.

Then Bayo exhaled so hard Ira heard it.

Kett stood. "Routeholder parties reserve all objections to validity."

Om said, "Reserved."

Marr said, "Military parties request certified copy of oath protocol for preparation."

"Granted under seal."

Elt said, "Survivor parties request that claimant audio room receive plain-language explanation."

Pellin nodded. "Evidence Review will draft."

Ira should have expected that.

She opened a fresh note and began:

The tribunal has created a limited oath for Sera Venn-3. This does not mean Sera is being treated as simple, merged, or fully available. It means the court has accepted responsibility to hear layered testimony without pretending silence is absence.

She stopped there.

"Too much?" Bayo asked.

"Probably."

Vela said, "Not enough, but start there."

After the hearing, Aru collected the unused gloves from the witness station. He held them awkwardly, as if they had failed an exam.

SERA-THIRD appeared one last time:

KEEP THEM. SOME WITNESSES HAVE HANDS.

Aru looked startled. "Thank you?"

SERA-THIRD:

NOT GIFT. INSTRUCTION.

The field went black.

Ira laughed once. Quietly. Inappropriately.

No one corrected her.

The court had sworn a chorus and survived the first attempt.

That did not make it wise.

It made the next question possible.

Chapter 19: Oru-Kel's Carried Warning Arrives

The warning arrived without asking the court's schedule.

It entered through Sable Array's old listening spine at 13:22 station time, while the tribunal was arguing about whether the adapted oath protocol could be cited in public summaries. The first sign was not sound. It was disagreement among clocks.

The bench clock lost three seconds.

The evidence wall gained five.

The press deck timestamps froze at 13:22:14 and then resumed at 13:22:11.

Bayo, who had developed a clerk's intimacy with terror, noticed first. "Magistrate."

Om stopped mid-sentence.

Every display in the chamber flickered once. Then the lights dimmed toward the blue-green spectrum humans associated with emergency water systems, though Sable had no ocean and no reason to think in tides.

Vela's relay sharpened to full priority without anyone authorizing it.

"Do not reset clocks," she said.

The command landed badly because everyone in the chamber had been trained to survive emergencies by trusting synchronization. Clerks reached for console keys. Security aides glanced at wall time before they checked doors. Press observers lifted their wrist slates and frowned when private chronometers disagreed with the public clock by fractions too small for fear and too large for comfort.

"Hands visible," Om said, not loudly.

The clerks froze.

Bayo did not. She moved both hands away from her keys, then spoke to the console without touching it. "Open drift-state preservation, chamber-wide. Capture without correction. Label every time source by origin, not authority."

Her screen answered in a color Ira had never seen on a court terminal: a dull iron gray, the shade systems used when no ranking source had yet been allowed to dominate the record.

DRIFT STATE PRESERVATION

MASTER STATION CLOCK: HOLDING

BENCH CLOCK: MINUS THREE POINT ONE SECONDS

EVIDENCE WALL: PLUS FIVE POINT FOUR SECONDS

PRESS DECK: FREEZE-RESTART EVENT

PERSONAL DEVICES: QUARANTINED, UNTRUSTED, RETAINED

The old discipline in the room fought the order. Ira could feel it in the way bodies tilted toward correction, as if unsynchronized time were a smell. In any ordinary hearing, the master station clock would have consumed the lesser clocks, rewritten the displays, and left behind a clean administrative note saying that drift had been reconciled. No one would call it destruction. The system would call it maintenance.

Vela's voice came from two speakers at once, out of phase by less than a breath. "Do not permit reconciliation. The disagreement is the evidence."

Kett turned on that as if it were weakness. "Broken clocks are not testimony."

"No," Pellin said before Ira could answer. His voice surprised him; it had the hoarse force of a man hearing an old order in a new room. "But the choice of which clock gets to repair the others is power. Until they are normalized, nobody owns the time."

The sentence changed the chamber more than the flicker had. It made the drift visible as an argument instead of a malfunction.

Marr looked from Pellin to Bayo. "Can you preserve the pre-correction impulse?"

"Already copying the refusal log," Bayo said. "If the station tried to heal itself before the warning arrived, we keep the wound."

Om's face tightened at the word, but he did not correct her. "Preserve the wound, then. Preserve the attempted cure. Preserve the authority that ordered the cure if authority can be named."

The iron-gray table widened across the evidence wall. Rows multiplied: bench, wall, press deck, advocate slates, routeholder terminals, public gallery caption feed, security pulse counter, atmospheric alarm relay. Some sources gave numbers. Some gave failure. One gave a blank so complete it looked intentional.

Ira watched the blank longer than the numbers. The Cinder record had been full of blanks that behaved like absence until pressure made them look like choices.

At the far side of the room, a junior technician whispered, "It's just a clock."

SERA-THIRD answered from the dark field before any human could:

JUST IS OFTEN WHERE BODIES ARE HIDDEN.

Station technicians would later admit that the automatic correction command had already launched. It failed before touching anything. A pulse moved through the listening spine and made the correction routine mark itself as unsafe.

That was when Oru-Kel arrived.

Not as image. Not as voice. As rhythm.

The tribunal chamber pressure shifted in intervals too precise to be ventilation and too embodied to be data. Three low pulses. A long absence. Two narrow pulses. A pressure fold that made Ira's ears ache. The wall displays tried to render it as waveform, then as hazard telemetry, then as acoustic math, then gave up and showed a warning field:

NONSTANDARD CARRIED SIGNAL

SOURCE AUTHORITY: SHOALS COMPACT WARNING CHAIN

ORISON MARKER: ORU-KEL-UNDER-QUIET

HANDLING LIMIT: DO NOT CONVERT WARNING INTO ADVISORY CONTEXT

Kett stood. "Magistrate, routeholder parties object to alien signal intrusion during active proceedings."

The rhythm pulsed again.

SERA-THIRD, still logged from the oath hearing though not active in testimony, appeared on the black field:

THE ROOM OBJECTS BADLY.

Om looked as if he might laugh and punish himself for it. "Objection noted and temporarily meaningless."

Marr had already opened the emergency evidence protocol. "Is this the carried warning from the Shoals Compact?"

Vela answered, "Yes. Or a continuation of it. Oru-Kel warned that Pale Arc rhythms had extra-Shoals relevance. We routed the Cinder inquiry packet into the warning chain after the timing discrepancy emerged."

"You did what?" Kett asked.

"Preserved relation," Vela said. "Try not to faint."

Pellin cut across them. "Archive Specialist, is the signal safe?"

"For bodies, likely. For assumptions, no."

The pressure rhythm repeated. This time the evidence wall accepted a partial rendering. Not translation. Rendering. A dark curve crossed the official Cinder timing line, intersecting Clock D and then reaching backward toward the M-14 waveform. At the intersection points, the wall marked small absences.

Ira leaned closer.

"Those are not data gaps," she said.

Vela's relay went silent.

Bayo whispered, "Then what are they?"

The answer came as text, slow and awkward, translated by the Shoals Compact carrier:

WARNING RELATION IDENTIFIES FALSE EMPTINESS.

SPACE MARKED EMPTY IN HUMAN NAVIGATION RECORD CONTAINS BOUNDARY RESPONSE.

CLOCKS BUILT FOR TRAFFIC DO NOT COUNT THE RESPONSE.

Oru-Kel did not speak English. The carried warning made English bear him badly.

Om said, "Clarify relation to Cinder record."

The pressure fold deepened. Half the chamber winced. Sable's old listening dishes outside the hull adjusted by fractions, as if the station itself were turning its head.

The wall displayed Cinder Gate telemetry.

Official navigation record:

17:43:31 - hostile alarm received.

17:44:02 - gate corridor turbulence detected.

17:44:19 - military command narrows transit window.

Then Oru-Kel's rhythm overlaid a second pattern:

17:41:48 - boundary response begins.

17:42:03 - local audio begins.

17:42:38 - correction pressure rises.

17:43:31 - hostile alarm received.

17:44:02 - human instruments name turbulence.

The chamber became very quiet.

Marr said, "Boundary response is not a recognized navigation event."

The warning translated:

YES.

It was the most devastating agreement Ira had heard all week.

Kett recovered first. "If the event is not recognized by human instruments, routeholder parties object to treating it as evidence of anything except alien interpretive overlay."

Vela said, "The M-14 waveform had a pre-alarm correction resonance. The carried warning independently marks a pre-alarm boundary response. That does not prove motive, but it destroys the assumption that the corridor was stable until hostile alarm."

"It introduces an alien pattern after the fact," Kett snapped.

Oru-Kel's rhythm struck the room hard enough to make the oath gloves slide half an inch on the witness table.

The translation field displayed:

AFTER FACT IS HUMAN COMFORT. WARNING ARRIVES WHEN RELATION CAN CARRY IT.

Pell, seated behind Marr, looked up sharply.

Ira saw him mouth a word.

No.

Not denial. Recognition.

Marr saw it too. "Mr. Pell?"

He stood without asking her permission, eyes fixed on the wall. "We had a secondary clock."

The room shifted toward him.

Om said, "Explain."

Pell swallowed. "Gate command used a correction clock for corridor stress, separate from public evacuation sequence. It was not called clock in the record. It was called pulse tolerance."

Vela turned toward the wall. "Where is it?"

Pell shook his head. "I don't know. It was command-side. Logistics received only thresholds."

Marr's face had gone very still. "Mr. Pell, do not speculate."

"I am not."

Kett said, "This is beyond the scope of the warning."

Oru-Kel's rhythm pulsed once.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

SECOND CLOCK.

Everyone stopped.

SERA-LEGAL:

TERM APPEARS IN POSTWAR COMMENTARY LAYER.

SERA-ARCHIVE:

NOT SAFE BEFORE. SAFER NOW.

Ira felt the room tilt into Chapter 20 before Chapter 19 had finished arriving.

Om asked, "Sera, can you provide the term without source trauma?"

SERA-ARCHIVE:

YES.

The black field displayed:

SECOND CLOCK WAS GATE PULSE.

The Orison pressure rhythm folded around the words.

For once, human and nonhuman warning did not agree by becoming the same. They agreed by pressing on the same false absence from different sides.

Pell sat down slowly.

Marr whispered to him. He shook his head.

Kett objected to everything: the warning, the Sera line, Pell's spontaneous statement, the display overlay, the pressure effect, the fact that the chamber clocks had not been reset. His objections stacked until Om held up one hand.

"Enough. The tribunal will not decide evidentiary weight during signal reception."

"Then what is happening?" Kett demanded.

Pellin answered. "Preservation."

Vela was already building a seal package. "Do not normalize the clocks. Preserve all drift states. Snapshot wall renderings. Record pressure sensor logs. Hash Sera's line separately. Pull gate command archives for pulse tolerance, correction clock, and secondary timing fields."

Bayo typed so fast Ira worried for his hands.

Om issued orders in parallel:

All chamber clocks frozen for forensic comparison.

Station automatic correction logs preserved.

Shoals Compact warning chain attached under handling limits.

Oru-Kel's carried warning treated as standing condition, not advisory context.

No party to characterize alien signal as proof, myth, metaphor, or malfunction pending review.

That last line pleased no one. Good.

The rhythm softened.

A new text appeared:

ORU-KEL-UNDER-QUIET CARRIES WARNING, NOT ANSWER.

HUMAN GATE RECORD NAMED EMPTY WHAT WAS NOT EMPTY.

THE CINDER SEQUENCE STEPPED ON RESPONSE BEFORE IT NAMED DANGER.

DO NOT MAKE WARNING INTO YOUR PROPERTY.

Then, after a pause:

DO NOT IGNORE BECAUSE IT IS NOT YOURS.

The pressure released.

Lights returned to ordinary tribunal white, which suddenly looked crude.

The bench clock remained wrong by three seconds.

The evidence wall remained wrong by five.

No one corrected them.

Ira realized she was holding her breath and let it out carefully.

Om adjourned for emergency technical review. This time no one objected. Even Kett seemed to understand that argument needed the dignity of not being obviously useless.

As the chamber emptied, Pell remained seated.

Ira approached before she had decided to.

Marr watched her with warning in her eyes. Ira stopped at the boundary of counsel space.

"Mr. Pell," she said. "Pulse tolerance."

He looked at her.

"Who received thresholds?"

"Logistics, gate command, routeholder navigation support, military repair carrier."

"Did risk models include them?"

"Some."

"Did the unbonded dependent removal happen before or after pulse tolerance changed?"

His face closed.

"I don't know."

"Do you suspect?"

Marr stood. "Do not answer."

Pell did not. But his hands closed around empty air as if remembering the water cup.

Ira returned to Evidence Review.

Bayo had already found three deprecated search terms:

PULSE TOLERANCE

CORRIDOR STRESS CLOCK

GATE PULSE SECONDARY

All three returned sealed command-side records.

All three required authority nobody in the room currently had.

Vela appeared on relay, eyes bright with dread. "Now we know where the next door is."

"Can we open it?"

"Not without burning something."

Ira looked at the frozen clocks, each wrong in its own way.

"Then we preserve the burn pattern first."

Vela nodded. "You have been listening."

Outside the chamber, Sable Array's old dishes continued to turn by microscopic degrees, following a warning that had arrived late only by human clocks.

For the first time since the tribunal opened, Ira wondered whether Cinder Gate had not failed in time.

Perhaps human time had failed around it.

Chapter 20: The Second Clock Was a Gate Pulse

Vela did not sleep after the emergency adjournment. Ira knew this because the first message arrived before station night had lowered the public light, the second arrived while the tribunal decks were still cycling spectators out through slow pressure doors, and the third arrived with no greeting at all.

FOUND THE CLOCK.

The words sat alone on Ira's tablet for seven seconds before the attachment unlocked.

Marr had confiscated the counsel suite's outer network twice since adjournment. The first time she had done it as discipline. The second time she had done it because the Array's public relays had begun repeating damaged fragments of the carried warning as if they were devotional music. "I do not want citizens learning law from a machine that arrived inside a wound," she had said.

Ira had not answered. She was learning law from worse things.

The attachment opened into a technical diagram that looked at first like a repair schedule. Meridian Gate stress bands moved across the screen in pale arcs. Beneath them sat three clocks. The public evacuation clock was labeled in blue. The boundary alert clock was labeled in amber. The third clock had no civic label, only a service code.

MCP-CORR-2.

Meridian Correction Pulse, secondary.

Ira stood from the fold-down desk. Her spine complained before her mind did. She had been bent over evidence for too long, hunting terms that had been designed to look harmless when read by tired people.

"Bayo," she called.

He was asleep in a chair with one boot still on the floor. At the sound of his name he opened his eyes without transition, as if sleep had been a legal fiction.

"What broke?"

"Vela found the second clock."

That brought him upright.

Marr emerged from the inner room, jacket unfastened, hair tied back without ceremony. "Do not open anything that requires sealed authority."

"It was delivered under tribunal preservation channel."

"That is not an answer."

"No," Ira said. "It is worse. It is admissible."

Marr crossed the room and took the tablet. Ira let her read in silence.

The diagram was not a plan to move people. It was a plan to move tolerances. The public clock had told stations when evacuation lanes would open, when routeholders could attach, when military carriers would surrender priority, when civilians could cross the legal boundary from local necessity into Compact claim.

The second clock had measured something else.

Gate stress.

Correction pressure.

The permissible violence done to a corridor before the corridor stopped pretending to be a road.

Marr reached the service code and went still.

"You recognize it," Ira said.

"I recognize the family."

"From tribunal discovery?"

"From after the war." Marr handed the tablet back carefully. "Cinder Gate was not the last gate to use correction pulses."

Bayo rubbed both hands over his face. "That would have been useful to mention yesterday."

"Yesterday I did not know the secondary clock was present in the civilian casualty record."

"Was it supposed to be?"

"No." Marr looked at Ira. "Which is why you will now become very unpopular with everyone who understands the diagram."

Ira opened Vela's full packet.

The first layer contained timestamp comparisons. Public evacuation notices at Cinder Nine showed one sequence. Boundary sensors showed another. Command side pulse logs, reconstructed from drift residue, showed a third. None of the clocks disagreed randomly. Each mismatch bent toward the same conclusion.

The gate had been corrected before the public emergency had been named.

Not repaired. Not stabilized. Corrected.

Someone had fired invisible pressure into the meridian corridor while civilians still believed they were waiting in an ordinary queue.

Vela's notes were clipped, which meant she was afraid.

The correction burst begins at 17:41:48 local, plus or minus four seconds.

The first civilian alarm enters station circulation at 17:43:31.

The first public evacuation lane update references an already degraded corridor geometry.

Therefore command knew corridor geometry was degraded before civilians were legally informed.

Ira read the last sentence three times.

The room seemed to narrow around it.

Every case needed a shape. At the beginning Ira had thought this one was shaped like abandonment: people with ships left people without ships to die. Then it had become concealment: officials renamed people until their deaths became administrative weather. Then it had become inheritance: the living defendants lived inside benefits built from that weather.

Now it was something colder.

The dead had not merely been left behind.

They may have been used as cover while command fought the gate.

"Can this be wrong?" Marr asked.

Ira appreciated the question because it was not hope disguised as analysis. It was the first duty of law: find the weakness before the powerful did.

"Yes," she said. "If the pulse residue was caused by boundary response, not command action. If Sera's drift line contaminated Vela's reconstruction. If the carried warning changed the reading by entering late. If the original clocks were synchronized badly enough to make sequence meaningless."

"Were they?"

"No."

Bayo leaned over her shoulder. "You have the confidence of a person about to ruin our evening."

Ira moved to the second layer.

It contained an old maintenance convention. Public clocks could drift by two seconds before correction. Boundary clocks by one. Command pulse clocks by one-fifth of a second.

The command clock had been the most accurate instrument at Cinder Gate.

It had also been the least public.

Bayo opened a blank validation grid and began assigning custody questions to each clock as if he were sorting witnesses before dawn. "Public evacuation clock. Source visible to civilians, altered by station broadcast authority, preserved in partial caption logs. Boundary alert clock. Source visible to station safety, altered by corridor sensors, preserved in alarm residue and emergency wall captures."

He paused at the third column.

"Command pulse clock," Ira said.

"Source not visible to civilians," Bayo entered. "Altered by gate command or whoever held correction authority. Preserved where?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Marr took the stylus from him and wrote four places in a hand so controlled it looked printed: maintenance buffer, carrier nav support, routeholder attach telemetry, casualty scheduling interface.

Ira stared at the last entry. "Casualty scheduling had the pulse clock?"

"Not the clock," Marr said. "A derivative. A tolerance flag. Something that told civil logistics when movement would make the correction harder."

"That is the clock," Bayo said.

Marr looked at him.

He did not retreat. "If a hidden instrument changes what civil logistics tells bodies to do, it is present in the civil record whether command names it or not."

Ira felt the sentence lock into place beside the drift table from the chamber. A clock did not need a face to govern people. It only needed to decide which instructions reached them, and which instructions arrived too late to matter.

She copied Bayo's grid into her notes and added a fifth custody question: who was allowed to know the derivative was derived?

Vela's packet had anticipated the question. A small appendix unfolded beneath the diagram, not large enough to be a formal disclosure and not small enough to be accidental. It listed three user classes cleared to receive MCP-CORR-2 tolerances during a cascade-risk event:

GATE COMMAND DUTY OFFICERS

MILITARY CARRIER NAVIGATION LIAISONS

CONTRACTED ROUTEHOLDER STABILITY SUPPORT

Bayo read the list once. "Civil evacuation officers are not on it."

"No," Ira said.

"Station medical?"

"No."

"Dependent housing wardens?"

"No."

Marr's mouth moved as if she were counting old procedures against new facts. "They may have received simplified advisories."

"Simplified from what?" Ira asked.

That was another kind of silence. Not ignorance. Legal silence. The kind made when everyone in the room understood that the missing word was more dangerous than the spoken ones around it.

Ira wrote the question anyway.

SIMPLIFIED FROM WHAT SOURCE?

The grid accepted the line and marked it unresolved.

For a moment, the counsel suite became as quiet as the tribunal had been when the clocks froze. Ira imagined Cinder Gate's public boards telling families to wait, routeholder attach systems receiving private tolerances, carrier liaisons adjusting burn windows, and someone somewhere watching a more accurate clock while people with less accurate clocks obeyed instructions that made sense only if they were meant to keep certain bodies still.

She forced the thought back into evidence.

"We need to prove the derivative reached logistics before the dormitory removals stalled," she said.

"And before anyone claims the advisory was generic crowd control," Bayo added.

Marr returned the stylus. "Then do not call it a hidden clock in argument yet. Call it a hidden dependency."

Ira wrote that down too, hating how useful the phrase was.

Vela had highlighted a phrase from a wartime operations manual:

Pulse timing shall supersede civil schedule where corridor integrity presents existential cascade risk.

Bayo made a small sound.

Marr said, "That is not criminal by itself."

"No."

"A gate collapse kills everyone."

"Yes."

"If command fired a correction burst early to prevent collapse, that may be necessity."

"Yes."

Marr's voice hardened. "Do not build a moral conclusion where the evidence only supports sequence."

Ira looked up. "I am not."

"You are thinking it."

"Thinking is still allowed in counsel suite."

"Barely."

Bayo pointed at the screen. "Sequence is enough for tomorrow. If they knew the corridor was degraded before the civilian alarm, every classification after that becomes suspect."

"Unless classification was part of the correction protocol," Marr said.

Ira turned back to the packet.

The third layer answered her.

Vela had found a routeholder navigation support memo attached to a salvage settlement file from twelve years after the war. It had survived because someone had filed it under insurance exposure instead of military operations.

The memo was ugly in the way harmless language could be ugly.

During MCP-CORR-2 events, mass instability is exacerbated by unscheduled claimant entry. Priority stability improves when dependent claimants remain locally grouped until corridor pressure releases.

Bayo read it once and swore softly.

Marr did not swear. That was how Ira knew it was bad.

"Dependent claimants," Ira said.

"Children, unbonded partners, station patients, laborers without transit claims," Bayo said. "Everyone who needed someone else to be legally real."

"Remain locally grouped."

"Left where they were."

The suite's lights dimmed one degree as station night completed its artificial descent. Outside the narrow window, Sable Array turned around its ruined star with bureaucratic patience. Antennas tracked signals older than most bodies in the room. Ira saw their reflection layered over the diagram until arcs and metal ribs became one shape.

Marr took the tablet again. "We need Vela live."

"She is on Shoals relay."

"Can Shoals certify the reconstruction?"

"They can certify the carried warning. They will not certify interpretation."

"Then we need command-side authentication."

"From whom?"

Marr's mouth tightened.

That was the problem. Every authority clean enough to authenticate the record had either been built after the war on compromised foundations or had inherited custody from institutions with an interest in failure. Cinder Gate had scattered blame across time. It had made every later seal look official and contaminated at once.

Bayo said, "We can ask Lael Shun."

"Lael is not here yet," Marr said.

"Then we ask Om for interim preservation authority."

"Om will ask whether we are about to accuse gate command of using civilian delay as a pressure-management tool."

Ira saved Vela's packet into tribunal working memory and into the off-array mirror they were not supposed to use unless evidence loss was imminent. She no longer trusted definitions of imminent.

"We are not accusing yet," she said. "We are asking why the most accurate clock in the disaster did not belong to the people scheduled to die by it."

No one answered.

The next message from Vela arrived while they were still standing.

DO NOT LET THEM FRAME THIS AS ONLY EARLY KNOWLEDGE.

Another attachment unlocked.

This one was smaller. A single table. Four columns: station group, correction pressure, advised movement, projected loss.

Cinder Nine.

Cinder Twelve.

Outer maintenance ring.

Unbonded dependent dormitories.

The table did not list names. It did not list crimes. It did not even list deaths directly. It listed how much movement each population would add to corridor instability if allowed into the gate path during the correction pulse.

Projected loss was written as a range.

Ira's eyes found the dormitories before she could stop them.

High if moved.

Severe if retained.

No advised movement.

The words had the cleanliness of a knife washed immediately after use.

Bayo stepped away from the desk.

Marr read the row once. "Do we know who created this?"

"Not yet."

"Do we know if command saw it?"

"Not yet."

"Do we know if it controlled action?"

"Not yet."
