Each answer was correct. Each answer was inadequate. Law could live with inadequate answers for a long time. People could not.

Ira closed the table and opened the chain data. The command clock pulsed in tiny marks, each one almost invisible until Vela's overlay turned them red. At 17:41:48, the first correction burst. At 17:42:38, pressure rose. At 17:43:31, public alarm. At 17:44:02, human instruments named turbulence.

The same order again and again.

The same mercy delayed until it became management.

"We need to bring this into open session," Bayo said.

"If we do, Sable Array will lock the chamber," Marr said.

"They cannot."

"They can call it crowd risk. They can say the carried warning destabilized public order. They can say dependent families will riot if they hear a table valued their motion against corridor survival."

"Would they be wrong?"

Marr looked at him. "No. That is why it will work."

Ira remembered Kett's face when the warning entered the room, not frightened exactly, but offended that reality had arrived without clearance. She remembered Pell's hands closing around an absent cup. She remembered Sera's three mouths making one sentence from broken continuity.

SECOND CLOCK WAS GATE PULSE.

The witness had not understood everything. Perhaps no witness did. But she had carried the simplest fact through damage and time: there had been another clock, and it had governed the one everyone else could see.

Ira composed a preservation motion.

She did not accuse. Marr stood beside her and made sure of it, removing adjectives, cutting heat from verbs, forcing the document into the plain cruelty of sequence.

The tribunal is asked to preserve and disclose all records relating to MCP-CORR-2, corridor pulse tolerance, dependent claimant movement advisories, routeholder navigation support, and casualty projection tables connected to Cinder Gate between 17:40 and 17:46 local disaster time.

Bayo added the exhibit references.

Marr added a narrow request for clock synchronization experts.

Ira added one sentence at the end and waited for Marr to object.

The public evacuation schedule cannot be understood without the command pulse schedule that preceded it.

Marr read it.

"Keep it," she said.

They filed at 03:18 station night.

Om's response came at 03:24.

Emergency review at first light. No public release pending argument. All parties preserve relevant records. No deletions, migrations, compression, or privilege redesignation.

Bayo stared at the order. "Privilege redesignation?"

Marr almost smiled. "He has seen institutions panic before."

At 03:31, a routeholder consortium filed an objection before officially receiving the motion.

At 03:34, Sable Array security requested permission to restrict public access to the chamber.

At 03:39, Pell sent one message to Ira through counsel relay.

You are close to the wrong door.

Marr read it over her shoulder. "Do not answer."

Ira did not.

She forwarded it into evidence preservation instead.

Morning on Sable Array was a lighting decision, not a sunrise. The tribunal chamber brightened by degrees until tired faces became official again. Spectators gathered outside the sealed doors in murmuring bands. Some held printed names. Some held nothing. Nothing was harder to look at.

Inside, the bench clock had been corrected overnight.

The evidence wall had been corrected too.

The witness clock remained five seconds wrong.

Ira noticed before anyone mentioned it.

Om entered, saw the clocks, and did not ask for correction.

"This tribunal reconvenes under emergency preservation review," he said. "Counsel, we are not trying the entire war before breakfast."

"No, Magistrate," Ira said.

"We are determining whether a hidden operational schedule is necessary to understand the schedule by which people lived or died."

"Yes, Magistrate."

Kett rose for the routeholders. His face had recovered its polish. That made him more dangerous.

"The motion is inflammatory speculation built from late-arriving Shoals material, damaged witness output, and technical diagrams counsel is not qualified to interpret."

Om looked at Ira. "Are you qualified to interpret meridian correction pulses?"

"No."

Kett spread his hands.

Ira continued, "That is why I am asking for preservation and expert disclosure instead of verdict."

The small sound from the public gallery was not applause. It was recognition.

Kett heard it too.

"The requested categories are overbroad," he said. "They include routeholder navigation material, military repair protocols, insurance exposure, and civil claimant movement. Disclosure would expose protected systems unrelated to the casualty classification issue."

Om's gaze sharpened. "Unrelated?"

Kett paused for half a heartbeat too long.

Ira marked it.

Marr marked it.

Bayo marked it with an actual note.

Kett recovered. "Not sufficiently related for emergency public litigation."

"This is not public release," Ira said. "It is preservation."

"Preservation today becomes insinuation tomorrow."

"Deletion today becomes history forever."

Om lifted one hand. "Enough."

The chamber settled.

Om turned to the evidence wall. "Display only the timestamp comparison, no casualty table."

The wall obeyed.

Three clocks appeared.

Blue, amber, red.

Public evacuation.

Boundary alert.

Command pulse.

The red line began first.

For one suspended moment, no one spoke. The whole room watched time betray its official story.

Om leaned forward.

"Counsel for routeholders," he said quietly, "if the red clock is authentic, your unrelated argument is dead."

Kett did not answer.

Pell, seated behind him, closed his eyes.

Ira did not feel victory. She felt the scale of the next question arrive.

If the second clock had governed the first, then the tribunal had not yet asked who abandoned Cinder Gate.

It had only asked who admitted it.

The real question was who had touched the clock before anyone was allowed to run.

Chapter 21: Triage or Selection

Om did not grant the motion immediately.

That frightened Ira more than denial would have.

Denial had clean edges. It could be appealed, attacked, named as cowardice or error. Delay had procedural weather. It filled the chamber without looking like a decision. It let every party pretend the tribunal was still breathing while oxygen moved toward those who already owned tanks.

"The tribunal will hear limited argument on whether MCP-CORR-2 records are relevant to casualty classification," Om said. "Not liability. Not guilt. Not moral history. Relevance."

Kett rose too quickly.

Marr touched Ira's wrist under the table.

Wait.

Ira waited.

Kett adjusted his cuffs, buying the half-second he needed to recover polish. "Magistrate, if this tribunal permits counsel to convert emergency gatekeeping into a general assault on wartime command decisions, no operational archive in the Meridian will remain usable. Correction pulses are ugly instruments. They exist because physics does not pause for public consent."

"Noted," Om said.

"The routeholders did not design military correction doctrine."

"Also noted."

"And they should not be punished because a junior advocate has discovered that evacuation, like every disaster response, involved sequence."

Ira felt the word enter the room.

Sequence.

It was harmless until it was not. It was a way to say some bodies had been first and some had been later without admitting hands had moved them.

Om turned to her. "Counsel Senn."

Marr's fingers lifted from Ira's wrist.

Ira stood.

"Triage has sequence," she said. "Selection has sequence. The difference is not the existence of order. The difference is whether the categories were honest."

The gallery shifted.

Kett smiled as if she had stepped where he wanted. "Then counsel admits she asks this tribunal to judge triage with peacetime sentiment."

"No."

"She asks us to imagine a collapsing gate as a courtroom."

"No."

"She asks us to condemn survival."

"No."

Om's eyes moved between them. "Counsel Senn, answer without letting him write your speech."

Ira nodded once.

"Triage tells the truth about constraint. It says there are three carriers, six hundred people, ten minutes, and no mercy in the math. Selection hides choice inside category. It says unbonded dependent, salvage encumbrance, local claimant, nonmobile asset, and asks the living to believe those words were discovered rather than invented."

She let the sentence stand alone because a crowded sentence would give Kett somewhere to hide. Then she turned to the evidence wall.

"Display relevance frame only."

Bayo put it up before anyone could object.

QUESTION ONE: WHAT CONSTRAINT EXISTED?

QUESTION TWO: WHO TRANSLATED CONSTRAINT INTO CATEGORY?

QUESTION THREE: WHAT DID AFFECTED PEOPLE RECEIVE AS EXPLANATION?

Kett said, "Advocacy chart."

"Yes," Ira said. "That is why I am not offering it as proof."

Om's mouth tightened, not with anger. With attention.

Ira continued, "If the answer to question one is corridor collapse, then triage may be necessary. If the answer to question two is civil medical staff sorting by need, that is one kind of record. If the answer is routeholder stability support sorting by legal friction, that is another. If the answer to question three is an honest warning, the tribunal examines necessity. If the answer is a false administrative label, the tribunal examines selection."

"Still argument," Kett said.

"Still relevance," Marr said before Ira could.

Om looked at her.

Marr stood slowly, making it clear she was not taking the floor from Ira but anchoring it. "Magistrate, limited relevance does not require counsel to prove the final moral character of the event. It requires enough connection to justify preservation and disclosure. The moving record already shows three time sources, a hidden command pulse clock, and category changes after pre-alarm correction pressure. The proposed records go exactly to whether the categories were downstream of a safety constraint, a beneficiary preference, or a mixture the tribunal cannot evaluate without seeing the source."

Kett's expression sharpened at beneficiary preference.

Good, Ira thought. There was a bruise there.

Om asked, "Counsel Senn, are you asking me to compel production of protected routeholder navigation systems?"

"No. I am asking the tribunal to preserve records bearing on whether routeholder navigation support received or influenced MCP-CORR-2 tolerances used in casualty classification."

"That sounds adjacent."

"It is adjacent if casualty categories were civil labels only. It is central if civil labels carried route stability instructions."

The public gallery had gone very still. Ira could feel survivors listening for the difference between what happened to them and what they had been told happened to them.

Kett spread one hand. "Every emergency label carries operational consequence."

"Then the tribunal should have no trouble preserving the source of the consequence."

Om lifted his eyes to the three clocks. Blue. Amber. Red.

For the first time, Ira saw the relevance question become visible to him. Not whether the dead deserved outrage. Not whether routeholders were guilty. Not whether wartime necessity had ugly permissions. A narrower thing, and therefore harder to evade: whether a hidden clock had entered public categories without public truth.

Kett's smile thinned.

"The MCP-CORR-2 records matter because they show whether the casualty categories changed after command knew the corridor was under correction pressure. If they did, then the tribunal must ask whether people were classified by need, by danger, or by usefulness to a hidden pulse schedule."

The chamber did not make a sound.

Pell did.

It was small. A breath caught behind teeth.

Ira looked at him.

He sat behind Kett, no longer in the witness chair, no longer protected by its formal violence. His face had the worn neutrality of a man who had survived too many rooms by becoming furniture inside them.

Om noticed the sound. "Mr. Pell."

Kett turned sharply. "Magistrate, Mr. Pell is not under examination."

"He is present as a noticed witness and former gate logistics officer." Om kept his voice mild. "Mr. Pell, do you require medical recess?"

Pell opened his eyes. "No, Magistrate."

"Then sit quietly."

"I can answer relevance."

Kett moved before thought reached his face. "No."

Marr leaned toward Ira. "Do not smile."

Ira had not realized she was close to doing it.

Om folded his hands. "Mr. Pell, if you speak now, you may expose yourself to further examination."

"I understand."

"You may expose institutions you served."

"They exposed themselves when they wrote it down."

That did move the gallery. A ripple, contained by fear.

Kett said, "Magistrate, I object to unsworn narrative."

"Then I will swear him for limited relevance examination." Om looked at Pell. "Do you consent?"

Pell stood. For one second he looked relieved. That made Ira dislike him and pity him at the same time.

"I consent."

The oath took less than a minute. The room changed around it. Public fatigue sharpened into hunger. Sable Array security shifted along the doors. Bayo opened a clean note file. Vela appeared on a side relay, expression unreadable through Shoals compression.

Om gestured to Ira. "Limited."

Marr whispered, "Foundation first. Do not chase confession."

Ira moved to the center floor.

"Mr. Pell, during the Cinder Gate emergency, did gate logistics receive MCP-CORR-2 timing?"

"Yes."

"Was that timing shared with public evacuation coordinators?"

"Not directly."

"Why not?"

"It was not a public instrument."

"That is an administrative answer. I am asking why."

Kett rose. "Argumentative."

"Sustained as phrased," Om said.

Ira adjusted. "What was the stated reason for withholding command pulse timing from public evacuation coordinators?"

Pell looked at the three clocks still displayed on the wall. "Panic. Misuse. Misinterpretation. The manuals said civilian traffic could destabilize correction if people moved outside authorized lanes."

"Were authorized lanes changed after the first correction burst?"

"Yes."

"Were casualty categories changed after the first correction burst?"

Pell's face closed.

"Yes," he said.

Kett sat down slowly, as if sudden motion would make the answer louder.

Ira heard Marr inhale.

"Which categories?" Ira asked.

Pell glanced at Kett.

Om said, "Answer if you know."

"Transit-eligible dependents. Unbonded dependents. Local claimants. Medical nontransfer. Salvage encumbrance. Essential repair labor."

Each term landed with the dull sound of a locked door.

"Were those categories purely descriptive?"

"No."

"What else did they do?"

Kett's chair scraped. "Magistrate."

Om did not look at him. "Mr. Pell?"

Pell swallowed. "They controlled movement."

The witness clock remained wrong by five seconds.

Ira found herself watching it while the answer entered the record. Five seconds did not matter to a person eating breakfast. It mattered to pulse timing. It mattered to a gate. It mattered to a warning that arrived with the instruction not to be converted into advisory context.

"Did they control movement because of capacity alone?" she asked.

Pell did not answer.

"Mr. Pell."

"No."

The word was almost too soft to hear.

Om heard it. "Repeat clearly."

"No."

Ira kept her voice level. "What else did they account for?"

"Corridor stress."

"Meaning the effect of moving certain groups through the gate during correction."

"Yes."

"Certain groups or certain numbers?"

Pell's eyes lifted to her.

There it was: the place where triage became selection.

"Both," he said.

Kett objected. Om overruled. Marr's pen stopped moving. Bayo stared at the wall as if the clocks had become weapons.

Ira did not rush.

"Explain both."

Pell's hands opened on the rail before him. Empty again. "The models treated bodies as mass, route claims as legal friction, escort requirements as delay, medical equipment as load instability, family separation as crowd risk, and unbonded dependents as claimant uncertainty."

"Claimant uncertainty?"

"Who had legal authority to move them. Who could sign for them. Who could compel them. Who could be held responsible if they destabilized a lane."

"Children."

Pell flinched.

Kett said, "Counsel is testifying."

"Sustained," Om said, but his voice had gone colder.

Ira rephrased. "Did the category of unbonded dependent include children?"

"Yes."

"Did it include children whose legal guardians were dead, unreachable, or outside the evacuation corridor?"

"Yes."

"Were those children less likely to be moved under the revised categories?"

Pell closed his eyes.

"Yes."

A woman in the gallery made a sound and covered it with both hands. Security looked toward her. Om looked toward security. Security stopped looking.

Ira let the silence hold for two breaths.

"Was that triage?" she asked.

Marr's head turned slightly.

The question was dangerous. It asked the witness to name law, not fact. But some facts were names people refused to say.

Kett rose. "Objection. Calls for legal conclusion, moral conclusion, historical performance, and whatever else counsel thinks this tribunal exists to indulge."

Om did not answer immediately.

He looked at Pell.

Then at Ira.

"Rephrase," he said.

Ira nodded.

"Mr. Pell, in the operational documents you saw, were the revised categories described as triage?"

"Sometimes."

"Were they ever described as selection?"

"No."

"Were they ever described as containment?"

Pell's face changed.

Not much. Enough.

"Yes."

Bayo's fingers moved.

Marr leaned forward.

Ira felt every person in the chamber notice them noticing.

"Containment of what?" Ira asked.

"Movement."

"Movement of whom?"

"Groups that would increase corridor pressure or disorder."

"Groups like unbonded dependents."

"Yes."

"Groups like medical nontransfer."

"Yes."

"Groups like local claimants without routeholder guarantees."

"Yes."

"Did containment improve survival for others?"

Pell answered quickly. "Yes."

The answer mattered. Ira had to let it matter.

"Did you believe that at the time?"

"Yes."

"Do you believe it now?"

Kett objected.

Om sustained.

Ira stepped back one pace. She could feel the trap opening on both sides. If she made Pell only a monster, the tribunal would reject her simplicity. If she made necessity too large, the dead would disappear inside physics.

She chose the narrow bridge.

"Mr. Pell, did anyone tell the contained groups that their delayed movement was being used to reduce corridor pressure for others?"

"No."

"Did public notices state that category changes were based in part on correction pulse requirements?"

"No."

"Did families receive accurate reasons why dependents were reclassified?"

"No."

"Were any of those omissions necessary to the physics of the gate?"

Pell looked toward Kett again.

Kett looked away.

That was an answer before the answer.

"No," Pell said.

The gallery broke.

Not loudly. Not into riot. It broke the way old hulls broke, stress finding cracks prepared long ago. A sob, a curse, a name repeated, a chair leg against flooring. Sable Array security stepped forward. Om struck the bench once.

"This tribunal will have order."

The room tried.

Ira waited until it did.

Om's voice lowered. "Counsel Senn, you have two more questions."

There were a hundred. There were ten thousand. She had two.

She looked at Pell, and for a moment she saw not the official but the human remnant of someone who had stood near a gate and learned that every answer killed someone.

"Mr. Pell, who had authority to change casualty categories after MCP-CORR-2 began?"

Kett said, "Privilege."

Om said, "Overruled for role identification. Names may be reserved."

Pell gripped the rail. "Gate command, routeholder navigation liaison, military repair carrier coordination, and civil logistics."

"Joint authority?"

"Operationally, yes."

One question left.

Ira could ask whether he had signed. She could ask whether routeholders benefited. She could ask whether the children knew. All of it mattered. None of it would survive the objection storm yet.

She asked the question that opened the next door.

"Where are the containment records kept?"

Kett went pale with anger.

Pell seemed almost grateful.

"Quiet Directorate archive," he said.

The name moved through the chamber before anyone explained it. It did not need explanation for those old enough to remember sealed wartime methods, disappearances filed as security hygiene, emergency powers that had outlived emergencies by changing clothes.

Om's face emptied.

Marr's pen resumed, slower than before.

Vela's relay flickered once.

"Magistrate," Kett said, "that archive is beyond this tribunal's mandate."

Ira turned from Pell to the bench.

"Then the mandate is standing in the wrong room."

Om looked at her for a long time.

"Counsel," he said, "do not mistake a good sentence for authority."

"No, Magistrate."

"But do file the archive request."

Kett protested. Om let him. The protest became part of the record, which was sometimes the only useful thing a protest could become.

Pell stepped down from the witness place and did not return to the routeholder table. For three uncertain seconds he stood between parties with nowhere lawful to sit.

Then he walked to the back wall and remained standing beneath the wrong witness clock.

Ira watched him.

Triage had been the word that let survivors sleep.

Selection was the word that woke the dead.

But containment was worse. Containment meant the dead had not merely been unlucky in the order of rescue. They had been held inside disaster because their movement interfered with someone else's chance to pass through it.

The first clock had told the public to wait.

The second clock had told command why waiting was useful.

Now a third thing had entered the room, older than both: an archive built to keep necessity from ever being tried under its real name.

Chapter 22: Station Mothers of Cinder Nine

The station mothers arrived without ceremony because ceremony had been used against them too many times.

There were four of them in the tribunal record, though only three entered the chamber on their own feet. The fourth appeared by medical relay from a low-gravity hospice two rings inward, her breath counted by a machine that insisted on being heard. Their names were Mara Venn, Tosca Rill, Eme Sar, and Jun Vale-of-Cinder, though Jun had changed her name six times since the war and told the clerk to use the one that would irritate the archive most.

"Vale-of-Cinder is not a registered family form," the clerk said.

Jun's relay image smiled with half her mouth. "Neither was orphan cargo."

The clerk stopped typing.

Om looked over his lenses. "Enter it as stated."

The morning session had been advertised as limited survivor context. Kett objected to the title before the witnesses were seated. "Survivor context" was, in his view, prejudicial, imprecise, and likely to invite testimony outside the scope of technical relevance.

Marr whispered, "He is afraid of old women."

Ira did not look at her. "He should be."

The gallery was full past comfort. Sable Array had opened two overflow rooms and still people stood in the corridors, watching delayed feeds under security observation. Printed names had multiplied overnight. Some were written cleanly. Some were copied from damaged lists with brackets where letters had been lost. A child outside the chamber held a sign that said only DO NOT CALL US LOAD.

Ira had seen it before entering and had needed thirty seconds in a washroom stall with her hands against the wall.

Now she stood in the tribunal center and made her voice ordinary.

"Mara Venn, where were you assigned during the Cinder Gate emergency?"

The first station mother adjusted the collar of a plain gray pressure jacket. She was ninety-one by Compact years, which meant nothing useful. War had made some people ancient at nineteen and unfinished at one hundred.

"Cinder Nine lower nursery and dependent dormitory."

"What was your role?"

"Station mother."

Kett rose. "For the record, that is not a legal office."

Mara looked at him. "That is why children lived long enough for your records to misname them."

The gallery stirred.

Om said, "Counsel, the witness may describe the local role. Legal classification can be addressed separately."

Kett sat.

Ira continued. "What did station mothers do?"

"We kept track of children whose parents worked rotating shifts, children whose guardians were dead, children whose guardians were in pressure sections, children whose names had not caught up to their bodies, children born during transit, and children nobody knew how to claim without starting a fight."

"Did you have authority to move them?"

"In practice, yes. On paper, only if someone important was too busy to object."

"During the emergency, did you receive evacuation instructions?"

"Three sets."

Ira brought the first notice onto the evidence wall. Blue clock. Public schedule.

"This one?"

"Yes."

"What did it instruct?"

"Group dependents by dormitory cluster. Prepare guardian chain. Await lane assignment."

"Was that unusual?"

"No. Stupid, but not unusual."

"Why stupid?"

"Because half the guardians were already outside the section or dead by then."

Kett rose and sat again without speaking.

Ira displayed the second notice. Amber clock. Boundary alert.

"This one?"

Mara's face hardened. "That one came after the hallway shook."

"What changed?"

"It said unbonded dependents required cargo confirmation."

For a moment the chamber lost language.

Om leaned forward. "Witness, repeat the phrase exactly as you remember it."

"Cargo confirmation."

Ira enlarged the notice.

The phrase sat in the middle of a paragraph about route attachment and transferable responsibility. It had the dull bureaucratic authority of something nobody expected to be read aloud by a person who had carried the consequences.

Kett said, "The term referred to manifest handling, not human status."

Mara turned toward him again. "I know what it referred to. They gave us tags."

Bayo sent the tag image to Ira's screen before she asked. Vela had found the scan in a salvage lot record attached to a memorial claim. Ira accepted the exhibit.

The wall showed a narrow strip of pressure-proof polymer. A faded number. A dormitory cluster. A load bracket.

No name.

"Did children wear these?" Ira asked.

"Some did. Some tore them off. Some were too small to understand why we were tying numbers to their wrists."

"Were names recorded elsewhere?"

"We had names."

"Where?"

Mara reached into the flat evidence pouch beside her chair. Security moved first; old habit, new room. Om lifted two fingers and they stopped. The pouch was already sealed under tribunal chain. Bayo checked the tag and nodded once.

Mara removed a strip of fabric folded around a thin memory slate. The fabric had been gray once. Time had turned it the color of old breath. Along one edge, names had been written in different inks, different hands, some overstitched when pressure leaks or children had worn the letters thin.

"Sleeve ledger," Mara said.

Kett stood. "Magistrate, if counsel intends to introduce sentimental objects as records-"

"Sit down," Jun said from the relay.

Om did not look at her. "Counsel Kett, finish only if your objection improves."

Kett changed course. "Authentication and relevance."

Mara unfolded the fabric with hands that had probably carried younger bodies while doors sealed. "Every station mother kept one. Official terminals lost children when their claims changed. Sleeves did not. If a child moved from nursery to repair hammock, the sleeve changed. If a parent died on a work shift, the sleeve changed. If a child got a new name because the old one drew debt collectors or military custody, the sleeve changed. We were not allowed to call it a registry."

"Why not?" Ira asked.

"Because registry meant authority. Authority meant liability. Liability meant someone would come take it."

The old slate flickered when Bayo connected it to the evidence cradle. It did not open cleanly. It had been repaired too many times by people without parts. Lines appeared in broken columns:

A-3 SLEEP ROW

MIRA/MIRO DEPENDING ON WHO ASKS

JUNBUG - BITES - NO MILK POWDER

SEN TWINS, NOT BOTH SEN, ASK MARA

UNNAMED BOY WITH BLUE WRIST STRING, ANSWERS TO TIK

The gallery did not ripple this time. It held still around the names, as if movement might erase them.

Kett said carefully, "These are informal notes."

"Yes," Mara said. "That is why they are accurate."

Ira saw Om write that down.

Marr leaned toward her. "Do not overreach."

Ira knew. The sleeve ledger did not prove the command pulse. It did not prove routeholder benefit. It did not prove the conversion sheet true in every row. It proved something narrower and crueler: when the official system claimed children could not be named, there were women in the room who had named them and been ignored.

"Magistrate," Ira said, "we offer the sleeve ledger only to show local name custody before and during the conversion event, and to support witness recognition of the category change."

"Limited purpose accepted," Om said.

Bayo marked the exhibit so quickly that Ira knew he had been waiting for permission to rescue it from sentiment.

"Did the evacuation system use them?"

"Not after the second notice."

"What did it use?"

"Load groups."

The word moved across the gallery like cold.

Ira turned to the bench. "Magistrate, I ask to display the Cinder Nine dependent conversion sheet."

Kett was already standing. "Objection. Authentication."

"The sheet came from routeholder settlement archives and matches tag sequences recovered from Cinder Nine dormitory debris," Ira said. "We offer for limited purpose of witness recognition and category conversion, not final truth of every entry."

Om looked to Kett.

Kett chose the wrong objection. "Even for limited purpose, it is inflammatory."

"Most evidence in this tribunal is inflammatory because people were burned by it," Om said. "Display with names redacted."

The wall changed.

Rows of children became numbers.

Dormitory A-3: 42.

Dormitory A-4: 37.

Nursery spillover: 18.

Unbonded medical dependent: 9.

Transit guardian unresolved: 64.

Movement advisement: hold.

Cargo equivalent: variable.

Jun Vale-of-Cinder laughed from the relay.

It was not humor. It was a blade.

"There," she said. "That is the line. Variable. We were not children. We were variable."

Om turned gently. "You will have your turn, Ms. Vale-of-Cinder."

"I am taking it from death on installment. Forgive my scheduling."

No one in the chamber knew what to do with that. Ira loved her for it and feared the damage of it.

Mara Venn kept her eyes on the wall. "I remember A-4."

"Tell the tribunal," Ira said.

"Tosca was there. Eme was at lower medical. I had A-3 and A-4. The younger ones had been sleeping in repair hammocks because the nursery seals were weak. When the second notice came, the public speaker said families should remain calm and prepare documentation. But the private line to our station desk said the gate would not accept unresolved guardian chains."

"What did that mean to you?"

"It meant I had forty minutes to invent parents for children whose parents were dead."

Tosca Rill, seated beside her, closed both eyes.

Ira asked, "Did you?"

"Yes."

Kett leaned forward.

Mara did not wait for him. "I lied on thirty-two chains. I made myself aunt, mother, bonded custodian, emergency sponsor, whatever the boxes wanted. Sixteen of those children passed the first inner lock."

"What happened to the others?"

"The lock rejected duplicate authority. Then the correction alarm sounded. Then nobody cared whether the boxes were honest because the lane closed."

Ira let the answer stand.

The tribunal had heard experts describe casualty classes. It had heard Pell describe containment. Now it heard the local translation: a woman at a desk inventing kinship faster than a machine could deny it.

Ira turned to Tosca Rill.

"Ms. Rill, were children counted as cargo at Cinder Nine?"

Tosca was smaller than Mara, with silver hair cut close to the scalp and hands that trembled until she placed them flat on the witness table.

"No," she said.

Kett looked relieved for less than a second.

Tosca continued. "Cargo got better treatment."

The gallery sound rose and fell.

"Explain," Ira said.

"Cargo had owners. Cargo had insured value. Cargo had route priority if it belonged to the right company. Cargo had seals that made people afraid to break them. Children without guardians had local affection and no enforceable claim. When the lane changed, cargo could be rerouted. Children had to be explained."

"Did you see cargo moved before children?"

"Yes."

"What kind?"

"Repair gels. Navigation cores. Two crates of ceramic seedstock. Personal strongboxes from officer housing. A crate marked devotional instruments that sounded like metal when dropped."

Kett objected to speculation on contents. Om sustained only that piece.

Ira asked, "Did any instruction compare children to cargo?"

Tosca nodded. "A supervisor told us to reduce dependent load profile."

"What did you understand that to mean?"

"Carry fewer children."

"Did he say fewer children?"

"No. People like that never spend plain words when ugly ones are available."

Bayo's note file flashed as he marked the line. Ira almost warned him not to admire it. Then she remembered people could still speak well from ruins and that dignity did not weaken evidence.

Eme Sar testified next.

She had been lower medical, responsible for children who could not walk unaided and adults whose bodies required machines. Her voice was quiet enough that Om ordered the chamber amplification raised.

"They gave us a mass budget," she said.

"Who did?" Ira asked.

"Civil logistics relay. It came with a routeholder header but a station authorization code."

"What did it require?"

"Thirty percent reduction in mobile support equipment before lane review."

"Could you comply?"

"Not without killing patients."

"What did you do?"

"I listed the oxygen sleds as cargo necessary for transfer."

"Were they accepted?"

"Two were. The rest were denied because the dependent attached to them lacked guardian confirmation."

"The machine accepted the sled before the child?"

Eme looked at Ira as if the answer should shame the air itself.

"Yes."

Jun's relay image flickered. The hospice machine counted her breath: inhale, pause, release.

Om called a recess. No one moved.

"This tribunal will recess for fifteen minutes," he repeated.

Still no one moved.

Finally Mara Venn stood. Tosca stood with her. Eme rose more slowly. The gallery parted for them as if rank had at last been corrected.

Ira remained at counsel table, afraid that if she sat she would not rise with the right face.

Marr placed a cup of water beside her.

"Drink."

Ira did.

"You are doing well," Bayo said.

Marr cut him a glance. "Do not say that to a lawyer in the middle of witness trauma."

"Right." He looked miserable. "You are proceeding effectively."

"Also bad," Marr said.

Ira almost laughed. It would have been unacceptable. It would also have saved her for three seconds.

Vela's relay opened on Ira's private line.

"The tag sequences match Pell's containment table," Vela said.

Ira closed her eyes. "How closely?"

"Not close. Exact. A-3, A-4, nursery spillover, unresolved guardian. The conversion from names to load groups occurred within eleven minutes after MCP-CORR-2."

"Can you certify?"

"I can certify the match. Not the intent."

"Intent is getting crowded without you."

Vela's face softened and vanished.

When the tribunal resumed, Jun Vale-of-Cinder asked to speak last from the relay. Om permitted it over Kett's objection that remote testimony was medically unreliable.

"Everything about me is medically unreliable," Jun said. "That is why I am still expensive."

Ira approached the relay stand.

"Ms. Vale-of-Cinder, how old were you during the Cinder Gate emergency?"

"Eight."

"Were you in the dependent dormitory?"

"A-4."

"Do you remember receiving a tag?"

"Yes."

"Do you remember what it said?"

"I remember what it did. It made adults stop saying my name."

Ira's prepared questions became useless.

Jun continued without permission. "Before the tag, I was Jun. Mara knew me. Tosca knew me. My brother called me Junbug because he had no taste. After the tag, I was A-4 unresolved, load variable, hold. When I cried, a man told station mother Mara to secure her cargo. She hit him with a ration scanner."

Mara looked straight ahead.

Om's mouth twitched once. "The assault is outside the scope."

"The scanner was light," Jun said. "The insult was heavy."

Even Kett failed to object quickly enough.

Ira asked, "Did you leave Cinder Nine?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"Mara cut my tag off and put me inside a crate of devotional instruments."

Kett stood. "Magistrate, this is theatrical."

Jun turned her failing eyes toward him through the relay. "I was eight in a box while adults outside decided whether I weighed too much. You will survive the theater."

Om said, "Sit down, counsel."

Kett sat.

Ira could feel the chamber leaning toward Jun. That too was dangerous. Sympathy could make law stupid if it was allowed to replace structure. She brought the structure back.

"Ms. Vale-of-Cinder, did the crate have a cargo route claim?"

"Yes."

"Did that claim pass review?"

"Yes."

"Would your dependent claim have passed review?"

"No."

"How do you know?"

"Because my brother's did not."

The room changed again.

No one breathed comfortably.

Ira asked, because the record required cruelty. "What was his name?"

"Sivin Vale. He was six. He had a guardian chain problem because our mother was dead and our father was in hull repair and I was in a box pretending to be bells."

"Did he survive?"

Jun's machine inhaled for her.

Pause.

Release.

"No."

Ira did not say she was sorry. The tribunal was full of sorry. Sorry had no evidentiary value and very little moral one when delivered by strangers after eighty years.

"What do you want this tribunal to understand?" she asked.

Kett objected. Om overruled before the second syllable ended.

Jun looked out from the relay with the calm of someone who had spent a lifetime outlasting rooms.

"Do not call it triage because the gate was cruel. The gate did not ask whether Sivin had a father with the right password. The gate did not print cargo tags. The gate did not know my name and then decide not to use it. People did that. Maybe frightened people. Maybe necessary people. Maybe people who saved others. But people. If you give their choice to physics, you kill my brother twice."

The chamber held the words.

Even the hospice machine seemed quieter.

Om looked down at his hands.

"The testimony is entered," he said.

Kett had no objection left that would not sound like confession.

Ira returned to counsel table. Marr did not speak. Bayo wiped his eyes once with the heel of his hand and pretended he had not.

On the evidence wall, the cargo tag remained enlarged beside the dependent conversion sheet. Number beside category. Category beside clock. Clock beside pulse.

The structure was visible now.

Not complete. Not enough. But visible.

Children had been made administratively lighter by removing the weight of their names.

And once names were gone, the system could carry them or leave them with equal cleanliness.

Chapter 23: A Salvage Debt Comes Due

Tamsin Vale did not enter through the witness door.

She came through the public gallery, which was either courage, contempt, or a navigational error made by someone who had spent too much of her life refusing official routes. Ira saw her before the clerk did: a compact woman in a black salvage coat, hair silver at the temples, hands bare despite the chill of Sable Array's tribunal deck. People recognized her in waves. The recognition was not simple. Some faces tightened with gratitude. Some with hate. Most with the exhausted confusion reserved for those who had survived because someone else had done an unforgivable thing nearby.

Security moved to intercept her.

Tamsin stopped, raised both hands, and said, "If I meant to smuggle something dangerous into this room, you would have thanked me for the box."

Om closed his eyes for one beat.

Marr murmured, "There are days I miss normal witnesses."

Ira looked at the docket. Tamsin was not scheduled until afternoon, after technical authentication. The morning had already torn through children, cargo, containment, and the public's remaining tolerance for euphemism. Bringing a salvage captain into that wound now was bad order.

Which meant it was probably true to the disaster.

Om opened his eyes. "Captain Vale, this tribunal has procedures."

"So did Cinder Gate."

Kett stood. "Magistrate, if unscheduled witnesses may now make entrances from the gallery, the routeholders request equal theatrical privileges."

Tamsin turned her head. "You had eighty years of them."

The gallery reacted before Om could stop it.

He struck the bench once. "Captain Vale, approach through the proper aisle or leave."

Tamsin looked at the aisle reserved for witnesses, then at the families seated beside her. An older man near the front gripped the back of the bench with both hands. A younger woman held a small sealed envelope against her chest as if it had a pulse.

Tamsin chose the witness aisle.

Ira felt the choice cost her.

The oath clerk began the standard form. Tamsin interrupted only once, at the promise to tell the whole truth.

"No one from Cinder Gate has that."

"Do you swear to tell the truth available to you?" Om asked.

The clerk looked horrified.

Tamsin said, "Yes."

Om allowed it.

Ira rose because she was counsel of record for survivor evidence. Marr did not stop her.

"Captain Vale, why did you come early?"

Tamsin looked past Ira to the families in the front rows. "Because they found me before you did."

"Who?"

"People alive because my crew stripped Cinder Nine."

The phrase struck harder than accusation because it lacked ornament.

Ira did not look at the families yet. "Stripped what?"

"Pressure doors. recycler cores, nav fiber, med seals, emergency batteries, nursery hammock frames, copper from prayer speakers, three bulkhead braces from lower A. Parts that made other sections hold long enough to detach."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, this testimony risks conflating salvage operations with casualty classification."

"Overruled for now," Om said. "I want to know whether the parts connect to movement decisions."

Tamsin's mouth twisted. "Everything connected to movement. That was the problem."

Ira asked, "Were you acting under authority?"

"Several."

"Name them."

"Station emergency salvage writ, routeholder transfer order, military repair carrier demand, and one unsigned instruction that paid better than all three."

Bayo's head came up.

Marr wrote one word: unsigned.

"Paid in what?" Ira asked.

"Priority berth for forty-six living people in exchange for immediate release of specific materials."

"Show the instrument," Marr said.

Tamsin's eyes shifted to her. "It is not clean."

"Nothing in this room is."

The salvage captain reached into her coat and removed a narrow slate wrapped in pressure cloth. Security objected with their bodies before their mouths could catch up. Om held up one hand, and the room balanced on it.

"Was that slate disclosed?" he asked.

"To Evidence Review, sealed," Bayo said, already checking the chain. "Filed as Vale supplemental ledger, pending authentication. I did not know she had a duplicate."

"I have the original," Tamsin said. "You have the copy that made me look less criminal."

Om's face became unreadable. "That was not your choice to make."

"It was exactly my choice to make. I am tired of choices vanishing because someone with authority files the copy."

For once, Marr did not correct the witness.

Bayo accepted the slate through a transparent evidence sleeve. The wall displayed only metadata first: time received, relay path, checksum fragments, payment marker, berth assignment bundle. The origin field was blank. Not redacted. Blank.

PRIORITY EXCHANGE NOTICE

RECIPIENT: HALF-MERCY SALVAGE GROUP

DELIVERABLE: LOWER A STRUCTURAL AND MEDICAL MATERIAL RELEASE

BENEFIT: FORTY-SIX BERTHS, CINDER TWELVE TRANSFER STRING

AUTHORITY: FIELD NECESSITY CHANNEL

SIGNATURE: NOT RECORDED

Kett stood slowly. "Magistrate, this is precisely the kind of unauthenticated artifact that contaminates proceedings."

"It contaminated them before it reached us," Ira said.

Om looked at her.

She kept her voice steady. "For limited purpose, Magistrate. Not to prove final authority. To show that the salvage decision was not an isolated act by Captain Vale, and that a specific exchange structure existed before the materials were removed."

Marr added, "And to preserve the original against replacement by a cleaner copy."

The last phrase struck Tamsin harder than any accusation. Ira saw it in her hands.

Om turned back to the slate. "Captain Vale, did this notice cause you to act?"

"Yes."

"Did you believe the berth benefit was real?"

"It was real."

"How do you know?"

Tamsin looked toward Revan Sol. "Because people boarded."

"From where?"

"Sections already classified as hold."

The gallery noise rose.

Om did not strike the bench this time. He let the sound show itself and fade.

"Did hold mean empty?" Ira asked.

Tamsin looked at her as if she had said something childish.

"No."

"Did it mean unrecoverable?"

"No."

"What did it mean?"

"It meant no one with a ship was being told to go there first."

Ira let that answer settle beside the children's tags.

"Who confronted you?"

Tamsin looked to the front row.

The older man stood before anyone called him. "Me."

Om said, "State your name."

"Revan Sol. Cinder Twelve, later Moonwake labor ring, now retired from being useful to anybody."

"Mr. Sol, sit until called."

"I sat eighty years."

Om's jaw tightened.

Marr whispered, "He will lose the room if he crushes him."

Om seemed to know it. "You may stand. Do not testify until asked."

Revan Sol remained standing.

Ira turned back to Tamsin. "Why did Mr. Sol confront you?"

"Because his wife died behind a pressure door my crew removed."

The younger woman in the front row made no sound, but her face folded around the sealed envelope.

Tamsin did not look away from Revan.

"The door did not kill her," she said.

"No," Revan answered from the gallery. "You took the thing that might have kept her air."

Kett rose again. "Magistrate."

Om spoke before he could. "Mr. Sol, you will be removed if you continue outside examination."

Revan nodded once, not in submission but in recognition of the room's shape.

Ira asked Tamsin, "Did you know there were living people behind that door?"

"I knew there might be."

"Did you check?"

"No."

"Why not?"

"Because checking would take six minutes, and the transfer order said the med deck on Cinder Twelve had nineteen minutes of seal integrity if we delivered the door clamp."

"Did it?"

"Yes."

"Did people on Cinder Twelve survive because of it?"

"Yes."

"How many?"

"At least thirty-one."

"Including Mr. Sol?"

Tamsin nodded.

Revan Sol's hands tightened on the bench.

Ira felt the room resist the fact. It wanted Tamsin to be cleanly guilty or cleanly absolved. The fact refused. The clamp taken from one dying section had helped another section live. The same act was theft and rescue. The same hand had removed hope and carried it elsewhere.

That did not make it neutral.

"Captain Vale," Ira said, "were salvage choices guided by casualty categories?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"If a section was move, we supported movement. If a section was hold, we took what could support movement somewhere else."

"Who told you which sections were hold?"

"Civil logistics at first. Then routeholder navigation. Then the unsigned voice."

"Voice?"

"Audio only. Low-band command relay. No name."

Bayo sent a note to Ira's tablet: Quiet Directorate?

Ira did not look at it long enough for anyone else to notice.

"Did the voice use the word containment?" she asked.

Tamsin's eyes narrowed.

"Yes."

The chamber tightened around the word.

"In what context?"

"Containment salvage priority."

"What did that mean?"

"Strip contained sections before pressure loss made parts unusable."

"Were contained sections occupied?"

"Some."

"By whom?"

"People who had lost movement priority."

Kett objected to hearsay, speculation, and relevance. Om sustained hearsay as to individual identities and overruled the rest.

Ira adjusted. "What did you personally observe?"

Tamsin looked down at her bare hands.

"Hands on glass."

No one moved.

"Explain."

"Lower A had a nursery partition with a small observation pane. The outer corridor pressure was bad. We had four minutes in suits. The salvage writ listed hammock frames and copper. I saw hands on the other side of the pane."

"Adult hands?"

"Some."

"Child hands?"

Tamsin did not answer immediately.

"Yes."

Mara Venn, seated in the gallery, covered her mouth.

Ira made herself continue. "Did you open the partition?"

"No."

"Why not?"

"Because if we opened it, we lost the corridor, the frames, the copper, and probably two of my crew. The frames became stretcher braces on Cinder Twelve. The copper kept a pump alive. The children behind the pane were in a section classified hold."

"Did you believe you were following triage?"

"I believed I was following orders that used triage's face."

Marr's pen stopped.

Om leaned back.

Kett said nothing.

Ira asked, "What do you believe now?"

This time Kett objected, and this time Om allowed the question.

Tamsin lifted her head. "I believe I let a classification make my eyes smaller."

The phrase entered the record like a confession and an indictment of everyone who had ever needed a form in order not to see a person.

Revan Sol spoke without permission. "My wife lived three hours after you took the clamp."

Om did not stop him quickly enough.

"Her name was Esta. She was a teacher. She kept nine children quiet while air thinned because she told them rescue hears calm voices better. When the clamp failed, she gave her mask to a boy who died anyway."

The younger woman with the envelope stood too.

"I am that boy's sister," she said.

Security moved. Om raised one hand and they froze.

The woman looked at Tamsin. "My brother's name was Halen. He was seven. My mother told me all my life that he died because no one could reach them. Now I hear someone reached the door well enough to take part of it."

Tamsin accepted the sentence without defense.

Ira's throat hurt.

The woman lifted the envelope. "This is a copy of the berth list from Cinder Twelve. My daughter exists because thirty-one people survived there. My husband descends from one. So what do I do with you?"

No lawyer in the room could answer that.

No law in the room could either.

Tamsin said, "I do not know."

The honesty did not help. It only refused to make the harm theatrical.

Om called the woman forward, swore her under the name Lena Or. Ira expected Kett to object to spontaneous testimony, but he sat as if any objection would make him the only person in the chamber still pretending this could be contained by order.

Lena placed the envelope on the evidence table.

"I want the berth list entered," she said. "Not to save her. Not to damn her. To show the debt."

Ira approached. "What debt?"

"The one families like mine were never allowed to name. We were told our survival was miracle, discipline, good routing, sacrifice. We were not told which wall was taken from whom. We were not told whose oxygen became our corridor. We were given gratitude without accounting."

Tamsin closed her eyes.

Lena looked at her. "I have thanked salvage crews every Founding Day since I was a child. I meant it. I still mean it. And now I want to know whether my thanks has Sivin Vale's blood in it."

Jun's relay remained dark, but the hospice machine's prior rhythm seemed to haunt the room.

Ira said, "The tribunal can enter the berth list and order chain comparison against salvage extraction records."

Kett found his voice. "With appropriate confidentiality."

Lena turned toward him. "No."

Om said, "Ms. Or, confidentiality may be required to protect living families."

"Living families have been protected by ignorance long enough."

Marr leaned close to Ira. "She is right morally. Legally, Om will split it."

He did.

The berth list would be preserved under seal pending redaction protocols. The salvage extraction records would be matched against casualty sections, movement categories, and containment advisories. Tamsin's testimony would remain public except for operational identifiers that could compromise still-active route systems.

No one was satisfied.

That was how Ira knew the order might be honest.

When Tamsin stepped down, she did not return to the witness waiting area. She walked to Revan Sol in the front row.

Security tensed.

Tamsin stopped at arm's length.

"I remember Esta's section," she said.

Revan's face worked.

"Do you remember her?"

"No."

He flinched.

Tamsin did not soften it. "I remember the clamp, the pane, the hands, the suit alarm, the crewman vomiting in his helmet after. I do not remember her because the order did not give me a name."

Revan's eyes filled. He looked older than he had ten seconds before.

"Then learn it now."

"Esta Sol," Tamsin said.

"Again."

"Esta Sol."

"Again."

Om let it happen.

Tamsin said the name a third time.

The chamber did not heal. Nothing so cheap happened.

But a debt changed shape when named aloud. It stopped being weather. It became a thing with weight, transferable no longer without record.

Ira looked at the evidence wall where children's tags, berth lists, salvage writs, and pulse clocks now formed a chain no one had designed but many had used.

The tribunal had begun with a failed gate.

Now it had reached the economy of failure: the way one person's wall became another person's corridor, one child's name became another family's survival, one captain's hands became a civilization's clean story.

Tamsin returned to the witness area at last.

Her salvage coat looked heavier than armor.

Chapter 24: Om Refuses to Recuse

The recusal motion arrived during lunch recess, which meant someone had drafted it before Tamsin finished testifying.

Kett filed first. The routeholders joined within four minutes. The civil logistics descendants joined six minutes after that with a narrower statement and more nervous punctuation. By the time Ira returned from a corridor interview with Lena Or, seven parties had asked Magistrate Om to remove himself from the tribunal.

Bayo met her outside Evidence Review with two cups of machine coffee and a face that said the machine had performed better than the law.

"They found another signature," he said.

Ira took the cup. "Om's?"

"Yes."

"On what?"

"A postwar admissibility memorandum for Cinder Gate salvage claims. Year 2361. He was junior compact assessor then."

Ira stopped walking.

The corridor traffic moved around her, survivors, clerks, security, advocates, technicians carrying cable cases, all of them pretending the building had not become a machine for producing new ways to be implicated.

"Did he disclose it?"

"He disclosed the 2362 ration priority review."

"Not this."

"Not this."

Marr came out of the review room behind him. "Because he may not have remembered."

Ira looked at her.

Marr's expression gave nothing away.

"Do you believe that?" Ira asked.

"I believe eighty years of emergency paperwork is enough to ruin honest memory and protect dishonest memory. I do not yet know which one this is."

They entered Evidence Review.

The memorandum waited on the main display. It was only six pages. That made it worse. Atrocity often hid in vast archives, but it also hid in short documents written by tired people who thought they were deciding procedure.

The signature sat on page six.

Orun Mered Om, Assistant Assessor.

Ira had never known his first name. He had become "Om" in tribunal speech, a bench, a voice, a tired pair of eyes behind old lenses. Seeing the full name made him smaller and more dangerous.

The memorandum concerned admissibility of salvage-derived survival claims where source sections were unavailable for direct casualty verification. Its conclusion was narrow:

Salvage necessity may be recognized without adjudicating the final status of persons remaining in source sections, provided no direct evidence establishes wrongful diversion.

Bayo read it aloud and then regretted doing so.

"That is a door," he said.

"It became one," Marr said.

Ira scrolled to the annotations. Someone older and higher than Om had written beside the conclusion:

Useful. Preserves claims without reopening war command.

Om's initials appeared beneath a clerical correction two lines later.

Not authorship. Not innocence.

Law loved that middle ground because it could stand there forever.

"If Kett wins recusal," Bayo said, "who replaces him?"

Marr opened the tribunal charter. "In theory, the Meridian Remnant Council appoints a reserve magistrate."

"In practice?"

"Two are dead. One sits on a routeholder pension board. One signed the Moonwake demilitarization immunity schedule. One is under medical seal. One refuses travel through Sable Array after the first recess riot."

"So no one clean."

"No one obviously cleaner."

Bayo copied the reserve list into a conflict grid because grief had made everyone allergic to adjectives. Names entered the left column. Offices entered the second. Known Cinder contact entered the third. Unknown Cinder contact received its own column, because absence had become too useful to leave unmarked.

RESERVE MAGISTRATE ONE: DECEASED

RESERVE MAGISTRATE TWO: ROUTEHOLDER PENSION BOARD

RESERVE MAGISTRATE THREE: MOONWAKE IMMUNITY SCHEDULE

RESERVE MAGISTRATE FOUR: MEDICAL SEAL, COGNITIVE ACCESS RESTRICTED

RESERVE MAGISTRATE FIVE: SABLE ARRAY TRAVEL REFUSAL AFTER PUBLIC ORDER INCIDENT

"This is not a list," Ira said.

"It is a trap with names," Bayo answered.

Marr leaned over the grid and added another column: appointing institution.

The screen became worse.

Every available path ran through the Meridian Remnant Council, the same council that had treated Cinder closure as settled legitimacy for decades. The reserve system had been designed for illness, death, ordinary bias, and political inconvenience. It had not been designed for a tribunal where the event being judged had helped build the offices that would appoint the substitute.

Ira pointed to the routeholder pension board entry. "If Om recuses and this one replaces him, Kett gets a cleaner signature and a dirtier dependency."

"Careful," Marr said. "Say that in court and he will ask whether you prefer visible guilt to presumed impartiality."

"I do."

"That is why I said careful."

Bayo added a footer before anyone stopped him:

NO REPLACEMENT CURRENTLY IDENTIFIED WITH LESSER DOCUMENTED CINDER ENTANGLEMENT AND GREATER PRACTICAL AVAILABILITY.

Marr read it twice. "Change lesser to lower. Courts trust lower more than lesser."

Bayo changed it.

Ira stared at the grid until the categories began to look like casualty classes. Available. Compromised. Unavailable. Sealed. Refuses travel. Even judges became movement problems when institutions controlled the lanes.

Ira stared at the signature.

She had wanted Om to be clean. She had not realized how badly until the want became evidence against her judgment.

"We cannot defend him because we need him," she said.

"No," Marr said. "We can defend jurisdiction if removal destroys the tribunal and replacement only changes the flavor of contamination."

"That sounds like corruption with better grammar."

"Most institutional survival does."

The afternoon session opened under security restrictions Om had not ordered personally. That detail mattered because Kett tried to use it anyway.

Om entered without robes.

The chamber noticed before the clerk did.

He wore a plain dark suit, no tribunal sash, no bench seal at the collar. For a moment he looked like an old man who had wandered into the place where his younger self had left a debt.

He did not sit.

"Before argument on the recusal motions," he said, "I will make a disclosure."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, the moving parties request that disclosure occur before an alternate authority."

"Denied."

"You cannot rule on the procedure of your own removal."

"I can maintain order long enough to state facts."

Kett remained standing. "The appearance of conflict is already fatal."

Om looked at him. "Counsel, if appearance were fatal, this tribunal would have died at empanelment."

That silenced even the gallery.

Om turned to the record wall. "Display my 2361 memorandum."

The wall obeyed.

His old signature enlarged behind him.

He did not turn away from it.

"I served as assistant assessor in the postwar salvage claims office from 2359 to 2363. My disclosed prior involvement included ration priority review, routeholder damage limitation disputes, and administrative certification of incomplete casualty ledgers. The memorandum now filed by routeholder counsel bears my signature. I did not disclose it because I did not recall it."

Kett made a sound.

Om lifted one hand. "Do not mistake that for defense."

Kett closed his mouth.

"The memorandum permitted salvage survival claims to proceed without final adjudication of persons remaining in source sections, absent direct evidence of wrongful diversion. That conclusion may have contributed to later institutional reluctance to investigate whether diversion was wrongful. I do not know if it did. I do know that the logic is now before this tribunal."

Ira watched the room listen harder than it wanted to.

Om continued. "I have reviewed the motion. I have reviewed the charter. I have reviewed the list of available replacement magistrates. There is no untainted authority waiting outside this chamber. There are only authorities with different archives."

Kett said, "That is precisely why you must remove yourself. The tribunal cannot proceed under a magistrate whose own work shaped the disputed silence."

"Perhaps."

The single word unsettled everyone.

Om sat at last.

"Counsel Senn," he said, "your position."

Ira stood slowly. There were answers that would help her case and answers that would help the tribunal. They were not necessarily the same.

"Magistrate, your prior memorandum creates a real conflict."

Marr's head turned sharply.

Kett looked almost pleased.

Ira kept going. "It is not merely appearance. The tribunal is examining whether salvage survival claims were preserved at the cost of adjudicating people left in source sections. Your 2361 memorandum sits inside that question."

Om nodded once.

"Therefore," Ira said, "if a competent, less conflicted magistrate existed, recusal would be required."

Kett's pleasure became alarm. He understood the hinge.

"But the moving parties have not identified one," Ira said. "The charter's reserve list is compromised, unavailable, or institutionally dependent on the same postwar settlements. Removal without replacement does not purify the tribunal. It suspends it until the implicated institutions can choose which conflict they prefer."

Kett objected. Om let him object and then asked Ira to finish.

"I ask for three safeguards," Ira said. "First, full disclosure of all records bearing your signature or initials related to Cinder Gate, salvage claims, casualty ledgers, containment categories, and routeholder settlements. Second, a standing right for parties to seek review of any ruling directly involving your prior acts. Third, appointment of an independent record auditor chosen by lot from outside the Meridian Remnant Council."

Marr's pen moved fast.

Bayo looked at Ira as if she had walked out over vacuum and found a maintenance rail.

Kett said, "That is invention."

"So is this tribunal," Ira said.

Om's eyes sharpened.

She added, "Magistrate."

The gallery almost breathed.

Kett gave his answer with polished fury. He argued that safeguards could not cure bias, that disclosure after exposure was no disclosure at all, that Om's continued authority would make every ruling suspect. He was not wrong. That was the difficulty. Bad-faith parties often found real flaws because real flaws were useful.

Civil logistics counsel offered a quieter version. She did not trust Om, she said, but feared a replacement selected by surviving route institutions more. The military repair carrier representative said almost nothing except that operational security required "stable adjudicative leadership," which caused three survivors to laugh openly.

Marr did not speak until Om asked whether counsel of record had anything further.

She stood from the table, older than Ira had ever seen her.

"Magistrate, I have argued for recusal in cleaner rooms over smaller conflicts. If this were any ordinary proceeding, I would have filed the motion myself."

Om accepted it without expression.

"This is not ordinary because every source of authority in this tribunal was built after the event by people who survived the event's settlement. The question is not whether you are clean. You are not. The question is whether your conflict can be made visible enough to constrain you, while the conflicts of a replacement would arrive wearing fresh seals."

Kett said, "Visibility is not impartiality."

Marr turned to him. "Neither is concealment."

Om asked Pell whether he wished to be heard because Pell had become a man without a table and still a witness whose testimony had opened the Quiet Directorate door.

Pell stood beneath the wrong clock.

"If Magistrate Om recuses, the routeholders will nominate someone who never signed the salvage memorandum because someone like me made sure the signatures went elsewhere."

Kett said, "Mr. Pell is not counsel."

"No," Pell said. "I am evidence with shoes."

Om rubbed his forehead. "This tribunal is becoming too quotable."

For the first time that day, the room released a small, damaged laugh.

Then Om ruled.

He did it standing.

"The motions for recusal are denied without prejudice."

The words hit the chamber in layers. Denied. Without prejudice. Not absolution. Not final peace.

"The conflict is real," Om said. "My prior memorandum is relevant to the history this tribunal must examine. I will not pretend otherwise. I will not call failure to remember innocence. The record will disclose all responsive documents bearing my name, signature, initials, office code, or assessor seal. An independent record auditor will be selected by lot from qualified non-Meridian jurisdictions. Any party may renew recusal after review of disclosed material or upon rulings directly concerning my prior acts."

Kett rose.

Om raised his voice.

"I am not recusing today because no untainted authority has been identified, because delay benefits institutions more than the dead, and because a visible conflict constrained by record is less dangerous than an invisible conflict installed for comfort."

He sat.

"You may appeal to the Remnant Council. I advise you to include a list of proposed replacements and their conflicts. We will all enjoy the exercise."

Kett sat slowly.

Ira realized her hands were cold.

Om turned to her. "Counsel Senn, file your Quiet Directorate archive request by 19:00. Include the salvage memorandum in the conflict disclosure bundle."

"Yes, Magistrate."

"And counsel?"

"Yes?"

"Never again imply invention as compliment in my courtroom."

"No, Magistrate."

This time the gallery's laugh was smaller but less broken.

Session adjourned early, not from mercy but from structural fatigue. Outside the chamber, argument bloomed in every corridor. Some survivors called Om corrupt. Some called him brave. Some said there was no difference if the record opened. Others said the tribunal had just taught power how to confess and remain seated.

Ira had no answer for them.

She found Om's 2361 memorandum again in Evidence Review and read the conclusion until the words stopped pretending to be neutral.

Salvage necessity may be recognized without adjudicating the final status of persons remaining in source sections.

That was the sentence that had made room for Tamsin's debt to remain unnamed, for Revan Sol's wife to become a missing condition, for Lena Or's gratitude to float free of accounting.

Om had not created the silence alone.

He had signed a plank in its floor.

Now he was standing on it while the tribunal cut downward.

Ira did not know whether that was justice or another form of collapse.

But at 18:42, she began drafting the Quiet Directorate request, because the only thing worse than a compromised judge was a clean record no one had forced open.

Chapter 25: The Quiet Directorate File

At 18:59, Ira filed a request that no system wanted to receive.

The tribunal clerk accepted it because the clerk had no imagination and therefore still trusted procedure. The request passed through three intake seals, two conflict flags, one automatic warning about wartime archives, and a red notice that said QUIET DIRECTORATE MATERIAL MAY CONTAIN LIVING SECURITY METHODS. Ira watched each obstruction appear on the filing screen and wrote the same answer into every justification field.

The material is necessary to determine whether casualty categories were manipulated before and during the Cinder Gate evacuation.

The machine asked for narrower scope.

Ira gave it names.

The filing field rejected the first version because it contained "emotionally descriptive terminology." It suggested replacements from an approved wartime archive taxonomy:

Operational correction sequence.

Civil movement friction.

Population stability concern.

Post-event narrative hygiene.

Bayo read the suggestions over her shoulder and made a sound that was almost a laugh. "Narrative hygiene."

"Do not repeat that where survivors can hear you."

"I was not admiring it."

"The system was."

Ira deleted the suggestions one by one. Each deletion felt less like drafting and more like refusing a small bribe. The machine wanted scope that could pass through old channels without waking them. It wanted file language that sounded like systems asking other systems about system events. It did not want children, cargo tags, hidden clocks, salvage clamps, or a witness who had stood under the wrong clock and said easiest.

Marr came back from the door. "It will process faster if you use archive taxonomy."

"It will process into the wrong thing."

"Yes."

Ira looked up.

Marr's face was tired, not gentle. "I am telling you the cost, not giving you advice."

The machine offered a compromise field: RELATED TECHNICAL TERMS.

Ira used it as a trap. She entered the official terms there, then attached the contested human terms as required anchors. If the archive wanted to translate the request into its own language, it would have to carry the words it had tried to avoid.

RELATED TECHNICAL TERMS: operational correction sequence; civil movement friction; population stability concern; post-event narrative hygiene.

REQUIRED ANCHORS: child-origin testimony; dependent category conversion; salvage debt; public notice delay; witness attribution risk.

The filing screen paused longer after that. For one hopeful second, Ira thought she had confused it.

Then the system asked whether required anchors contained protected identities.

"Yes," she typed.

It asked whether protected identities were necessary.

She looked at the wall where the index would not yet appear, at the clocks frozen from the previous hearing, at Om's old memorandum in the conflict bundle.

"Yes," she typed again.

The machine accepted the yes as if it had no idea what yes cost.

MCP-CORR-2.

Containment salvage priority.

Dependent claimant movement advisories.

Cargo confirmation.

Quiet Directorate archive location for Cinder Gate operational classification.

The machine asked for protective handling.

Ira gave it Om's order.

The machine asked whether the requesting counsel understood that unauthorized disclosure could trigger criminal penalties under postwar security continuity statutes.

Ira typed yes.

Bayo, watching over her shoulder, said, "That is the most legally expensive yes I have ever seen."

"It is still cheaper than no."

Marr stood by the door, speaking to someone on a muted line. Her posture said she was calm. The hand holding the tablet said otherwise.

At 19:04, the request disappeared from active docket.

Bayo leaned closer. "Did it fail?"

"No." Ira refreshed the screen. "It was taken."

"Taken by whom?"

The screen answered before she did.

TRANSFERRED TO QUIET REVIEW CHANNEL.

No docket number.

No clerk stamp.

No appeal path.

Just a small gray mark beside the filing, like a fingerprint left by someone who had wiped the rest of the room clean.

Marr ended her call. "Om's chambers just lost access to the request."

Ira looked up. "He ordered it."

"Apparently the order triggered the channel and the channel outranks him until challenged."

"Can he challenge?"

"Yes."

"Will it matter?"

"That depends on whether the Quiet Directorate is dead, sleeping, or employed under a new name."

Bayo laughed once. Neither of them joined him.

The first response arrived at 19:17.

It was not addressed to Ira. It was addressed to the tribunal itself, which meant every party received it at the same time and no one could pretend ignorance belonged to someone else.

QUIET REVIEW CHANNEL ACKNOWLEDGES PRESERVATION ORDER.

MATERIAL RESPONSIVE IN PART.

DISCLOSURE CONDITIONED ON METHOD PROTECTION.

INITIAL INDEX TO FOLLOW.

Kett objected before the index followed. His objection contained seven grounds, all written as if he had prepared them in childhood and merely been waiting for a useful disaster. Overbreadth. Security. Privilege. Institutional incapacity. Tribunal conflict. Public risk. Insufficient authentication of witness-derived triggers.

Om answered in one line:

Objections preserved. Index first.

At 19:31, the initial index arrived.

Evidence Review's lights dimmed automatically as if the room had been instructed to respect a funeral.

The index contained only file titles, dates, custodial marks, and handling restrictions. No substance. No names beyond office codes. Yet Ira felt the shape of the thing before she read the fourth line.

QD-CG/17-40/REDIRECT-LADDER

QD-CG/17-41/MASS-STABILITY HUMAN FACTORS

QD-CG/17-41/DEPENDENT MOTION SUPPRESSION

QD-CG/17-42/CIVIL NOTICE LANGUAGE

QD-CG/17-42/SALVAGE PRIORITY CONTAINMENT

QD-CG/17-43/CLAIMANT UNCERTAINTY EXPLOITATION

QD-CG/17-44/POST-EVENT MEMORY RISK

QD-CG/17-46/ATTRIBUTION MANAGEMENT

No one spoke.

The room did not need content yet. Titles were enough to make old defenses begin dying.

Marr sat down slowly.

"Post-event memory risk," Bayo said.

Ira read the title again. It looked back at her without shame.

Vela's relay opened on its own authority from Shoals.

"I have the index," she said.

"How?"

"The tribunal channel mirrored it because Om's preservation order included external audit notification."

Marr looked at Ira. "You added that?"

"I copied your safeguard language."

"Good."

Vela's face was pale through compression. "There is a problem."

Bayo gestured helplessly at the index. "Only one?"

"The dates are wrong."

Ira leaned toward the relay. "Wrong how?"

"The file titles use local disaster time, but the custodial creation marks are earlier. Some entries were opened before 17:40."

"Before the first correction burst?"

"Yes."

Marr took the tablet from Ira's hand and magnified the index. "That may be administrative preparation."

"It may," Vela said. "But one file has a creation mark at 16:58."

The time entered the room like a new witness.

Sixteen fifty-eight.

Before the carried warning entered Cinder channels.

Before boundary response.

Before public alarm.

Before the sequence they had spent two days forcing into view.

Ira found the line.

QD-CG/16-58/CASUALTY CLASS PREPARATION.

Handling restriction: sealed method, living lineage, directorate inheritance.

Bayo whispered, "They had the class before the disaster."

Marr said, "Do not say that yet."

"It says that."

"It says a file title and a timestamp. We will not die of discipline now."

Ira appreciated the rebuke. She also understood why Bayo had spoken. Some evidence did not wait for permission to become meaning.

The door to Evidence Review opened.

Pell stood outside with two Sable Array guards behind him.

Marr was on her feet first. "Why is he here?"

One guard said, "Witness Pell requested protective custody transfer to tribunal space."

"On whose authority?"

"Magistrate Om's emergency witness order."

Pell looked older than he had in the chamber. Not frightened. Worse. Decided.

"I saw the index," he said.

Ira did not ask how. Everyone implicated seemed to receive secrets faster than the people appointed to inspect them.

Marr said, "You are represented?"

"Not anymore."

"That is foolish."

"Yes."

Bayo closed the index on the main display. Too late, but correct.

Pell looked at Ira. "The 16:58 file is not preparation for a possible disaster."

Marr stepped between them. "Do not make unsworn statements in counsel workspace."

"Then swear me."

"We are not in session."

"Then record me."

"No," Marr said. "You will not use panic to force counsel into mishandling protected testimony."

Pell's mouth tightened. "That is exactly what we used to say when civilians guessed correctly."
