The sentence stopped her.

Ira took out her tablet and opened a tribunal witness preservation note, not testimony, not examination, but a record of attempted disclosure. "Mr. Pell states he wishes to disclose context concerning QD-CG/16-58/CASUALTY CLASS PREPARATION. Counsel Marr objects to unsworn workspace statement. Counsel Senn requests emergency session."

Marr looked at her, then nodded once.

"Good," she said.

Om convened the emergency session at 20:06 with no public gallery, only party counsel, the witness, the record auditor's empty chair, and a sealed transcript channel that displayed a warning every thirty seconds.

The warning said:

METHOD-SENSITIVE MATERIAL. DO NOT REPEAT OUTSIDE AUTHORIZED REVIEW.

Everyone read it once and then began pretending not to see it.

Pell took the oath for the third time in two days.

Om's robes had returned, but their authority looked thinner after the recusal hearing. Or perhaps Ira was only seeing the seams.

"Mr. Pell," Om said, "you requested protective transfer. Why?"

"Because the index means the Directorate file survived, and if it survived, so did the names of people who made it."

Kett, appearing through remote counsel wall, said, "Magistrate, routeholder parties object to any testimony about file content before protective review."

"Sustained in part," Om said. "Mr. Pell may describe his knowledge of the file's existence and general purpose without revealing protected method."

Pell smiled faintly. "That distinction would have pleased them."

"It does not please me," Om said. "It constrains you."

Pell nodded.

Ira stood. "Mr. Pell, did you know of QD-CG/16-58/CASUALTY CLASS PREPARATION during the Cinder Gate emergency?"

"Not by that file name."

"Did you know of a casualty class preparation process before 17:40?"

"Yes."

Marr's pen moved once and stopped.

"Who initiated it?"

Kett objected.

Om said, "Role, not names."

Pell looked at the sealed warning, then at the wrong witness clock, which had been brought into the closed session as if error had become evidence in its own right.

"Quiet Directorate liaison embedded in gate command."

"What was the stated purpose?"

"To prepare civil language and movement categories in case correction pressure required selective delay."

"Before correction pressure existed?"

"Before it was visible to most instruments."

"Was a correction event expected?"

Pell did not answer quickly enough.

Ira felt the room lean.

"It was modeled," he said.

"Modeled as likely?"

"Modeled as possible under hostile boundary stress."

"Did command expect hostile boundary stress?"

Kett objected on security grounds. Om sustained.

Ira changed angle. "Did the 16:58 process include categories later used at Cinder Nine?"

"Yes."

"Unbonded dependents?"

"Yes."

"Cargo confirmation?"

"The phrase was in language options."

"Containment salvage priority?"

"Not at 16:58. That came after correction pressure began."

The answer was careful. Careful answers could be more useful than dramatic ones because they showed where the witness refused to exaggerate.

"What did the 16:58 file do?" Ira asked.

Kett objected again. Om looked at Pell before ruling.

Pell said, "It prepared a method for making future delay appear like ordinary classification."

The sealed transcript warning flashed.

METHOD-SENSITIVE MATERIAL. DO NOT REPEAT OUTSIDE AUTHORIZED REVIEW.

Ira understood then why the method might still be alive. Not because Cinder Gate's physics remained secret. Because the administrative technique did.

Make a choice before the public knows there is a choice.

Build categories that look descriptive.

Let frightened operators apply them under pressure.

Afterward, call the result triage.

No algorithm needed to survive for the method to live. It lived in forms, in timing, in who received clocks, in who was given plain words and who was given labels.

Om's face had gone very still.

"Mr. Pell," he said, "were casualty classes prepared before civilian danger was announced?"

"Yes."

"Were they prepared because command knew specific civilians would die?"

"No."

Kett exhaled too loudly.

Pell continued. "They were prepared because command knew that if the gate became unstable, certain civilians would be administratively easiest to delay."

No one exhaled after that.

Marr closed her eyes for one second.

Ira looked down at her notes and saw that her hand had written:

EASIEST TO DELAY.

Not most dangerous.

Not impossible to move.

Easiest.

"How were those civilians identified?" Ira asked.

Kett's objection was immediate. "Method."

Om sustained. "Counsel Senn, not in this session."

"Magistrate, the existence of the method is already in testimony."

"The existence, not the operational details."

"If the method remains protected, the tribunal cannot determine whether present systems still use it."

Om's eyes met hers. "I know."

That answer frightened her more than resistance.

He turned to the remote wall. "Counsel Kett, routeholder parties will preserve all category design tools, claimant uncertainty scoring instruments, emergency language libraries, and civil movement delay templates derived from or resembling Quiet Directorate methods."

Kett said, "That is impossibly broad."

"Yes," Om said. "It is. Preserve them anyway."

Civil logistics counsel began to speak, stopped, and asked for five minutes to confer.

"Denied," Om said. "You may confer after preserving."

The military repair carrier representative asked whether obsolete wartime methods embedded in current emergency manuals counted.

Om looked at Pell.

Pell looked at Ira.

Ira answered because the record needed someone to say it plainly.

"Yes."

Om said, "Yes."

The sealed warning flashed again. It seemed less like a caution now and more like a confession by machine.

METHOD-SENSITIVE MATERIAL. DO NOT REPEAT OUTSIDE AUTHORIZED REVIEW.

At 21:12, the closed session adjourned under expanded preservation order. No public release. No content disclosure. No method description. But the index itself remained part of the tribunal record, and the fact of responsive Quiet Directorate material could not be made to disappear without everyone seeing the hand move.

Pell was taken to a protected witness compartment.

Before the guards led him out, Ira asked one final question under Om's permission.

"Why come to us?"

Pell looked back at the sealed wall.

"Because the file title is not the worst one."

"What is?"

Kett objected, but too late for the shape of fear to remain hidden.

Om allowed no answer.

Pell gave one anyway, not as content, not as method, only as a name.

"Attribution Management."

Then the guards took him.

Evidence Review felt colder after the session. Bayo stood at the main display and did not touch it. Marr filed preservation supplements with a precision that looked almost violent.

Ira reopened the index and read the titles again.

The Quiet Directorate file had not answered the tribunal's question.

It had changed the tense.

Until now, Cinder Gate had been a disaster whose aftermath was concealed.

Now it might be a disaster whose concealment had arrived early.

Chapter 26: Evidence With Teeth

The file itself arrived at 02:13.

Not the whole file. Nothing in Cinder Gate ever arrived whole. The Quiet Review Channel released a protected extract under seven locks, three redactions, and one instruction that required the tribunal's independent auditor to certify she had not been born, employed, educated, subsidized, medically treated, transported, or politically sheltered by any Meridian successor institution.

The auditor's name was Sel Inoya. She appeared on relay from the Tharsis Treaty Court with gray hair cut in a severe line and the expression of someone who had made a career from disappointing powerful people in language they could not easily appeal.

"I certify independence for purposes of provisional review," she said. "I reserve the right to become more annoyed as facts develop."

Om looked tired enough to accept that as formal.

The closed review room held fewer people than the prior emergency session. Om. Auditor Inoya. Ira and Marr. Kett for routeholders. Civil logistics counsel, whose name Ira had finally learned was Nemi Aral. A military repair carrier advocate. A sealed technical witness officer. Bayo, admitted as evidence coordinator after Marr argued that excluding the only person who knew where half the records were would be a triumph of purity over function.

Pell was not present.

His protected compartment had gone dark at midnight under witness rest protocol. Ira had checked the seal twice and hated herself both times for wanting him awake.

The file opened on the wall as a set of pages whose margins carried scars. Redaction bars covered names, method details, route identifiers, and something labeled "present-day derivative risk." Enough remained to hurt.

QD-CG/16-58/CASUALTY CLASS PREPARATION

Contingency Language and Movement Delay Architecture

Quiet Directorate Liaison Cell, Cinder Gate Event Shadow Desk

Kett objected to the phrase "event shadow desk" before anyone read further.

Inoya looked at him. "On what ground?"

"Prejudicial terminology."

"It is their terminology."

"It implies improper pre-event activity."

"It records pre-event activity."

Kett's mouth closed.

Inoya turned to Om. "I recommend reading before fear edits grammar."

Om nodded. "Proceed."

The first page was an intake summary. Most of it concerned hostile boundary stress, route instability, public order, and civil language management. Ira's eyes moved past jargon by discipline, not because it failed to matter but because every clause wanted her attention and she had learned that documents used complexity the way smoke used wind.

Then she found the sentence.

If correction intervention becomes necessary, pre-designated delay classes should be activated before civil notice to prevent self-directed claimant surge.

Bayo stopped breathing loudly enough for Ira to hear it.

Marr whispered, "There."

Evidence with teeth did not shout. It bit and held.

Om asked the record to mark the sentence.

Kett objected. "Context required."

"Granted," Om said. "We will read the context."

That was worse for Kett.

The next paragraph explained that civil populations could not be told correction pressure was expected or underway because unmanaged movement would increase corridor instability. It recommended preparing "delay classes" using existing administrative categories: guardianship uncertainty, local-only claimant status, medical nontransfer, work dependency, salvage encumbrance, and route guarantor absence.

The categories were familiar now.

They had sat on children's wrists.

They had sorted oxygen sleds from patients.

They had told Tamsin which walls could become someone else's corridor.

Ira read the paragraph again and found what made her stomach turn.

The file did not say these groups were impossible to move.

It said they were less likely to generate immediate enforceable challenge if delayed.

Nemi Aral, civil logistics counsel, put one hand over her mouth.

Kett saw it. "Counsel, compose yourself."

She lowered her hand. "Do not instruct me."

Om looked at her. "Counsel Aral?"

Nemi stood as if pulled by a line she did not control. "Magistrate, civil logistics requests permission to enter a limited statement."

Kett said, "This is not the time."

"That is becoming the official slogan of everyone who had time," Nemi said.

Om allowed the statement.

Nemi looked at the file, not at the room. "My office inherited civil logistics seals. We have argued that category changes were field improvisations under collapse. If this document is authentic, that position is materially incomplete."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, counsel cannot concede authenticity."

"I did not," Nemi said. "I conceded that my prior argument is now bleeding."

Auditor Inoya made a note. Ira decided she liked her when Inoya did not smile.

They moved to page two.

The heading was:

Expected Delay Resistance by Civil Class.

Redactions covered scoring methods, but the table's visible columns remained.

Class.

Immediate challenge risk.

Movement complexity.

Public sympathy risk.

Recommended language.

Unbonded dependents: low immediate challenge, high sympathy risk if individualized, recommended language: guardian chain unresolved.

Medical nontransfer: medium challenge, high sympathy risk, recommended language: equipment stabilization pending.

Local claimants without route guarantee: low challenge, medium sympathy risk, recommended language: lane assignment pending.

Essential repair labor: high challenge, low sympathy risk if framed as duty, recommended language: station integrity requirement.

Salvage encumbrance: low challenge, low sympathy risk, recommended language: asset recovery priority.

Ira's hands curled beneath the table.

Marr saw and placed her own hand flat on the surface, visible, controlled. A reminder. Anger had to become questions or it would become noise.

Om's voice was rough. "Auditor Inoya, provisional authentication?"

"Custodial marks match the index. Hash chain intact from Quiet Review release. Internal formatting consistent with Directorate material I have seen in unrelated war-method cases. That is not final authentication, but it is strong enough for preservation and controlled adversarial review."

"Counsel Kett?"

Kett stood slowly. He had the look of a man choosing which wall to abandon.

"The table is horrifying," he said.

No one expected that.

He continued, "It is also not proof that routeholders adopted or implemented this method. The Directorate may have prepared language. Gate command may have ignored it. Civil logistics may have refused it. Field conditions may have converged independently on similar categories because those categories existed before and were obvious in emergency."

It was the best argument available.

Ira hated that it was not stupid.

Om turned to her. "Counsel Senn."

She stood.

"Counsel Kett is correct that the file is not by itself proof of implementation by every party. It is proof that before the public emergency, planners expected a casualty class defined not by danger alone but by challenge resistance. The tribunal now has a duty to compare this file to later notices, tags, salvage writs, and testimony."

"And if they match?" Om asked.

"Then field improvisation becomes a story told after paperwork."

Kett objected to rhetoric. Om sustained it, but the sentence remained in everyone's notes.

Auditor Inoya advanced to page three.

This page had more redaction than text. Its heading survived:

Public Sympathy Containment.

The visible material advised that individual names should be minimized in early notices for delayed classes because "identity-specific appeals generate route disruption." It recommended cluster numbers, guardian chain status, and equipment identifiers as substitutes.

Mara Venn had testified that after the tag adults stopped saying Jun's name.

The method had known why.

Bayo sent a note to Ira's screen:

Cargo tags are no longer just consequence. They are implementation evidence.

Ira nodded once.

Marr added:

Need sequence chart: 16:58 file -> 17:41 pulse -> 17:42 category language -> 17:43 public alarm -> tag conversion -> salvage priority.

Ira copied it into the case spine.

Page four contained the first unredacted reference to attribution.

Post-event attribution should emphasize physics, hostile action, and local queue failure. Avoid language implying predesignation of delayed classes. Where individual accounts conflict with civil notice sequence, preserve accounts as trauma-fragmented unless corroborated by command instruments.

Om said nothing for so long that even Inoya looked up.

Ira thought of Sera Venn-3, three strands of one damaged witness trying to speak without betraying memory. She thought of the court nearly forcing her into coherence because law preferred a single throat.

Trauma-fragmented.

They had prepared to use injury as discredit before the injured spoke.

Marr's voice was very quiet. "Magistrate, the witness protection protocols for Sera must be revised before further examination."

"Yes," Om said.

Kett objected automatically, then seemed to hear himself and stopped.

Inoya advanced to the final visible page.

It was a routing sheet.

Most names were redacted. Roles were not.

Quiet Directorate liaison.

Gate command legal.

Civil logistics language desk.

Routeholder navigation advisory.

Military repair carrier coordination.

Salvage liaison reserve.

The roles matched Pell's testimony.

Not perfectly. Worse than perfectly. They matched with the small variations that made fabrication less likely.

Om asked each party whether they disputed role relevance.

Kett disputed implementation.

Nemi did not dispute relevance.

The military advocate disputed scope and requested a classified annex.

Marr asked that all derivative current manuals be frozen.

Ira asked that Sera's prior testimony be treated as potentially corroborated rather than merely damaged.

Om granted preservation, denied public release, ordered a comparative sequence chart, expanded Sera's protections, and required every institution represented in the room to identify whether it possessed any file, manual, template, training note, or automated decision tool containing equivalent categories or recommended language.

The order did not remain language for long.

Preservation receipts began appearing on the side wall before Om finished dictating conditions. Civil logistics first: two emergency movement manuals, one deprecated claimant-screening library, eight language templates marked inactive, three marked current. Nemi Aral read the list with the expression of a person watching the floor disclose a staircase underneath her own office.

Routeholder counsel filed under protest and produced nothing for thirty-eight seconds.

Auditor Inoya looked at the empty column. "Counsel Kett."

"We are determining whether responsive materials exist."

"No," Inoya said. "You are determining whether to admit they exist before the system admits it for you."

The routeholder column populated.

NAVIGATION SUPPORT DECISION AIDS: 4

CLAIMANT UNCERTAINTY SCORING REFERENCES: 11

EMERGENCY LANGUAGE LIBRARIES: 6

HISTORICAL TRAINING EXEMPLARS: 19

CURRENT USE STATUS: DISPUTED

Ira felt the room tilt. Current use status disputed was not a denial. It was a door someone had not closed quickly enough.

The military repair carrier representative submitted a narrower list and immediately attached a classified annex request. Om accepted the annex request for handling, not for delay. The distinction made the advocate's jaw tighten.

Bayo whispered, "The order has teeth."

Marr answered without moving her lips much. "Teeth can still bite the wrong record."

She was right. The preservation wall was seductive. Numbers looked like proof because they were neat. Four decision aids. Eleven references. Six libraries. Nineteen exemplars. But a count did not say who used a tool, when, on whom, or whether the tool had inherited method or merely inherited vocabulary. Ira made herself write that down before wanting became conclusion.

Auditor Inoya requested an external mirror for the receipts.

Kett objected. "The tribunal has not established external custody necessity."

Inoya looked at the preservation wall. "The necessity is arriving in columns."

Then Auditor Inoya added the sentence that made the file's teeth sink deeper.

"I also recommend immediate preservation outside this tribunal."

Om looked at her.

"Why?"

"Because the handling restriction includes 'living lineage.' In my court, that phrase has meant the method was inherited into later administrative systems."

Marr's face changed.

"Later than Cinder Gate?" Ira asked.

"Yes."

"How much later?"

Inoya glanced at the redactions. "I cannot answer from the extract. But the restriction would be unusual if the method died with the war."

Ira thought of Mars predictive labor records. Children of the Red Algorithm Echo lay ahead in the outline of a case she had not yet reached, but the shape was already visible: categories migrating under new names, old casualty logic finding peacetime work.

Kett understood the implication too. "This tribunal has no mandate over current systems."

"It has mandate over evidence of Cinder Gate," Ira said. "If current systems are derivative, they are chain evidence."

"That theory has no limiting principle."

"Neither did the method."

Om lifted a hand before the argument could ignite.

"The tribunal will not try every successor system today. It will preserve lineage evidence sufficient to determine whether Cinder Gate methods were inherited. Scope to be argued after production of the comparative sequence chart."

Kett asked for stay pending appeal.

Om denied it.

Kett asked for stay pending security review.

Inoya said, "Security review has begun. It is me."

Kett looked at her as if she had personally insulted his ancestors.

"Your independence is provisional," he said.

"So is your confidence," she answered.

Om did not quite smile.

At 04:02, the closed review ended.

Ira walked out into a corridor where station night had forgotten to become morning. Sable Array's lights held at maintenance dim. Cleaners moved around clusters of sleeping survivors with the tenderness of people trying not to disturb evidence. Someone had taped DO NOT CALL US LOAD to the wall beside the chamber doors. Security had not removed it.

Bayo carried the sequence chart assignment like a physical burden.

"We have them," he said.

Marr stopped walking.

"No."

He blinked.

"We have a file," she said. "We have a method. We have role matches. We have teeth. We do not have them. Do you understand the difference?"

Bayo looked down. "Yes."

Her voice softened by one degree. "Good. Because if you say that in the wrong room, Kett will make the whole case about your certainty instead of their categories."

Ira leaned against the corridor wall. The metal was cold through her jacket.

"He is not wrong about one thing," she said.

Marr waited.

"The file does not prove every hand that used it."

"No."

"It proves someone sharpened the knife before anyone admitted there would be cutting."

Marr's eyes moved to the taped sign.

"Then we find the hands."

At 04:19, Ira sent Vela the protected portions she was allowed to see and a summary of the portions she was not. Vela answered three minutes later:

SERA MUST NOT BE FORCED INTO LINEAR TESTIMONY.

Ira wrote back:

Om ordered protection revision.

Vela replied:

NOT ENOUGH. THEY PLANNED TO CALL DAMAGE UNRELIABLE BEFORE DAMAGE SPOKE.

Ira stared at the message until the words doubled.

Evidence with teeth could bite the wrong person if handled badly.

The tribunal had a method now. It had a pre-event file. It had proof that categories were not merely desperate labels invented in collapse but tools prepared for the moment collapse made them useful.

But Sera was still the witness whose fractured memory had pointed them there.

If the file proved her right by destroying her, the tribunal would become another machine that used the injured for passage.

Ira closed her tablet.

Morning would bring argument over public release, current systems, routeholder implementation, and whether the tribunal's mandate could survive the evidence it had requested.

Before that, she needed to rewrite her questions for Sera.

Not to make the witness clearer.

To make the court less dangerous.

Chapter 27: Sera Splits Under Question

Ira rewrote the questions until they stopped looking like questions.

By 07:30, her tablet held a list of invitations, anchors, and exits. No demand for sequence unless Sera offered it. No request to choose one memory over another. No pressure to identify what the damaged record could only surround. Vela reviewed the draft from Shoals and removed every word that looked harmless to a lawyer and violent to a fork.

Do not ask "which version is true."

Do not ask "what happened next" unless she supplies a clock.

Do not use "remember" as a command.

Do not call contradiction contradiction until after preservation.

She added a second column because Vela had sent no approval mark after the first.

STOP CONDITIONS:

strand-output acceleration beyond clerk capture

body distress not chosen as testimony

custodial warning from Oriole or Glassward relay

question requires strand priority

question converts refusal into adverse inference

any party uses "version" to mean disposable self

Marr read the new column over her shoulder. "You are giving opposing counsel a map of how to stop your own examination."

"No. I am giving the court a map of when my examination has stopped being examination."

"Kett will use every line."

"Then he will have to say out loud which harm he wants to trigger."

Marr did not answer immediately. She took Ira's tablet, changed "body distress not chosen as testimony" to "medical distress not voluntarily offered as testimony," then handed it back.

"Precision," she said. "If you are going to build a guardrail, make sure no one can call it fog."

Ira accepted the edit. The phrase felt colder and stronger. Law did that sometimes: took a humane impulse, stripped the warmth from it, and made it more likely to survive.

Vela's next message arrived as Ira saved the draft.

ADD EXIT LANGUAGE.

Ira typed:

The witness may stop one strand without stopping all strands.

Vela replied:

AND MAY STOP ALL WITHOUT ADVERSE MEANING.

Ira added it.

Ira accepted every change.

Marr read the final draft and said, "This is not examination."

"No."

"It is a guided landing."

"Yes."

"Kett will call it indulgence."

"He can call it weather if he wants. The file says they planned to use damage against her before she existed in the room."

Marr handed the tablet back. "Then make the court understand the difference between protecting testimony and protecting a conclusion."

The chamber reopened at 09:00 under layered restrictions. No full public release of the Quiet Directorate extract. No method details. The index could be referenced. The existence of a pre-event casualty class preparation file could be stated. The table language remained sealed pending audit. Sera's testimony would occur under revised witness protocol, meaning the tribunal would preserve parallel answers rather than forcing immediate reconciliation.

Kett objected before Sera appeared.

"The protocol gives witness damage privileged status over adversarial testing."

Ira stood. "The prior protocol gave adversarial testing privileged status over witness survival."

Om looked from one to the other. "Neither slogan decides the issue."

Auditor Inoya sat beside the bench, provisional independence made visible by a separate seal on her chair. She had slept, if at all, in a jurisdiction where sleep was considered inefficient. "The question," she said, "is whether fractured output can be preserved in a manner that permits later comparison without forcing collapse at intake."

Kett bowed slightly. "Auditor, with respect, every unreliable witness would prefer that standard."

Inoya's expression did not change. "Every damaged archive would too. Courts learned to handle them because paper cannot be bullied into shame. People require more skill."

Om approved the revised protocol.

Sera Venn-3 entered as three presences and one legal problem.

The primary body came through the witness door under Glassward custodian escort. She looked paler than before, though perhaps Ira was only noticing the cost of having been believed. The archive strand appeared on the evidence wall in a narrow silver frame. The third strand, the one that had laughed at bad objections and named the second clock, flickered through an auxiliary relay that refused to synchronize cleanly.

The clerk began the oath.

Sera-primary said, "I have already promised."

Sera-archive said, "Oath stored."

Sera-third said, "Promise repeats badly under pressure."

The clerk looked at Om.

Om said, "The prior oath remains. Witness protocol acknowledges continuity variance."

Kett rose. "Routeholder parties maintain objection to treating variance as continuity."

"Maintained," Om said.

Ira approached the witness boundary and stopped farther away than before. Vela had marked a line in the floor plan. Too close increased compliance pressure. Too far made abandonment. Ira did not know how Vela knew that. She trusted it anyway.

"Sera," Ira said, "the tribunal has received a Quiet Directorate index. You do not need to interpret it. You do not need to agree with it. I am going to show you only the file titles Om has permitted into open record. If a title contacts a line you carry, tell us how the contact feels before you try to make it chronological."

Kett rose halfway.

Om said, "Sit."

Kett sat.

Ira displayed the first title.

QD-CG/17-41/MASS-STABILITY HUMAN FACTORS.

Sera-primary looked at it and blinked.

Sera-archive produced a low tone.

Sera-third said, "Too many legs in a narrow throat."

Ira wrote the phrase exactly. "Does that attach to a place?"

Sera-primary: "Gate mouth."

Sera-archive: "Lane C. Lane F. Lane not named because naming changed after pressure."

Sera-third: "Children were not legs. They counted them like legs."

Kett's pen moved.

Ira did not chase. "Preserve all three."

The record marked them separately.

She displayed the second title.

QD-CG/17-41/DEPENDENT MOTION SUPPRESSION.

Sera-primary put both hands flat on the witness rail.

Sera-archive said, "Suppression means hold."

Sera-third said, "Suppression means hand over mouth."

Sera-primary whispered, "Mara sang."

The chamber seemed to lean toward that, but Ira kept her voice low.

"Who is Mara in that line?"

"Station mother."

"What did she sing?"

Sera-primary's mouth moved without sound.

Sera-third supplied, "Rescue hears calm voices better."

Jun's testimony returned to the room without being cited.

Kett rose. "Magistrate, the witness is cross-contaminating prior testimony."

Ira closed her eyes for half a second.

Om said, "On what basis?"

"The phrase was heard yesterday from a survivor witness. The witness now repeats it."

Sera-archive said, "Phrase existed before yesterday."

Kett turned. "Can you prove that?"

Sera-third laughed once. "Can you prove yesterday did not steal it?"

"Magistrate."

Om lifted a hand. "Counsel Senn, foundation."

Ira nodded. "Sera, do you carry any record of the phrase before this tribunal?"

Sera-primary shook her head.

Sera-archive said, "Audio fragment. Damaged. Lower A. Timestamp unstable."

Sera-third said, "Not proof. Tooth mark."

"Can you produce the fragment?"

Sera-archive flickered.

Glassward custodian Oriole-of-Archive stepped forward for the first time that morning. "Producing the fragment may destabilize the fork."

Kett said, "Then the answer cannot be tested."

Ira felt the pressure of the old court trying to return: produce or be discounted, cohere or be dismissed, bleed cleanly or be called unreliable.

Om looked at Auditor Inoya.

Inoya said, "Mark as unproduced claimed fragment. Preserve without weight determination."

Kett objected to the phrase "claimed" and to the lack of immediate weight. Om overruled both in opposite directions, which pleased no one.

Ira displayed the third title.

QD-CG/17-42/CIVIL NOTICE LANGUAGE.

Sera-primary said, "The speaker lied politely."

Sera-archive said, "Civil notice did not say correction. Civil notice said assignment. Assignment. Assignment. Await assignment."

Sera-third said, "The second clock had already bitten."

"Do you know that from hearing the notice or from later reconstruction?" Ira asked.

Sera-primary: "Hearing."

Sera-archive: "Reconstruction."

Sera-third: "Both is not a crime."

Kett rose. "It is a problem."

Ira turned to him. "For whom?"

Marr's pen stopped.

Om said, "Counsel."

"Withdrawn."

It was not withdrawn. Not really.

They moved through the next titles slowly. Salvage priority containment produced the image of hands on glass from Sera-primary, a parts list from Sera-archive, and from Sera-third a sentence that made Tamsin lower her head in the witness waiting row:

"They took the wall after deciding the room was already gone."

Claimant uncertainty exploitation produced names that were not names: A-4 unresolved, medical nontransfer nine, local-only cluster Cinder Twelve, repair labor retained. The archive strand offered them as categories. The primary strand flinched at each one. The third strand said, "Names are heavier. They made the gate lighter by dropping them."

Ira preserved all of it.

Then came the title Pell had feared.

QD-CG/17-46/ATTRIBUTION MANAGEMENT.

The chamber changed before Sera spoke.

Kett straightened. Nemi Aral put down her stylus. Auditor Inoya leaned back slightly, not away, but enough to see all three Sera outputs at once.

Ira said, "You do not have to answer if contact harms you."

Sera-primary looked at her with sudden anger.

"Harm is not the problem."

Sera-archive said, "Attribution means where blame is sent."

Sera-third said, "No. Attribution means where blame is buried."

The witness rail lights flickered.

Oriole-of-Archive spoke sharply. "Stability."

Sera-primary turned toward the evidence wall. "They said gate failure."

Sera-archive: "They said hostile boundary stress."

Sera-third: "They said local queue disorder."

Sera-primary: "They said mothers panicked."

Sera-archive: "They said salvage necessity."

Sera-third: "They said trauma makes clocks wrong."

The three voices began overlapping.

Om leaned forward. "Witness protocol slow."

Sera-primary: "They said the children moved wrong."

Sera-archive: "They said the children could not be moved."

Sera-third: "They said the children were never scheduled."

Oriole reached the edge of the witness boundary. "Sera, reduce output."

Sera-primary: "Do not reduce me."

Sera-archive: "Reduction was the method."

Sera-third: "Do not make us smaller to fit the room."

Ira stepped closer despite Vela's floor mark.

"Sera, you can split. The record will hold separate lines."

Kett stood. "Magistrate, counsel is coaching fragmentation."

"Counsel is preventing witness collapse," Marr said.

"Counsel is selecting the form of testimony most advantageous to her theory."

Sera-third snapped, "Theory did not leave me in three pieces. People did."

The relay distorted.

Sera-archive began displaying text faster than the clerk could mark it:

GATE FAILURE ATTRIBUTION

HOSTILE ACTION ATTRIBUTION

LOCAL DISORDER ATTRIBUTION

SALVAGE NECESSITY ATTRIBUTION

TRAUMA DISCOUNT ATTRIBUTION

DEPENDENT NONCOMPLIANCE ATTRIBUTION

PUBLIC ORDER ATTRIBUTION

Then a line appeared in red.

PREPARE BEFORE SURVIVOR CONSOLIDATION.

Inoya stood.

"Preserve screen state."

Bayo was already moving.

Kett shouted an objection that contained no legal ground.

Om struck the bench. "Order."

There was none.

Sera-primary began speaking to someone not in the room.

"I did not lose you. They changed where you were in the story."

Sera-archive emitted a flat tone, then another, each one tagged with a timestamp that did not match the tribunal clock.

Sera-third whispered, "If I choose one account, the others die."

Ira heard Vela's warning in memory.

SERA MUST NOT BE FORCED INTO LINEAR TESTIMONY.

Kett's voice cut through. "This is exactly why the witness cannot be relied upon. The output is incompatible with itself."

Sera-primary looked at him.

For one terrible second she was entirely present.

"So was Cinder Gate."

The chamber went still.

Sera-archive followed: "Incompatibility preserves event complexity."

Sera-third: "He wants one lie because three truths make bad furniture."

Even Om seemed unable to decide whether to sanction the sentence or frame it.

Ira turned to the bench. "Magistrate, the witness is not failing. She is refusing to destroy variance the Directorate planned to exploit."

Kett said, "That is argument."

"Yes," Ira said. "And I will make it after we preserve her."

Oriole-of-Archive was at Sera-primary's side now, not touching, hands open. "Sera, choose anchor."

Sera-primary shook her head. "No."

"Choose body."

"No."

"Choose room."

"No."

Sera-archive text jittered:

CHOICE DEMAND RESEMBLES CONSOLIDATION HARM.

Sera-third said, very clearly, "I will not betray damaged memory for your clean transcript."

That was when the witness rail alarm sounded.

Not loud. A soft medical chime. The kind of sound designed to make crisis polite.

Om ordered recess.

Kett objected to recess while an answer was pending.

Marr turned on him with a fury so cold Ira felt it from three steps away. "There is no answer pending that outranks a witness's mind."

Kett said, "The tribunal cannot become hostage to witness architecture."

Ira almost spoke. Om spoke first.

"The tribunal has been hostage to institutional architecture since this proceeding opened. We will survive accommodating a human one."

Recess entered over objection.

The public gallery did not move. No one seemed to know whether leaving would abandon Sera or admit that the room had become too much to bear.

Ira stayed at the witness boundary until Oriole looked at her.

"Back."

Ira stepped back.

Oriole guided Sera-primary through a grounding sequence that used no command verbs. The archive strand dimmed but did not vanish. The third strand flickered in and out, muttering fragments Ira did not let herself write down because not every wound had to become evidence the instant it bled.

Bayo preserved the screen state.

Inoya certified the preservation chain.

Om remained on the bench, head bowed, hands folded, as if leaving would make the harm administrative.

When Sera stabilized enough to be escorted out, she stopped beside Ira.

All three outputs aligned for one sentence.

"Do not make me simple."

Ira answered, "I won't."

It was a promise she had no right to make alone.

But she made it anyway.

After Sera left, Kett renewed his motion to strike the session as unreliable, prejudicial, and procedurally defective.

Om denied striking. He reserved weight. He ordered all three output lines preserved separately. He ordered expert briefing on fractured witness handling. He ordered the attribution management screen state placed under seal for controlled review.

Then he looked at Ira.

"Counsel, before next examination, submit a harm-limiting protocol with specific stop conditions."

"Yes, Magistrate."

Kett said, "So counsel may continue building a case from contradictions."

Om's eyes moved to him slowly.

"Counsel, this tribunal has spent days hearing from institutions that called contradiction necessity, concealment security, and children cargo. You will forgive me if I no longer assume consistency is the first sign of truth."

Kett sat.

The chamber emptied in stunned increments.

Ira remained long enough to watch the wrong witness clock finally stop.

It froze five seconds behind the bench clock.

Bayo noticed too. "Maintenance?"

Ira looked at the blank place where Sera's archive strand had been.

"No," she said. "A marker."

She did not know whether that was technically true.

But she knew the stopped clock belonged in the record.

Some evidence survived by refusing to catch up.

Chapter 28: Ira Stops the Court

The harm-limiting protocol began as a document and became an argument before Ira finished the first page.

She wrote it in Evidence Review while the stopped witness clock sat under seal on a portable stand beside the table. Bayo had insisted on bringing it down from the chamber before maintenance could repair the embarrassment. The clock's face showed the wrong time with stubborn dignity. Five seconds behind. Frozen. No longer a malfunction anyone could quietly correct.

Marr disliked the clock in the room.

"It is theatrical," she said.

"So was calling children cargo."

"Do not use my own style against me."

"Your style has useful edges."

Marr gave her a look that would have terrified a junior advocate with more sleep. "Edges cut both ways."

Ira returned to the protocol.

Stop conditions:

1. Any witness output indicating consolidation harm.

2. Any conflict between strands that produces medical alert, archive instability, or involuntary prioritization pressure.

3. Any party question requiring the witness to choose a single account where preservation of variance remains possible.

4. Any demand that Sera rank memories by legal usefulness before expert review.

5. Any recurrence of the phrase "clean transcript" in witness distress output.

Bayo read over her shoulder. "That last one is oddly specific."

"So was the harm."

"Kett will say you are making the witness judge the questions."

"No. I am making the court listen when the witness tells us a question is doing damage."

Bayo opened a second page without being asked and titled it TEST WITHOUT COLLAPSE.

"Do not make it cute," Ira said.

"It is not cute. It is a filing strategy with resentment."

The page had three columns. The first held Kett's likely questions in their cleanest form. The second translated each into a form that could be asked without requiring a strand to dominate. The third named what external evidence would test the answer.

KETT FORM: Which account is true?

SAFE FORM: Which strands carry contact with this evidence, and what does each contact claim or refuse?

EXTERNAL TEST: clock drift, tag sequence, audio edge, custody hash.

KETT FORM: Did the child hear this phrase?

SAFE FORM: Does any strand carry source-line audio, later reconstruction, or commentary contact with this phrase?

EXTERNAL TEST: fragment existence, timestamp quality, prior witness match, contamination record.

KETT FORM: Are contradictory outputs unreliable?

SAFE FORM: What condition makes the outputs diverge, and does divergence map to known event complexity?

EXTERNAL TEST: Quiet Directorate attribution method, category shifts, independent notice records.

Marr read the page and did not immediately object. That was practically applause.

"This helps," she said. "It shows you are not evading cross. You are translating it into a form that preserves the thing being tested."

Bayo looked pleased for half a second before remembering the hour.

Ira added one line at the bottom:

No question is barred solely because it is difficult. A question is paused only when its form threatens to manufacture the unreliability it purports to test.

Marr sat across from her. "There is a line."

"I know."

"Do you?"

Ira looked up.

Marr's expression was not accusation. It was worse: concern that had survived experience.

"There is a line between protecting a witness and arranging the shape of truth around what you can bear to ask," Marr said. "You promised Sera you would not make her simple. Do not let that become a promise that you will not challenge her."

Ira's first answer was anger. She did not speak it. The file, the children, Tamsin's debt, Pell's careful shame, Om's signature, Sera's three voices: all of it had taught her that the first answer was often the one trained into you by fear.

"I still have to test her," Ira said.

"Yes."

"But I do not have to break her to do it."

"Correct."

"And if the only test Kett accepts requires breaking her?"

Marr leaned back. "Then you make the court choose in public."

The protocol went to Om at 11:12.

Kett objected at 11:18.

At 12:03, Om reconvened a procedural session that everyone pretended was not the next fight over whether truth had to arrive undamaged to be useful.

Sera was not present. Oriole-of-Archive attended in her place, carrying Glassward authority and visible exhaustion. Vela appeared from Shoals relay, which Kett objected to because Shoals had now become, in his phrasing, "a partisan architecture of witness preservation." Auditor Inoya attended without comment. That was becoming her most threatening form.

Om opened with the protocol.

"Counsel Senn proposes stop conditions for further Sera Venn-3 examination. Counsel Kett opposes. Counsel Marr joins with modifications. Auditor Inoya recommends adoption pending expert review. Glassward custodial authority requests additional strand-specific exit criteria. Shoals archive specialist Cho requests that no one in this room use the word clarity as if it were harmless."

Kett stood. "Magistrate, this is no longer a tribunal. It is becoming a set of rituals designed to protect a favored witness from contradiction."

Ira stood too.

Om raised one finger. "One at a time. Counsel Kett."

Kett paced two steps from his table, just enough motion to suggest reason under pressure. "The witness has produced incompatible accounts, unverified fragments, argumentative statements, and medically interrupted output. Counsel now asks for authority to stop examination whenever the witness resists consolidation. That prevents cross-examination on the precise weakness at issue."

"And your proposal?" Om asked.

"A clean sequence examination. One strand at a time. Questions limited to fact. Witness instructed to answer yes, no, unknown, or unable. Contradictions marked. Medical intervention only upon objective threshold by custodian, not witness language."

Oriole-of-Archive said, "That will harm her."

Kett turned. "Respectfully, custodian, harm is not the same as unreliability being exposed."

Vela's relay crackled. "You are describing exposure as if the witness is hiding. She is holding multiple injuries in parallel because the event arrived that way."

"Ms. Cho is not counsel."

"No," Vela said. "I am one of the people preventing counsel from converting a living archive into a confession device."

Om said, "Enough."

Ira waited for her turn, fingers resting on the protocol. The paper copy mattered. It forced the argument into something that could be touched, annotated, entered, appealed.

"Counsel Senn," Om said.

She stepped to the center.

"Kett is right about one thing. Cross-examination must be real. The tribunal cannot accept Sera's outputs as immune because they are damaged. But cross-examination is not the same thing as forcing a damaged system to fail in the way most convenient for the examiner."

Kett said, "I object to being described as seeking failure."

"Sustained as to motive," Om said. "Continue without mind reading."

Ira nodded. "The proposed routeholder examination requires Sera to choose one strand, one sequence, one answer form, and one definition of contradiction. That does not test her testimony. It changes the witness before testing begins."

"It asks for intelligibility," Kett said.

"It asks for a shape the Quiet Directorate file specifically planned to exploit. Trauma-fragmented. Local disorder. Witness inconsistency. Public sympathy containment. The file shows an attribution method prepared to discredit damaged accounts. If this tribunal now forces Sera into the failure mode that method expected, we do their work under seal and call it procedure."

The chamber did not move.

Om's eyes lowered to the protocol.

Kett said, "The file does not give counsel permission to suspend adversarial process."

"I am not asking to suspend it. I am asking to stop when the process becomes evidence of harm rather than a search for fact."

Auditor Inoya spoke. "That distinction is administrable."

Kett turned toward her. "With respect, how?"

Inoya lifted the protocol. "By marking stop conditions, preserving the unanswered question, preserving the reason for interruption, and permitting later expert-guided reentry. The question is not erased. It is quarantined until the tribunal knows whether asking it destroys what it seeks."

Marr's mouth moved almost imperceptibly. Ira thought she might have said good.

Om asked Oriole for Glassward's position.

"Sera can be questioned," Oriole said. "She cannot be harvested."

Kett exhaled sharply.

Oriole's eyes went to him. "You think that is rhetoric. It is technical. Some forks survive by distributing stress. If forced into priority collapse, they will produce a line that looks clean and is false because the cost of clean output is suppression of surviving branches."

"Then how can any answer be trusted?"

"By preserving the branches and comparing them to external evidence."

"That is not testimony as law recognizes it."

Om said, "Law recognized corporate persons, dead claims, inherited seals, and cargo tags for children. It can stretch."

Kett looked as if he wanted to object to the history of jurisprudence.

The protocol was adopted with modifications at 13:26.

The next examination began at 14:10.

Ira had argued to postpone it. Oriole wanted rest. Vela wanted review. Marr wanted the court to avoid the appearance that Sera's collapse had made her unreachable. Kett wanted immediate cross, which made Ira distrust immediate cross even more. Om split the difference badly, as judges often did when every option injured something: a limited reentry, no routeholder cross yet, only Ira to complete preservation foundation under stop conditions.

Sera returned without the third relay visible.

That absence frightened Ira.

Sera-primary sat at the witness rail. Sera-archive appeared in its narrow frame, dimmer than before. Oriole stood closer than protocol usually allowed. The stopped clock sat on the evidence table, five seconds late forever.

Om addressed the witness gently.

"Sera Venn-3, the tribunal will proceed under revised stop conditions. Do you understand?"

Sera-primary said, "Understand is large."

Sera-archive displayed:

PROTOCOL RECEIVED.

Sera-primary added, "Where is third?"

Oriole answered before anyone else could. "Resting."

"No," Sera-primary said.

The archive frame flickered:

THIRD WITHHELD TO REDUCE ADVERSARIAL TARGET.

Kett stood. "Magistrate, if one strand is withheld, the testimony is curated."

Ira felt the room move toward the old disaster.

Om looked to Oriole.

Oriole's face had gone rigid. "The third strand destabilized after yesterday's session. Glassward custody determined limited presentation was safer."

"Was the tribunal informed?" Om asked.

"No."

That was a problem. Even Ira knew it. Protection done invisibly became another form of control.

Kett seized it. "Magistrate, we now have custodians choosing which version of a witness appears based on strategic risk."

Sera-primary whispered, "Not version."

No one heard except Ira.

Kett continued, "The routeholders move to suspend all Sera testimony until full strand availability or strike it entirely."

Sera-archive displayed:

STRIKE = ERASURE.

Sera-primary said, louder, "Not version."

Ira raised one hand. "Magistrate, stop condition one."

Om looked at the protocol. "Witness output indicating consolidation harm?"

"Yes. Also stop condition three. The court is discussing availability as if a strand is a document withheld for advantage rather than a part of witness architecture under stress."

Kett said, "This is argument, not a stop."

Sera-primary gripped the rail. "Not version."

The archive frame flashed:

VERSION LANGUAGE CAUSES PRIORITY PRESSURE.

The medical rail chimed once.

Soft. Polite. Catastrophic.

Ira heard herself before she decided.

"Stop the examination."

Om lifted his gaze. "Counsel, you have not begun your foundation."

"Then foundation can wait."

Kett said, "Convenient."

Ira turned on him, not with Marr's cold fury but with something sharper and less trained. "No. It is inconvenient. It is terrible for my case. It leaves the strongest witness half-preserved and the worst file half-explained. It gives you an argument I will spend days fighting. I am stopping because the witness is telling us the room is hurting her and the protocol means nothing if it only works when convenient."

The gallery was silent.

Om did not rule.

Ira faced the bench. "Magistrate, I move to halt my own examination of Sera Venn-3 pending full strand-health review, transparent custodial disclosure, expert guidance on language triggers, and a revised plan that does not reward any party for forcing medical alarms."

Kett said, "Routeholders oppose delay."

"Of course you do," Ira said.

"Counsel," Om warned.

She stopped. Barely.

Marr stood beside her. "The motion is joined."

Bayo looked startled, then stood too, though he had no procedural reason to.

Nemi Aral stood from civil logistics. "Civil logistics does not oppose."

The military advocate remained seated.

Auditor Inoya said, "The auditor recommends halt."

Oriole-of-Archive closed her eyes.

Sera-primary loosened one hand from the rail.

Sera-archive displayed:

HALT RECOGNIZED.

Then, after a flicker:

NOT ERASURE.

That decided Om before his ruling did.

"Examination halted," he said.

Kett objected.

"Preserved."

"Magistrate, this creates perverse incentive for any witness architecture to signal harm when examination becomes difficult."

Om leaned forward. "Counsel, if you can produce evidence that Sera Venn-3 is manufacturing medical instability to avoid your questions, file it. If your argument is only that acknowledging harm makes examination harder, I agree and remain unmoved."

Kett sat with the expression of a man storing appeal grounds in neat boxes.

Om ordered the halt terms into the record. Full strand-health review. Custodial disclosure of why third had been withheld. Expert guidance. No adverse inference from the halt until briefing. No further use of "version" language for Sera's strands without expert review. The stopped clock preserved as context marker, not proof of technical fact.

At the last phrase, Bayo looked mildly offended on behalf of the clock.

Sera was escorted out without collapse.

That seemed like victory until Ira remembered victory usually had more motion in it.

In the corridor, Kett caught her before Marr could intercept.

"You know what you just did," he said.

"I stopped hurting a witness."

"You taught every party that your case cannot survive ordinary scrutiny."

"Ordinary scrutiny did not find the Quiet Directorate file. Damaged testimony did."

"Damaged testimony pointed where it was convenient to point."

Ira felt exhaustion try to become cruelty. She held it down.

"You can make that argument in session."

"I will."

"Good."

He stepped closer. "If Sera never returns, your promise to her may cost the dead their case."

Marr arrived then, but did not speak.

Ira answered because Kett had found the fear under everything.

"If the case requires us to destroy a witness in order to honor the dead, then the Quiet Directorate is not as gone as we keep pretending."

Kett's face changed, not much.

He left.

Marr watched him go. "He was not entirely wrong."

"I know."

"That is the worst part of today."

"No," Ira said. "The worst part is that I almost kept going."

Marr said nothing.

They returned to Evidence Review where the stopped clock waited beside the protocol. Bayo had added a label:

Witness clock. Stopped during Sera Venn-3 preservation sequence. Technical cause undetermined.

Ira read it twice.

"Good label," she said.

"No metaphors."

"No metaphors."

Vela called at 17:05.

"You halted."

"Yes."

"Because you had to, or because you were afraid?"

Ira almost lied.

"Both."

Vela's face softened through the relay. "Good."

"Good?"

"Fear tells you where harm is. It should not command you. It should be heard."

Ira looked at the protocol, the clock, the sealed file index, the growing sequence chart, all the apparatus they had built to make truth survivable.

"What if Sera cannot come back?"

"Then you preserve what she gave without spending what remains of her."

"And if that is not enough?"

Vela did not answer quickly.

"Then the tribunal learns whether it wants justice or only usable victims."

After the call, Ira opened the sequence chart again. The chain from 16:58 to tags, salvage, attribution, and Sera's fracture ran across the screen in red and blue. There were gaps. There were too many gaps. Kett would live in them. Routeholders would build houses in them. The Directorate, if anything like it still existed, had designed gaps as habitat.

Ira added one more marker:

14:10, revised Sera examination halted by examining counsel after witness signaled consolidation harm.

Bayo read it over her shoulder.

"That makes us look weak."

"It makes us look accurate."

"Both can be true."

"Yes."

He nodded, accepting the lesson unhappily.

Outside Evidence Review, the tribunal corridors filled with rumor. Some said Sera had broken. Some said Ira had hidden her. Some said Om had lost control. Some said the routeholders had been denied their rights. Some said, quietly, that for the first time the court had stopped before taking what it wanted.

Ira did not know which story would survive.

But that night, when the public feed posted the procedural order, someone in the corridor taped a new sign beneath DO NOT CALL US LOAD.

It read:

DO NOT MAKE US SIMPLE.

Chapter 29: Public Order by Oxygen Ration

By morning, the signs had multiplied into a wall.

DO NOT CALL US LOAD.

DO NOT MAKE US SIMPLE.

ESTA SOL HAD A NAME.

SIVIN VALE WAS SIX.

WHO OWNS THE SECOND CLOCK?

The last one appeared in three different hands, which meant the phrase had already escaped the tribunal and become public property. Sable Array security did not remove the signs. Removing them would have required deciding which paper was protest, which was memorial, and which was evidence. Instead security placed a transparent barrier half a meter in front of the wall and called it preservation.

Ira arrived before formal session and found families sleeping upright in the corridor. Some had ration blankets. Some had brought their own air scrubber masks, old models polished by habit. A child traced the letters on DO NOT MAKE US SIMPLE without touching the barrier. Her mother pulled her hand back anyway.

"Don't give them a reason," the mother whispered.

Ira heard it and hated how quickly everyone understood who them meant.

Bayo met her at the chamber door with a maintenance bulletin on his tablet.

"You need to see this before Marr does."

"That sentence has never improved my morning."

He handed it over.

SABLE ARRAY PUBLIC SAFETY NOTICE

Due to crowd density near tribunal decks and increased strain on local circulation, access to auxiliary oxygen refresh points may be prioritized for credentialed personnel, registered residents, medical exemptions, and orderly queue groups. Noncompliant assemblies may be relocated to lower-consumption corridors.

Below the notice, a small map blinked open. Tribunal Deck Seven had been divided into colors.

Green: credentialed access.

Blue: resident access.

Amber: medical request pending.

Gray: unregistered assembly.

The wall of signs sat inside gray.

Ira watched a refresh-point attendant wheel a canister station toward the corridor corner, hesitate, and consult the same map. Two survivors rose carefully from the floor. One had a portable pulse cuff blinking yellow. The attendant looked at their paper tags, then at the map, then at the public safety officer beside him.

"They are not registered," the officer said.

"She has a medical blink," the attendant answered.

"Medical desk has to confirm."

"The medical desk is two decks down."

"Then they need a corridor escort."

The woman with the blinking cuff sat back down before the argument finished. Not because she had been ordered to. Because people who had survived administrative rooms learned to retreat before the form made retreat mandatory.

Bayo saw Ira watching. "Do not go out there yet."

"Why?"

"Because if you go before filing, security can call it counsel interference. If you file first, it becomes evidence."

Ira hated that he was right. She hated more that he had learned to be right.

Ira read it twice because the first time her mind rejected the shape.

"They cannot be this stupid."

"They are calling it airflow management."

"They threatened oxygen."

"Prioritized refresh points."

"They threatened oxygen with better grammar."

Marr arrived behind them and took the tablet from Ira's hand without greeting. Her face did not change as she read, which meant her anger had gone formal.

"Who issued it?"

"Array public safety office," Bayo said.

"Under whose authority?"

"Station governor's emergency crowd protocol."

"When?"

"Forty minutes after the procedural order posted."

Ira looked back at the corridor wall. Families. Names. Children. People who had come to hear why their dead had been delayed by categories now being warned that their own access to breathable comfort depended on orderly grouping.

History did not repeat. It audited laziness.

Marr said, "We file immediately."

"On what ground?" Bayo asked.

"Witness intimidation, public access interference, coercive echo of contested methods, contamination of tribunal environment, and because I dislike idiots who hand us exhibits."

Ira was already opening a motion.

Before she finished the caption, the public address system chimed.

The corridor quieted in the way crowds quieted when machinery spoke about bodies.

"Attention tribunal deck visitors," the station voice said. "For safety and comfort, please maintain registered queue clusters. Auxiliary oxygen refresh access will be available to compliant groups at intervals designated by public safety personnel. Unregistered assemblies may be asked to relocate."

No one moved.

Then an old woman laughed.

Not loudly. Not happily. Recognition again.

"Queue clusters," she said. "They found a new tag."

The phrase moved through the corridor faster than security could.

New tag.

New tag.

New tag.

Ira saw a security officer touch his earpiece. The officer looked young enough to have learned the Fracture Wars from clean modules and memorial animations. He did not look cruel. That made Ira more afraid. Cruelty was not required for a system to threaten air. Compliance was enough.

Inside the chamber, Om had not yet taken the bench. Kett stood with two routeholder assistants, reading the notice with an expression of carefully managed distance. Nemi Aral looked sick. The military advocate appeared unsurprised, which Ira marked.

Marr filed the emergency motion at 08:47.

Om entered at 08:52, read it at the bench, and did not sit.

"Array Public Safety representative to the chamber."

A station official approached from the side entrance. He was broad-shouldered, neat, and wearing the pale green badge of environmental authority. His name strip read COR VENNIK. Ira had seen him in corridors for days, always near doors, always speaking softly to people with weapons.

Om said, "Mr. Vennik, did your office issue this notice?"

"Yes, Magistrate."

"Did you consult the tribunal?"

"No, Magistrate. Station circulation falls under Array operational authority."

"Did you consult survivor liaison?"

"No."

"Did you consult anyone who has listened to the last three days of testimony?"

Vennik paused. That pause saved him from saying no too quickly.

"The notice concerns safety, not testimony."

The gallery responded with a sound like metal bending.

Om struck the bench. "Order."

Vennik continued, "Crowd density has exceeded predicted load. Portable scrubbers are being depleted faster than forecast. We have medical exemptions, residents with priority claims, and tribunal personnel requiring clear access. The notice is neutral."

Ira stood. "Magistrate."

Om looked at her. "You filed the motion. Proceed."

She approached the center floor with the public safety notice on the evidence wall beside the dependent motion suppression titles. She had not planned the visual. Bayo had. Sometimes he had instincts worth trusting.

"Mr. Vennik, what is a registered queue cluster?"

"A group logged with public safety for corridor management."

"How does a group register?"

"Through deck access officers."

"What information is required?"

"Number of persons, medical needs, resident status, tribunal credential status, and assembly purpose."

"Assembly purpose?"

"To route groups appropriately."

"If a group lists protest, what happens?"

Vennik's eyes moved to the wall of signs visible through the chamber glass. "Protest is not restricted."

"That was not my question."

"It may affect placement to reduce conflict."

"Placement away from chamber access?"

"If necessary."

"Away from auxiliary oxygen refresh points?"

"If corridor load requires."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, this examination is converting station logistics into theater."

The gallery hissed.

Om said, "Sit down, counsel. The tribunal is examining whether station logistics is converting testimony into coercion."

Kett sat.

Ira kept her eyes on Vennik. "What makes a group compliant?"

"Following public safety direction."

"Including relocation?"

"Yes."

"If a group refuses relocation, what happens to oxygen refresh access?"

"No one is denied breathable atmosphere."

"That was not my question."

Marr's pen moved fast.

Vennik adjusted his badge. "Auxiliary refresh points are discretionary comfort and medical support infrastructure."

"Comfort?"

"Supplemental."

"For elderly survivors waiting in a crowded tribunal corridor?"

"Medical exemptions are available."

"Who grants them?"

"Public safety medical desk."

"Where is that desk?"

"Lower deck."

"How does an elderly survivor in a noncompliant assembly reach it?"

Vennik did not answer.

Ira let the silence do the first work.

Then she asked, "Are you aware that this tribunal is examining wartime use of movement categories, claimant status, and public order language to delay vulnerable populations?"

"Generally."

"Are you aware that the phrase orderly queue has appeared in evidence as part of public notice language during Cinder Gate?"

"I am not an evidence officer."

"Are you aware that oxygen access was used during the Fracture Wars as a crowd control instrument?"

The military advocate stood. "Security privilege."

Om's eyes snapped to him. "Sit."

"Magistrate, present-day station security protocols are implicated."

"They implicated themselves when they threatened oxygen outside my courtroom."

The advocate sat.

Vennik looked less certain now. Not guilty, not innocent. A man discovering that the form he used every day had old teeth.

"Mr. Vennik," Ira said, "who drafted the notice?"

"My office."

"From a template?"

"Yes."

"What template?"

"Public order environmental strain notice."

"How old is it?"

"I do not know."

"Preserve it."

Om said, "Ordered."

Vennik looked toward the station counsel table. There was no station counsel table. That seemed to surprise him.

Ira pressed. "Does the template include registered queue clusters?"

"Yes."

"Compliant groups?"

"Yes."

"Prioritized oxygen refresh access?"

"Yes."

"Relocation to lower-consumption corridors?"

"Yes."

"Does it include guidance on assembly purpose?"

"Yes."

"Does it include language for memorial gatherings, protests, or claimant groups?"

Vennik hesitated.

The room heard it.

"Yes," he said.

Auditor Inoya, seated beside the bench, made a note.

Ira looked at Om. "Magistrate, this template may be derivative lineage evidence."

Kett was on his feet. "That is absurd."

Nemi Aral stood too, surprising everyone. "It is not absurd."

Kett turned on her. "Civil logistics has no interest in expanding this tribunal into station management."

"Civil logistics has an interest in not watching the same category language walk into the corridor wearing a fresh badge."

The gallery sound rose again.

Om did not strike the bench. He let the sentence reach the back row.

Vennik said, "This is not wartime coercion. It is environmental safety."

Ira turned back. "That may be true. But safety language can still coerce when the thing managed is breath."

"We are responsible for keeping people alive."

"So was Cinder Gate command."

He flinched.

She regretted the cruelty and kept the question because regretting a question did not make it wrong.

"Mr. Vennik, were any groups actually denied refresh access this morning?"

"No."

"Were any warned they would lose priority?"

"Yes."

"Which groups?"

"Unregistered assemblies near tribunal doors."

"Assemblies holding survivor signs?"

"Some."

"Families of Cinder Gate dead?"

"I do not know."

"Because you did not collect names?"

"We collected group descriptors."

The chamber stopped.

Ira felt the phrase land before Vennik understood he had said it.

Group descriptors.

Not names.

Group descriptors.

Marr closed her eyes.

Bayo put both hands on the evidence table.

Om's voice was quiet. "Mr. Vennik, all public safety crowd-control templates, notices, registration forms, assembly purpose codes, environmental strain protocols, oxygen refresh prioritization rules, and related training material are preserved immediately. No deletion, migration, template update, or terminology change."

Vennik nodded. "Yes, Magistrate."

"The notice is suspended."

"Magistrate, station governor authority..."

"The notice is suspended for tribunal decks pending jurisdictional briefing. If the governor disagrees, the governor may appear in this chamber and explain why the families of people delayed by category should accept oxygen prioritization by group descriptor outside the hearing room."

Vennik swallowed. "Yes, Magistrate."

"Auxiliary refresh access?"

"Open."

"Medical desk?"

"Mobile teams to corridor."

"Registration?"

"Voluntary for safety assistance only. No assembly purpose required."

Om sat at last.

"Make the announcement."

Vennik hesitated, then activated his station channel.

The public address system chimed again.

This time the corridor seemed to hold its breath out of choice.

"Attention tribunal deck visitors," Vennik said, voice less smooth than the machine's. "The prior public safety notice is suspended. Auxiliary oxygen refresh access is open. Medical assistance will come to corridor groups. Registration is voluntary and not required for access. No assembly will be relocated for lack of registration."

For a moment nothing happened.

Then someone outside began to cry.

Not with relief exactly. Relief was too clean. The sound carried anger, humiliation, memory, and the exhaustion of people who had recognized an old instrument before the people holding it did.

Ira returned to counsel table.

Marr did not look at her. "You pushed hard."

"Too hard?"

"Ask me after appeal."

Bayo whispered, "Group descriptors."

"I know."

"They keep inventing exhibits."

"They keep inheriting them."

Om ordered a recess to allow medical teams into the corridor. This time nobody objected except Kett, and even Kett shaped his objection as preservation of argument rather than opposition to breath.

During recess, Ira went outside.

She should have stayed in Evidence Review. She should have drafted the lineage motion. She should have let survivor liaison handle the corridor. Instead she stepped through the chamber doors into the wall of signs and people and stopped beneath the barriered memorial papers.

The old woman who had laughed at "queue clusters" watched her.

"You made them say it out loud," the woman said.

"The notice is suspended."

"That is not what I meant."

Ira waited.

"They always say it in rooms where we have to behave," the woman said. "Today they had to say it where we could hear."

Ira had no answer that would not cheapen it.

A medical team moved past with portable refresh canisters. People made space. No one rushed the equipment. Order arrived without threat when the thing offered was care instead of compliance.

At the far end of the corridor, a young security officer removed his helmet and handed his own canister to a man with a walker. His supervisor saw and did not stop him.

Small mercies did not redeem systems.

They did, however, keep people breathing while systems were forced open.

Back inside, the suspended notice became Exhibit PS-1. Bayo added it to the sequence chart under a new line:

Present-day lineage echo: oxygen refresh prioritization by registered queue cluster and group descriptor.

Marr changed "echo" to "possible lineage evidence."

Bayo sighed.

"No metaphors," he said.

"No metaphors," Ira agreed.

But privately, she thought echo was the right word.

Cinder Gate had shouted through eighty years and found fresh air in a station notice.

Now the tribunal had to decide whether it had merely heard resemblance, or whether the old method had been breathing through new lungs all along.

Chapter 30: Lael Shun Under Censure

Lael Shun arrived late and under objection.

That was how Bayo announced her, standing in Evidence Review with a transit notice in one hand and a disciplinary bulletin in the other. He had begun carrying documents physically when he wanted people to understand that the absurdity had weight.

"Which objection?" Ira asked.

"Several. Routeholders object to her authority. Sable Array objects to her security clearance. The Meridian Remnant Council objects to her tone in prior correspondence. The Compact Historical Conduct Office objects to the tribunal asking for her before she was formally released from censure."

Marr took the disciplinary bulletin. "She was censured?"

"Three times."

"For?"

"Refusing to certify postwar settlement completeness, publishing minority notes in sealed annex summaries, and calling a Council reconciliation report 'a museum label pasted over a wound.'"

Ira looked up from the sequence chart. "That last one was the formal wording?"

"That was the censured phrase."

Marr read silently, then handed the bulletin back. "I may like her."

Lael's shuttle docked at 10:40 station time. By 10:47, Kett had filed a motion to limit her appearance. By 10:51, Sable Array security had requested that she surrender all independent recording instruments. By 10:58, Lael Shun entered the tribunal chamber carrying no visible luggage, wearing a plain travel coat, and looking like someone who had misplaced patience several jurisdictions ago.

She was shorter than Ira expected. Older too. Not fragile. Compressed. Her hair was white, braided close to the skull. Her left eye had been replaced with a dark analytic lens that did not try to resemble flesh. When she stopped before the bench, the lens clicked once, a small sound in a room trained to fear small sounds.

Om looked down at her credentials.

"Lael Shun, formerly Compact Historical Conduct Office, special examiner for postwar settlement integrity, currently under administrative censure."

"Currently appealing administrative censure."

"Noted."

"Repeatedly ignored."

"Also noted."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, the routeholder parties object to Ms. Shun being treated as Compact authority. The Compact that empowered her office no longer exists in operational form, her office has limited archival remit, and her censure undermines reliability."

Lael turned her dark lens toward him. "Counsel, reliability and obedience are not synonyms."

"Nor are censure and courage."

"No. Sometimes censure is earned by incompetence. In my case the record is available."

Om lifted one hand before the exchange could become enjoyable. "Ms. Shun, what authority do you claim here?"

"Limited."

The answer did not please anyone.

Om waited.

Lael continued, "I do not claim authority to validate this tribunal. I do not claim authority to cure its conflicts. I do not claim authority to speak for the dead Compact as if the corpse retained a mouth. I claim authority to authenticate certain Compact-era settlement integrity processes, identify when records were excluded from reconciliation under false completeness, and certify whether Cinder Gate materials fall within unresolved conduct review."

Om leaned back. "State the limits affirmatively."

Lael seemed to approve of the demand. "I can authenticate office marks, ledger custody, settlement completeness classifications, minority-note suppression flags, and unresolved conduct referrals. I can identify whether a file was closed because a question was answered or because the office decided the question had become inconvenient."

"And what can you not do?"

"I cannot testify that every closed file concealed wrongdoing. I cannot convert a censure into proof of courage. I cannot turn Compact dust into current jurisdiction. I cannot say this tribunal becomes clean because I stand in it. I cannot decide whether Cinder Gate was a crime."

Kett said, "Then your usefulness is narrow."

"Good," Lael said. "Broad usefulness is how experts become furniture."

Om's eyes warned her.

She adjusted without apology. "My usefulness is narrow enough to test."

Auditor Inoya spoke for the first time. "That is the first comforting sentence I have heard today."

Kett objected to the auditor offering commentary. Om sustained the objection and asked the clerk to mark the limit of Lael's foundation. Bayo did so with visible pleasure, creating a four-line box on the evidence wall:

LAEL SHUN FOUNDATION:

AUTHENTICATE COMPACT-ERA SETTLEMENT INTEGRITY MARKS.

IDENTIFY FALSE-COMPLETENESS CLOSURE INDICATORS.

NO VALIDATION OF TRIBUNAL, LIABILITY, OR IMPLEMENTATION WITHOUT FURTHER CHAIN.

Marr whispered, "Precise."

Kett said, "She admits she cannot validate the tribunal."

"I was not asked to launder it," Lael said.

The gallery reacted before Om could stop it.

Om struck the bench lightly, almost reluctantly. "Order. Ms. Shun, you will confine yourself to answers."

"Then ask better questions."

Ira looked down so no one could see her face.

Om's mouth tightened. "You are still under tribunal authority while in this chamber."

"Yes, Magistrate."

"Good."

"Limited authority, of course."

Auditor Inoya made a note that might have been professional admiration.
