Om swore Lael as an expert witness over routeholder objection. The oath settled strangely on her. Some witnesses wore oath like armor. Lael wore it like a tool she had seen misused and still refused to discard.

Marr conducted foundation because Ira was too close to the sequence chart and because Marr knew how to make expertise enter a room without giving it the keys.

"Ms. Shun, did you review the Quiet Directorate index released in this proceeding?"

"Yes."

"Did you review the protected extract from QD-CG/16-58/CASUALTY CLASS PREPARATION?"

"The portions made available under auditor supervision."

"Do those materials resemble known Compact-era conduct files?"

"Yes."

Kett objected to resemble. Om allowed the answer for foundation.

Marr asked, "In what way?"

"Structure. Handling marks. Role routing. The phrase 'living lineage.' The distinction between event management and attribution management. The use of civil language desks as method carriers. The presence of post-event memory risk before survivor consolidation."

At the last phrase, the gallery stirred.

Lael's human eye moved toward them. "I am sorry. That phrase is as ugly as it sounds."

Om said, "The witness will avoid commentary."

"That was translation."

"Avoid both unless asked."

Marr continued. "Does the presence of these markers prove implementation at Cinder Gate?"

"No."

Kett looked satisfied.

Lael turned slightly toward him. "It proves the file is not an accidental administrative note by a frightened clerk. It is method architecture."

Kett's satisfaction narrowed.

"Define method architecture," Marr said.

"A repeatable administrative design intended to produce similar outcomes across uncertain events without requiring every operator to understand the full design."

Ira felt the phrase fit too well around the oxygen notice.

Marr did too. "Could such architecture survive beyond the original event?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"Templates. Training modules. emergency manuals, liability language, software defaults, office folklore, risk categories, audit exemptions, and people promoted because they can make harm look orderly."

Nemi Aral closed her eyes.

Marr asked, "Does Cinder Gate appear in Compact unresolved conduct review?"

Lael took a document from her coat. A physical document, sealed in translucent film.

Kett objected before he knew what it was.

Om said, "Counsel, wait to be offended by something identifiable."

Lael placed the document on the evidence table.

"This is a certified excerpt from the Compact Historical Conduct Office unresolved conduct ledger. Cinder Gate appears under three entries: evacuation casualty attribution, salvage diversion, and memory consolidation risk. All three were marked administratively satisfied in 2363 without underlying method review."

Om's face changed at the date.

His memorandum had been 2361.

The salvage settlements had moved in those years like doors closing down a corridor.

Marr asked, "Who marked them satisfied?"

Lael looked at the sealed film. "The excerpt redacts names pending Council release. Roles are visible. Settlement assessor panel, routeholder liaison, civil logistics reconciliation, military repair claims office, and Quiet Directorate inheritance custodian."

"Quiet Directorate inheritance custodian," Ira repeated before she could stop herself.

Om looked at her.

"Apologies, Magistrate."

Lael's dark lens clicked. "No apology needed for recognizing the nail in the floor."

Om sighed. "Ms. Shun."

"Answering only, Magistrate."

"You were not asked a question."

"Then I withdraw the nail."

The gallery almost laughed. It was not amusement. It was the sound of a room learning that authority could be punctured and still function.

Kett rose for cross.

"Ms. Shun, you have made a career of dissent from postwar reconciliation bodies, correct?"

"Incorrect."

"You deny publishing minority notes?"

"No. I deny that dissent is a career. It pays badly and ruins invitations."

"You were censured for refusing to certify settlement completeness."

"Yes."

"Because you applied standards no settlement process could meet."

"Because the settlement process claimed completeness while excluding method review."

"You understand that civilizations after war require closure."

Lael looked at him for a long moment.

"Counsel, civilizations after war require sewage management. Closure is often what officials call the smell when they stop noticing it."

Om said, "Witness."

"Withdrawn as metaphor."

Bayo, at the evidence table, muttered, "No metaphors."

Ira nearly lost her composure.

Kett did not. He was too practiced. "You cannot tell this tribunal that Cinder Gate was criminal."

"No."

"You cannot tell this tribunal that routeholders implemented Quiet Directorate methods."

"Not from the material I have seen."

"You cannot tell this tribunal that present-day Sable Array templates derive from wartime method architecture."

"Not yet."

"Then your testimony is suspicion dressed as expertise."

"No," Lael said. "It is boundary marking. I am telling the tribunal where certainty ends so interested parties cannot move the fence in the dark."

Auditor Inoya looked up.

Kett tried another route. "Is it your position that this tribunal is legitimate?"

The chamber waited.

Ira understood the trap. If Lael said yes, Kett would use her limited authority to pretend every conflict had been cured by Compact dust. If she said no, he would use her to attack the proceeding. Either way, he wanted the witness to become a seal.

Lael did not oblige.

"This tribunal is necessary," she said.

"That was not my question."

"It is my answer."

"Legitimate or illegitimate?"

"Contested."

Kett smiled. "Then why are you here?"

"Because contested authority is sometimes the only authority not yet fully captured by the people contesting it."

Om's expression did something complicated.

Kett pressed. "So you refuse to validate the tribunal."

"Correct."

"But you assist it."

"Yes."

"That is inconsistent."

"No. Validation says the room is clean. Assistance says the room is the one currently holding the evidence."

The answer landed harder than any endorsement could have.

Marr wrote it down.

Ira did too.

Kett shifted to censure. He entered Lael's disciplinary history, the censured phrases, the administrative findings that she had exceeded remit, undermined settlement confidence, and introduced unresolved moral language into technical reports. Lael admitted each underlying act and disputed every conclusion.

"Did you call a Council reconciliation report a museum label pasted over a wound?"

"Yes."

"Do you stand by that?"

"No."

Kett paused. He had expected defiance.

Lael said, "It was too generous to museum labels. They usually identify the dead object correctly."

Om removed his lenses and rubbed his eyes.

"Ms. Shun," he said, "the tribunal is not improved by every sharp thing you can say."

"Few institutions are."

"You will answer with less ornament."

"Yes, Magistrate."

Kett used the exchange well. "This is precisely the problem. The witness is not neutral. She is hostile to reconciliation, hostile to settlement, hostile to closure."

Lael answered before Om could stop her. "I am hostile to false completion."

Om let that one stand.

Kett returned to his table with enough material to attack her credibility and not enough to remove her usefulness. That was the best Ira could have hoped for.

Om allowed limited redirect.

Marr asked only one question.

"Ms. Shun, what should this tribunal not do with your testimony?"

Lael seemed to respect the question.

"Do not use me as a blessing. Do not say Compact authority has arrived and therefore the proceeding is purified. Do not say my censure proves I am brave or unreliable. Do not say method architecture proves implementation before you build the chain. Do not let anyone claim uncertainty means there is nothing to preserve. And do not confuse closure with completion."

Marr sat.

Om admitted Lael's ledger excerpt under seal pending redaction review, accepted her limited expert foundation, and ordered the Remnant Council to produce conflict and censure records related to Cinder Gate unresolved conduct entries. He also ordered briefing on whether "Quiet Directorate inheritance custodian" referred to a defunct office, successor role, or living authority.

At "living authority," the military advocate looked down.

Ira saw it.

So did Lael.

So did Inoya.

The room's attention shifted by a degree.

The advocate recovered too late.

Om marked the reaction without comment. The record did not, but rooms had records beneath records.

After adjournment, Lael found Ira beside the corridor sign wall.

"You are Senn."

"Yes."

"You halted Sera Venn-3 examination."

"Yes."

"Good."

Ira was too tired to accept praise cleanly. "It may damage the case."

"Most necessary acts do."

"That does not make them good."

"No. It makes them accountable."

Lael looked at the signs through the transparent barrier. Her dark lens adjusted with a soft click.

"The tribunal will be pressured to become a machine for turning pain into usable findings," she said. "Resist that, but do not become precious. Pain unused is not honored. Pain consumed is not justice. There is a narrow bridge."

"Does anyone cross it?"

"Sometimes. Badly. With witnesses shouting at them."

Ira almost smiled.

Lael's face remained severe. "Do not trust me too much."

"I was not planning to."

"Good. I am censured for reasons that are mostly political and partly temperamental. Both matter."

"Why tell me?"

"Because the first way institutions launder themselves is by finding one dissenter and pretending her presence proves the institution can hear dissent."

Ira looked back at the chamber doors.

"You really will not validate this tribunal."

"No."

"But you think it is necessary."

"Yes."

"That is uncomfortable."

"Truth often lacks furniture."

Ira heard Bayo's voice in memory: no metaphors.

Lael glanced at her. "You are thinking something impolite."

"Only internally."

"Keep some things that way. It helps."

Then Lael Shun walked toward the auditor rooms, still under censure, still under objection, carrying limited authority like a blade she refused to pretend was a crown.

Ira watched her go and felt no comfort.

That, she thought, might be the point.

Chapter 31: The Orison Refusal Trace

Shoals answered the tribunal at 03:03, which meant either Vela had not slept or Shoals considered sleep a local custom unfit for emergencies.

The message arrived through three relays, two preservation seals, and one line of text so plain it looked almost rude.

ORISON REFUSAL TRACE AVAILABLE UNDER CONDITION.

Bayo read it from the Evidence Review display and made a sound halfway between relief and dread.

"Only one condition?"

Vela appeared before Ira could answer. Her image was broken at the edges by Shoals compression, but her eyes were sharp enough to make the room feel underprepared.

"Not one," Vela said. "One category of condition. Many teeth."

Marr entered carrying tea she had not drunk. "Define Orison refusal trace."

Vela looked from Marr to Ira to Bayo, then to the stopped witness clock still occupying its portable stand like a stubborn minor official.

"Oru-Kel's carried warning passed through an Orison boundary node before it reached Sable Array. The node refused conversion."

"Conversion into what?" Ira asked.

"Advisory context. Access map. Route clearance. Any operational use beyond warning preservation."

Bayo leaned forward. "So the node logged a refusal?"

"It logged refusal, pressure, attempted interpretation, and the parties that asked the warning to become useful."

Marr's face changed at useful.

So did Ira's.

Everything in the tribunal had become dangerous when someone argued it had to be useful. Sera useful. Pain useful. Signs useful. Salvage useful. Now even warning had needed protection from being turned into a tool.

Vela sent a document.

The header read:

SHOALS-ORISON CUSTODIAL OFFER

Refusal Trace: Oru-Kel-Under-Quiet / Cinder Gate Warning Chain

Permitted Use: Evidence Preservation and Temporal Boundary Corroboration

Prohibited Use: Access Reconstruction, Shoals Approach Modeling, Orison Node Inference, Warning-to-Route Conversion, Advisory Extraction

Ira read the prohibited list twice.

"They are afraid we will turn the warning into a map."

"Not afraid," Vela said. "Certain someone will try."

Marr set down her cold tea. "Can the trace prove Cinder Gate timing?"

"It can corroborate that a warning chain existed before Sable Array received it. It can show where the warning resisted conversion. It can compare pressure signatures against the second clock without revealing Orison geometry."

"Can it show who attempted conversion?"

Vela's expression tightened. "Roles, not names. The trace was not designed for your law."

"Few decent things are," Marr said.

Om convened a sealed conditions hearing at 09:00. The public gallery was excluded, which caused another corridor argument and three more signs. This time the signs did not accuse the tribunal of concealment. They asked for the reason to be posted before exclusion. Om ordered it posted:

Closed hearing to determine whether warning evidence can be preserved without converting warning into access.

Lael Shun arrived five minutes late and sat beside Auditor Inoya without asking permission. Kett objected. Om said she was admitted as limited expert observer for conduct lineage. Kett objected to that phrase. Om preserved the objection and looked older.

Vela appeared on the evidence wall, not in the witness frame. Shoals had insisted. Witness frames implied compelled answer. Vela's role was custodian relay, not witness. Kett objected to that too.

"Counsel," Om said, "at some point your objections will need to become fewer and better."

"I will attempt efficiency, Magistrate."

"That was not a compliment."

Kett bowed.

Vela displayed the custodial offer.

Kett read the prohibited uses and smiled for the first time that morning. "Magistrate, this is not evidence. It is evidence wrapped in foreign veto."

Vela said, "It is evidence wrapped in survival."

"Shoals cannot dictate the tribunal's use of admitted material."

"Then Shoals will not provide the material."

"That is obstruction."

"No. It is refusal."

The word made the chamber attend.

Lael Shun's dark lens clicked softly.

Om leaned forward. "Ms. Cho, explain the distinction."

Vela nodded once. "Obstruction prevents lawful inquiry to hide truth. Refusal prevents an inquiry from becoming the harm it studies. The Orison node refused to let a warning become access. Shoals repeats that refusal for the trace."

Kett stood. "Beautiful, but meaningless. If the trace has probative value, adversarial parties must test it."

"Yes," Vela said.

"Testing includes reconstructing the route by which it traveled."

"No."

"Then it is not testable."

"It is testable for timing, custody, pressure signature, and conversion attempt. It is not testable as a doorway."

Bayo whispered, "A warning is not a gate."

Ira wrote it down.

Marr saw and shook her head once. Not now.

Om asked Auditor Inoya for position.

Inoya had the custodial offer open beside three legal references and what appeared to be a treaty Ira did not recognize.

"Conditional evidence is not unusual when disclosure risks active harm," Inoya said. "The question is whether the condition prevents meaningful challenge. Here, Shoals offers independent hash custody, pressure signature comparison, role-level conversion attempt logs, and external reviewer access under non-reconstruction covenant. That may be sufficient."

Bayo brought up the proposed testing protocol while Inoya spoke. It was spare, almost ugly in its restraint:

TEST ONE: HASH CONTINUITY OF TRACE PACKAGE.

TEST TWO: PRESSURE SIGNATURE COMPARISON AGAINST MCP-CORR-2 TIMING LAYER.

TEST THREE: ROLE-LEVEL CONVERSION ATTEMPT LOG, NO ROUTE GEOMETRY.

TEST FOUR: EXTERNAL REVIEWER ACCESS UNDER NON-RECONSTRUCTION COVENANT.

PROHIBITED: PATH INFERENCE, NODE MODELING, ACCESS SIMULATION, WARNING-TO-ROUTE DERIVATIVE.

Ira watched Kett read it. He was looking for a hole large enough to call unfairness. There were holes. There had to be. Any condition strong enough to protect the trace also limited the party who wanted to break it open. The question was not whether the condition was perfect. The question was whether the remaining tests could still make the evidence bleed if it was false.

Marr leaned toward Ira. "Hash tells us whether the package changed. Pressure comparison tells us whether the timing relation is plausible. Role logs tell us who tried to convert without naming corridors."

"And external review?"

"Lets someone not Shoals say the first three are real."

"Enough?"

Marr did not soften the answer. "Enough for preservation. Not enough for final liability."

Ira wrote that into her notes before hope could improve it.

Kett said, "May be is not due process."

"Neither is demanding a weapon because it has fingerprints."

Lael Shun spoke without waiting to be called. "The historical conduct record supports the condition."

Om looked at her.

She added, "If permitted."

"Briefly."

"Cinder Gate shows repeated conversion of protective categories into management tools. Warning into advisory, witness into usable narrative, crowd into queue cluster, delay into triage. Shoals is identifying a known pattern before surrendering material to it."

Kett said, "This is theory."

"It is pattern evidence."

"It is moral suspicion."

"Those often start as the same thing and separate under review."

Om held up a hand. "Enough."

Ira had not spoken yet. She felt the pressure to be the reasonable advocate, to argue that the tribunal could be trusted, that conditions could be honored, that the court was not Cinder Gate. Lael's warning from the corridor returned: the first way institutions launder themselves is by finding one dissenter. There was another way too. By asking a lawyer to say this room was different before the room had earned it.

Om turned to her. "Counsel Senn."

She stood.

"The tribunal should accept the condition."

Kett's eyebrows lifted, pleased by the opening.

Ira continued. "Not because Shoals outranks the tribunal. Not because their refusal language is beyond challenge. Because the requested trace concerns an event where warnings, classifications, and damaged testimony were repeatedly converted into instruments of control. The condition is relevant to reliability. If we cannot preserve a warning without trying to make it operationally useful, then we prove we are not competent to understand it."

Vela's image flickered once. Ira could not tell whether that was relay instability or emotion.

Kett said, "You are surrendering cross-examination."

"No. I am limiting the object of cross-examination to the evidentiary question: what does the trace prove, and what does it not prove?"

"It may prove less than you want."

"Good. Then it will be safer than most of our certainty."

Marr gave Ira the smallest possible nod.

Om asked Nemi Aral for civil logistics position. She supported admission under condition. The military advocate opposed, then requested access for "operational review," which did not help his opposition. Lael looked at him again. He looked down again. This time the record noticed because Inoya asked that the request be marked.

Om ruled at 10:22.

"The tribunal accepts the Shoals-Orison custodial condition provisionally. The refusal trace will be admitted under sealed preservation. Permitted uses: temporal boundary corroboration, custody verification, pressure signature comparison, and role-level record of conversion attempts. Prohibited uses: route reconstruction, access modeling, Orison node inference, warning-to-route conversion, advisory extraction, or any derivative operational use."

Kett rose. "Routeholders object and reserve appeal."

"Preserved."

"We further request that if the evidence cannot be fully tested, adverse inference against routeholder parties be barred."

"Premature."

"Then preserve."

"Preserved."

Vela transmitted the trace at 10:31.

It did not look like a document. It looked like weather captured by a machine that hated metaphors: bands of pressure, rejection pulses, role markers, custody seals, and a repeated symbol that meant refusal in Orison notation. The symbol was simple: a curve interrupted before it became a circle.

Bayo stared. "That is..."

"Do not say pretty," Marr warned.

"I was going to say annoying."

"Allowed."

Vela guided them through the readable layer.

"Here, Oru-Kel's carried warning enters the Shoals-Orison boundary. Here, an advisory conversion request attaches. The node refuses. Here, a second request asks for contextual routing. Refused. Here, a pressure comparison request. Allowed, stripped of route geometry. Here, later, after Sable Array receipt, a tribunal preservation ping. Allowed."

Ira leaned closer. "Who made the first conversion request?"

"Role-level only."

"Show it."

Vela highlighted a mark.

ROUTEHOLDER NAVIGATION SUPPORT / LEGACY CHANNEL

Kett was standing before the highlight finished.

"That does not identify a party in this proceeding."

"Correct," Vela said.

"Legacy channel could be automated."

"Correct."

"Or unrelated to any current routeholder."

"Possible."

Marr wrote each concession down. Ira appreciated that. The case needed what the trace did not prove as much as what it did.

Ira asked, "What was requested?"

"Advisory context."

"Meaning?"

"Convert warning into navigational relevance. If allowed, the warning could help infer where not to go, where stress moved, perhaps where a hidden corridor had resisted collapse."

"Useful," Ira said.

"Yes."

"And prohibited."

"Yes."

The second request appeared.

COMPACT SECURITY METHOD / INHERITED TOKEN

The room went quiet.

Lael Shun's lens clicked twice.

Om said, "Ms. Cho, repeat the role."

"Compact Security Method, inherited token."

The military advocate stood. "Magistrate, that label may implicate classified successor systems."

Inoya said, "Then they exist?"

The advocate stopped.

Om's voice was calm. "Counsel, sit down unless you intend to answer that question."

He sat.

Ira felt the trace enter the case spine like a new bone. Routeholder navigation support. Compact security method. Inherited token. Conversion attempts made against a warning before and after the very institutions in the chamber claimed the warning was too damaged, too late, too foreign to matter.

Vela showed the refusal mark again.

"The node refused both because warning chains are not roads. A warning is a duty, not a privilege."

Kett said, "That is Shoals doctrine, not law."

Vela looked tired. "It is why the evidence still exists."

No one answered that.

The trace's timing layer corroborated Oru-Kel's carried warning arrival sequence without opening Orison geometry. It placed the warning's boundary contact before Sable Array's damaged receipt, after the Cinder Gate event, and alongside pressure signatures compatible with the second clock. It did not prove who abandoned whom. It did not prove who ordered categories. It proved that before the tribunal ever saw the warning, two inherited systems had tried to turn it into something else.

Om ordered the trace preserved under condition.

Bayo added it to the sequence chart:

Orison refusal trace: warning preserved; conversion attempts by routeholder legacy channel and Compact security inherited token refused.

Marr changed "conversion attempts" to "role-level conversion attempts."

Bayo accepted the edit without protest.

After the hearing, Vela stayed on private relay.

"You argued for the condition," she said.

"It was the right argument."

"It was also against your appetite."

Ira did not deny it.

Vela looked past her, perhaps at some Shoals instrument Ira would never understand. "When I first received Oru-Kel's warning, I wanted to open everything. Every seal. Every route. Every hidden tooth. I told myself access was justice."

"Was it?"

"Sometimes. Sometimes access is just hunger with better ethics."

Ira thought of the corridor signs, Sera's halted testimony, Tamsin's salvage coat, Lael refusing validation, Om sitting on a plank he had signed.

"How do you know the difference?"

Vela smiled without comfort. "Too late, usually."

The relay closed.

In the main room, the refusal symbol remained on the wall: a curve interrupted before it became a circle.

Ira asked Bayo to print it for the evidence board.

Marr said, "No symbols on the public board without explanatory caption."

"I know."

"And no metaphors."

Ira looked at the broken circle.

"It is not a metaphor."

Marr followed her gaze and did not argue.

For once, refusal had left a trace.

Chapter 32: Pell's Necessary Lie

Pell asked to testify before anyone called him.

That alone made Kett request a competency review, a privilege review, a protective status review, and a recess long enough for reason to "return to the proceeding." Om granted the privilege review, denied the recess, and asked Pell whether he understood that volunteering testimony after protective transfer could damage him in ways no tribunal order could repair.

Pell stood beneath the witness clock, which had been replaced but not trusted. The stopped clock remained in Evidence Review. The new clock kept accurate time with the anxious obedience of a substitute.

"I understand," Pell said.

Marr leaned toward Ira. "He has decided what he wants to spend."

"Himself?"

"Or someone else."

Pell took the oath again. It had begun to feel less like ceremony and more like erosion. Each time he promised truth, another layer of institutional speech came off him, and Ira could see more clearly the man beneath: not noble, not monstrous, not safe.

Om addressed him first.

"Mr. Pell, you requested to testify concerning casualty terminology."

"Yes."

"You are not compelled at this moment."

"I know."

"You may answer narrowly."

"I will try."

Kett stood. "Magistrate, routeholder parties renew objection to unscheduled witness testimony and request counsel appointment for Mr. Pell."

Pell looked at him. "I had counsel. Counsel wanted me quieter."

"A common feature of competent representation," Kett said.

Om said, "Mr. Pell, do you waive counsel for this limited testimony?"

"Yes."

"Foolishly?"

"Probably."

"The record will note knowing waiver. Counsel Senn, proceed with care."

Ira stood. The room expected attack. Pell expected something worse: relief. He had the look of a man prepared to be punished because punishment would simplify him. Ira had learned not to grant simplification cheaply.

"Mr. Pell, why did you ask to testify?"

He looked at the evidence wall where the Orison refusal mark still sat under explanatory caption.

"Because refusal is expensive when people are dying."

Kett's face tightened.

Ira said, "Explain."

"The tribunal has heard that categories were changed. That unbonded dependents, local claimants, medical nontransfer, salvage encumbrance, all of it moved after correction pressure began. It has heard that categories were prepared before public notice. That is true."

The gallery was silent.

Pell continued. "But there is one change the file will not explain because it was mine."

Ira felt Marr become still beside her.

"What change?"

"I changed 'nontransferable dependent mass' to 'guardian chain unresolved.'"

No one spoke for a moment because the first phrase had the violence of a thing dragged from a basement.

Mara Venn, seated in the gallery, closed her eyes.

Jun Vale-of-Cinder's relay was not present. Ira was grateful for that and ashamed of the gratitude.

Om said, "Repeat the original term."

Pell swallowed. "Nontransferable dependent mass."

Bayo wrote it down with visible disgust.

Ira asked, "Where did that term appear?"

"In a Quiet Directorate language option set attached to civil notice preparation. It was not meant for public release. It was internal."

"Why did you change it?"

"Because if operators saw that phrase during collapse, they would understand those children as weight."

"And guardian chain unresolved was better?"

Pell looked at her then, directly.

"Yes."

The answer angered the room because it sounded like defense.

Ira let it anger the room.

"Why?"

"Because guardian chain unresolved at least implied a problem that could be solved by a person. A signature. A lie. An aunt invented in a hurry. A station mother willing to commit fraud. Nontransferable dependent mass implied physics."

Mara Venn opened her eyes.

Ira did not look away from Pell. "Did you intend station mothers to lie?"

"I hoped they would."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, this testimony exposes third parties to fraud allegations."

Om's gaze moved to him. "I suspect the station mothers will survive being accused of saving children."

Kett sat.

Pell said, "I could not remove the delay class. I could not give them ships. I could not tell the public that correction pressure made their movement useful to postpone. I had a language desk code and ninety seconds before the next notice package."

"So you changed a category name."

"Yes."

"Did that save lives?"

"Yes."

"How many?"

"I do not know."

"Did it cost lives?"

Pell closed his eyes.

"Yes."

The answer was quieter.

Ira stepped closer. "How?"

"Guardian chain unresolved sounded procedural. It made delay acceptable to administrators who would have recoiled at mass. It made the category easier to defend afterward. It gave people like me language to say we had not reduced children to load because the public term did not say load."

He opened his eyes.

"It helped Mara Venn invent guardians. It helped settlement panels avoid asking why guardians were needed for children standing in front of them."

There it was. The necessary lie with both edges.

Marr's pen moved.

Lael Shun, sitting under limited observer status, watched Pell without expression. Ira wondered how many such sentences she had heard from men who wanted absolution to arrive as nuance.

Ira asked, "When you changed the term, did you know the original class had been prepared before correction pressure?"

"No."

"When did you learn?"

"After. During settlement review."

"Did you disclose it?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Pell's hands gripped the witness rail.

"Because by then I knew some children had lived through the gap I made, and I knew some had died inside the category I softened, and I could not bear any hearing that would decide the softer word was the crime."

Kett stood again. "Magistrate, the witness is narrating psychology."

"Overruled," Om said. "We are far past pretending psychology did not write records."

Pell looked grateful. Ira distrusted that too.

"Mr. Pell," she said, "do you ask this tribunal to treat your change as exculpatory?"

"No."

"Mitigating?"

"I don't know."

"Necessary?"

He looked toward the gallery before answering.

"At the time, yes."

"Now?"

"Still yes. Also wrong."

The room did not know what to do with that. Law preferred clean oppositions: necessary or wrong, life-saving or criminal, lie or mercy. Pell had brought a sentence that refused to choose.

Ira asked, "If you had not changed the term, what would have happened?"

"Some operators might have seen the cruelty sooner."

"Would that have changed movement?"

"Maybe."

"Would more children have died?"

"Maybe."

"Would fewer?"

"Maybe."

"Then how can you call it necessary?"

Pell's face tightened. "Because inside the ninety seconds I had, I did not have maybe. I had a term that made children mass and a term that made them legally misplaced. I chose the lie that left a door open."

Mara Venn stood in the gallery.

Om said, "Ms. Venn."

"Let me answer him."

"You are not under examination."

"Then swear me again."

Kett objected. Om overruled without looking at him.

Mara took the oath from where she stood because making her walk to the witness rail would have been absurd theater even for this tribunal.

Om said, "Briefly."

Mara looked at Pell. "Guardian chain unresolved let me lie."

Pell nodded.

"It let me move sixteen."

His face folded.

"It also made me spend forty years thinking I had failed paperwork instead of fighting a category built before we were told danger had a name."

Pell did not defend himself.

Mara continued, "So I will not thank you. I will not damn you alone. I will say this: your better word still made me beg a machine to recognize children I knew by name."

Pell whispered, "I know."

"No," Mara said. "You know now."

Om let the correction stand.

Ira felt the case shift again. Pell had wanted the tribunal to understand necessity. Mara had answered with time. Knowing after was not knowing then. A necessary lie could still steal decades from the people forced to live under it.

Kett cross-examined because he had to.

"Mr. Pell, your testimony is that you changed language to preserve possibility of movement."

"Yes."

"You did not create the delay class."

"No."

"You did not order any child held."

"No."

"You did not design MCP-CORR-2."

"No."

"You acted under emergency conditions with incomplete information."

"Yes."

"And your act saved at least some lives."

Pell hesitated.

Kett leaned in. "That is your testimony."

"Yes."

"Then counsel Senn's case against institutional category manipulation depends on condemning the very discretion that allowed survivors to exist."

Ira stood. "Objection."

Om said, "Sustained. Counsel will ask questions."

Kett smiled slightly. "Mr. Pell, should emergency personnel be criminalized for improving cruel language under pressure?"

Pell looked at Ira, then at Mara, then at the wall where the refusal mark curved and stopped.

"No."

Kett turned. "Nothing further."

It was effective.

Worse, it was not empty.

Ira rose for redirect with Marr's warning in her bones: challenge without breaking, test without simplifying.

"Mr. Pell, should emergency personnel be excused from disclosing cruel language after pressure passes?"

"No."

"Should settlement panels be allowed to use improved public language to avoid reviewing the original category?"

"No."

"Should institutions praise the lie that saved sixteen while burying the class that condemned others?"

"No."

"Should the tribunal treat your ninety-second choice as the same moral object as the pre-event method file?"

Kett objected.

Om thought for a moment. "Overruled. The witness may answer if he understands."

Pell said, "No."

"Why not?"

"Because my choice was inside the trap. The file built the trap."

Ira let that stand.

Then she asked the question that hurt more.

"Did your better word help the trap survive settlement?"

Pell gripped the rail again.

"Yes."

"How?"

"It made the record sound less monstrous."

"And less monstrous records are easier to close."

"Yes."

Bayo raised a hand from the clerk table before Ira could continue. "Magistrate, Evidence Review has a comparison ladder from the settlement archive. It was not marked responsive because the final phrase differs from the field phrase."

Om looked at Ira. "Counsel?"

"Offer for limited foundation," Ira said. "Terminology lineage, not truth of the underlying classification."

Kett objected on ordinary revision grounds, because of course he did. Every harmful sentence had once been a draft.

Om allowed display.

The evidence wall opened four narrow lines:

FIELD TERM: NONTRANSFERABLE DEPENDENT MASS.

NOTICE SUBSTITUTION: GUARDIAN CHAIN UNRESOLVED.

SETTLEMENT DRAFT: DEPENDENT GUARDIANSHIP STATUS UNCONFIRMED.

FINAL RESPONSE: CIVIL CUSTODY AMBIGUITY REVIEWED UNDER EMERGENCY ADMINISTRATION.

The room read downward and became quieter with each line. Nothing had disappeared. That was the trick. The child did not vanish from the language in a single act. The child became dependent, then status, then ambiguity, then administration.

Ira asked, "Mr. Pell, did you make all four revisions?"

"No. Only the first substitution."

"But the first substitution made the later revisions easier?"

Pell's mouth opened, closed, then chose accuracy. "Yes."

"How?"

"A search for mass would have found the method file. A search for guardian chain found civil exceptions. A search for custody ambiguity found settlement panels talking to themselves."

Marr's pen stopped for the first time all afternoon.

Lael Shun said from observer seating, "That is not merely softening. It is migration."

Kett stood. "Expert conclusion without examination."

Om said, "The tribunal hears it as an offered characterization, not a ruling."

Lael did not apologize. "Then characterize it this way: ordinary drafting changed the address of the evidence."

Pell looked at the four lines as if each had been written by a different version of him and all had learned to sign his name.

Lael Shun wrote something down.

Om asked whether any party had further questions. No one did.

Before Pell stepped down, he looked toward Mara.

"I am sorry."

Mara did not answer immediately.

"Sorry is not useless," she said at last. "It is just small."

Pell nodded.

He stepped down and returned to protected seating, not absolved, not condemned enough for anyone's appetite.

Ira sat.

Bayo leaned close. "Did that help us?"

Marr answered before Ira could. "Yes. And it helped them."

"How is that good?"

"It is not good. It is evidence."

The phrase should have comforted Ira. It did not.

On the sequence chart, she added a new line:

17:42 language substitution by Nadir Pell: "nontransferable dependent mass" changed to "guardian chain unresolved"; immediate effect possibly enabled improvised guardian fraud; later effect softened settlement review.

Bayo looked at the line.

"That is a lot for one sentence."

"So was the lie."

Marr did not correct her.

That evening, in the corridor, someone added another sign beneath the others.

BETTER WORDS STILL HELD US.

Ira stood before it for a long time.

Then she returned to Evidence Review and moved Pell's necessary lie from the margin of the case to its center.

Chapter 33: The Dead Were Scheduled Last

The ranking hid inside a repair budget.

That was Bayo's discovery, and he resented it on professional grounds.

"No one should be allowed to hide atrocity in maintenance accounting," he said at 02:20, eyes red, hair flattened on one side from falling asleep against the evidence table.

Ira looked up from Pell's language substitution chain. "They hid children in cargo."

"Yes, but budgets are rude."

Marr, who had been reviewing Lael's unresolved conduct ledger, did not look up. "Arguably the least persuasive moral complaint so far."

Bayo sent the document to the main display.

It was labeled:

CINDER GATE EMERGENCY RESOURCE STABILIZATION DRAFT

Preliminary Priority Attachment for Repair Carrier Routing

Time mark: 17:06 local.

Ira straightened.

"Before the first correction burst."

"Before the boundary response," Bayo said. "Before public notice. Before most damage reports. And before anyone could honestly rank sections by actual condition."

Marr stood.

That alone changed the room.

The draft looked like every budget document Ira had ever hated: columns, codes, cost exposure, redundancy assumptions, political sponsor fields, labor dependency markers, routeholder impact estimates, and a column titled stabilization priority. The title pretended to concern machinery. The rows did not.

Cinder Inner Transfer Hub: Priority 1.

Routeholder liaison residence ring: Priority 1.

Military repair carrier dock: Priority 1.

Civil logistics command deck: Priority 2.

Seedstock vault and navigation core storage: Priority 2.

Cinder Twelve med-recycler spine: Priority 3.

Outer maintenance ring: Priority 5.

Cinder Nine dependent dormitories: Priority 6.

Lower A nursery spillover: Priority 6.

Local claimant overflow: Priority 7.

Unbonded dependent dormitories: Priority 7.

Ira read it once. Then again. Her mind tried to find a technical explanation because technical explanations were often kinder than evidence.

"Maybe priority means repair difficulty."

Bayo shook his head. "No. Look at the adjacent columns."

He highlighted them.

POLITICAL CONTINUITY VALUE.

ROUTE IMPACT.

CLAIM PRESSURE.

PUBLIC VISIBILITY IF LOST.

LABOR RECONSTITUTION COST.

No damage severity column.

No casualty estimate.

No pressure reading.

No corridor distance.

No oxygen remaining.

There was, however, a balancing column.

Bayo expanded it with two fingers and then seemed to regret making the text larger.

STABILIZATION LOSS IF DEFERRED.

The high-priority rows carried numbers, short comments, and routing consequences.

Cinder Inner Transfer Hub: cascading route failure; immediate confidence break; sponsor exposure severe.

Routeholder liaison residence ring: treaty continuity disruption; high visibility; successor dispute likely.

Military repair carrier dock: loss of mobile stabilization capacity; repair chain collapse probable.

The lower rows had different language.

Cinder Nine dependent dormitories: low system effect; claim consolidation manageable; guardian chain unresolved.

Lower A nursery spillover: noncritical infrastructure; movement creates corridor friction; dependent mass unbonded.

Unbonded dependent dormitories: no sponsor continuity; low route effect; delayed claim emergence likely.

Ira felt the earlier testimony click into an uglier shape. "Delayed claim emergence."

Bayo nodded once. "If the claimants die, their claims arrive later, weaker, or not at all."

Marr said, "Careful."

"I am being careful. That is what it says."

"It says likely delayed. It does not say desired."

Bayo's face reddened. "If I build a bridge and label the weak side likely to collapse under poor people, I do not get to pretend surprise when poor people fall."

No one answered him. The room had that late-night archive silence where a metaphor could become too close to evidence and therefore dangerous.

Ira copied the phrase into her notes without the anger:

DELAYED CLAIM EMERGENCE LIKELY.

Then she wrote the question beside it:

Was later silence treated as proof of lower harm, or as a benefit of earlier deferral?

Marr saw the line and did not tell her to delete it.

Marr's voice was flat. "Who produced it?"

"Military repair carrier planning cell with routeholder navigation advisory and civil logistics input. Draft status. Not final."

"How did we get it?"

"Lael's ledger production cross-referenced a settlement budget. The file was misclassified as reimbursement basis for repair carrier fuel."

Ira stared at Priority 7.

The dead were scheduled last before anyone had admitted they were dying.

Om convened hearing at 08:30 under public access because sealing this document would have required everyone to pretend numbers were more dangerous than what they numbered. The gallery filled with a different kind of silence. Technical files bored people. This one did not. Everyone understood lists.

Kett objected to admissibility, authenticity, relevance, timing interpretation, and inflammatory use of preliminary drafts.

Om heard him out. That mattered. If Om had cut him off, Kett would have worn censorship like a clean coat.

"Magistrate," Kett said, "the draft is not a casualty schedule. It is a repair stabilization aid. Priority in a repair budget cannot be equated with human worth."

Ira stood before Om could ask.

"Then let us test that."

Om nodded.

Bayo authenticated chain custody as evidence coordinator. Auditor Inoya certified the document's hash trail from Lael's production set. Lael testified that preliminary drafts often revealed excluded criteria more honestly than final settlement summaries because they had not yet been cleaned for institutional memory.

Kett cross-examined her hard.

"Preliminary drafts can also be inaccurate."

"Yes."

"Incomplete."

"Yes."

"Misleading without operational context."

"Yes."

"Then the tribunal should be cautious."

"Very."

He turned as if he had won.

Lael added, "Caution is not burial."

Om admitted the draft for limited purpose: timing, criteria, and comparison against later movement and salvage decisions, not final proof of any individual death.

Ira called Bayo for the sequence foundation because sometimes the person who found the thing needed to show how ugly it was.

"Mr. Anik, what is the time mark on the draft?"

"17:06 local."

"What significant records postdate 17:06?"

"Boundary response at 17:41:48. First correction burst. Public alarm at 17:43:31. Human instrument turbulence at 17:44:02. Most section-specific damage reports begin after 17:45."

"What significant records predate 17:06?"

"Hostile stress modeling, Quiet Directorate casualty class preparation at 16:58, and administrative readiness notices. No confirmed section damage reports in the tribunal record before 17:06."

"What criteria appear in the draft?"

Bayo read them.

Political continuity value.

Route impact.

Claim pressure.

Public visibility if lost.

Labor reconstitution cost.

Each phrase entered the chamber like a door closing.

"What criteria do not appear?"

"Damage severity. Oxygen remaining. Number of children. Medical dependency count. Actual casualty risk. Section pressure status."

"Compare the draft priorities to later casualty outcomes."

Kett objected to scope. Om allowed a limited comparison.

Bayo displayed the overlay.

Priority 1 and 2 sections received earliest repair carrier stabilization, evacuation routing support, or salvage protection. Priority 6 and 7 sections received hold notices, delayed movement, or salvage extraction without rescue attempt. Not perfectly. Not mechanically. But enough.

Cinder Nine dependent dormitories: Priority 6, guardian chain unresolved, cargo confirmation, high loss.

Lower A nursery spillover: Priority 6, load group conversion, severe loss.

Unbonded dependent dormitories: Priority 7, dependent motion suppression, no advised movement.

Outer maintenance ring: Priority 5, repair labor retention, partial survival, later classified essential duty.

Marr looked at Ira from counsel table.

Go slow.

Ira did.

"Mr. Anik, does the overlay prove the draft caused the deaths?"

"No."

"Does it prove final command implementation?"

"No."

"What does it prove?"

Bayo swallowed. "That before damage reports arrived, the sections later abandoned or delayed were already ranked low by criteria unrelated to immediate damage."

"Say the criteria plainly."

"Political value. Route value. claim pressure. Visibility. Labor replacement cost."

"Were the dead scheduled last?"

Kett stood. "Objection. Argumentative."

Om sustained.

Ira nodded. "Withdrawn."

She did not need the sentence as answer. The table had already said it.

Kett cross-examined Bayo with competence.

"Priority 7 in a repair budget may simply mean fewer repair resources should go there because other locations stabilize more systems."

"Yes."

"That could save more lives overall."

"Yes."

"A nursery spillover may have low repair priority because it lacks system leverage."

Bayo's jaw tightened. "Yes."

"So the document could reflect grim engineering triage, not political abandonment."

"If it had engineering criteria, yes."

Kett paused.

Bayo continued, "But this draft did not rank by system leverage alone. It ranked by political continuity value and claim pressure."

"Claim pressure can affect evacuation disorder."

"Yes."

"Public visibility if lost can affect civil panic."

"Yes."

"Labor reconstitution cost can affect survival after disaster."

"Yes."

Kett moved closer. "Then each criterion has operational relevance."

Bayo looked at the table, then at him.

"So did calling children cargo."

Kett objected to the answer. Om struck it from the transcript. The gallery heard it anyway.

Ira called Nemi Aral next, against Marr's first instinct and with her final permission.

Nemi took the oath as civil logistics counsel turned reluctant witness. Kett objected to counsel-testimony conflicts. Om permitted limited testimony about inherited office records after Nemi waived participation for that segment.

Ira asked, "Counsel Aral, do you recognize the criteria?"

"Yes."

"From where?"

"Postwar civil logistics continuity manuals."

"Are they still used?"

Nemi looked toward the station doors, where the suspended oxygen notice had become its own shadow.

"Some have descendants."

"Explain descendants."

"Political continuity value became governance continuity exposure. Claim pressure became claim complexity. Public visibility if lost became public confidence risk. Labor reconstitution cost remains mostly unchanged in some industrial emergency manuals."

"Are those always improper?"

"No."

"When are they improper?"

Nemi took a breath. "When they are used before human danger is honestly assessed, or when they substitute institutional inconvenience for human need."

Ira let that sentence stand.

Marr wrote it down.

Kett declined cross because any question would either help Nemi explain or make him look like he was defending institutional inconvenience as moral category.

Then Mara Venn stood again.

Om saw her and said, tiredly, "Ms. Venn, this is not an open forum."

"No," she said. "It is a room where tables keep finding my children."

He closed his mouth.

Ira did not call her. Om did.

The oath again.

Mara looked at the priority table on the wall.

"A-3 and A-4 were not valuable enough."

Kett objected.

Om said, "Sustained as conclusion. Witness may describe her understanding."

"My understanding is that A-3 and A-4 were not valuable enough."

No one laughed. No one breathed easily.

Ira approached gently. "Ms. Venn, before today, had anyone shown you a priority ranking from 17:06?"

"No."

"Had anyone told you dependent dormitories were assigned low repair priority before public alarm?"

"No."

"What were you told?"

"That damage moved faster than help. That rescue could not reach us in time. That confusion made lists unreliable. That everyone did the best they could after knowing too little too late."

"And now?"

Mara looked at Priority 6.

"Now I learn they knew enough early to put us at the bottom."

Kett objected again. Om sustained again. The record became a fence around something everyone could still see.

Lael Shun asked permission to add a limited expert observation. Om allowed it over objection.

"Pre-event ranking does not by itself prove intent to kill," Lael said. "But it defeats one settlement myth: that abandonment order emerged only after unavoidable damage made choices obvious. This document shows a value structure waiting for damage."

Ira felt the phrase take hold.

A value structure waiting for damage.

That was what the Quiet Directorate file had been. What Pell's lie had softened. What the oxygen notice had echoed. What the Orison node had refused to become.

Om ordered the draft preserved, expanded production to all pre-damage priority instruments, and required parties to produce any document ranking Cinder Gate sections before 17:41 by criteria other than direct hazard. He also ordered a public version of the ruling with the table redacted only for surviving personal identifiers, not institutional categories.

Kett requested stay.

Om denied.

Kett requested seal.

Om denied in part.

Kett requested cautionary language that preliminary repair priority is not casualty order.

Om granted it.

Ira was glad. The truth did not need overstatement. Overstatement gave the powerful something to kill.

After session, she stood before the evidence wall alone. Bayo had left the priority table up beside the sequence chart. The rows were simple enough for a child to understand and complicated enough for institutions to litigate for a decade.

Marr joined her.

"You almost asked the chapter title."

"The dead were scheduled last?"

"Yes."

"I withdrew it."

"Good."

"Because it was unfair?"

"Because it was unnecessary."

Ira looked at Priority 7.

"Sometimes I hate law for making the strongest sentence inadmissible."

Marr's expression softened. "Sometimes the strongest sentence is closing argument. You are not there yet."

Ira nodded.

In the corridor, the signs changed again. Someone added numbers beside names. Priority numbers. Not all accurate. Some guessed. Some angry. Some painfully exact because families had begun doing what institutions had delayed for eighty years: putting human names beside administrative order.

SIVIN VALE, AGE SIX, PRIORITY 7?

ESTA SOL, TEACHER, WHAT PRIORITY?

A-4 WAS NOT LOW VALUE.

Ira did not stop them.

Neither did security.

For once, the corridor was better at evidence than the archive.

Chapter 34: A Warship Without a War Returns

The warship arrived as a pension dispute.

Bayo found it in the Ash Belt naval law archive at a time of night when legal categories began to look like insults. He had been searching for pre-damage priority instruments, following Lael's phrase about value structures waiting for damage, when a retired crew petition from the demilitarized carrier Sable Knife surfaced under fuel reimbursement, crew disability, and "noncombat emergency authority clarification."

The ship name made Marr look up.

"Sable Knife was decommissioned before Cinder Gate."

"Demilitarized," Bayo said. "Not dead."

Ira opened the file.

The petition was signed by nineteen surviving crew members, none of them officers, all of them old enough now to have outlived the institutions that had told them to keep quiet. Their representative was Tovin Rees, former warrant engineer, later station school mechanic, currently suing the Ash Belt Naval Pension Board for denying war injury benefits on the ground that Sable Knife was not legally at war when it responded to Cinder Gate.

Marr read the first page and made a low sound.

"What?" Ira asked.

"If they were not at war, their command could not invoke combat salvage authority."

Bayo said, "And if they did invoke it?"

Marr scrolled to the attached log.

"Then someone used war powers without a war."

The room changed around that sentence.

At 06:10, Ira requested emergency production from the Ash Belt Naval Pension Board. At 06:19, the board denied relevance. At 06:27, Lael Shun sent one message to the board copying Auditor Inoya and Magistrate Om:

Your denial appears historically adventurous.

At 06:41, the board produced the file.

Marr read the message three times. "I definitely like her."

The pension file contained crew injury records, command fragments, demilitarization certificates, and one audio transcript from Sable Knife's auxiliary bridge at 17:39 Cinder local. The ship had been attached to a repair carrier convoy, stripped of legal combat status after the armistice, and licensed only for emergency engineering support. Its weapons were cold, sealed, and supposedly irrelevant.

Its command codes were not.

The transcript opened with alarm noise and someone named Rees saying, "We are not a warship."

The commander answered, "We are whatever keeps the gate from killing the Belt."

Then the legal officer said the phrase that brought the file into Ira's case:

Invoking Ash Belt Naval Emergency Salvage Law, hostile boundary exception, priority extraction and containment authority.

Marr sat down.

Bayo whispered, "Containment."

Ira looked at the time.

17:39.

Before correction burst.

Before public alarm.

Before Cinder Nine knew what name danger would wear.

Om convened the hearing at 11:00. The pension board sent counsel who looked offended to be in a room where old filings had become alive. The military repair carrier advocate arrived with two assistants and no expression. Kett looked relieved because a new institution had entered the blast radius.

Tovin Rees appeared by relay from a small Ash Belt habitat whose background was cluttered with pipe insulation, hanging tools, and a wall of children's drawings. He had broad hands, a patched throat implant, and the wary dignity of a man who had spent decades being told his injury was administrative.

The clerk swore him.

Rees looked at the oath text on his screen. "Do I get pension credit for telling the truth?"

Om said, "Not from this tribunal."

"Then I'll do it out of habit."

Ira liked him immediately, which meant she watched herself carefully.

Marr handled foundation.

"Mr. Rees, what was your role aboard Sable Knife during the Cinder Gate emergency?"

"Warrant engineer, life support and hull pressure."

"Was Sable Knife legally commissioned as a warship?"

"No."

"What was its status?"

"Demilitarized repair escort. We hauled tools, sealed weapons, and old officers who missed being obeyed quickly."

Kett objected to commentary. Om sustained. Rees did not apologize.

Marr continued. "Did Sable Knife respond to Cinder Gate?"

"Yes."

"Under what authority?"

Rees laughed once, and the throat implant flattened the sound. "Depends which hearing you ask. Pension board says engineering support. Command log says hostile boundary exception. Settlement summary says voluntary repair assistance. My spine says combat maneuver under illegal acceleration."

The gallery murmured.

Marr displayed the auxiliary bridge transcript.

"Is this accurate?"

"Parts. The transcript omits alarms and one officer vomiting."

"But the words?"

"Yes."

"Who said, 'We are not a warship'?"

"I did."

"Why?"

"Because the commander ordered combat salvage posture, and we had spent two years being told we were no longer combat anything."

"What did combat salvage posture permit?"

The military advocate stood. "Magistrate, operational law."

Om looked at him. "That is not an objection."

"Security."

Lael Shun, from the observer row, said, "Historical conduct."

Om said, "The witness may answer generally."

Rees leaned closer to his relay. "It let command prioritize assets, seize salvage, override local civil movement, and classify zones as denied recovery if entry threatened mission stability."

Ira felt the sequence chart rearrange itself in her mind.

Denied recovery.

Containment salvage priority.

Hold.

Priority 7.

Marr asked, "Was there a declared war?"

"No."

"Was there a declared hostile engagement?"

"Not that we received."

"Was hostile boundary stress real?"

"Yes."

"Could it have killed people?"

"Yes."

"Did that make Sable Knife a warship?"

Rees looked away from the camera for a second. "No. But it made people speak to us like we were."

Kett cross-examined first because he wanted distance between routeholders and naval command.

"Mr. Rees, routeholder navigation did not command Sable Knife."

"No."

"Routeholders did not invoke Ash Belt Naval Emergency Salvage Law."

"Not on my bridge."

"Routeholders did not order combat salvage posture."

"No."

"So your testimony concerns military overreach, not routeholder liability."

Rees scratched his jaw. "Counsel, when a man in one uniform says war law and another man in a routeholder channel gives him coordinates, I do not know what liability you call the handshake."

Kett's eyes sharpened. "What routeholder channel?"

Bayo was already moving.

Marr looked at Ira.

There it was.

Ira rose. "Mr. Rees, did Sable Knife receive routeholder coordinates before invoking combat salvage posture?"

The military advocate objected. Kett objected to the answer being outside direct. The pension counsel objected because pension counsel had decided objection was safer than listening.

Om overruled all but security specifics.

Rees said, "Yes."

"What did they identify?"

"High-value stabilization nodes."

"Did those correspond to the 17:06 repair priority draft?"

Rees looked at the display as Bayo brought the draft up. His old eyes moved line by line.

"Not all. Enough to smell the same kitchen."

Om said, "Plainly."

"Priority 1 and 2, mostly. Command deck, liaison ring, carrier dock, seedstock, nav core storage. We were told those nodes preserved Belt continuity."

"Who used that phrase?"

"Bridge legal officer, reading from a packet."

"Do you have the packet?"

Rees smiled without pleasure. "That is why my pension is denied."

The file appeared in production ten minutes later, because Tovin Rees had kept a copy inside a school environmental control manual for thirty-six years. He claimed it was because pension boards lost things. Ira suspected it was because mechanics trusted machines more than archives.

The packet header:

ASH BELT NAVAL EMERGENCY SALVAGE LAW

Hostile Boundary Exception Guidance

Cinder Gate Continuity Preservation Attachment

Time mark: 17:34.

The attachment listed the same political continuity criteria as the repair draft with an added field:

Naval Override Justification.

Below it sat a routing block that had been hidden in the pension copy because the board had stamped it irrelevant to injury causation.

REQUEST ORIGIN: CIVIL LOGISTICS CONTINUITY DESK.

NAVIGATION CONFIRMATION: ROUTEHOLDER ADVISORY CHANNEL.

EMERGENCY LEGAL REVIEW: ASH BELT NAVAL AUXILIARY COUNSEL.

OPERATING STATUS: DEMILITARIZED ESCORT, TEMPORARY HOSTILE-BOUNDARY EXCEPTION REQUESTED.

Marr read the block once and then asked Bayo to enlarge the routing signatures.

They were not signatures in the formal sense. They were system acceptances, little stamps of passage from one office to another, each too small to look like command and too complete to be accidental. Civil logistics had asked for continuity protection. Routeholder advisory had confirmed which nodes mattered. Naval auxiliary counsel had supplied the old law. Sable Knife had received the result as if necessity had arrived already dressed in authority.

Ira said, "Mr. Rees, did anyone on the bridge understand this as a civil request?"

"No. It came in naval format."

"Did anyone understand it as routeholder direction?"

"No. It came through naval relay after route confirmation."

"Did anyone understand it as war authority?"

Rees looked tired. "Yes. That was the point of putting it in that shape."

The military advocate objected.

Om asked, "To the last sentence?"

"Speculation."

Om looked at Rees. "Do you have a foundation for intent?"

"No. I have a foundation for effect."

Om let that answer stand.

Ira wrote the distinction down. Intent could hide in sealed rooms. Effect crossed bridges, opened locks, closed corridors, and left a demilitarized ship speaking war over civilian air.

Ira felt the room tilt.

Marr asked the question.

"Mr. Rees, when did Sable Knife receive confirmed damage reports from Cinder Nine?"

"After 17:45."

"When did it receive this continuity preservation attachment?"

"17:34."

"Before damage."

"Before confirmed damage."

"Before public alarm."

"Yes."

"Did the attachment rank dependent dormitories?"

"Not by that name."

"How?"

Rees read from the display.

"Civilian low-continuity dependent clusters. No naval override recommended except riot containment or disease breach."

The phrase hit the chamber so hard that even Om did not immediately move.

Mara Venn stood in the gallery, then sat again as if her legs had remembered procedure before her heart did.

Ira could not look at her.

Kett did not object. Perhaps he understood that objecting to the phrase would make him its guardian.

The military advocate stood instead.

"Magistrate, this document requires classified review."

Auditor Inoya said, "It required classified review eighty years ago. Today it requires daylight with redactions where genuinely necessary."

The advocate turned toward her. "You are not qualified to assess Ash Belt naval security."

Rees laughed. "Neither were we, apparently."

Om struck the bench once.

Lael Shun asked to speak. Om allowed it.

"Ash Belt Naval Emergency Salvage Law was intended for active combat theaters, not post-armistice civil evacuation. The hostile boundary exception allowed temporary extension if an external hostile force created immediate operational threat. Its use here before confirmed damage, alongside political continuity criteria, suggests war law was used to give legal muscle to a pre-existing value ranking."

"Suggests," Kett said.

Lael turned her dark lens toward him. "Yes. That is why we are still in a tribunal rather than a sentencing chamber."

Om ordered the attachment preserved, ordered the Ash Belt Naval Pension Board to produce all Sable Knife materials, and ordered the military repair carrier office to identify who transmitted continuity preservation guidance to demilitarized vessels.

Then Rees asked if he could say one more thing.

Om permitted it.

Rees leaned into the relay. "We were not villains on that bridge. Some of us broke ourselves trying to keep sections alive. My best friend died sealing a recycler spine. The commander thought he was saving the Belt. Maybe he was saving parts of it. But when law tells a man he is at war, he stops asking civilians whether they consent to being terrain."

The chamber held the sentence.

Ira wrote it down exactly.

After Rees disconnected, the room remained quieter than usual. The case had gained another institution, another authority, another way necessity had put on an old uniform and walked through a civilian disaster.

Bayo added Sable Knife to the sequence chart:

17:34 continuity preservation attachment sent to demilitarized repair escort; 17:39 hostile boundary exception invoked; combat salvage posture used before public alarm and before confirmed Cinder Nine damage.

Marr edited "combat salvage posture used" to "combat salvage posture invoked."

"We do not yet know all use," she said.

Bayo nodded.

Ira looked at the line.

"Invoked is bad enough."

That evening, Tovin Rees sent one private note through tribunal relay:

Tell the station mothers some of us heard the children. We were told the sound was panic contamination.

Ira did not forward it immediately.

She printed it, sealed it, and placed it in the witness contact file for Mara Venn's consent.

Some messages were not evidence first.

Some were burdens looking for permission to land.

Chapter 35: The Archive Wants a Verdict

Glassward sent a petition with no signature and too many seals.

That was the first warning.

Oriole-of-Archive brought it to Evidence Review in person, which was the second. She had not left Sera's custody chamber since the halted examination except to answer direct tribunal orders. Now she stood at Ira's table wearing a gray archive mantle over her ordinary clothes, the mantle's edges threaded with tiny custody lights that changed color as it authenticated itself to the room.

Marr looked at the mantle. "That is formal."

"It is coercive," Oriole said.

Ira closed the Sable Knife production folder. "Against whom?"

"Against me first. Against Sera if I fail."

Bayo took one step away from the petition as if it might open by itself.

Oriole placed it on the table.

The title appeared in Glassward legal script and then translated into tribunal language:

Petition for Continuity Election Prior to Adjudicative Reliance

Ira read the title twice.

"Continuity election."

Oriole's face had gone hard. "They want Sera to choose which strand is legally her."

Marr said, "For what purpose?"

"Admissibility. Liability protection. Future custody. Survival."

No one spoke.

The word survival did not belong with petition titles. That never stopped institutions.

Ira opened the translation.

The petition argued that Sera Venn-3's fractured outputs could not sustain legal personhood across contested proceedings unless a primary continuity line was elected and certified. Without election, Glassward warned, Sera risked classification as an unstable derivative archive rather than a witness-person. The language was careful, grief-polished, and cruel in the way care could become cruel when administered by people responsible for preventing loss at scale.

Marr read over Ira's shoulder. "They are saying if she stays plural, she may not survive as a legal person."

"They are saying if she chooses, the other strands become evidentiary material," Oriole said. "Not person."

Bayo whispered, "Archive wants a verdict before the court does."

Oriole looked at him. "The archive always wants verdict. Verdict is cheaper than custody."

Om convened the petition hearing under partial seal because Glassward personhood doctrine involved living archive protocols not publicly released. The gallery hated the closure and understood it at the same time. That was becoming the tribunal's signature cruelty.

Sera was not present.

Ira objected to proceeding without her.

Oriole objected to bringing her.

Kett objected to any proceeding that would strengthen Sera's testimony without exposing it to cross-examination.

Lael Shun objected to the phrase "unstable derivative archive" as historically contaminated.

Auditor Inoya objected to everyone objecting before the petition had been read into a manageable record.

Om accepted the last objection first.

Glassward's remote council appeared as three figures in white-gold frames: Custodian Sen, Custodian Palr, and a legal intelligence that called itself Continuity Clerk Six. Ira disliked the intelligence immediately, then checked the dislike. It might be doing exactly what it had been built to do, which did not make it harmless.

Custodian Sen spoke.

"Glassward does not seek harm to Sera Venn-3."

Oriole said, "That is not evidence."

Om glanced at her.

She lowered her head. "Apologies."

Sen continued. "The tribunal's halted examination and subsequent preservation of incompatible outputs create a custodial emergency. If the witness is to remain a legal person rather than a contested evidentiary object, Glassward doctrine requires continuity election."

Ira stood. "Doctrine created for what situation?"

Continuity Clerk Six answered. Its voice was soft and genderless. "Forked testimony, archive repair after catastrophic duplication, inheritance disputes, medical continuity conflicts, criminal responsibility of restored persons, and posthumous reconstruction claims."

"Was it created for a maintenance child damaged by a wartime evacuation atrocity and then used as a witness against the institutions that classified her?"

The clerk paused for less than a second. "No exact precedent."

"Then do not say requires as if the law arrived before the harm."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, counsel is arguing against Glassward doctrine without expertise."

Om said, "Sustained as to form. Counsel Senn, ask questions."

Ira turned to Custodian Sen. "What happens if Sera elects primary continuity?"

Sen folded her hands. "The chosen line holds legal personhood. Nonchosen lines are preserved as internal archive material, available under custodial control."

"Can nonchosen lines speak?"

"Not independently in legal proceedings."

"Can they refuse?"

"They are not separately recognized."

"Can they suffer?"

The three Glassward frames did not move.

Oriole did.

Custodian Palr answered, "Suffering is not the test for continuity."

Ira felt the room sharpen.

"What is?"

"Coherent responsibility, stable self-reference, custody sustainability, and recognition by external legal systems."

"External systems like this tribunal."

"Yes."

"So Glassward wants Sera to become simpler because our law punishes her for being damaged."

Kett objected. Om sustained. Ira accepted it because the question had already landed where it needed to.

Marr took over before Ira's anger could make the next question worse.

"Custodian Sen, if Sera does not elect a continuity line, what are the consequences?"

"Custody review. Possible suspension of external testimony. Internal stabilization. In extreme cases, partition."

Bayo looked sick.

Marr's voice lowered. "Define partition."

Continuity Clerk Six answered. "Separation of unstable branches into noncommunicating archival states to prevent cascading identity harm."

"You mean silence."

"We mean preservation."

Oriole said, "You mean silence that files better."

Om warned her again, but softly.

Lael asked permission to question. Om allowed limited expert inquiry.

"Custodian Sen, has Glassward ever used continuity election to protect an archive from hostile legal exploitation?"

"Yes."

"Has it ever used continuity election to make a legally inconvenient person admissible?"

Sen did not answer quickly.

Lael's dark lens clicked.

"Yes," Sen said.

"Was that later criticized?"

"By you."

"By the Glassward Review Synod."

"Yes."

"What did the Synod say?"

Custodian Palr answered. "That continuity election under external adjudicative pressure risks converting care into evidence management."

Lael sat.

The sentence remained standing.

Ira understood then that Glassward was not simply villainous. That made it harder. They had learned from prior harm and were still repeating it because the alternative was ungovernable risk. Institutions loved governed harm.

Om asked Oriole for her position.

Oriole stepped forward. Her hands were steady in a way her face was not.

"I oppose election."

Custodian Sen looked wounded. "Oriole."

"No. You sent a formal mantle and an unsigned petition. Do not use my name like intimacy remains uncontested."

The remote frame flickered.

Oriole continued. "Sera's strands are not convenient. They are not stable under ordinary process. They are not easy to defend. But election now would make one line survive by turning the others into material. That repeats the tribunal's central harm: choosing administrative survivability over damaged truth."

Om asked, "Can Sera continue without election?"

"Yes."

The Glassward council reacted.

Oriole lifted her chin. "With cost."

"To whom?"

"To Sera. To custody. To the tribunal. To every party that must learn how not to force ordinary answers from extraordinary injury."

"Can Glassward bear that cost?"

Oriole looked at the three frames.

"It can. It does not want to."

Om asked, "What would custody require without election?"

Oriole had the answer ready, which meant she had been arguing with herself before the petition arrived.

"A plural custody ledger. Each strand remains linked to the others unless a specific harm event requires temporary separation. Questions are routed through a consent gate instead of a primary line. Adversarial prompts are translated into strand-safe form before presentation. Any answer carries an origin mark identifying which strand, overlap, silence, or conflict produced it. No unanswered conflict is cleaned into coherence for court convenience."

Continuity Clerk Six said, "That protocol is resource-heavy."

"Yes."

"It reduces evidentiary clarity."

"It reveals where clarity is false."

Custodian Sen said, "It may allow contradictory outputs to remain legally active."

Oriole turned toward the frame. "Sera is contradictory because she was preserved from a contradictory event. You cannot heal a broken record by deleting every edge that cuts the reader."

Kett rose halfway. "Magistrate, this proposed protocol would make cross-examination nearly impossible."

Marr answered before Ira did. "No. It would make bad cross-examination obvious."

Om looked at Oriole. "Could opposing counsel test reliability under your protocol?"

"Yes. Counsel may test source, consistency, external corroboration, harm response, and memory lineage. Counsel may not demand that Sera destroy internal dissent so the answer fits a familiar witness box."

Lael Shun added, "That distinction matters. Fragmentation can impair reliability. Forced integration can also falsify it."

The legal intelligence dimmed for a moment, consulting something or pretending to.

"Glassward cannot guarantee indefinite preservation under such conditions," it said.

Oriole's voice thinned but did not break. "Then say that honestly. Say resources, not doctrine. Say burden, not personhood."

Custodian Palr said, "That is unfair."

"Yes," Oriole said. "So is partition."

Kett rose for his own argument.

"Magistrate, routeholders take no position on Glassward internal custody except insofar as the tribunal cannot rely on a witness whose legal personhood is unresolved by her own custodians."

Ira turned slowly.

There it was.

Not cruelty. Use.

Kett continued. "If Glassward requires election for legal stability, the tribunal should not invent a more generous personhood doctrine merely because counsel Senn needs the witness."

Ira stood. "Sera pointed us to the second clock, the attribution method, and the exact danger the Quiet Directorate file prepared to exploit. I need the witness because the witness is evidence of the harm, not because she is convenient."

"A distinction without procedural value."

Auditor Inoya spoke. "It has procedural value if the procedure is not stupid."

Om looked at her.

"Professional conclusion," Inoya said.

Om seemed too tired to object to the phrasing.

Ira asked to enter Sera's prior sentence into this hearing's record:

Do not make me simple.

Kett objected as hearsay, slogan, emotional manipulation, and prior unsworn distress output.

Om admitted it for limited purpose: witness preference expressed during tribunal-supervised custody event, not proof of any factual matter.

The words appeared on the wall.

DO NOT MAKE ME SIMPLE.

Glassward's three frames stared at them, or performed the visual equivalent.

Continuity Clerk Six said, "Witness preference is relevant but not controlling."

Marr answered, "Neither is institutional exhaustion."

Om recessed for twenty minutes and returned with a ruling that sounded like it had aged him while he wrote it.

"Glassward's petition for mandatory continuity election is denied without prejudice."

Oriole's shoulders lowered by a fraction.

Om continued. "The tribunal will not require Sera Venn-3 to choose a single continuity line as a condition of preserving personhood or testimony. The tribunal recognizes that Glassward custody faces real stability concerns.
