It also recognizes that forced election at this stage risks converting a damaged witness into a more usable but less truthful legal object."

Kett rose.

Om raised a hand.

"I am not done."

Kett sat.

"Sera's future testimony remains subject to harm-limiting protocol, expert review, and adversarial challenge. No party may argue that refusal to elect continuity, by itself, renders her a nonperson, archive object, or legally unavailable witness. Glassward may seek emergency custody relief upon specific evidence of imminent harm. It may not obtain efficiency by preemptive simplification."

Continuity Clerk Six asked whether the tribunal claimed authority to override Glassward personhood doctrine.

Om said, "No. I claim authority to decide what this tribunal will demand from a witness before it hears her."

Custodian Sen asked whether Glassward remained responsible for costs.

Om said, "Yes."

Palr said, "That is not sustainable."

Oriole answered before Om could. "Neither was Sera."

No one spoke after that for several seconds.

The petition was sealed with public summary. The summary used careful language. It did not say Glassward tried to make Sera choose which self deserved to live in law. It said continuity election denied pending further review.

Outside, the corridor signs changed again anyway.

DO NOT MAKE US SIMPLE became:

DO NOT MAKE HER SIMPLE.

Ira found Oriole in the side hall after session. The archive mantle's lights had gone dull.

"Are you all right?" Ira asked.

Oriole gave her a look. "No."

"Is Sera?"

"Also no."

"Will Glassward retaliate?"

"Glassward will convene. That is worse. Retaliation is at least decisive."

Ira leaned against the wall. "I am sorry."

"For what?"

"Making your custody harder."

Oriole looked toward the sealed hearing room. "Harder is not the same as wrong."

It was the closest thing to forgiveness anyone had offered that day.

Later, in Evidence Review, Bayo added the ruling to the sequence chart under Sera protection:

Continuity election denied; tribunal refuses forced simplification for legal survival.

Marr changed "refuses" to "declines to require."

Bayo stared at her.

"Really?"

"Yes."

Ira looked at the edited line.

"Leave both in the working notes," she said. "Use Marr's version for court."

Bayo did.

That night Sera sent no message. No strand appeared. No archive tone. No joke from the third line.

Only silence.

Ira tried not to interpret it.

She failed.

Chapter 36: Tamsin Opens the Salvage Hold

Tamsin Vale asked for a private inventory room and got a public hearing instead.

She did not argue when Om refused privacy. That worried Ira more than Tamsin's anger would have. The salvage captain only nodded, removed one glove, then the other, and placed both on the witness table as if she were surrendering tools before surgery.

"There are personal effects," Tamsin said. "Some families should not see them without choice."

Om looked at survivor liaison, then at Marr, then at the public gallery where people had begun learning to brace before evidence arrived.

"The tribunal will hear inventory foundation in public," he said. "Individual items will be displayed only after family notice and consent where identifiable."

Tamsin accepted that too.

Kett rose with an objection so formal it sounded tired of itself. "Magistrate, salvage possession is not chain of custody."

Tamsin looked at him. "No. Sometimes it is theft with storage discipline."

Om warned her. The warning had no force. Everyone knew the sentence had already done its work.

The salvage hold was not physically on Sable Array. It orbited two transfer points away inside Tamsin's old carrier, a patched vessel named Mercy of Liars because salvage crews preferred honest names for dishonest work. The hold appeared by tribunal relay as a narrow chamber lined with pressure lockers. Each locker bore a number, a section mark, and a red tag that read DO NOT DECLARE.

Bayo stared at the tag. "Declare to whom?"

Tamsin heard him. "To the people who paid us."

Marr leaned forward. "Who paid you?"

"Routeholder settlement intermediaries. Salvage courts. One military repair office. Two private memorial brokers. And an anonymous account that stopped paying after I refused to burn the hold."

The gallery reacted to burn.

Om said, "Captain Vale, clarify."

"The contract required destruction of unclaimed personal debris after evidentiary review."

"Did review occur?"

"No."

"Did you destroy the debris?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Tamsin looked at the relay image of the hold. "Because debris does not wrap a child's tooth in cloth."

No one spoke for several seconds.

Ira felt the case leave abstraction again. It had been files, clocks, priority tables, naval authority, archive doctrine. Now it was going to become things people had touched.

Tamsin opened the first locker.

The camera moved inside.

Objects rested in vacuum-safe trays: a cracked ration scanner, three woven hair bands, a strip of nursery hammock fabric, a small ceramic bell, a teacher's stylus with teeth marks along the side, a pressure tag labeled A-4 unresolved, two prayer speaker copper coils, a child's boot, one half-melted name patch reading SIV.

Jun Vale-of-Cinder's relay was not active. Mara Venn was present.

Mara made a sound Ira had never heard from a human throat.

Om halted display.

Mara stood. "No."

"Ms. Venn," Om said gently.

"Do not close it."

"You may step out."

"I stepped out eighty years ago because they told me to hold the next group calm." Her voice shook. "Do not close it."

Om looked to survivor liaison. The liaison nodded once.

The display remained.

Tamsin did not look away. That was perhaps the only courage available to her.

Ira approached the witness table. "Captain Vale, how did these items come into your possession?"

"Lower A salvage extraction. We were ordered to strip reusable material before pressure rot. Personal debris was to be separated, screened for high-value identifiers, and destroyed if unclaimed."

"Did you separate it?"

"Yes."

"Did you screen it?"

"Not the way they meant."

"Explain."

"High-value identifiers meant officer seals, routeholder property markers, insured cargo tags, navigational cores, anything with claim value. I kept everything with a name, partial name, handmade mark, or evidence of being carried by a person."

"Why?"

Tamsin's mouth tightened. "At first because I was angry."

"At whom?"

"The clerk who called a nursery hammock reusable fiber."

The gallery shifted.

"Later?"

"Because I understood the hold was the only place where the categories had failed."

Ira let that stand before asking, "Failed how?"

"A table could call them low continuity. A notice could call them unresolved. A salvage writ could call them debris. But the things would not behave. A boot stayed a boot. A bell stayed a bell. The name patch stayed almost a name."

Kett objected to narrative. Om overruled with a look that suggested even patience had begun taking sides.

Marr took over foundation. "Captain Vale, did anyone instruct you to destroy the hold?"

"Yes."

"Who?"

"Salvage court auditor first. Then routeholder settlement intermediary. Then anonymous account through black relay."

"Do you have records?"

"Yes."

"Produce them."

Tamsin opened another file.

Payment instructions, destruction schedules, compliance reminders, and one message written in language too casual to have understood what it touched:

The personal material complicates clean closure. Dispose after identifier harvest.

Lael Shun, observing from the side, closed her eye.

Om ordered the destruction instructions preserved.

Kett stood. "Magistrate, there is no evidence routeholder parties in this proceeding authored the anonymous instruction."

Ira said, "No one said they did."

"The implication is obvious."

Marr answered, "Then perhaps stop recognizing yourself in unnamed conduct."

Om's gavel struck before Kett's reply formed.

"Counsel Marr."

"Withdrawn."

It was not withdrawn. Very little in this tribunal ever was.

Tamsin opened more lockers.

The tribunal did not display everything. It logged. It sealed. It notified. It let family representatives choose whether to view. But some items had no family left in the chamber and had to enter as unidentified human property. That phrase became unbearable by the fourth tray.

Unidentified human property: hand-stitched sleeve.

Unidentified human property: maintenance child's drawing slate.

Unidentified human property: adult wedding cord fused to pressure tag.

Unidentified human property: toy navigation wheel.

Bayo stopped typing once. Marr took over until he could resume.

Survivor liaison requested a pause before the next tray.

Om granted it.

No one left. That was not courage exactly. It was the stunned discipline of people afraid that absence might become another kind of abandonment.

The liaison stood at the evidence station and drafted a viewing grid in real time:

ITEM HAS POSSIBLE FAMILY IDENTIFIER.

ITEM HAS PARTIAL NAME.

ITEM HAS LOCAL SECTION MARK ONLY.

ITEM HAS NO IDENTIFIER BUT HUMAN USE IS APPARENT.

ITEM IS TOO DAMAGED FOR PUBLIC DISPLAY.

Then she added a column the old settlement forms had never contained:

FAMILY MAY DECLINE KNOWLEDGE WITHOUT LOSING CLAIM.

Kett objected to the word claim. "The tribunal has not determined compensable status."

The liaison looked at him with the dead patience of someone who had spent too many years translating grief into forms. "I did not say money. I said claim."

Om let the column stand.

Ira watched Tamsin read it. The salvage captain's face changed at the last line, not with relief, but with recognition of a cruelty she had not known she had committed. Keeping the hold intact had preserved choice in one direction and destroyed it in another. Families had been denied the right not to know, the right to know later, the right to receive a name without a courtroom watching, the right to refuse a fragment that would make a death newly sharp.

Mara Venn raised her hand from the gallery.

Om said, "Ms. Venn?"

"Add one more."

The liaison waited.

Mara's voice was rough. "Family may ask that an item be named without being shown."

The liaison typed it.

Tamsin closed her eyes.

For the first time that day, the tribunal did not merely preserve evidence. It made room around it.

Ira asked Tamsin, "Why reveal this now?"

"Because the priority table made me understand what the hold was."

"What was it?"

"Not guilt. Not absolution. Counter-inventory."

"To what?"

"To every list that ranked them without names."

Om asked, "Captain, did you alter any item?"

"No."

"Did you remove any for private use?"

Tamsin's face hardened. "No."

"Did you withhold them from families?"

"Yes."

The answer entered sharply.

Ira asked, "Why?"

"Because if I announced the hold, settlement offices would seize it, clean it, return selected items with apology language, destroy the rest under hygiene order, and call me unstable. I told myself keeping it intact was better."

"Was it?"

Tamsin looked at Mara Venn.

"I don't know."

Mara answered from the gallery. "You kept Sivin's patch from fire."

Tamsin flinched.

Mara continued, "You also kept it from us."

"Yes."

"Both matter."

"Yes."

The tribunal had become good at holding both by now. That did not make it easier.

Om ordered a family consent protocol for salvage hold review, independent preservation of all items, and immediate suspension of any destruction instruction related to Cinder Gate personal material. He also ordered production from memorial brokers, salvage courts, routeholder settlement intermediaries, and military repair offices associated with disposal payments.

Kett objected to breadth.

Om said, "The objection is preserved. The personal material is preserved first."

After hearing, Tamsin remained at the witness table while people left in uneven silence.

Ira approached.

"Captain."

"Do not ask whether I feel relieved."

"I wasn't going to."

"Good. I feel robbed."

"Of what?"

"The lie that I was protecting the hold from everyone else."

Ira did not answer.

Tamsin looked at the blank evidence wall. "Some of those things should have been in hands decades ago. Some would not have survived if they had been. Both are true and neither forgives me."

"Mara said both matter."

"Mara is more generous than law."

"Mara is more precise than law."

Tamsin almost smiled and failed.

In Evidence Review, Bayo added the salvage hold to the sequence chart:

Post-event counter-inventory: personal effects preserved against destruction instructions; evidence of name-bearing material classified for disposal after identifier harvest.

Marr did not edit it.

That was how Ira knew the line was clean enough.

Later, when family consent review began, Mara Venn requested to see the half-melted SIV patch first. Ira was present only as counsel observer. Tamsin was not permitted in the room. That was right and also cruel.

Mara touched the display glass, not the patch.

"Sivin Vale," she said.

The name entered the item log.

Not as cargo.

Not as load.

Not as unresolved.

As a child who had owned a piece of cloth and a name large enough to survive fire, theft, silence, and the long cold patience of a salvage hold.

Chapter 37: Children of the Red Algorithm Echo

The Mars records arrived because Nemi Aral broke a rule.

She did not announce it that way. Civil logistics counsel never used dramatic language when precise cowardice would do. She sent a sealed packet to the tribunal at 05:12 with a cover note that read:

Possible derivative lineage material. Produced under protest by my office before protest can be drafted by others.

Marr read the note and said, "That is almost a confession of professional ethics."

The packet contained predictive labor allocation records from Mars reconstruction zones, years after Cinder Gate, decades before Ira was born. The files belonged to the Red Algorithm Echo, a civil planning system used to assign children of displaced families into apprenticeship, oxygen maintenance, tunnel agriculture, med-porter work, and what the records called "low-disruption labor futures."

Ira had heard of the Red Algorithm Echo in school. Everyone had. It was taught as a cautionary system from the early reconstruction era: too deterministic, too class-bound, eventually dismantled after labor riots and a famous set of hearings in Valles Marineris. The lessons had been clean. Bad algorithm, reform, better oversight.

The tribunal packet made the lesson bleed.

Bayo opened the first comparison table.

Columns from Cinder Gate:

Guardian chain unresolved.

Local claimant only.

Medical nontransfer.

Labor reconstitution cost.

Public visibility risk.

Claim pressure.

Columns from Mars:

Custody chain unresolved.

Settlement-local dependent.

Medical mobility cost.

Labor recovery value.

Public confidence exposure.

Claim complexity.

No one spoke.

Marr sat down before reading the next page. "How did Nemi get this?"

"Civil logistics inherited Mars reconstruction templates," Bayo said. "The packet says they were archived under workforce stabilization."

Ira looked at the mapped terms. They were not identical. That made them worse. Dead terms could be copied. Living terms adapted.

Lael Shun arrived without being called, as if the building had learned to summon her by shame.

She read the table once.

"There," she said.

"There what?" Ira asked.

"Living lineage."

Om convened hearing at 09:40. Nemi Aral appeared not at counsel table but in the witness chair, because her production had made her office a source. She looked like someone who had decided to walk into a fire before being pushed and found the difference morally insufficient.

Kett objected to Mars relevance. The routeholder parties, he argued, had no operational control over reconstruction algorithms used decades later on another world. The tribunal should not expand Cinder Gate into a history of all administrative harm.

Ira expected Om to agree in part.

He did.

"This tribunal will not try the Red Algorithm Echo," Om said. "It will determine whether Mars records are relevant to method lineage, settlement closure, and inherited benefit. Counsel Senn, proceed narrowly."

Narrowly.

The word had become either discipline or trap depending on who held it.

Ira stood before Nemi.

"Counsel Aral, did your office produce the Mars predictive labor records?"

"Yes."

"Why?"

"Because the category mappings match Cinder Gate-derived civil logistics templates closely enough that withholding them would mislead the tribunal."

"Were these mappings known to your office before this proceeding?"

"Known as reconstruction-era terminology, yes. Known as potential Cinder Gate lineage, no."

"What changed?"

Nemi looked toward the priority table still preserved in evidence. "The 17:06 draft. The Quiet Directorate file. The public safety template. Together they made the Mars records look less like parallel bureaucracy and more like inheritance."

Kett rose. "Speculation."

Om said, "Sustained as conclusion. Counsel, establish basis."

Ira nodded. "What is the basis for possible inheritance?"

Nemi displayed metadata.

"Template origin strings. Civil logistics language desk markers. Continuity risk field names. Redacted source code comments referencing QD delay classes. And this."

She enlarged a single line from an early Mars implementation guide:

Do not identify low-yield dependents by name in first-pass allocation; cluster-level review reduces appeal drag.

The chamber went cold.

Mara Venn was not present. Ira was grateful again, ashamed again.

Lael said quietly, "Appeal drag."

Om looked at her. "Ms. Shun."

"Apologies."

Nemi continued. "The system did not decide who died. Mars was reconstruction, not evacuation. But it decided which children were routed into futures that were harder to escape."

"What kinds of children?" Ira asked.

Nemi read the fields.

Custody chain unresolved.

Settlement-local dependent.

Low sponsor leverage.

Medical mobility cost.

Labor recovery value below threshold.

"Were names used?"

"Not in first pass."

"Why?"

Nemi looked at the implementation guide.

"To reduce appeal drag."

The phrase had nowhere to hide now.

Ira asked for the example row.

Kett objected. Om allowed one redacted row for foundation only.

Nemi displayed it with the name field still sealed:

FIRST PASS: CLUSTER K-RED-14. CUSTODY CHAIN UNRESOLVED. LOW SPONSOR LEVERAGE. LABOR RECOVERY VALUE BELOW THRESHOLD. APPEAL RISK LOW.

SECOND PASS AFTER WORK PERFORMANCE: AGRICULTURE TRACK ACCEPTABLE. SCHOOL ACCESS CONDITIONAL ON QUOTA. INDIVIDUAL REVIEW DEFERRED.

FINAL REVIEW, AGE SIXTEEN: PRODUCTIVE PLACEMENT CONFIRMED. PRIOR EDUCATIONAL DEPRIVATION NOT COMPENSABLE; NO TIMELY APPEAL.

The row was not spectacular. No death order. No scream. No black seal. Just a child becoming a cluster, a cluster becoming labor, labor becoming proof that the earlier narrowing had worked well enough to excuse itself.

Ira asked, "Was the lack of timely appeal later used to deny correction?"

Nemi's face tightened. "Yes."

"After the first pass suppressed names to reduce appeal drag."

"Yes."

"So the system created the condition it later treated as procedural peace."

Kett stood. "Argument."

Om sustained.

Nemi answered anyway, softly enough that the transcript nearly missed it. "That is what it did."

The clerk looked at Om.

Om said, "The last answer is stricken as nonresponsive."

Everyone had heard it.

Kett cross-examined with the careful aggression of a man defending a boundary everyone could see was morally inadequate.

"Counsel Aral, the Red Algorithm Echo did not classify children for death."

"No."

"It allocated reconstruction labor."

"Yes."

"Mars faced oxygen scarcity, housing instability, food production collapse, and postwar orphan populations."

"Yes."

"Labor assignment was necessary to survival."

"Some assignment, yes."

"And the system was later reformed."

"Yes."

"So this tribunal is hearing about a flawed reconstruction tool, not Cinder Gate."

Nemi took a breath.

"It is hearing about a flawed reconstruction tool built from categories that may descend from Cinder Gate."

"May."

"Yes."

"No further."

It was a good cross. It put the gap back in the room. Kett lived in gaps. But now the gap had a label.

May.

Ira called Lael for expert lineage.

Lael did not overstate. That made her useful and maddening.

"The Mars records do not prove direct transfer by themselves," she said. "They do show semantic continuity, field continuity, and institutional benefit. The same administrative logic appears adapted from emergency delay to reconstruction allocation: reduce names, cluster vulnerable dependents, prioritize institutional continuity, treat appeal as friction."

Om asked, "Is that enough for production?"

"Yes."

"For liability?"

"Not by itself."

"For history?"

Lael's dark lens clicked.

"History is less afraid of patterns than liability is."

Kett objected to the sentence. Om sustained it and ordered it marked as expert commentary outside legal conclusion. Ira wrote it down anyway.

Then the tribunal heard from a Mars witness.

Her name was Senna Ro, former Red Echo child, now a tunnel agronomist with hands scarred by decades of mineral cuts. She appeared by relay from a greenhouse under low orange light.

"I was not at Cinder Gate," she said before anyone asked.

Om nodded. "We understand."

"No," Senna said. "You don't. People always think if you were not at the original wound, you should be grateful your scar is smaller."

The chamber quieted.

Ira asked, "What was your Red Echo classification?"

"Settlement-local dependent, custody unresolved, labor recovery low. Later corrected to medium after I fixed a pump no one thought I understood."

"Were you assigned work?"

"Tunnel algae scrape at nine. Oxygen wick sorting at eleven. Agriculture apprenticeship at twelve. School if quota allowed."

"Were you named in the first-pass allocation?"

"No."

"How do you know?"

Senna held up a copy of her file. "I spent twenty years getting the unredacted version. First pass called me Cluster K-Red-14, female probable, custody unresolved, appeal risk low."

"Appeal risk low?"

"No one with standing to complain."

Ira felt the old phrases line up behind the new one.

Guardian chain unresolved.

Claim pressure low.

No immediate challenge.

Appeal risk low.

Senna looked into the relay. "I am not dead. I have a life. I grow food in rock. I am proud of that. Do not use me as proof that the system was murder. It was not. It was a narrowing. It found children nobody could appeal for and made their futures cheaper."

That was the sentence that made the Mars records enter the room.

Not as distraction.

As afterlife.

Om allowed limited admission for method lineage and ordered all civil logistics records connecting Cinder Gate, postwar settlement, Mars reconstruction, and predictive labor allocation preserved. He barred argument that Mars harm proved Cinder Gate liability. He allowed argument that shared logic could show living lineage.

No one was satisfied.

That had become the smell of useful rulings.

After the hearing, Nemi Aral found Ira outside Evidence Review.

"My office will hate me."

"Yes."

"Some of them should."

"Also yes."

Nemi looked exhausted. "I kept thinking civil logistics inherited mess. Not guilt. Mess. Then I saw appeal drag."

Ira said, "Mess can be inherited guilt if people benefit from not cleaning it."

Nemi nodded slowly. "That sounds like something I will regret hearing."

"Most useful sentences do."

Bayo added the Mars line to the sequence chart:

Postwar lineage evidence: Red Algorithm Echo predictive labor categories mirror Cinder Gate delay logic; names suppressed at first pass to reduce appeal drag.

Marr changed "mirror" to "substantially resemble."

Bayo did not even sigh this time.

That evening, the corridor signs gained red dust drawn in cheap pigment around the names from Cinder Gate. Under them someone had written:

THE CHILDREN WHO LIVED WERE ALSO SORTED.

Ira stood before it, thinking of Sivin Vale's patch and Senna Ro's greenhouse hands.

The case had begun with the abandoned dead.

Now the living were arriving with records of what abandonment learned to become.

Chapter 38: Ira Loses the Simple Case

Ira's simple case died at 14:16.

It died politely, in a ruling with numbered paragraphs, after three days of evidence had made it emotionally inevitable and legally insufficient. That was law's particular talent: refusing to become easier just because everyone in the room finally understood where grief wanted to go.

The motion had been Marr's idea and Ira's appetite.

They filed it after the Mars hearing, not because they believed it would end the tribunal, but because the record now supported a narrow finding: Commander Halden Rusk, dead since 2368, had invoked emergency authorities, accepted continuity rankings, permitted casualty-class language, and presided over Cinder Gate's operational abandonment. A limited liability ruling against his command estate would give survivors something concrete. A name. A finding. A sentence the public could hold.

Kett did not oppose as fiercely as Ira expected.

That should have warned her.

The dead commander could absorb a great deal. He had no counsel with a future, no office with current contracts, no shareholders, no heirs with meaningful power, and no ability to embarrass living institutions except by association. Routeholders were prepared to call him tragic. Civil logistics was prepared to call him overwhelmed. Military repair carriers were prepared to call him unauthorized. Settlement offices were prepared to call him an anomaly. Everyone could mourn a dead man if mourning him closed the door behind them.

Om saw it before Ira let herself.

He entered with the ruling already written.

"This tribunal addresses the motion for limited finding concerning Commander Halden Rusk's sole operational liability."

Sole.

The word sounded different when Om said it.

Ira sat very still.

Marr's expression did not change, which meant she had heard it too.

Om summarized the evidence. Rusk's command logs. Sable Knife. Hostile boundary exception. Continuity preservation attachment. Acceptance of repair priorities. Failure to provide accurate public notice. Use of combat salvage posture before confirmed section damage. He did not soften any of it. That made the ending worse.

"The record supports grave questions regarding Commander Rusk's conduct," Om said. "It may ultimately support findings against his command. But the motion asks more. It asks this tribunal to treat Rusk as the central bearer of liability in a manner that would narrow the proceeding around a dead decision-maker."

Kett looked down.

Nemi Aral closed her eyes.

The military advocate watched Om as if calculating which words could still be survived.

Om continued. "The evidence currently before the tribunal does not permit that simplification."

The gallery murmured.

Om struck the bench once.

"Order."

Ira felt heat rise in her face. Not embarrassment alone. Anger. She had known the motion was narrow. She had known it was partly strategic. She had not realized how badly she wanted the tribunal to say one person had held the knife.

Om read the core holding.

"Cinder Gate's abandonment, as presently evidenced, was produced by interacting systems: pre-event casualty class preparation, political continuity ranking, civil language substitution, routeholder navigation support, Ash Belt naval emergency authority, salvage extraction, post-event attribution management, and settlement closure. Commander Rusk may have acted unlawfully within that system. The system did not originate in him, and the record does not support assigning sole liability to his dead command."

Sole again.

This time it sounded like a door closing on comfort.

Kett stood after the ruling and did something more damaging than victory. He looked solemn.

"Routeholder parties appreciate the tribunal's refusal to turn tragedy into scapegoating."

Ira wanted to throw something heavy and procedural at his head.

Marr's hand touched her sleeve under the table.

Do not.

Kett continued. "We renew our position that liability must be tied to direct action, not historical atmosphere."

Lael Shun, from the observer row, said quietly, "Atmosphere is what people breathe."

Om looked at her. "Ms. Shun."

"Withdrawn."

Kett did not respond. He had what he needed for the moment: the simple finding denied, the dead commander not enough, the living institutions not yet pinned.

Om invited Ira to speak for preservation of remaining issues.

She stood because sitting would have looked like defeat and because it was defeat.

"The survivors do not oppose the tribunal's refusal to make the case simpler than the evidence."

Marr's head turned a fraction. That was not the sentence they had prepared.

Ira kept going. "We preserve all arguments concerning Commander Rusk's direct liability. We also accept that a dead commander cannot carry categories prepared before his order, language inherited after his death, settlement closure performed by others, or benefits still held by living institutions."

Kett rose. "Magistrate, counsel is arguing beyond the ruling."

"She is preserving scope," Om said. "Briefly."

Ira looked at the gallery.

Mara Venn was not there. Senna Ro was light-minutes away. Tamsin was in custody review. Sera was silent. The people present still carried all of them.

"The motion failed," Ira said. "The case did not."

She sat.

It was not elegant. It was accurate. Sometimes that had to be enough.

After session, the corridor did not know how to react. Some survivors were furious. Some were confused. Some had hoped for a name they could spit at. A dead commander's name would have done. It would have been a container. It would have meant the tribunal could finish one grief before opening the next.

Revan Sol found Ira near the evidence board.

"So no one did it," he said.

The accusation was exhausted rather than hostile.

"No," Ira said. "That is not what the ruling says."

"It says the dead man did not do it alone."

"Yes."

"Dead men are easier to punish."

"Yes."

He looked at her for a long time. "My wife is still dead."

"Yes."

"Do not let them make complicated mean nobody."

Ira felt the sentence enter deeper than any ruling.

"I won't."

"You said something like that to Sera."

"I did."

"You are collecting hard promises."

"I know."

Revan nodded, not satisfied, not expecting to be.

In Evidence Review, Bayo was angry enough to reorganize files alphabetically, which was how Ira knew he was close to useless.

"They will use this," he said.

"Yes."

"Kett is probably celebrating."

"Probably."

"The military office will say Rusk exceeded authority. Routeholders will say Rusk accepted guidance independently. Civil logistics will say language desks were subordinate. Settlement offices will say they closed what command gave them. Everyone gets smaller."

Marr entered behind him. "Then stop giving them one large target."

Bayo looked at her.

She pointed to the sequence chart. "The ruling tells us what to prove. Not sole liability. Operational benefit. Inherited authority. Coordinated reliance. Who used the system, who fed it, who closed it, who profited from it, who still invokes its descendants."

Ira stared at the chart.

Commander Rusk's name sat in the middle because they had put it there. Around him, lines reached backward to the Quiet Directorate file, sideways to routeholder channels, downward to salvage courts, forward to Mars, outward to Glassward, Shoals, Ash Belt law, Sable Array oxygen templates, settlement ledgers.

The dead commander had been a node.

Not the net.

Marr took the ruling from Ira's slate and marked four sentences in plain gray.

"This is what survives denial," she said.

Bayo leaned over. "That sounds like a depressing legal course."

"Most useful ones are." Marr tapped the first mark. "One: Rusk remains in. Two: sole liability is out. Three: system interaction is recognized. Four: the tribunal has not yet determined which interactions create present duty."

Ira read the marks again.

Rusk remains in.

Sole liability is out.

System interaction is recognized.

Present duty unresolved.

The shape steadied her more than comfort would have. Denial had not erased the evidence. It had removed the wrong container.

Bayo said, "Present duty is where they will fight."

"Yes," Marr said.

"Because current duty reaches current offices."

"Yes."

"And current offices hire current counsel."

Marr gave him a tired look. "You are learning."

"We need a beneficiary map," Ira said.

Bayo stopped reorganizing.

Marr nodded once. "Now you are thinking like the case you have, not the case you wanted."

That hurt because it was true.

Ira opened a new board.

Title:

Inherited Operational Benefit

She added columns:

Institution.

Action before event.

Action during event.

Action after event.

Benefit retained.

Current derivative.

Evidence source.

Bayo watched the columns appear and smiled for the first time that day.

"Kett will hate this."

"Kett will understand it," Marr said. "That is better."

Ira added the first row:

Routeholder navigation support.

Legacy channel conversion attempt.

Continuity routing.

Settlement exposure reduction.

Priority access and liability narrowing.

Current route advisory templates.

Sources: Orison trace, 17:06 draft, Sable Knife attachment, settlement ledgers.

Then civil logistics.

Then Ash Belt military repair.

Then salvage courts.

Then Glassward custody.

Then Sable Array public safety.

The board was ugly. Not morally ugly, though that too. Structurally ugly. It refused story shape. It looked like work.

That was why it might survive.

That evening, the public summary of Om's ruling posted. The corridor signs changed faster than security could preserve them.

RUSK WAS NOT ENOUGH.

COMPLICATED IS NOT NOBODY.

WHO BENEFITED?

The last sign appeared again and again.

Who benefited?

Who benefited?

Who benefited?

Ira stood before the words until they stopped looking like accusation and began looking like method.

She had lost the simple case.

At last, she could see the harder one.

Chapter 39: The Living Defendants Smile

The living defendants smiled with their mouths closed.

They did it in corridors, at counsel tables, inside carefully worded statements to the public feed. No one celebrated. Celebration would have been vulgar. They expressed respect for the tribunal's nuance, sorrow for the dead, appreciation for procedural rigor, concern about overextension, commitment to continued cooperation, and relief that grief had not been allowed to become scapegoating.

Every phrase arrived polished enough to reflect someone else's face.

Bayo collected them into a folder titled SMILES.

Marr renamed it Public Responses.

Bayo renamed his private copy SMILES AGAIN.

Ira did not tell him to stop.

The routeholder consortium's statement was the worst because it was best.

The tribunal has wisely recognized that complex disasters cannot be reduced to one villain. We remain committed to helping the Meridian community heal through fact-based process, not inherited blame.

"Inherited blame," Bayo said. "They are inoculating against inherited benefit."

Marr nodded. "Because they read your board."

"Our board."

"Ira's board. Your typography."

Ira looked at the public responses spread across the evidence table. Routeholders. Civil logistics. Ash Belt military repair. Salvage courts. Sable Array public safety. Glassward, more restrained, saying only that continuity doctrine remained under review. Each institution had found the same shelter in Om's ruling: complexity as fog.

They had mistaken her loss for their weather.

At 10:00, Om convened argument on scope after the Rusk ruling. Kett arrived relaxed. Not careless. Never that. But the tension around his eyes had eased. The military advocate spoke to him before session. The civil logistics table kept distance, but less than before. Even the pension board counsel looked hopeful, as if the dead commander might absorb enough gravity to pull Sable Knife away from them.

Ira watched them settle.

Marr leaned close. "Do not punish them for smiling."

"I wasn't going to."

"You were considering it."

"Only internally."

"Keep some things that way. It helps."

Ira glanced at her.

Marr did not smile.

Om opened the session. "The tribunal has denied the motion for sole liability. We now address the scope of remaining institutional inquiry."

Kett rose first.

Of course he did.

"Magistrate, the ruling properly recognizes that Commander Rusk cannot serve as a simplistic container for tragedy. But the same reasoning cautions against counsel Senn's attempt to expand this tribunal into a roving examination of every institution that touched Cinder Gate, postwar settlement, Mars reconstruction, Glassward custody, and present-day station management. Complexity is not license."

Ira wrote that down.

Complexity is not license.

Kett continued. "Routeholder parties propose a disciplined scope: direct operational decisions at Cinder Gate between 17:34 and 17:46, documents transmitted to command, and communications actually relied upon by decision-makers. Benefits, descendants, and alleged lineage should be excluded unless tied to direct command action."

It sounded reasonable. That made it dangerous.

Om turned to Ira. "Counsel Senn."

She stood with the inherited benefit board behind her. Not all of it. Marr had made her remove adjectives, color coding, and one arrow Bayo had labeled the snake eats its shed skin. What remained was columns. Institution. Action before event. During. After. Benefit retained. Current derivative. Evidence.

Ugly. Durable.

"The tribunal's ruling means Commander Rusk cannot bear sole liability because the evidence shows system interaction. Counsel Kett now asks the tribunal to preserve the first half of that reasoning and kill the second. He accepts complexity when it prevents a finding against the dead. He rejects it when it reaches living benefit."

Kett stood. "Objection. Mischaracterizes."

Om said, "Argument. Sit."

Kett sat.

Ira touched the first row.

"Inherited operational benefit is not inherited blame. Blame asks who should feel guilty. Benefit asks who retained advantage, authority, liability protection, access, money, doctrine, or institutional position because the harmful method was used and later closed without full review."

She let the distinction hang.

"The question is not whether routeholders personally abandoned Sivin Vale. The question is whether routeholder systems supplied continuity rankings, received priority routing, avoided later exposure through settlement closure, and still hold templates or privileges derived from that arrangement."

Kett's relaxation faded by one degree.

Ira moved to the next row.

"The question is not whether civil logistics murdered children. The question is whether its language desks converted delay classes into defensible categories, whether those categories softened settlement review, and whether descendants of that logic moved into Mars labor allocation or current public safety templates."

Nemi Aral looked down. Not in shame alone. Recognition.

"The question is not whether Ash Belt repair crews were villains. The question is whether war law was invoked without a war to give legal force to political continuity ranking, and who kept the benefits of that legal fiction."

The military advocate's jaw tightened.

"The question is not whether salvage captains were monsters. The question is whether salvage courts and intermediaries paid to destroy personal material after identifier harvest because clean closure had financial and legal value."

Tamsin was not present. Ira was glad. The row could stand without using her face.

"And the question is not whether Sable Array's public safety office intended to echo wartime coercion. The question is why present templates still reach for breath, queue clusters, and group descriptors when public order becomes inconvenient."

Om looked at the board for a long time.

Kett stood for response. "This is elegant theory. It is also functionally limitless. Any institution that survived after Cinder Gate can be accused of benefit. Any modern policy can be called derivative. Any settlement can be reopened because someone dislikes its moral atmosphere."

"Then propose a limiting principle," Om said.

Kett paused.

Ira saw it.

Marr saw it.

Bayo wrote: HE HAS NONE THAT SAVES HIM.

Kett recovered. "Direct reliance."

Om leaned back. "Define."

"An institution should remain within scope only if evidence shows it directly relied on a Cinder Gate method or record to obtain a concrete legal or operational advantage."

It was not bad. It was too narrow, but it was real.

Ira asked, "Would a reserve ledger count?"

Kett's eyes narrowed. "If tied to the event."

"A template?"

"If used to decide rights in the event."

"A public statement preserving a settlement position after notice?"

"No. Statements are advocacy."

"A current emergency form descended from a Cinder Gate category?"

"No. Too remote."

"A current emergency form with source comments naming the category?"

Kett hesitated. "Potentially discoverable. Not within liability."

Marr wrote the word potentially as if it were a door number.

Ira said, "That is why direct reliance is not enough. It catches the hand on the lever and misses the office that kept the lever installed after being told what it did."

Om looked from Ira to Kett. "Counsel Senn, your limiting principle must not become metaphor."

"Agreed."

That answer surprised Kett more than an objection would have.

Om turned to Ira. "Can you work with that?"

Marr's eyes warned her.

Do not reject discipline because you like reach.

Ira nodded slowly. "With modifications."

"State them."

"Direct reliance, knowing retention after notice, or current derivative use where lineage is supported by documents."

Kett objected immediately.

Om lifted a hand. "Again, slowly."

Ira repeated it.

"Direct reliance captures the original event. Knowing retention after notice captures settlement and postwar closure: once institutions had reason to know the method was contaminated, did they keep the benefit? Current derivative use captures living systems only where documentary lineage exists, not mere resemblance."

Lael Shun's dark lens clicked once.

Auditor Inoya made a note.

Nemi Aral stood. "Civil logistics can accept that as a production standard."

Every living defendant looked at her.

She did not sit.

"Not as liability standard," she added. "Production."

Om nodded. "Understood."

The military advocate refused. Routeholders refused. Salvage court counsel attempted a procedural answer so dull that Om stopped him midway and asked whether he had a position.

Glassward submitted a written statement accepting derivative-use inquiry where personhood protections were preserved.

Sable Array public safety asked whether current emergency templates could be reviewed in camera.

Inoya said, "If the camera has witnesses."

No one knew whether that was a joke. No one risked asking.

Om ruled near midday.

"The tribunal adopts a provisional scope for institutional inquiry: direct reliance on Cinder Gate methods or records; knowing retention of legal, operational, financial, or custodial benefit after notice of contamination; and current derivative use where documentary lineage supports more than resemblance. Parties will produce records accordingly. This is a production standard, not a liability finding."

Kett requested stay.

Denied.

Military requested security screening.

Granted under auditor supervision.

Routeholders requested exclusion of public statements.

Denied for statements relating to retained benefit or settlement position.

Civil logistics requested permission to produce explanatory memoranda.

Granted, with source records.

The smiles did not vanish. They learned to hide better.

After session, Kett approached Ira at the evidence table.

"You understand what you have done."

"Usually not until later."

"You have made benefit sound like guilt."

"No. You heard guilt because you recognized benefit."

His expression hardened.

"Inherited benefit will punish survival."

"Only if survival required retaining someone else's unreviewed harm."

"That is every civilization."

Ira looked at him. "Then every civilization should keep better records."

He left without answering.

Bayo appeared beside her. "That was almost a good line."

"Almost?"

"Marr would cut civilization."

Marr, behind them, said, "I would cut every."

Ira almost laughed. It felt dangerous and human.

By evening, production notices began returning with extensions, objections, privilege flags, sealed annex claims, and one accidental spreadsheet from a routeholder subsidiary showing post-settlement liability reserve reductions after Cinder Gate claims closure.

Bayo opened it and went very still.

"Ira."

She came over.

The numbers were simple.

Exposure before settlement closure.

Exposure after closure.

Reserve released.

Route expansion capital funded.

New corridor contracts.

Benefit retained.

Not metaphor.

Money.

Marr looked over Ira's shoulder.

"There is your smile," she said.

Outside in the corridor, someone had changed the signs again.

WHO BENEFITED?

Under it, in smaller letters:

COUNT IT.

Ira returned to the board and added a new column.

Amount.

The harder case had found its teeth.

Chapter 40: Moonwake Holds Space

Moonwake answered after everyone had learned to stop waiting for it.

The signal arrived in the middle of a production fight about reserve releases, corridor expansion capital, and whether a routeholder subsidiary's accidental spreadsheet could be clawed back as privileged because embarrassment had occurred before intent. Bayo was arguing with a clerk. Marr was arguing with Kett. Ira was arguing with a column that refused to reconcile because one settlement currency had been indexed to oxygen futures and another to route hours.

Then every screen in Evidence Review went gray.

For one second, Ira thought attack.

The next second, Shoals relay authentication appeared.

Vela's face cut through, compressed and bright-eyed.

"Stop what you are doing."

Marr looked up. "We are in the middle of three fights."

"You have a fourth."

A new signal mark opened on the wall:

MOONWAKE DELAYED RELAY

ORIGIN: MOONWAKE HOLDING HABITAT

CHAIN: Oru-Kel-Under-Quiet / Shoals-Orison Refusal / Sable Array Tribunal Mirror

MESSAGE AGE: 79 years, 11 months, 6 days, local adjusted

Bayo stood so quickly his chair hit the floor.

"Moonwake received the warning."

Vela said, "Moonwake received something. We authenticate before poetry."

Marr pointed at Bayo. "Pick up the chair."

He did, badly.

The tribunal convened within twenty minutes. Om entered with no robe again, only the dark suit he wore when the proceeding had become too structural for costume. Kett objected to surprise evidence. The military advocate objected to unreviewed external relay. Routeholder counsel objected to the signal chain. Lael Shun objected to everyone using the word surprise when Moonwake had been missing for eighty years.

Om allowed Vela to present authentication foundation.

The public gallery was open. That mattered. Moonwake was not a party. It was a wound beyond the tribunal's original map: a holding habitat that had dropped out of contact during the Fracture Wars and become rumor, memorial, liability footnote, and occasionally political myth. Some believed it had received no warning. Some believed it had refused evacuation. Some believed it had been sacrificed to hide Cinder Gate's failure. The tribunal had not yet had enough evidence to ask.

Now the signal sat on the wall.

Vela spoke carefully.

"Shoals received a delayed relay through an old lunar scatter path activated by the Orison refusal trace review. The relay contains Moonwake holding habitat's automated acknowledgement of Oru-Kel's carried warning and a human message appended approximately nine minutes later."

"Automated acknowledgement first," Om said.

Vela displayed it.

WARNING CHAIN RECEIVED.

ORIGIN ACCEPTED: Oru-Kel-Under-Quiet.

CONVERSION REFUSED.

HOLDING SPACE.

REQUESTING PROOF OF CINDER GATE STATUS BEFORE ROUTE COMMITMENT.

Ira heard the gallery react to holding space.

Not fleeing. Not refusing. Holding.

Moonwake had not ignored the warning. It had preserved space and asked for proof before moving.

Kett stood. "This does not concern Cinder Gate abandonment."

Ira turned before she could stop herself.

Om spoke first. "Counsel, sit down before that sentence ages poorly."

Kett sat.

Vela continued. "The human message is damaged. We have partial audio, partial text reconstruction, and habitat status telemetry. I recommend limited play."

Om nodded.

The audio began with static and breathing.

A woman's voice entered, thin but steady.

"This is Moonwake holding coordination. We receive the carried warning. We are not converting it to route. We are holding space for Cinder Gate dependents if proof arrives. Repeat: holding space. We need Cinder Gate status. We need claimant lists. We need dependent counts. Do not send advisory routing without proof. We can hold six hours at ration depth. Maybe eight if ring three accepts dark."

The sound fractured.

Another voice, farther away: "Tell them children first if they have no guardians."

The first voice returned.

"Children without guardians are claimants under Moonwake emergency sanctuary. We will sign."

Mara Venn was in the gallery.

She covered her mouth.

Ira gripped the table.

The message continued.

"Cinder Gate, send proof. Send names if you have names. Send clusters if names are lost. Send anything. We will make legal room after. Do not wait for perfect chain."

The audio broke into static, then returned once more.

"We are holding."

Then nothing.

Om did not move.

No one did.

For days, the tribunal had heard categories that delayed people because guardianship was unresolved. Moonwake had offered to solve the category from the other side: send them, we will sign.

Bayo whispered, "They had a door."

Marr did not correct him.

Kett recovered first because that was what Kett did.

"Magistrate, the message is tragic but not dispositive. There is no proof Cinder Gate received Moonwake's request."

Vela said, "Correct."

"No proof the holding space remained available."

"Correct."

"No proof routing to Moonwake was physically possible under correction pressure."

"Correct."

"No proof any defendant in this proceeding suppressed it."

"Not yet."

Kett looked almost relieved by her discipline.

Ira was grateful for it too. The message had enough force without overclaiming.

Om asked, "What does the telemetry show?"

Vela displayed habitat status.

Moonwake had shifted to ration depth. Ring three had accepted dark. Nonessential agriculture light dropped. Thermal margins narrowed. Docking corridor two remained reserved for external arrivals for five hours, forty-two minutes.

"They really held," Bayo said.

Vela nodded. "Yes."

She opened a secondary pane. "The holding order also triggered internal consent records."

The screen showed Moonwake's ring votes in clipped habitat language:

RING ONE: HOLDING SPACE ACCEPTED. RATION DEPTH TWO.

RING TWO: HOLDING SPACE ACCEPTED. MEDICAL LIGHT PRESERVED.

RING THREE: DARK ACCEPTED FOR SIX HOURS. REVIEW AT FOUR.

RING FOUR: OBJECTION ENTERED; OVERRIDDEN BY SANCTUARY MAJORITY.

DOCKING CREW: VOLUNTEERS SIXTEEN. PRESSURE SUITS NINE. CHILD RESTRAINTS IMPROVISED FROM CARGO WEB.

Ira read the last line twice.

Child restraints. Not as punishment. As preparation to receive bodies too small for standard transit harness. Someone on Moonwake had looked at a warning, believed just enough, and cut cargo web into rescue gear before proof arrived.

Marr asked, "Did they know how many?"

"No," Vela said. "The request asks for dependent counts because they did not know whether to hold for twenty, two hundred, or none."

"And still they held."

"Yes."

Kett said nothing. That was wise.

Mara Venn had both hands folded in her lap. Ira could see the pressure of her fingers from counsel table.

The military advocate asked whether Moonwake's holding created risk to its own population.

"Yes," Vela said.

"Could that have killed people?"

"Yes."

"Did it?"

"We do not know."

Lael Shun leaned forward. "Holding space is not harmless. That is why it is evidence."

Om asked for role-level relay analysis.

Vela displayed the path. Moonwake's request had attempted transmission toward Cinder Gate command, civil logistics, and routeholder navigation support. Two routes failed under war interference. One reached a relay buffer controlled by routeholder navigation support legacy channel.

The room changed.

Kett stood slowly. "Buffer receipt is not human receipt."

"Correct," Vela said.

"Legacy channel may have been unattended."

"Possible."

"Automated systems may have discarded it as stale."

"Possible."

"You cannot say routeholders knew."

"No."

Ira looked at the beneficiary board.

Direct reliance. Knowing retention after notice. Current derivative use.

Moonwake added another question: who failed to preserve proof that someone had offered space?

Marr saw her looking. "Careful."

Ira nodded.

She stood. "Magistrate, the survivors ask preservation of all relay buffers, discard logs, stale-message protocols, and routeholder legacy channel intake settings for the Moonwake request."

Kett objected to burden.

Om granted preservation and deferred production burden.

Nemi Aral stood. "Civil logistics will search for claimant list transmission attempts to Moonwake."

The military advocate reluctantly agreed to search repair carrier comms for Moonwake routing references.

Kett preserved objection.

Then Moonwake's final text reconstruction appeared. Vela had not meant to display it yet; Ira saw the flicker of surprise on her face. Shoals compression corrected itself and the line came through.

IF CINDER GATE CANNOT SEND PROOF, SEND WITNESS.

The chamber took the words in silence.

Send witness.

Eighty years later, the tribunal was full of them.

Om ordered the line sealed pending authentication, then immediately ordered a public summary because sealing those words entirely would have been another kind of injury.

After session, Mara Venn asked to hear the message again in a private family room. Ira attended as counsel observer. Vela replayed the line:

Children without guardians are claimants under Moonwake emergency sanctuary. We will sign.

Mara did not cry this time.

"We invented guardians," she said. "They were offering to become them."

Ira said nothing.

"Did Cinder know?"

"We do not know."

"Find out."

"Yes."

It was another hard promise.

In Evidence Review, Bayo added Moonwake to the sequence chart:

Moonwake held docking space for Cinder Gate dependents; requested proof, names, clusters, or witness; routeholder legacy buffer received request, human receipt unproven.

Marr edited nothing.

That silence was its own event.

The corridor signs changed by evening.

MOONWAKE HELD SPACE.

WHO CLOSED THE DOOR?

Ira stood before the new sign and felt the case widen again.

Not because Moonwake proved abandonment.

Because it proved an alternative had existed long enough to ask for evidence.

And somewhere between a held door and a dead child, the proof had failed to travel.

Chapter 41: The Beneficiary Map Burns

The beneficiary map burned at 09:17, during a public explanation of the word amount.

Bayo was at the evidence wall, which should have been the first sign that something would go wrong. He had insisted he could explain reserve releases without making the survivors hate arithmetic. Marr had doubted this. Ira had doubted it more. Om had allowed the demonstration because public summaries of sealed financial records had begun producing rumors stranger than truth.

The map filled the chamber wall in restrained colors Marr had approved after removing all of Bayo's heat gradients. Institutions sat in columns. Arrows marked action, closure, retained benefit, current derivative. Amounts appeared only where authenticated: released liability reserves, corridor expansion capital, settlement exposure reductions, salvage disposal payments, pension denials, template maintenance budgets.

No metaphors.

Numbers.

Bayo pointed to the routeholder subsidiary row.

"This figure does not prove liability. It proves reserve release after claims closure. The legal question is whether that release was retained after notice of contamination."

Someone in the gallery shouted, "Money from children."

Om struck the bench. "Order."

Bayo swallowed and did not look at Ira. "The record does not support that phrase as stated."

Marr's pen paused.

Good, Ira thought.

Then the wall went white.

Not blank. White. Too bright, as if every pixel had been ordered to become a small sun.

Ira heard Bayo say, "That is not me."

The evidence wall cracked from the center outward.

Sound arrived after light: a flat pressure slap, glass laminate popping, people gasping, security shouting, Om's bench alarm, the public gallery surging and then freezing because nobody knew which direction was safer. The beneficiary map vanished behind a wash of burning data, colors shredding into lines that looked almost like rain.

Ira hit the floor because Marr hit her first.

Heat passed over the counsel table.

The chamber lights dropped to emergency red.

For three seconds, the tribunal became Cinder Gate in miniature: alarms, bad air, bodies sorted by proximity to exits, people calling names before instructions arrived.

Then training fought panic.

Security sealed the doors.

Om's voice cut through the alarm. "Medical first. No one leaves until pressure and threat are cleared. Counsel, status."

Marr was already up. "Senn alive. Anik?"

Bayo was on one knee beneath the smoking wall, blood running from a cut above his eyebrow. "I hate arithmetic."

"Alive," Marr said.

"Offended," Bayo corrected.

Ira's ears rang. The wall still smoked. The map was gone. Not merely hidden. The display core had fused through its central stack.

Kett stood at routeholder table, pale with fury or fear. Nemi Aral had her arms around a clerk. The military advocate was shouting at security. Lael Shun sat very still, dark lens recording until a guard told her to stop and she said, "No."

Om ordered the chamber feed preserved.

The feed had already failed.

That was the second sign.

Inoya found the third before the smoke cleared.

"The feed did not fail from heat," she said.

Om turned toward her. "Explain."

Inoya knelt beside an emergency access port with a hand scanner braced against her wrist. "The chamber recorder kept local capture for nine seconds after public feed loss. Public distribution cut first. Internal audit capture cut second. Wall overload came third."

Kett said, "Meaning?"

"Meaning the room was meant to remember less than the people in it."

The security chief objected to that as speculation.

Inoya did not look at him. "Then I will say it technically. The interruption sequence prioritized witness-facing record channels over physical damage. Someone wanted the public record blind before the display burned."

Bayo pressed a cloth to his forehead and muttered, "So not panic code."

"No," Inoya said. "A panic code would preserve audit first and display last. This did the reverse."

Ira looked at the gallery. People were still checking one another for burns, repeating names, touching sleeves, counting who had remained. The attack had not merely tried to frighten them. It had tried to make their fear unverifiable.

Vela appeared on Ira's private tablet before the public relay returned.

"Do not touch the wall."

Ira coughed. "Vela?"

"Do not touch the wall. Do not reboot. Do not let Sable Array security image it first."

"It burned."

"The visible map burned. The source did not."

Marr heard and leaned close. "Say that again."

Vela's face sharpened through compression. "The source did not burn. Bayo's display was generated from my uncropped archive mirror and local court extracts. The attacker hit the visual layer, not the archive. Preserve power state."

Ira looked at the smoking evidence wall.

For once, uncropped was not a philosophy. It was survival.

Om convened emergency response from the bench because moving him would have made the room feel abandoned. Medical teams entered under security escort. Two spectators had flash burns. One clerk had a broken wrist. Bayo refused treatment until Marr threatened to have him declared temporarily irrational, which worked because he respected procedure more than pain.

Sable Array security tried to take control of the wall.

Ira stood in front of it.

"No."

The security chief stared at her. "Counsel, move."

"Tribunal evidence. Magistrate's preservation order. No reboot, no extraction, no imaging outside auditor supervision."

"There may be an active threat."

"Then preserve the threat."

Om said, "Chief, you will secure the chamber perimeter. Auditor Inoya will supervise technical preservation."

The chief looked at Inoya.

Inoya had appeared from the side corridor with a portable forensic kit and the expression of someone whose annoyance had matured into purpose.

"Touch the wall without my seal," she said, "and I will make your procedure a public exhibit."

The chief stepped back.

Lael Shun approached the ruined display after Inoya cleared the immediate hazard. "This was not to destroy evidence."

Kett barked, "How could you possibly know that?"

Lael turned. "Because serious destruction would target source, mirrors, court extracts, Vela's Shoals relay, and Bayo's working files. This targeted the thing the public could see."

Bayo, while a medic sealed his eyebrow, said, "They burned the picture."

Ira looked at the gallery, at the injured spectators, at the corridor signs visible through cracked chamber glass.

"They burned confidence."

Marr said, "Do not get poetic in an attack scene."

"Noted."

Then the public wall outside the chamber changed.

Every corridor display flashed the same message:

BENEFIT IS NOT GUILT.

The phrase repeated three times, then vanished.

No one in the chamber spoke.

Kett looked genuinely horrified. That mattered. Not innocence. But something. The attack had spoken in language too close to his argument, and he understood the danger before anyone accused him.

"Routeholder parties condemn this," he said.

Om looked at him. "I have not asked."

"I am saying it before anyone mistakes silence."

"Noted."

Ira believed him, which did not comfort her.

Vela sent the uncropped map backup to every authorized mirror under Om's emergency order. Bayo's working files matched. Court extracts matched. The accidental spreadsheet remained preserved. The visible map could be rebuilt.

But the chamber could not be unburned.

At 11:02, Om made a public statement from the damaged bench.

"An attack on the display of evidence is not an argument about evidence. The tribunal will preserve the damaged system, reconstruct the map from source records, and continue. No party will benefit from interruption."

The gallery, shaken and bandaged, did not cheer.

They stayed.

That was stronger.

In Evidence Review, relocated to a backup room, Bayo reopened the beneficiary map from Vela's mirror. The rows returned one by one. Routeholder reserve release. Civil logistics template lineage. Ash Belt emergency authority. Salvage disposal payments. Glassward election petition. Sable Array oxygen notice. Moonwake buffer. Amount.

The map lived.

It looked smaller on the backup display.

It also looked harder to kill.

Ira added a new row:

Attack on visible beneficiary display.

Action during proceeding: destruction of public evidence visualization, message "BENEFIT IS NOT GUILT."

Benefit attempted: public confusion, intimidation, delay, narrowing of inquiry.

Evidence source: damaged wall, corridor display capture, Vela uncropped mirror, auditor forensic preservation.

Marr read it.

"Attempted benefit is not retained benefit."

"I know."

"Make a separate incident table."

Ira did.

Outside, security finally removed the corridor signs because broken glass had scattered over them. Survivors objected until medical staff explained that people were bleeding through their shoes.

By afternoon, new signs appeared beyond the security line.

THE MAP LIVES.

COUNT IT TWICE.

Bayo saw the feed and touched the bandage above his eye.

"Count it twice is good."

Marr said, "For a sign."

"For a motion?"

"No."

Ira looked at the rebuilt map and the incident table beside it.

The attacker had failed to erase the evidence.

But they had proved the evidence had become dangerous enough to burn.

Chapter 42: Sable Array Locks Down

Sable Array locked down in layers.

First came the polite layer: visitors were asked to remain in assigned tribunal sectors while safety checks continued. Then the honest layer: pressure doors closed between public corridors and transit spokes. Then the old layer, the one everyone recognized before the announcement finished: movement required authorization, groups were to remain where they were, and public safety would manage air, access, and information until threat resolution.

The corridor heard the words and became still.

No one needed to explain why.

Ira stood in the backup Evidence Review room while the station voice repeated instructions. Bayo sat on the floor with a bandage over one eye, refusing a chair because he said chairs had betrayed him twice. Marr was at the door speaking to tribunal security. Om remained in the damaged chamber. Vela occupied three screens at once, distributing mirror hashes to Shoals, Glassward, and Auditor Inoya's external court.

"Lockdown protocol resembles the suspended public safety template," Bayo said.

"Of course it does," Ira said.

"That was not a legal analysis."

"I am off duty for twelve seconds."

"No, you are not."

He was right.

The first real conflict began outside the family consent room, where survivors refused relocation to a lower corridor. Security wanted to clear the area because the damaged wall might have a second-stage device. Families refused because the salvage hold review items were inside and because movement orders had become morally radioactive.

The second conflict began at the routeholder table, where private security contractors attempted to escort their principals to a sealed transit lift under diplomatic access.

The third began when an Ash Belt veterans' militia, present as observers after Tovin Rees's testimony, blocked the lift with their bodies and said no one with sealed records left before the public did.

By the time Ira reached the corridor, three kinds of authority were shouting at each other.

Sable Array security had weapons.

Tribunal security had jurisdiction.

The veterans had mass, age, and the moral confidence of people whose pensions had already been denied.

Survivors had signs, injuries, and no patience left for being sorted.

Marr arrived beside Ira. "Do not step into the middle."

"I wasn't going to."

"You were."

"Only internally."

"That joke is aging."

Om's voice came through the corridor speakers, not the station system. That mattered.

"This is Magistrate Om. Tribunal emergency authority is active. No party, counsel, witness, observer, or institutional representative will depart Sable Array tribunal sectors without tribunal clearance. Public safety will coordinate with tribunal security, not replace it. Medical need outranks evidentiary convenience. Evidence preservation outranks institutional comfort. No group will be relocated without reason stated on record."

The corridor breathed, but not easily.

The routeholder contractors did not move.

Neither did the veterans.

Ira saw Kett behind the contractors, pale and furious.

"Counsel Senn," he called, "this is unlawful detention."

"Then file," Marr said before Ira could answer.

Kett looked at her. "With what network?"

Vela's voice came from Ira's tablet. "External filing mirror remains available for emergency rights petitions. Counsel Kett has access."

Kett's expression shifted. He had expected the answer to be no. So had the people around him.

Marr said, "File."

He did not.

The veterans' leader, a woman with a burn-scarred scalp and Sable Knife insignia pinned upside down, lowered her arms by a fraction. "If they can file, they can wait."

That became the first fragile rule.

If you can file, you can wait.

The second rule came from Mara Venn, who had emerged from the family consent room holding a sealed item receipt, not the item itself.

"Medical first," she said.

No one argued with her.

She pointed to the injured spectators, then to Bayo, then to an old man whose portable scrubber had begun flashing amber. The veterans made a lane. Security used it. No one called it a queue cluster.

Ira noticed that and wrote it down later.

The third rule came from Om, relayed through tribunal security:

Every closed door gets a named reason.

Tribunal security tried to make that a sentence in the incident stream. Ira made them make it a ledger.

The first card went on Door A-12, where a pressure sweep had sealed one corridor and left two survivor groups staring at each other through reinforced glass.

DOOR A-12: PRESSURE SWEEP, NOT CLASS SEPARATION.

An Ash Belt woman read it, tapped the glass once with the back of her knuckles, and stopped shouting. On the other side a routeholder aide stopped pretending not to hear her.

The second card went beside the transit lift where the veterans had formed their line.

TRANSIT LIFT 3: SEALED FOR THREAT CONTAINMENT, NOT PRIORITY EVACUATION.

Kett's junior counsel objected to the phrasing until Marr asked whether he preferred the transcript to say "private escape prevention." The objection withdrew itself.

The third card took longer because every word touched someone living.

FAMILY CONSENT ROOM: OCCUPIED BY CHOICE OF AFFECTED FAMILIES; NO RELOCATION ABSENT SPECIFIC HAZARD.

Mara Venn read that one before it was posted. She crossed out passive custody and wrote choice hard enough to dent the backing board. No clerk corrected her.

The fourth card went on the routeholder sector hold.

ROUTEHOLDER SECTOR HOLD: EVIDENCE PRESERVATION AND EQUAL ACCESS TO EMERGENCY FILING MIRROR.

That did not make the held principals less angry. It did make their anger fileable.

The fifth card stayed green.

MEDICAL LANE GREEN: OPEN FOR SCRUBBER FAILURE, BURN CARE, MOBILITY SUPPORT.

Ira watched the cards change the corridor by inches. The reason did not make the door kind. It made the door answerable.

Bayo looked up when Ira repeated it. "That is good."

"For a sign?"

"For civilization, if Marr lets me keep every."

Marr said, "I heard that."

The lockdown stretched.

Hours in a station without horizon did not pass. They accumulated. Sable Array's old metal ticked with thermal adjustment. The burned chamber smelled of plastic and hot glass. The backup Evidence Review room became command, clinic, archive, and argument pit. Vela kept mirrors alive. Inoya supervised forensic preservation. Lael Shun bullied the Remnant Council into accepting emergency external custody of duplicate records. Oriole-of-Archive sent one message: Sera stable, no testimony.

Ira read it three times.

Stable. No testimony.

Both mattered.

At 16:20, Sable Array public safety identified the attack vector: a maintenance display driver installed during the overnight chamber repair after Sera's witness clock stopped. It had entered through a contractor credential tied to a shell company funded by a routeholder litigation services pool, two intermediaries removed.

Kett demanded the finding be sealed until verified.

Om refused sealing but allowed cautionary language.

Kett then did something Ira did not expect. He ordered the routeholder principals to remain under tribunal control and produce the litigation services pool records voluntarily by midnight.

One principal protested.

Kett turned on him. "Someone burned my argument on a wall. Sit down."

Ira heard it from across the room.

She believed him again.

Still not comfort.

At 18:05, a rumor spread that militias were taking over the station. At 18:12, the veterans' leader handed her sidearm to tribunal security and told every armed observer to do the same or leave the survivor corridor. Three refused. Other veterans removed them without drama.

At 19:30, survivors began reading names aloud to keep the corridor calm.

Not all names were from Cinder Gate anymore.

Senna Ro.

Tovin Rees's dead friend whose name had arrived by private addendum: Pellan Orr.

Esta Sol.

Sivin Vale.

Unknown A-4.

Unknown medical nontransfer nine.

Moonwake Holding Coordinator, name not yet authenticated.

Names became structure where orders could not.

Ira found Om near midnight in the damaged chamber, sitting beneath the ruined evidence wall. The bench lights were off. He looked smaller without them.

"Magistrate."

"Counsel."

"You should be in medical review."

"So should half the station."

"That is not an answer."

"No. It is a habit."

She stood beside the bench and looked at the burned wall. In the emergency dim, the fused glass reflected them both badly.

Om said, "This is what happens when a tribunal becomes real."

"It gets attacked?"

"It becomes worth attacking."

Ira hated the sentence because it sounded like comfort and was not.

"Will the lockdown hold?"

"For tonight."

"And tomorrow?"

"Tomorrow everyone will call what they did today necessary."

She thought of Cinder Gate, of all the people who had likely done the same by smaller degrees until necessity became archive.

"Then we record tonight carefully."

Om looked at her.

"Yes," he said. "We do."

At 02:11, Sable Array announced controlled lockdown continuation under tribunal coordination. No one cheered. No one trusted it. But the doors had reasons posted beside them. Medical lanes remained open. Filing mirrors remained live. Evidence mirrors held. Armed observers were disarmed or documented. Institutional principals remained in sector. Survivors were not moved.

It was not freedom.

It was a better cage than the first one offered.

Ira entered that sentence nowhere official.

In the progress notes, Bayo wrote:

Lockdown contained without loss of tribunal evidence.

Marr changed contained to stabilized.

Ira let it stand.

Outside the damaged chamber, someone taped one new sign to the temporary barrier:

EVERY CLOSED DOOR GETS A REASON.

Security left it there.

Chapter 43: The Advocate Becomes Evidence

They introduced Ira's family at 09:04.

Not in person. That would have been too honest. The filing arrived as a sealed conflict supplement from a routeholder subsidiary that had never before admitted it possessed historical ration archives. The title was careful enough to draw blood without splashing:

Potential Advocate Interest Arising from Ash Belt Ration Continuity Benefit.

Bayo saw it first and said, "No."

Marr took the tablet from him.

Her face changed by nothing.

That was how Ira knew it was bad.

"Give it to me," Ira said.

Marr did not.

"Marr."

"Sit down first."

"No."

Marr looked at her over the tablet. "Then stand professionally."

Ira stood professionally.

The supplement alleged that Ira Senn's grandmother, Lio Senn, had received Ash Belt emergency ration continuity status three months after Cinder Gate through a settlement-linked labor stabilization program. The program had been funded in part by route expansion capital released after Cinder Gate claims closure. Lio's ration status had allowed her family to remain in the Belt rather than transfer to a debt dormitory. Ira's legal education stipend, two generations later, had come from a descendant civic fund seeded by the same stabilization pool.

The filing did not say Ira was corrupt.

It did not need to.

It said she was a beneficiary.

The word looked different when it wore her name.

Vela asked for five minutes before the hearing opened. Om gave her three.

She used them to put the archive chain on the small review wall, not the public evidence wall. The record did not arrive as a confession. It arrived as an accounting branch: ration continuity, labor stabilization, civic education, emergency residence, all braided through postwar funds with names designed to lose origin without quite lying.

"Source confidence?" Marr asked.

"Medium-high on the ration award," Vela said. "Medium on the fund genealogy. Low on intent, because intent is not in the record."

"Conveniently absent," Bayo said.

"Commonly absent," Lael corrected from the doorway. "That is how survival programs become clean. Nobody writes the sentence that makes everyone look at the source."

Ira kept her hands flat on the table. Lio Senn's name sat in a row with dates beside it. The letters looked too small for a woman who had once mended a broken ration heater with a spoon handle and a curse.

Marr drew four columns on a slate because she did not trust anyone in the building to remember categories under pressure.

RECIPIENT. CONTROLLER. CONCEALER. PRESENT USER.

"If Kett collapses these, he wins a moral panic," Marr said. "If we deny the first one, he wins a credibility fight. We concede recipient if proved. We fight controller, concealer, and present use unless evidence supports them."

Ira read the columns until they stopped being law and became a map of her own fear.

"Where am I?" she asked.
